  2925	[1] FINE SPICED WINE
  2926	  _CONDITUM PARADOXUM_
  2927	
  2928	THE COMPOSITION OF [this] EXCELLENT SPICED WINE [is as follows]. INTO
  2929	A COPPER BOWL PUT 6 SEXTARII [1] OF HONEY AND 2 SEXTARII OF WINE; HEAT
  2930	ON A SLOW FIRE, CONSTANTLY STIRRING THE MIXTURE WITH A WHIP. AT THE
  2931	BOILING POINT ADD A DASH OF COLD WINE, RETIRE FROM STOVE AND SKIM.
  2932	REPEAT THIS TWICE OR THREE TIMES, LET IT REST TILL THE NEXT DAY, AND
  2933	SKIM AGAIN. THEN ADD 4 OZS. OF CRUSHED PEPPER [2], 3 SCRUPLES OF
  2934	MASTICH, A DRACHM EACH OF [nard or laurel] LEAVES AND SAFFRON, 5
  2935	DRACHMS OF ROASTED DATE STONES CRUSHED AND PREVIOUSLY SOAKED IN WINE
  2936	TO SOFTEN THEM. WHEN THIS IS PROPERLY DONE ADD 18 SEXTARII OF LIGHT
  2937	WINE. TO CLARIFY IT PERFECTLY, ADD [crushed] CHARCOAL [3] TWICE OR AS
  2938	OFTEN AS NECESSARY WHICH WILL DRAW [the residue] TOGETHER [and
  2939	carefully strain or filter through the charcoal].
  2940	
  2941	    [1] _Sextarii._ Tor. _partes XV_; G.-V. _pondo XV_;
  2942	    List. _partes XV ... pondo lib.... qui continent
  2943	    sextarios sex_. One sextarius (a "sixth") equals about
  2944	    1-1/2 pint English.
  2945	
  2946	    [2] Pepper. _Piperis uncias IV_--ordinarily our black or
  2947	    white pepper grains, but in connection with honey,
  2948	    sweets, and so forth, the term "pepper" may just as well
  2949	    stand for our allspice, or even for any spicing in
  2950	    general.
  2951	
  2952	    [3] Charcoal. Still a favorite filterer for liquors.
  2953	
  2954	    List. Apicius is correct in starting his book with this
  2955	    formula, as all meals were started with this sort of
  2956	    mixed drink.
  2957	
  2958	    Tor. deviates from the other texts in that he elaborates
  2959	    on the cooking process.
  2960	
  2961	
  2962	[2] HONEY REFRESHER FOR TRAVELERS
  2963	  _CONDITUM MELIZOMUM _[1]_ VIATORIUM_
  2964	
  2965	THE WAYFARER'S HONEY REFRESHER (SO CALLED BECAUSE IT GIVES ENDURANCE
  2966	AND STRENGTH TO PEDESTRIANS) [2] WITH WHICH TRAVELERS ARE REFRESHED BY
  2967	THE WAYSIDE IS MADE IN THIS MANNER: FLAVOR HONEY WITH GROUND PEPPER
  2968	AND SKIM. IN THE MOMENT OF SERVING PUT HONEY IN A CUP, AS MUCH AS IS
  2969	DESIRED TO OBTAIN THE RIGHT DEGREE OF SWEETNESS, AND MIX SPICED WINE
  2970	NOT MORE THAN A NEEDED QUANTITY; ALSO ADD SOME WINE TO THE SPICED
  2971	HONEY TO FACILITATE ITS FLOW AND THE MIXING.
  2972	
  2973	    [1] Tor. _Melirhomum_; _non extat_. G.-V. M.
  2974	    _perpetuum_, i.e., having good keeping qualities.
  2975	
  2976	    [2] Tor. reads thus whereas others apply "endurance" to
  2977	    the honey itself. The honey could not be preserved
  2978	    (_perpetuum_) by the addition of pepper. Any addition,
  2979	    as a matter of fact, would hasten its deterioration
  2980	    unless the honey were boiled and sealed tight, which the
  2981	    original takes for granted.
  2982	
  2983	
  2984	
  2985	II
  2986	
  2987	
  2988	[3] ROMAN VERMOUTH
  2989	  _ABSINTHIUM ROMANUM_ [1]
  2990	
  2991	ROMAN VERMOUTH [or Absinth] IS MADE THUS: ACCORDING TO THE RECIPE OF
  2992	CAMERINUM [2] YOU NEED WORMWOOD FROM SANTO [3] FOR ROMAN VERMOUTH OR,
  2993	AS A SUBSTITUTE, WORMWOOD FROM THE PONTUS [4] CLEANED AND CRUSHED, 1
  2994	THEBAN OUNCE [5] OF IT, 6 SCRUPLES OF MASTICH, 3 EACH OF [nard]
  2995	LEAVES, COSTMARY [6] AND SAFFRON AND 18 QUARTS OF ANY KIND OF MILD
  2996	WINE. [Filter cold] CHARCOAL IS NOT REQUIRED BECAUSE OF THE
  2997	BITTERNESS.
  2998	
  2999	    [1] G.-V. _Apsinthium_.
  3000	
  3001	    [2] The mention of a name in a recipe is very
  3002	    infrequent. Camerinum is a town in Umbria.
  3003	
  3004	    [3] Now Saintonge, Southern France.
  3005	
  3006	    [4] Black Sea Region.
  3007	
  3008	    [5] Weight of indefinite volume, from Thebæ, one of the
  3009	    several ancient cities by that name. List. thinks it is
  3010	    an Egyptian ounce, and that the author of the recipe
  3011	    must be an African.
  3012	
  3013	    [6] Wanting in Tor.; G.-V. _costi scripulos senos_.
  3014	
  3015	
  3016	
  3017	III
  3018	
  3019	
  3020	[4] ROSE WINE [1]
  3021	  _ROSATUM_
  3022	
  3023	MAKE ROSE WINE IN THIS MANNER: ROSE PETALS, THE LOWER WHITE PART
  3024	REMOVED, SEWED INTO A LINEN BAG AND IMMERSED IN WINE FOR SEVEN DAYS.
  3025	THEREUPON ADD A SACK OF NEW PETALS WHICH ALLOW TO DRAW FOR ANOTHER
  3026	SEVEN DAYS. AGAIN REMOVE THE OLD PETALS AND REPLACE THEM BY FRESH ONES
  3027	FOR ANOTHER WEEK; THEN STRAIN THE WINE THROUGH THE COLANDER. BEFORE
  3028	SERVING, ADD HONEY SWEETENING TO TASTE. TAKE CARE THAT ONLY THE BEST
  3029	PETALS FREE FROM DEW BE USED FOR SOAKING.
  3030	
  3031	    [1] Used principally as a laxative medicine. List. These
  3032	    wines compounded of roses and violets move the bowels
  3033	    strongly.
  3034	
  3035	
  3036	[5] VIOLET WINE
  3037	  _VIOLATIUM_
  3038	
  3039	IN A SIMILAR WAY AS ABOVE LIKE THE ROSE WINE VIOLET WINE IS MADE OF
  3040	FRESH VIOLETS, AND TEMPERED WITH HONEY, AS DIRECTED.
  3041	
  3042	
  3043	[6] ROSE WINE WITHOUT [1] ROSES
  3044	  _ROSATUM SINE ROSA_
  3045	
  3046	ROSE WINE WITHOUT ROSES IS MADE IN THIS FASHION: A PALM LEAF BASKET
  3047	FULL OF FRESH CITRUS LEAVES IS IMMERSED IN THE VAT OF NEW WINE BEFORE
  3048	FERMENTATION HAS SET IN. AFTER FORTY DAYS RETIRE THE LEAVES, AND, AS
  3049	OCCASION ARISES, SWEETEN THE WINE WITH HONEY, AND PASS IT UP FOR ROSE
  3050	WINE.
  3051	
  3052	    [1] A substitute.
  3053	
  3054	
  3055	
  3056	IV
  3057	
  3058	
  3059	[7] LIBURNIAN OIL
  3060	  _OLEUM LIBURNICUM_
  3061	
  3062	IN ORDER TO MAKE AN OIL SIMILAR TO THE LIBURNIAN OIL PROCEED AS
  3063	FOLLOWS: IN SPANISH OIL PUT [the following mixture of] ELECAMPANE,
  3064	CYPRIAN RUSH AND GREEN LAUREL LEAVES THAT ARE NOT TOO OLD, ALL OF IT
  3065	CRUSHED AND MACERATED AND REDUCED TO A FINE POWDER. SIFT THIS IN AND
  3066	ADD FINELY GROUND SALT AND STIR INDUSTRIOUSLY FOR THREE DAYS OR MORE.
  3067	THEN ALLOW TO SETTLE. EVERYBODY WILL TAKE THIS FOR LIBURNIAN OIL. [1]
  3068	
  3069	    [1] Like the above a flagrant case of food adulteration.
  3070	
  3071	
  3072	
  3073	V
  3074	
  3075	
  3076	[8] TO CLARIFY MUDDY WINE
  3077	  _VINUM EX ATRO CANDIDUM FACIES_
  3078	
  3079	PUT BEAN MEAL AND THE WHITES OF THREE EGGS IN A MIXING BOWL. MIX
  3080	THOROUGHLY WITH A WHIP AND ADD TO THE WINE, STIRRING FOR A LONG TIME.
  3081	THE NEXT DAY THE WINE WILL BE CLEAR [1]. ASHES OF VINES HAVE THE SAME
  3082	EFFECT.
  3083	
  3084	    [1] Ex Lister whose version we prefer. He says, _Alias
  3085	    die erit candidum_ while Tor. adds white salt, saying,
  3086	    _sal si adieceris candidum_, same as Tac. This is
  3087	    unusual, although the ancients have at times treated
  3088	    wine with sea water.
  3089	
  3090	
  3091	
  3092	VI
  3093	
  3094	
  3095	[9] TO IMPROVE A BROTH [1]
  3096	  _DE LIQUAMINE EMENDANDO_ [2]
  3097	
  3098	IF BROTH HAS CONTRACTED A BAD ODOR, PLACE A VESSEL UPSIDE DOWN AND
  3099	FUMIGATE IT WITH LAUREL AND CYPRESS AND BEFORE VENTILATING [3] IT,
  3100	POUR THE BROTH IN THIS VESSEL. IF THIS DOES NOT HELP MATTERS [4] AND
  3101	IF THE TASTE IS TOO PRONOUNCED, ADD HONEY AND FRESH SPIKENARD [5] TO
  3102	IT; THAT WILL IMPROVE IT. ALSO NEW MUST SHOULD BE LIKEWISE EFFECTIVE
  3103	[6].
  3104	
  3105	    [1] List. _Liquamen, id est, garum_. Goll. Fish sauce.
  3106	
  3107	    [2] Tor. _Qui liquamen corruptum corrigatur_.
  3108	
  3109	    [3] Dann. Ventilate it. Goll. Whip the sauce in fresh
  3110	    air.
  3111	
  3112	    [4] List., G.-V. _si salsum fuerit_--if this makes it
  3113	    too salty--Tor. _si hoc nihil effecerit_.
  3114	
  3115	    [5] Tor. _novem spicam immittas_; List. _Move spica_;
  3116	    Goll.-Dann. stir with a whip.
  3117	
  3118	    [6] A classic example of Apician confusion when one
  3119	    interpreter reads "s" for "f" and "_novem_" for "_move_"
  3120	    and another reads something else. Tor. is more correct
  3121	    than the others, but this formula is beyond redemption.
  3122	    Fate has decreed that ill-smelling broths shall be
  3123	    discarded.
  3124	
  3125	
  3126	
  3127	VII
  3128	
  3129	
  3130	[10] TO KEEP MEATS FRESH WITHOUT SALT FOR ANY LENGTH OF TIME
  3131	   _UT CARNES SINE SALE QUOVIS TEMPORE RECENTES SINT_
  3132	
  3133	COVER FRESH MEAT WITH HONEY, SUSPEND IT IN A VESSEL. USE AS NEEDED; IN
  3134	WINTER IT WILL KEEP BUT IN SUMMER IT WILL LAST ONLY A FEW DAYS. COOKED
  3135	MEAT MAY BE TREATED LIKEWISE.
  3136	
  3137	
  3138	[11] TO KEEP COOKED SIDES OF PORK OR BEEF OR TENDERLOINS
  3139	   _CALLUM PORCINUM VEL BUBULUM ET UNGUELLÆ COCTÆ UT DIU DURENT_
  3140	
  3141	PLACE THEM IN A PICKLE OF MUSTARD, VINEGAR, SALT AND HONEY, COVERING
  3142	MEAT ENTIRELY, AND WHEN READY TO USE YOU'LL BE SURPRISED.
  3143	
  3144	    V. Method still popular today for pickling raw meats.
  3145	    The originals treat of cooked meats (Tor. _nucula
  3146	    elixa_; G.-V. _unguellæ coctæ_; Tac. _nucella cocta_).
  3147	    Dispensing with the honey, we use more spices, whole
  3148	    pepper, cloves, bay leaves, also onions and root
  3149	    vegetables. Sometimes a little sugar and wine is added
  3150	    to this preparation which the French call _marinade_ and
  3151	    the Germans _Sauerbraten-Einlage_.
  3152	
  3153	
  3154	
  3155	VIII
  3156	
  3157	
  3158	[12] TO MAKE SALT MEAT SWEET
  3159	   _UT CARNEM SALSAM DULCEM FACIAS_
  3160	
  3161	YOU CAN MAKE SALT MEATS SWEET BY FIRST BOILING THEM IN MILK AND THEN
  3162	FINISHING THEM IN WATER.
  3163	
  3164	    V. Method still in practice today. Salt mackerel, finnan
  3165	    haddie, etc., are parboiled in milk prior to being
  3166	    boiled in water or broiled or fried.
  3167	
  3168	
  3169	
  3170	IX
  3171	
  3172	
  3173	[13] TO KEEP FRIED FISH
  3174	   _UT PISCES FRICTI DIU DURENT_
  3175	
  3176	IMMEDIATELY AFTER THEY ARE FRIED POUR HOT VINEGAR OVER THEM.
  3177	
  3178	    Dann. Exactly as we today with fried herring and river
  3179	    lamprey.
  3180	
  3181	
  3182	[14] TO KEEP OYSTERS
  3183	   _OSTREA UT DIU DURENT_
  3184	
  3185	FUMIGATE A VINEGAR BARREL WITH PITCH [1], WASH IT OUT WITH VINEGAR AND
  3186	STACK THE OYSTERS IN IT [2]
  3187	
  3188	    [1] Tor. _vas ascernum_, corrected on margin, _ab
  3189	    aceto_. List. _vas ab aceto_, which is correct. G.-V.
  3190	    _lavas ab aceto_; V. the oysters? unthinkable! Besides
  3191	    it would do no good.
  3192	
  3193	    [2] Goll. Take oysters out of the shell, place in
  3194	    vinegar barrel, sprinkle with laurel berries, fine salt,
  3195	    close tight. V. Goll's authority for this version is
  3196	    not found in our originals.
  3197	
  3198	    V. There is no way to keep live oysters fresh except in
  3199	    their natural habitat--salt water. Today we pack them in
  3200	    barrels, feed them with oatmeal, put weights on them--of
  3201	    no avail. The only way English oysters could have
  3202	    arrived fresh in Imperial Rome was in specially
  3203	    constructed bottoms of the galleys.
  3204	
  3205	
  3206	
  3207	X
  3208	
  3209	
  3210	[15] MAKING A LITTLE LASER GO A LONG WAY
  3211	   _UT NUCIA _[1]_ LASERIS TOTO TEMPORE UTARIS_
  3212	
  3213	PUT THE LASER [2] IN A SPACIOUS GLASS VESSEL; IMMERSE ABOUT 20 PINE
  3214	KERNELS [pignolia nuts]
  3215	
  3216	IF YOU NEED LASER FLAVOR, TAKE SOME NUTS, CRUSH THEM; THEY WILL IMPART
  3217	TO YOUR DISH AN ADMIRABLE FLAVOR. REPLACE THE USED NUTS WITH A LIKE
  3218	NUMBER OF FRESH ONES [3]
  3219	
  3220	    [1] List. and G.-V. _uncia_--ounce. Making an ounce of
  3221	    laser go a long way. Tor. _nucea_; Tac. _nucia_. Lister,
  3222	    fond of hair-splitting, is irreconcilably opposed to
  3223	    Tor., and berates Caspar Barthius for defending Tor.
  3224	    List. _Quam futilis sit in multis labor C. Barthii ut
  3225	    menda Torini passim sustineat, vel ex hoc loco
  3226	    intelligere licet: Et enim lege modo uncia pro nucea cum
  3227	    Humelbergio, & ista omnia glossemata vana sunt._
  3228	
  3229	    V. both readings, _uncia_ or _nucia_ are permissible,
  3230	    and make very little difference. We side with Tor. and
  3231	    Tac. because it takes more than an ounce of laser to
  3232	    carry out this experiment.
  3233	
  3234	    [2] _Laser_, _laserpitium_, cf. dictionary.
  3235	
  3236	    [3] V. This article illustrates how sparingly the
  3237	    ancients used the strong and pungent laser flavor [by
  3238	    some believed to be _asa foetida_] because it was very
  3239	    expensive, but principally because the Roman cooks
  3240	    worked economically and knew how to treat spices and
  3241	    flavors judiciously. This article alone should disperse
  3242	    for all time all stories of ancient Rome's extravagance
  3243	    in flavoring and seasoning dishes. It reminds of the
  3244	    methods used by European cooks to get the utmost use out
  3245	    of the expensive vanilla bean: they bury the bean in a
  3246	    can of powdered sugar. They will use the sugar only
  3247	    which has soon acquired a delicate vanilla perfume, and
  3248	    will replace the used sugar by a fresh supply. This is
  3249	    by far a superior method to using the often rank and
  3250	    adulterated "vanilla extract" readily bottled. It is
  3251	    more gastronomical and more economical. Most commercial
  3252	    extracts are synthetic, some injurious. To believe that
  3253	    any of them impart to the dishes the true flavor desired
  3254	    is of course ridiculous. The enormous consumption of
  3255	    such extracts however, is characteristic of our
  3256	    industrialized barbarism which is so utterly indifferent
  3257	    to the fine points in food. Today it is indeed hard for
  3258	    the public to obtain a real vanilla bean.
  3259	
  3260	    Cf. also notes regarding flavoring to Nos. 276-7, 345
  3261	    and 385.
  3262	
  3263	
  3264	
  3265	XI
  3266	
  3267	
  3268	[16] TO MAKE HONEY CAKES LAST
  3269	   _UT DULCIA DE MELLE DIU DURENT_
  3270	
  3271	TO MAKE HONEY CAKES THAT WILL KEEP TAKE WHAT THE GREEKS CALL YEAST [1]
  3272	AND MIX IT WITH THE FLOUR AND THE HONEY AT THE TIME WHEN MAKING THE
  3273	COOKY DOUGH.
  3274	
  3275	    [1] Tor. and Tac. _nechon_; G.-V. _cnecon_; Dann.
  3276	    _penion_.
  3277	
  3278	
  3279	[17] SPOILED HONEY MADE GOOD
  3280	   _UT MEL MALUM BONUM FACIAS_
  3281	
  3282	HOW BAD HONEY MAY BE TURNED INTO A SALEABLE ARTICLE IS TO MIX ONE PART
  3283	OF THE SPOILED HONEY WITH TWO PARTS OF GOOD HONEY.
  3284	
  3285	    List. _indigna fraus_! V. We all agree with Lister that
  3286	    this is contemptible business. This casts another light
  3287	    on the ancients' methods of food adulteration.
  3288	
  3289	
  3290	[18] TO TEST SPOILED HONEY
  3291	   _MEL CORRUPTUM UT PROBES_
  3292	
  3293	IMMERSE ELENCAMPANE IN HONEY AND LIGHT IT; IF GOOD, IT WILL BURN
  3294	BRIGHTLY.
  3295	
  3296	
  3297	
  3298	XII
  3299	
  3300	
  3301	[19] TO KEEP GRAPES
  3302	   _UVÆ UT DIU SERVENTUR_
  3303	
  3304	TAKE PERFECT GRAPES FROM THE VINES, PLACE THEM IN A VESSEL AND POUR
  3305	RAIN WATER OVER THEM THAT HAS BEEN BOILED DOWN ONE THIRD OF ITS
  3306	VOLUME. THE VESSEL MUST BE PITCHED AND SEALED WITH PLASTER, AND MUST
  3307	BE KEPT IN A COOL PLACE TO WHICH THE SUN HAS NO ACCESS. TREATED IN
  3308	THIS MANNER, THE GRAPES WILL BE FRESH WHENEVER YOU NEED THEM. YOU CAN
  3309	ALSO SERVE THIS WATER AS HONEY MEAD TO THE SICK.
  3310	
  3311	ALSO, IF YOU COVER THE GRAPES WITH BARLEY [bran] YOU WILL FIND THEM
  3312	SOUND AND UNINJURED.
  3313	
  3314	    V. We keep grapes in cork shavings, bran and saw dust.
  3315	
  3316	
  3317	[20] TO KEEP POMEGRANATES
  3318	   _UT MALA GRANATA DIU DURENT_ [1]
  3319	
  3320	STEEP THEM INTO HOT [sea] WATER, TAKE THEM OUT IMMEDIATELY AND HANG
  3321	THEM UP. [Tor.] THEY WILL KEEP.
  3322	
  3323	    [1] Tor. _conditura malorum Punicorum_; Tac. _mala
  3324	    granata_; G.-V. _mala et mala granata_.
  3325	
  3326	
  3327	[21] TO KEEP QUINCES
  3328	   _UT MALA CYDONIA DIU SERVENTUR_
  3329	
  3330	PICK OUT PERFECT QUINCES WITH STEMS [1] AND LEAVES. PLACE THEM IN A
  3331	VESSEL, POUR OVER HONEY AND DEFRUTUM [2] AND YOU'LL PRESERVE THEM FOR
  3332	A LONG TIME [3].
  3333	
  3334	    [1] V. Excellent idea, for the stems, if removed, would
  3335	    leave a wound in the fruit for the air to penetrate and
  3336	    to start fermentation. Cf. also the next formula.
  3337	
  3338	    [2] G.-V. _defritum_, from _defervitum_; _defrutum_ is
  3339	    new wine, spiced, boiled down to one half of its volume.
  3340	
  3341	    [3] This precept would not keep the fruit very long
  3342	    unless protected by a closefitting cover and
  3343	    sterilization. Cf. No. 24.
  3344	
  3345	
  3346	[22] TO PRESERVE FRESH FIGS, APPLES, PLUMS, PEARS AND CHERRIES
  3347	   _FICUM RECENTEM, MALA, PRUNA, PIRA, CERASIA UT DIU SERVES_
  3348	
  3349	SELECT THEM ALL VERY CAREFULLY WITH THE STEMS ON [1] AND PLACE THEM IN
  3350	HONEY SO THEY DO NOT TOUCH EACH OTHER.
  3351	
  3352	    [1] See the preceding formula.
  3353	
  3354	
  3355	[23] TO KEEP CITRON
  3356	   _CITRIA UT DIU DURENT_ [1]
  3357	
  3358	PLACE THEM IN A GLASS [2] VESSEL WHICH IS SEALED WITH PLASTER AND
  3359	SUSPENDED.
  3360	
  3361	    [1] Tor. _conditura malorum Medicorum quæ et citria
  3362	    dicuntur_. V. Not quite identified. Fruit coming from
  3363	    Asia Minor, Media or Persia, one of the many varieties
  3364	    of citrus fruit. Probably citron because of their size.
  3365	    Goll. Lemon-apples; Dann. lemons (oranges). List.
  3366	    _Scilicet mala, quæ Dioscorides Persica quoque & Medica,
  3367	    & citromala, Plinius item Assyria appellari dicit_.
  3368	
  3369	    [2] G.-V. _vas vitreum_; Tac. and Tor. _vas citrum_; V.
  3370	    a glass vessel could not be successfully sealed with
  3371	    plaster paris, and the experiment would fail; cf. note 3
  3372	    to No. 21.
  3373	
  3374	
  3375	[24] TO KEEP MULBERRIES
  3376	   _MORA UT DIU DURENT_
  3377	
  3378	MULBERRIES, IN ORDER TO KEEP THEM, MUST BE LAID INTO THEIR OWN JUICE
  3379	MIXED WITH NEW WINE [boiled down to one half] IN A GLASS VESSEL AND
  3380	MUST BE WATCHED ALL THE TIME [so that they do not spoil].
  3381	
  3382	    V. This and the foregoing formulæ illustrate the
  3383	    ancients' attempts at preserving foods, and they betray
  3384	    their ignorance of "processing" by heating them in
  3385	    hermetically sealed vessels, the principle of which was
  3386	    not discovered until 1810 by Appert which started the
  3387	    now gigantic industry of canning.
  3388	
  3389	
  3390	[25] TO KEEP POT HERBS
  3391	   [_H_]_OLERA UT DIU SERVENTUR_
  3392	
  3393	PLACE SELECTED POT HERBS, NOT TOO MATURE, IN A PITCHED VESSEL.
  3394	
  3395	
  3396	[26] TO PRESERVE SORREL OR SOUR DOCK
  3397	   _LAPÆ _[1]_ UT DIU SERVENTUR_
  3398	
  3399	TRIM AND CLEAN [the vegetable] PLACE THEM TOGETHER SPRINKLE MYRTLE
  3400	BERRIES BETWEEN, COVER WITH HONEY AND VINEGAR.
  3401	
  3402	ANOTHER WAY: PREPARE MUSTARD HONEY AND VINEGAR ALSO SALT AND COVER
  3403	THEM WITH THE SAME.
  3404	
  3405	    [1] The kind of vegetable to be treated here has not
  3406	    been sufficiently identified. List. and G.-V.
  3407	    _rapæ_--turnips--from _rapus_, seldom _rapa_,--a rape,
  3408	    turnip, navew. Tac. and Tor. _Lapæ_ (_lapathum_), kind
  3409	    of sorrel, monk's rhubarb, dock. Tor. explaining at
  3410	    length: _conditura Rumicis quod lapathon Græci, Latini
  3411	    Lapam quoque dicunt_.
  3412	
  3413	    V. Tor. is correct, or nearly so. Turnips, in the first
  3414	    place, are not in need of any special method of
  3415	    preservation. They keep very well in a cool,
  3416	    well-ventilated place; in fact they would hardly keep
  3417	    very long if treated in the above manner. These
  3418	    directions are better applied to vegetables like dock or
  3419	    monk's rhubarb. Lister, taking Humelbergii word for it,
  3420	    accepts "turnips" as the only truth; but he has little
  3421	    occasion to assail Torinus as he does: _Torinus lapam
  3422	    legit, & nullibi temeritatem suam atque inscientiam
  3423	    magis ostendit._
  3424	
  3425	    Now, if Torinus, according to Lister, "nowhere displays
  3426	    more nerve and ignorance" we can well afford to trust
  3427	    Torinus in cases such as this.
  3428	
  3429	
  3430	[27] TO KEEP TRUFFLES
  3431	   _TUBERA UT DIU SERVENTUR_
  3432	
  3433	THE TRUFFLES WHICH MUST NOT BE TOUCHED BY WATER ARE PLACED ALTERNATELY
  3434	IN DRY SAWDUST; SEAL THE VESSEL WITH PLASTER AND DEPOSIT IT IN A COOL
  3435	PLACE.
  3436	
  3437	    Dann. Clean [peel] the truffles ... in another vessel
  3438	    place the peelings, seal the vessels.... V. this would
  3439	    be the ruin of the truffles, unless they were
  3440	    "processed" in the modern way. Our originals have
  3441	    nothing that would warrant this interpretation.
  3442	
  3443	
  3444	[28] TO KEEP HARD-SKINNED PEACHES
  3445	   _DURACINA PERSICA UT DIU DURENT_
  3446	
  3447	SELECT THE BEST AND PUT THEM IN BRINE. THE NEXT DAY REMOVE THEM AND
  3448	RINSING THEM CAREFULLY SET THEM IN PLACE IN A VESSEL, SPRINKLE WITH
  3449	SALT AND SATURY AND IMMERSE IN VINEGAR.
  3450	
  3451	
  3452	
  3453	XIII
  3454	
  3455	
  3456	[29] SALTS FOR MANY [ILLS]
  3457	   _SALES CONDITOS AD MULTA_
  3458	
  3459	THESE SPICED SALTS ARE USED AGAINST INDIGESTION, TO MOVE THE BOWELS,
  3460	AGAINST ALL ILLNESS, AGAINST PESTILENCE AS WELL AS FOR THE PREVENTION
  3461	OF COLDS. THEY ARE VERY GENTLE INDEED AND MORE HEALTHFUL THAN YOU
  3462	WOULD EXPECT. [Tor. MAKE THEM IN THIS MANNER]: 1 LB. OF COMMON SALT
  3463	GROUND, 2 LBS. OF AMMONIAC SALT, GROUND [List. AND G.-V. 3 OZS. WHITE
  3464	PEPPER, 2 OZS. GINGER] 1 OZ. [Tor. 1-1/2 OZ.] OF AMINEAN BRYONY, 1 OF
  3465	THYME SEED AND 1 OF CELERY SEED [Tor. 1-1/2 OZ.] IF YOU DON'T WANT TO
  3466	USE CELERY SEED TAKE INSTEAD 3 OZS. OF PARSLEY [SEED] 3 OZS. OF
  3467	ORIGANY, 1 OZ. OF SAFFRON [List. and G.-V. ROCKET] 3 OZS. OF BLACK
  3468	PEPPER [1] 1-1/2 OZS. ROCKET SEED, 2 OZS. OF MARJORAM [List. and G.-V.
  3469	CRETAN HYSSOP] 2 OZS. OF NARD LEAVES, 2 OZS. OF PARSLEY [SEED] AND 2
  3470	OZS. OF ANISE SEED.
  3471	
  3472	    [1] In view of the white pepper as directed above, this
  3473	    seems superfluous. White pepper and ginger omitted by
  3474	    Tor.
  3475	
  3476	    This is one of the few medical formulæ found in Apicius.
  3477	
  3478	    Edward Brandt, _op. cit._, Apiciana No. 29, points out
  3479	    the similarity of this formula with that of the
  3480	    physician, Marcellus, who lived at Rome under Nero,
  3481	    Marcell. med. 30, 51.
  3482	
  3483	
  3484	
  3485	XIV
  3486	
  3487	
  3488	[30] TO KEEP GREEN OLIVES
  3489	   _OLIVAS VIRIDES SERVARE_
  3490	
  3491	TO KEEP OLIVES, FRESH FROM THE TREE, IN A MANNER ENABLING YOU TO MAKE
  3492	OIL FROM THEM ANY TIME YOU DESIRE JUST PLACE THEM [in brine]. [1]
  3493	HAVING BEEN KEPT THUS FOR SOME TIME THE OLIVES MAY BE USED AS IF THEY
  3494	HAD JUST COME OFF THE TREE FRESH IF YOU DESIRE TO MAKE GREEN OIL OF
  3495	THEM.
  3496	
  3497	    [1] The original does not state the liquid in which the
  3498	    olives are to be placed.
  3499	
  3500	    Hum. _in illud, legendum puto, in muriam_.
  3501	
  3502	    Hum. is correct. Olives are preserved in brine to this
  3503	    day.
  3504	
  3505	    Schuch's version of this formula (his No. 27) follows
  3506	    our No. 28, together with his own No. 28, To Keep
  3507	    Damascene Plums [etc.] which is wanting in List., G.-V.,
  3508	    and all the earlier editions because it is from the
  3509	    codex Salmasianus and will be found among the Excerpts
  3510	    of Vinidarius at the end of the Apician recipes.
  3511	
  3512	
  3513	
  3514	XV
  3515	
  3516	    [CUMINATUM. Hum., List. and G.-V.--Tac. and Tor. at the
  3517	    end of Book I.]
  3518	
  3519	
  3520	
  3521	XVI
  3522	
  3523	
  3524	[31] LASER FLAVOR
  3525	   _LASERATUM_
  3526	
  3527	[Tor.] LASER IS PREPARED IN THIS MANNER: LASER (WHICH IS ALSO CALLED
  3528	LASERPITIUM BY THE ROMANS, WHILE THE GREEKS CALL IT SILPHION) FROM
  3529	CYRENE [1] OR FROM PARTHIA [2] IS DISSOLVED IN LUKEWARM MODERATELY
  3530	ACID BROTH; OR PEPPER, PARSLEY, DRY MINT, LASER ROOT, HONEY, VINEGAR
  3531	AND BROTH [are ground, compounded and dissolved together].
  3532	
  3533	    [1] Cyrene, a province in Africa, reputed for its fine
  3534	    flavored laser.
  3535	
  3536	    [2] Parthia, Asiatic country, still supplying _asa
  3537	    fœtida_.
  3538	
  3539	    The African root furnishing laser was exterminated by
  3540	    the demand for it. Cf. Laser in Index.
  3541	
  3542	
  3543	[32] ANOTHER [LASER]
  3544	   _ALITER_
  3545	
  3546	[ANOTHER LASER FLAVOR WHICH TAKES] PEPPER, CARAWAY, ANISE, PARSLEY,
  3547	DRY MINT, THE LEAVES [1] OF SILPHIUM, MALOBATHRUM [2] INDIAN
  3548	SPIKENARD, A LITTLE COSTMARY, HONEY, VINEGAR AND BROTH.
  3549	
  3550	    [1] Tor. _Silphij folium_; List. _Sylphium, folium_;
  3551	    G.-V. _Silfi, folium_, the latter two interpretations
  3552	    meaning _silphium_ (laser) _and leaves_ (either nard or
  3553	    bay leaves) while both Tor. and Tac. (_silfii folium_)
  3554	    mean the leaves of _silphium_ plant.
  3555	
  3556	    [2] _Malobathrum_, _malobatrum_, _malabathrum_--leaves
  3557	    of an Indian tree, wild cinnamon.
  3558	
  3559	
  3560	
  3561	XVII
  3562	
  3563	
  3564	[33] WINE SAUCE FOR TRUFFLES
  3565	   _ŒNOGARUM _[1]_ IN TUBERA_
  3566	
  3567	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CORIANDER, RUE, BROTH, HONEY AND A LITTLE OIL.
  3568	
  3569	ANOTHER WAY: THYME, SATURY, PEPPER, LOVAGE, HONEY, BROTH AND OIL.
  3570	
  3571	    [1] Also _Elæogarum_.
  3572	
  3573	    V. Directions wanting whether the above ingredients are
  3574	    to be added to the already prepared _garum_, which see
  3575	    in dictionary. Gollmer gives the following direction for
  3576	    _garum_: Boil a _sextarium_ of anchovies and 3
  3577	    _sextarii_ of good wine until it is thick _purée_.
  3578	    Strain this through a hair sieve and keep it in glass
  3579	    flask for future use. This formula, according to Goll.
  3580	    should have followed our No. 9; but we find no authority
  3581	    for it in the original.
  3582	
  3583	    _Oenogarum_ proper would be a _garum_ prepared with
  3584	    wine, but in this instance it is the broth in which the
  3585	    truffles were cooked that is to be flavored with the
  3586	    above ingredients. There is no need and no mention of
  3587	    _garum_ proper. Thus prepared it might turn out to be a
  3588	    sensible sauce for truffles in the hands of a good
  3589	    practitioner.
  3590	
  3591	    Note the etymology of the word "garum," now serving as a
  3592	    generic name for "sauce" which originally stood for a
  3593	    compound of the fish _garus_.
  3594	
  3595	    Cf. _Garum_ in index.
  3596	
  3597	
  3598	
  3599	XVIII
  3600	
  3601	
  3602	[34] OXYPORUM
  3603	   _OXYPORUM_
  3604	
  3605	[Tor. OXYPORUM (WHICH SIGNIFIES "EASY PASSAGE") SO NAMED BECAUSE OF
  3606	ITS EFFECT, TAKES] 2 OZS. OF CUMIN, 1 OZ. OF GINGER [List. 1 OZ. OF
  3607	GREEN RUE] 6 SCRUPLES OF SALTPETER, A DOZEN SCRUPLES OF PLUMP DATES, 1
  3608	OZ. OF PEPPER AND 11 [List. 9] OZS. OF HONEY. THE CUMIN MAY BE EITHER
  3609	ÆTHIOPIAN, SYRIAN OR LYBIAN, MUST BE FIRST SOAKED IN VINEGAR, BOILED
  3610	DOWN DRY AND POUNDED. AFTERWARDS ADD YOUR HONEY. THIS COMPOUND, AS
  3611	NEEDED, IS USED AS OXYPORUM.
  3612	
  3613	    Cf. No. 111, A Harmless Salad.
  3614	
  3615	    Bran. _op. cit._, p. 25-6, of Greek origin.
  3616	
  3617

  3619	XIX
  3620	
  3621	
  3622	[35] HYPOTRIMA [1]
  3623	   _HYPOTRIMA_
  3624	
  3625	[Tor. HYPOTRIMA, MEANING IN LATIN A PERFECT MESS OF POTAGE, REQUIRES
  3626	THIS]: PEPPER, LOVAGE, DRY MINT, PIGNOLIA NUTS, RAISINS, DATE WINE,
  3627	SWEET CHEESE, HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH, WINE, OIL, MUST OR REDUCED MUST
  3628	[2]
  3629	
  3630	    [1] List. and G.-V. _Hypotrimma_.
  3631	
  3632	    V. This formula, lacking detailed instructions, is of
  3633	    course perfectly obscure, and it would be useless to
  3634	    debate over it.
  3635	
  3636	    [2] Tor. and Tac. _cariotam_; Sch. _cariotum_; List. and
  3637	    G.-V. _carœnum_. This (_carenum_) is new wine boiled
  3638	    down one half of its volume. _Cariotum_ is a palm wine
  3639	    or date wine.
  3640	
  3641	
  3642	
  3643	XX
  3644	
  3645	
  3646	[36] OXYGARUM, AN AID TO DIGESTION
  3647	   _OXYGARUM DIGESTIBILE_
  3648	
  3649	[Tor. OXYGARUM (WHICH IS SIMILAR TO GARUM OR RATHER AN ACID SAUCE) IS
  3650	DIGESTIBLE AND IS COMPOSED OF]: 1/2 OZ. OF PEPPER, 3 SCRUPLES OF
  3651	GALLIC SILPHIUM, 6 SCRUPLES OF CARDAMOM, 6 OF CUMIN, 1 SCRUPLE OF
  3652	LEAVES, 6 SCRUPLES OF DRY MINT. THESE [ingredients] ARE BROKEN SINGLY
  3653	AND CRUSHED AND [made into a paste] BOUND BY HONEY. WHEN THIS WORK IS
  3654	DONE [or whenever you desire] ADD BROTH AND VINEGAR [to taste].
  3655	
  3656	    Cf. Note to No. 33.
  3657	
  3658	
  3659	[37] ANOTHER [OXYGARUM] [1]
  3660	   _ALITER_
  3661	
  3662	1 OZ. EACH OF PEPPER, PARSLEY, CARRAWAY, LOVAGE, MIX WITH HONEY. WHEN
  3663	DONE ADD BROTH AND VINEGAR.
  3664	
  3665	    [1] Wanting in Torinus.
  3666	
  3667	
  3668	
  3669	XXI
  3670	
  3671	
  3672	[38] MORTARIA [1]
  3673	   _MORTARIA_
  3674	
  3675	MORTARIA ARE PREPARATIONS MADE IN THE MORTAR. PLACE IN THE MORTAR
  3676	[Tor.] MINT, RUE, CORIANDER AND FENNEL, ALL FRESH AND GREEN AND CRUSH
  3677	THEM FINE. LOVAGE, PEPPER, HONEY AND BROTH [2] AND VINEGAR [3] TO BE
  3678	ADDED WHEN THE WORK IS DONE.
  3679	
  3680	    Ex Tor. first sentence wanting in other texts.
  3681	
  3682	    [1] List. and G.-V. _moretaria_, from _moretum_.
  3683	
  3684	    [2] Dann. calls this "_Kalte Schale_" which as a rule is
  3685	    a drink or a cold refreshing soup, popular on the
  3686	    Continent in hot weather. Not a bad interpretation if
  3687	    instead of the broth the original called for wine or
  3688	    fruit juices.
  3689	
  3690	    V. _Mortaria_ are ingredients crushed in the mortar,
  3691	    ready to be used in several combinations, similar to
  3692	    the ground fine herbs, _remoulade_, in French cuisine
  3693	    that may be used for various purposes, principally for
  3694	    cold green sauces.
  3695	
  3696	    [3] Wanting in Tor.
  3697	
  3698	
  3699	
  3700	[XV]
  3701

  3703	[39] CUMIN SAUCE FOR SHELLFISH
  3704	   _CUMINATUM IN OSTREA ET CONCHYLIA_
  3705	
  3706	[Tor. CUMIN SAUCE (SO CALLED BECAUSE CUMIN IS ITS CHIEF INGREDIENT)
  3707	FOR OYSTERS AND CLAMS IS MADE OF] PEPPER, LOVAGE, PARSLEY, DRY MINT,
  3708	MALABAR LEAVES, QUITE SOME CUMIN, HONEY, VINEGAR, AND BROTH.
  3709	
  3710	
  3711	[40] ANOTHER [CUMIN SAUCE] [1]
  3712	   _ALITER_
  3713	
  3714	PEPPER, LOVAGE, PARSLEY, DRY MINT, PLENTY OF CUMIN, HONEY, VINEGAR AND
  3715	BROTH.
  3716	
  3717	    [1] wanting in List.
  3718	
  3719	    The cumin sauce formulæ are under chap. XV in G.-V.,
  3720	    following our No. 30.

  3732	The intricate design of the perforation denotes that this strainer was
  3733	used for straining wine. Various other strainers of simpler design,
  3734	with and without handles, were used in the kitchen and bakery. Ntl.
  3735	Mus., Naples, 77602; Field M., 24307.}

  3755	
  3756	Round bowl, fluted symmetrically, with three claw feet, resting on
  3757	molded bases. Ntl. Mus., Naples, 74000; Field M., 24028.}
  3758	
  3759	
  3760	
  3761	
  3762	BOOK II. MINCES
  3763	
  3764	_Lib. II. Sarcoptes_ [1]
  3765	
  3766	
  3767	    CHAP.   I. FORCEMEATS, SAUSAGE, MEAT PUDDINGS, MEAT LOAVES.
  3768	    CHAP.  II. HYDROGARUM, SPELT PUDDING AND ROUX [2].
  3769	    CHAP. III. SOW'S MATRIX, BLOOD SAUSAGE.
  3770	    CHAP.  IV. LUCANIAN SAUSAGE.
  3771	    CHAP.   V. SAUSAGE.
  3772	
  3773	    [1] Tor. _Artoptes_; Tac. _Artoptus_. This may have been
  3774	    derived from _artopta_--a vessel in which bread and
  3775	    pudding are baked. However, Sarcoptes is the better
  3776	    word, which is Greek, meaning "chopped meats."
  3777	
  3778	    [2] Tac. _Ambolatum_, and so in Tor. p. 15, _De
  3779	    Ambolato_. Cap. IIII. cf. our note following No. 58.
  3780	
  3781	
  3782	
  3783	I
  3784	
  3785	
  3786	[41] MINCED DISHES
  3787	   _ISICIA_
  3788	
  3789	THERE ARE MANY KINDS OF MINCED DISHES [1] SEAFOOD MINCES [2] ARE MADE
  3790	OF SEA-ONION, OR SEA CRAB-FISH, LOBSTER, CUTTLE-FISH, INK FISH, SPINY
  3791	LOBSTER, SCALLOPS AND OYSTERS [3]. THE FORCEMEAT IS SEASONED WITH
  3792	LOVAGE [4], PEPPER, CUMIN AND LASER ROOT.
  3793	
  3794	    [1] Tor. Sentence wanting in other texts. V. Forcemeats,
  3795	    minced meats, sausage. Tor. _Hysitia_, from _Isicia_.
  3796	    This term is derived from _insicium_, from _salsicium_,
  3797	    from _salsum insicium_, cut salt meat; old French
  3798	    _salcisse_, _saulcisse_, modern French _saucisse_,
  3799	    meaning sausage. This is a confirmation of the meaning
  3800	    of the word _salsum_--meaning primarily salt meat, bacon
  3801	    in particular. It has survived in modern French
  3802	    terminology in _salés_ more specially _petits
  3803	    salés_--small rashers of bacon. _Salsum_ has caused much
  3804	    confusion in some later formulæ. Cf. notes to Nos. 148,
  3805	    150, 152.
  3806	
  3807	    [2] V. fish forcemeats, fish balls, fish cakes and
  3808	    similar preparations.
  3809	
  3810	    [3] Scallops and oysters wanting in List. and G.-V.
  3811	
  3812	    [4] Wanting in List.
  3813	
  3814	
  3815	[42] CUTTLE-FISH CROQUETTES
  3816	   _ISICIA DE LOLLIGINE_ [1]
  3817	
  3818	THE MEAT IS SEPARATED FROM BONES, SKIN [and refuse] CHOPPED FINE AND
  3819	POUNDED IN THE MORTAR. SHAPE THE FORCEMEAT INTO NEAT CROQUETTES [2]
  3820	AND COOK THEM IN LIQUAMEN [3].
  3821	
  3822	THEY ARE DISPLAYED NICELY ON A LARGE DISH.
  3823	
  3824	    V. This formula plainly calls for fish balls braised or
  3825	    stewed in broth. Ordinarily we would boil the fish first
  3826	    and then separate the meat from the bones, shred or chop
  3827	    it fine, bind with cream sauce, flour and eggs; some add
  3828	    potatoes as a binder, and fry.
  3829	
  3830	    [1] G.-V. _lolligine_; Tor. _loligine_, which is
  3831	    correctly spelled.
  3832	
  3833	    [2] Tac. and Tor. _in pulmento tundes_. G.-V. _fulmento_
  3834	    which is wrong. _Pulmentum_, abbreviated for
  3835	    _pulpamentum_, from _pulpa_. It means a fleshy piece of
  3836	    fish or meat, a tid-bit.
  3837	
  3838	    [3] The original says _in liquamine fricatur_--fry in
  3839	    l., which is impossible in the sense of the word,
  3840	    frying. Either "frying" here stands for cooking,
  3841	    stewing, braising, poaching, or else the so mysterious
  3842	    _liquamen_ must here mean deep fat. Most likely these
  3843	    fish forcemeat balls were fried in olive oil. Cf. ℞
  3844	    No. 46.
  3845	
  3846	
  3847	[43] LOBSTER OR CRABMEAT CROQUETTES
  3848	   _ISICIA DE SCILLIS VEL DE CAMMARIS AMPLIS_ [1]
  3849	
  3850	THE SHELLS OF THE LOBSTERS OR CRABS [which are cooked] ARE BROKEN, THE
  3851	MEAT EXTRACTED FROM THE HEAD AND POUNDED IN THE MORTAR WITH PEPPER AND
  3852	THE BEST KIND OF BROTH. THIS PULP [is shaped into neat little cakes
  3853	which are fried] AND SERVED UP NICELY [2].
  3854	
  3855	    [1] _Scilla_ or _squilla_, squill, sea-onion, also a
  3856	    crab, _cammarus amplus_, large lobster, langouste, spiny
  3857	    lobster.
  3858	
  3859	    [2] The original omits the mode of cooking the fish. A
  3860	    case where it is taken for granted that the shellfish is
  3861	    boiled in water alive. The broth (_liquamen_) is a thick
  3862	    fish sauce in this case, serving as a binder for the
  3863	    meat, conforming to present methods.
  3864	
  3865	    Dann. Fill this into sausage casing. There is no
  3866	    authority for this.
  3867	
  3868	
  3869	[44] LIVER KROMESKIS
  3870	   _OMENTATA_ [1]
  3871	
  3872	OMENTATA ARE MADE IN THIS MANNER: [lightly] FRY PORK LIVER, REMOVE
  3873	SKIN AND SINEWS FIRST [2]. CRUSH PEPPER AND RUE IN A MORTAR WITH [a
  3874	little] BROTH, THEN ADD THE LIVER, POUND AND MIX. THIS PULP SHAPE INTO
  3875	SMALL SAUSAGE, WRAP EACH IN CAUL AND LAUREL LEAVES AND HANG THEM UP TO
  3876	BE SMOKED. WHENEVER YOU WANT AND WHEN READY TO ENJOY THEM TAKE THEM
  3877	OUT OF THE SMOKE, FRY THEM AGAIN, AND ADD GRAVY [3].
  3878	
  3879	    [1] From _omentum_--caul, the membrane enclosing the
  3880	    bowels. Hence "omen." Minced meats wrapped in caul and
  3881	    fried are kromeskis in kitchen terminology.
  3882	
  3883	    [2] First--an after thought so characteristic in
  3884	    culinary literature, proof enough that this formula
  3885	    originated in a kitchen. The _ante tamen_ of the
  3886	    original belongs to this sentence, not to the next, as
  3887	    the editors have it.
  3888	
  3889	    [3] Wanting in G.-V. The original continues without
  3890	    interruption to the next, an entirely new formula.
  3891	
  3892	
  3893	[45] [BRAIN SAUSAGE]
  3894	   [_ISICIA DE CEREBELLIS_] [1]
  3895	
  3896	PUT IN THE MORTAR PEPPER, LOVAGE AND ORIGANY, MOISTEN WITH BROTH AND
  3897	RUB; ADD COOKED BRAINS AND MIX DILIGENTLY SO THAT THERE BE NO LUMPS.
  3898	INCORPORATE FIVE EGGS AND CONTINUE MIXING WELL TO HAVE A GOOD
  3899	FORCEMEAT WHICH YOU MAY THIN WITH BROTH. SPREAD THIS OUT IN A METAL
  3900	PAN, COOK, AND WHEN COOKED [cold] UNMOULD IT ONTO A CLEAN TABLE. CUT
  3901	INTO HANDY SIZE. [Now prepare a sauce] PUT IN THE MORTAR PEPPER,
  3902	LOVAGE AND ORIGANY, CRUSH, MIX WITH BROTH PUT INTO A SAUCE PAN, BOIL,
  3903	THICKEN AND STRAIN. HEAT THE PIECES OF BRAIN PUDDING IN THIS SAUCE
  3904	THOROUGHLY, DISH THEM UP, SPRINKLED WITH PEPPER, IN A MUSHROOM DISH
  3905	[2].
  3906	
  3907	    [1] The Original has no title for this dish.
  3908	
  3909	    [2] List. and G.-V. here start the next formula, but
  3910	    Tor. continues without interruption. Cf. Note 2 to No.
  3911	    46.
  3912	
  3913	
  3914	[46] A DISH OF SCALLOPS
  3915	   _ISICIA EX SPONDYLIS_ [1]
  3916	
  3917	[Lightly] COOK SCALLOPS [or the firm part of oysters] REMOVE THE HARD
  3918	AND OBJECTIONABLE PARTS, MINCE THE MEAT VERY FINE, MIX THIS WITH
  3919	COOKED SPELT AND EGGS, SEASON WITH PEPPER, [shape into croquettes and
  3920	wrap] IN CAUL, FRY, UNDERLAY A RICH FISH SAUCE AND SERVE AS A
  3921	DELICIOUS ENTRÉE [2].
  3922	
  3923	    [1] Sch. _sfondilis_; G.-V. _sphondylis_; List.
  3924	    _spongiolis_. According to Lister, this is a dish of
  3925	    mushrooms, but he is wrong. He directs to remove sinews
  3926	    when mushrooms haven't any, but shellfish have. Torinus
  3927	    is correct. Gollmer makes the same mistake, believing
  3928	    _spondyli_ to be identical with _spongioli_. He and
  3929	    Danneil take _elixata_ for "choice" when this plainly
  3930	    means "cooked." If one were not sure of either word, the
  3931	    nature of the subject would leave no room for any doubt.
  3932	    Cf. note 1 to Nos. 115-121.
  3933	
  3934	    [2] We may find a reason for the combination of these
  3935	    last three distinctly different formulæ into one article
  3936	    in the following explanation. It is possible that these
  3937	    dishes were served together as one course, even on one
  3938	    platter, thus constituting a single dish, as it were.
  3939	    Such a dish would strongly resemble platters of
  3940	    "_fritures_" and "_fritto misto_" (mixed fried foods)
  3941	    esteemed in France and Italy. We, too, have "Shore
  3942	    Dinners" and other "Combination Platters" with lobster,
  3943	    crabs, scallops, shrimps, mushrooms, tomatoes--each
  3944	    article prepared separately, but when served together
  3945	    will form an integral part of ONE dish.
  3946	
  3947	    The above formulæ, though somewhat incomplete, are good
  3948	    and gastronomically correct. A combination of these
  3949	    _isicia_ such as we here suggest would be entirely
  3950	    feasible and would in fact make a dish of great
  3951	    refinement, taxing the magiric artist's skill to the
  3952	    utmost. We would class them among the _entremets chauds_
  3953	    which are often used on a buffet table or as hot _hors
  3954	    d'œuvres_.
  3955	
  3956	
  3957	[47] ANOTHER KIND OF KROMESKIS [1]
  3958	   _ALITER ISICIA OMENTATA_
  3959	
  3960	FINELY CUT PULP [of pork] IS GROUND WITH THE HEARTS [2] OF WINTER
  3961	WHEAT AND DILUTED WITH WINE. FLAVOR LIGHTLY WITH PEPPER AND BROTH AND
  3962	IF YOU LIKE ADD A MODERATE QUANTITY OF [myrtle] BERRIES ALSO CRUSHED,
  3963	AND AFTER YOU HAVE ADDED CRUSHED NUTS AND PEPPER [3] SHAPE THE
  3964	FORCEMEAT INTO SMALL ROLLS, WRAP THESE IN CAUL, FRY, AND SERVE WITH
  3965	WINE GRAVY.
  3966	
  3967	    [1] Wanting in Lister.
  3968	
  3969	    [2] Fine wheat flour, cream of wheat.
  3970	
  3971	    [3] Either pepper corns or allspice.
  3972	
  3973	    The original leaves us in doubt as to the kind of meat
  3974	    to be used, if any.
  3975	
  3976	
  3977	
  3978	II
  3979	
  3980	
  3981	[48] DUMPLINGS OF PHEASANT
  3982	   _ISICIA PLENA_
  3983	
  3984	[Lightly roast choice] FRESH PHEASANTS [cut them into dice and mix
  3985	these with a] STIFF FORCEMEAT MADE OF THE FAT AND THE TRIMMINGS OF THE
  3986	PHEASANT, SEASON WITH PEPPER, BROTH AND REDUCED WINE, SHAPE INTO
  3987	CROQUETTES OR SPOON DUMPLINGS, AND POACH IN HYDROGARUM [water seasoned
  3988	with garum, or even plain salt water].
  3989	
  3990	
  3991	[49] DUMPLINGS AND HYDROGARUM
  3992	   _HYDROGARATA ISICIA_
  3993	
  3994	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE AND JUST A SUSPICION OF PELLITORY, MOISTEN WITH
  3995	STOCK AND WELL WATER, ALLOW IT TO DRAW, PLACE IT IN A SAUCE PAN, BOIL
  3996	IT DOWN, AND STRAIN. POACH YOUR LITTLE DUMPLINGS OF FORCEMEAT IN THIS
  3997	LIQUOR AND WHEN THEY ARE DONE SERVE IN A DISH FOR ISICIA, TO BE SIPPED
  3998	AT THE TABLE.
  3999	
  4000	
  4001	[50] CHICKEN FORCEMEAT
  4002	   _ISICIA DE PULLO_
  4003	
  4004	[Raw] CHICKEN MEAT, 1 LB. OF DARNEL [1] MEAL, ONE QUARTER PINT OF
  4005	STOCK AND ONE HALF OUNCE OF PEPPER.
  4006	
  4007	    [1] Tor. _lolæ floris_; Hum.-List. and G.-V. _olei
  4008	    floris_--virgin olive oil?--first choice flour? Goll.
  4009	    olive (violet?) flowers; Dann. Olive oil.
  4010	
  4011	    The suggestion of oil is plausible because of the lack
  4012	    of fat in chicken meat, but the quantity--1 lb.--is out
  4013	    of question. Moreover, the binder would be lacking. This
  4014	    is found in the Torinus rendering.
  4015	
  4016	    His _lolæ floris_ should read _lolii_--from
  4017	    _lolium_--darnel rye grass or ray grass which was
  4018	    supposed to have intoxicating qualities, injurious to
  4019	    the eye sight.--Ovid and Plautus. The seeds of this
  4020	    grass were supposed to possess narcotic properties but
  4021	    recent researches have cast doubt upon this theory.
  4022	
  4023	    A little butter, fresh cream and eggs are the proper
  4024	    ingredients for chicken forcemeat. Any kind of flour for
  4025	    binding the forcemeat would cheapen the dish. Yet some
  4026	    modern forcemeats (sausage) contain as much as fifty
  4027	    percent of some kind of meal. The most effective is that
  4028	    of the soya bean which is not starchy.
  4029	
  4030	
  4031	[51] CHICKEN BROTH ANOTHER STYLE
  4032	   _ALITER DE PULLO_
  4033	
  4034	CHICKEN MEAT, 31 PEPPERCORNS CRUSHED, 1 CHOENIX [1] FULL OF THE VERY
  4035	BEST STOCK, A LIKE AMOUNT OF BOILED MUST AND ELEVEN MEASURES [2] OF
  4036	WATER. [Put this in a sauce pan] PLACE IT UPON THE FIRE TO SEETH AND
  4037	EVAPORATE SLOWLY.
  4038	
  4039	    [1] V. 2 _sextarii_; Tor. _chœnicem, cenlicem_; List.
  4040	    _calicem_.
  4041	
  4042	    [2] _chœnices_?--left in doubt.
  4043	
  4044	    This seems to be a chicken broth, or essence for a sauce
  4045	    or perhaps a medicine. Torinus mentions the chicken
  4046	    meat, the others do not.
  4047	
  4048	    The original without interruption continues to describe
  4049	    the _isicium simplex_ which has nothing to do with the
  4050	    above.
  4051	
  4052	
  4053	[52] PLAIN DUMPLING WITH BROTH
  4054	   _ISICIUM SIMPLEX_
  4055	
  4056	TO 1 ACETABULUM [1] OF STOCK [2] ADD 7 OF WATER, A LITTLE GREEN
  4057	CELERY, A LITTLE SPOONFUL OF GROUND PEPPER, AND BOIL THIS WITH THE
  4058	SAUSAGE MEAT OR DUMPLINGS. IF YOU INTEND TAKING THIS TO MOVE THE
  4059	BOWELS THE SEDIMENT SALTS [3] OF HYDROGARUM HAVE TO BE ADDED [4].
  4060	
  4061	    [1] A measure, 15 Attic drachms.
  4062	
  4063	    [2] _liquamen_.
  4064	
  4065	    [3] Tor. _pectines, alias peces hydrogaro conditi_;
  4066	    List. _sales_; G.-V. _fæces_.
  4067	
  4068	    [4] V. The formula is unintelligible, like No. 52 and
  4069	    others, perhaps just another example of medicinal
  4070	    cookery, dishes not only intended to nourish the body
  4071	    but to cure also certain ills. Authors like Hannah
  4072	    Wolley (The Queen-like Closet, London, 1675) and as late
  4073	    as the middle of the 18th century pride themselves in
  4074	    giving such quasi-Apician formulæ.
  4075	
  4076	
  4077	[53] [Rank of] DISHES
  4078	   _ISICIA_
  4079	
  4080	[Entrées of] PEACOCK OCCUPY THE FIRST RANK, PROVIDED THEY BE DRESSED
  4081	IN SUCH MANNER THAT THE HARD AND TOUGH PARTS BE TENDER. THE SECOND
  4082	PLACE [in the estimation of the Gourmets] HAVE DISHES MADE OF RABBIT
  4083	[1] THIRD SPINY LOBSTER [2] FOURTH COMES CHICKEN AND FIFTH YOUNG PIG.
  4084	
  4085	    [1] List. and G.-V. Pheasant.
  4086	
  4087	    [2] Wanting in the above. Dann. Crane fourth.
  4088	
  4089	    _Isicia_, like in the foregoing formula, commences to
  4090	    become a generic term for "dishes."
  4091	
  4092	
  4093	[54] POTTED ENTRÉES
  4094	   _ISICIA AMULATA AB AHENO_ [1]
  4095	
  4096	GROUND PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, VERY LITTLE SILPHIUM, A PINCH OF
  4097	GINGER AND A TRIFLE OF HONEY AND A LITTLE STOCK. [Put on the fire,
  4098	and when boiling] ADD THE ISICIA [sausage, meat balls and so forth] TO
  4099	THIS BROTH AND COOK THOROUGHLY. FINALLY THICKEN THE GRAVY WITH ROUX
  4100	[2] BY SOWING IT IN SLOWLY AND STIRRING FROM THE BOTTOM UP [3].
  4101	
  4102	    [1] Tor. _multa ab alieno_; Brandt _[a]mul[a]ta ab
  4103	    aheno_; List. _amylata_--French: _liés_. _Ab aheno_--out
  4104	    of the pot.
  4105	
  4106	    [2] French, for a mixture of wheat or rice flour with
  4107	    fats or liquids to thicken fluids. _Amylum_, or _amulum_
  4108	    which hereafter will occur frequently in the original
  4109	    does not cover the ground as well as the French term
  4110	    _roux_. The quality of the "binder" depends upon the
  4111	    material in hand. Sometimes the fat and flour are
  4112	    parched, sometimes they are used raw. Sometimes the
  4113	    flour is diluted with water and used in that form.
  4114	
  4115	    [3] List. and G.-V. _sorbendum_; Tor. _subruendum_.
  4116	
  4117	
  4118	[55] ANOTHER [THICK ENTRÉE GRAVY]
  4119	   _ALITER_
  4120	
  4121	GRIND PEPPER WHICH HAS BEEN SOAKED OVERNIGHT, ADD SOME MORE STOCK AND
  4122	WORK IT INTO A SMOOTH PASTE; THEREUPON ADD QUINCE-APPLE CIDER, BOILED
  4123	DOWN ONE HALF, THAT IS WHICH HAS EVAPORATED IN THE HEAT OF THE SUN TO
  4124	THE CONSISTENCY OF HONEY. IF THIS IS NOT AT HAND, ADD FIG WINE [1]
  4125	CONCENTRATE WHICH THE ROMANS CALL "COLOR" [2]. NOW THICKEN THE GRAVY
  4126	WITH ROUX OR WITH SOAKED RICE FLOUR AND FINISH IT ON A GENTLE FIRE.
  4127	
  4128	    [1] Tor. _cammarum_, which should read _caricarum_--wine
  4129	    of Carica figs.
  4130	
  4131	    [2] V. the Roman equivalent for "_singe_," "monkey,"
  4132	    "_Affe_,"--(the _vulgo_ French is literally translated
  4133	    into and in actual use in other languages) caramel color
  4134	    made of burnt sugar to give gravies a palatable
  4135	    appearance. Cf. No. 73.
  4136	
  4137	    The reference by the original to "which the Romans call
  4138	    'color'" indicates, according to Brandt, that this
  4139	    formula is NOT of ROMAN origin but probably a
  4140	    translation into Latin from a Greek cookery book.
  4141	
  4142	    This is an interesting suggestion, and it could be
  4143	    elaborated on to say that the entire Apicius is NOT of
  4144	    Roman origin. But why should the Greeks who in their
  4145	    balmy days were so far in advance of Rome in culinary
  4146	    matters go there for such information?
  4147	
  4148	    It is more likely that this reference to Rome comes from
  4149	    the Italian provinces or the colonies, regions which
  4150	    naturally would look to Rome for guidance in such
  4151	    matters.
  4152	
  4153	
  4154	[56] ANOTHER AMULATUM
  4155	   _AMULATUM ALITER_
  4156	
  4157	DISJOINT A CHICKEN AND BONE IT. PLACE THE PIECES IN A STEW PAN WITH
  4158	LEEKS, DILL AND SALT [water or stock] WHEN WELL DONE ADD PEPPER AND
  4159	CELERY SEED, THICKEN WITH RICE [1] ADD STOCK, A DASH OF RAISIN WINE OR
  4160	MUST, STIR WELL, SERVE WITH THE ENTRÉES.
  4161	
  4162	    [1] G.-V. _oryzam_; Tor. ditto (and on margin) _oridam_;
  4163	    Hum. _oridiam legendum orindam_--a kind of bread. Dann.
  4164	    and Goll. rice flour.
  4165	
  4166	    In a general way the ancient formula corresponds exactly
  4167	    to our present chicken fricassée.
  4168	
  4169	
  4170	[57] SPELT OR FARINA PUDDING
  4171	   _APOTHERMUM_
  4172	
  4173	BOIL SPELT WITH [Tor. PIGNOLIA] NUTS AND PEELED ALMONDS [1] [G.-V.
  4174	AND] IMMERSED IN [boiling] WATER AND WASHED WITH WHITE CLAY SO THAT
  4175	THEY APPEAR PERFECTLY WHITE, ADD RAISINS, [flavor with] CONDENSED WINE
  4176	OR RAISIN WINE AND SERVE IT IN A ROUND DISH WITH CRUSHED [2] [nuts,
  4177	fruit, bread or cake crumbs] SPRINKLED OVER IT [3].
  4178	
  4179	    [1] V. We peel almonds in the same manner; the white
  4180	    clay treatment is new to us.
  4181	
  4182	    G.-V. and--which is confusing.
  4183	
  4184	    [2] The original: _confractum_--crushed, but what? G.-V.
  4185	    pepper, for which there is neither authority nor reason.
  4186	    A wine sauce would go well with it or crushed fruit.
  4187	    List. and Goll. Breadcrumbs.
  4188	
  4189	    [3] This is a perfectly good pudding--one of the very
  4190	    few desserts in Apicius. With a little sweetening
  4191	    (supplied probably by the condensed wine) and some
  4192	    grated lemon for flavor it is quite acceptable as a
  4193	    dessert.
  4194	
  4195	
  4196	[58] DE AMBOLATO CAP. IIII
  4197	
  4198	    Ex Torinus, not mentioned by the other editors. The
  4199	    sense of this word is not clear. It must be a recipe or
  4200	    a chapter the existence of which was known to Torinus,
  4201	    for he says: "This entire chapter is wanting in our
  4202	    copy."
  4203	
  4204	
  4205	
  4206	III
  4207	
  4208	
  4209	[59] A DISH OF SOW'S MATRIX
  4210	   _VULVULÆ BOTELLI_ [1]
  4211	
  4212	ENTRÉES [2] OF SOW'S MATRIX [3] ARE MADE THUS: CRUSH PEPPER AND CUMIN
  4213	WITH TWO SMALL HEADS OF LEEK, PEELED, ADD TO THIS PULP RUE, BROTH [and
  4214	the sow's matrix or fresh pork] CHOP, [or crush in mortar very fine]
  4215	THEN ADD TO THIS [forcemeat] INCORPORATING WELL PEPPER GRAINS AND
  4216	[pine] NUTS [4] FILL THE CASING [5] AND BOIL IN WATER [with] OIL AND
  4217	BROTH [for seasoning] AND A BUNCH OF LEEKS AND DILL.
  4218	
  4219	    [1] G.-V. _Vulvulæ Botelli_; Sch. _Vulvulæ isiciata_;
  4220	    Tor. _De Vulvulis et botellis_. See note No. 3.
  4221	
  4222	    [2] V. "_Entrées_" out of respect for the ancients who
  4223	    used them as such; today we would class such dishes
  4224	    among the "_hors d'œuvres chauds_."
  4225	
  4226	    [3] V. _Vulvula_, dim. for _vulva_, sow's matrix. Cf.
  4227	    _vulva_ in dictionary. Possible, also possible that
  4228	    _volva_ is meant--a meat roll, a croquette.
  4229	
  4230	    [4] V. Combinations of chopped nuts and pork still in
  4231	    vogue today; we use the green pistachios.
  4232	
  4233	    [5] V. The casings which were filled with this forcemeat
  4234	    may have been the sow's matrices, also caul. The
  4235	    original is vague on the point.
  4236	
  4237	
  4238	[60] LITTLE SAUSAGE
  4239	   _BOTELLUM_ [1]
  4240	
  4241	BOTELLUM IS MADE OF [2] HARD BOILED YOLKS OF EGG [3] CHOPPED PIGNOLIA
  4242	NUTS, ONION AND LEEKS, RAW GROUND PINE [4] FINE PEPPER, STUFF IN
  4243	CASINGS AND COOK IN BROTH AND WINE [5].
  4244	
  4245	    [1] V. _Botelli_, or _botuli_, are sausage of various
  4246	    kind; (French, Boudin, English, Pudding). Originally
  4247	    made of raw blood, they are in fact, miniature blood
  4248	    sausage. The absence of meat in the present formula
  4249	    makes me believe that it is not complete, though hard
  4250	    boiled yolk when properly seasoned and mixed with the
  4251	    right amount of fat, make a tasty forcemeat for sausage.
  4252	
  4253	    [2] Tor. _Botellum sic fades ex oui_; Sch. and G.-V.
  4254	    _sex ovi_--the number of eggs is immaterial.
  4255	
  4256	    [3] Dann. Calf's Sweetbreads.
  4257	
  4258	    [4] Goll. _Thus crudum_--raw blood. _Thus_ or _tus_ is
  4259	    either frankincense or the herb, ground-pine. Dann.
  4260	    Rosemary. Hum. _Thus crudum lege jus crudum_--jus or
  4261	    broth which would make the forcemeat soft. There is no
  4262	    reason for changing "_thus_" into "_jus_!"
  4263	
  4264	    [5] G.-V. _Adicies liquamen et vinum, et sic coques_.
  4265	    Tor. & _vino decoquas_.
  4266	
  4267	
  4268	
  4269	IV
  4270	
  4271	
  4272	[61] LUCANIAN SAUSAGE
  4273	   _LUCANICÆ_
  4274	
  4275	LUCANIAN SAUSAGE [or meat pudding] ARE MADE SIMILAR TO THE ABOVE:
  4276	CRUSH PEPPER, CUMIN, SAVORY, RUE, PARSLEY, CONDIMENT, LAUREL BERRIES
  4277	AND BROTH; MIX WITH FINELY CHOPPED [fresh Pork] AND POUND WELL WITH
  4278	BROTH. TO THIS MIXTURE, BEING RICH, ADD WHOLE PEPPER AND NUTS. WHEN
  4279	FILLING CASINGS CAREFULLY PUSH THE MEAT THROUGH. HANG SAUSAGE UP TO
  4280	SMOKE.
  4281	
  4282	    V. Lister's interesting remarks about the makers of
  4283	    these sausages are given in the dictionary. Cf. Longano.
  4284	
  4285	
  4286	
  4287	V
  4288	
  4289	
  4290	[62] SAUSAGE
  4291	   _FARCIMINA_
  4292	
  4293	POUND EGGS AND BRAINS [eggs raw, brains cooked] PINE NUTS [chopped
  4294	fine] PEPPER [whole] BROTH AND A LITTLE LASER WITH WHICH FILL THE
  4295	CASINGS. FIRST PARBOIL THE SAUSAGE THEN FRY THEM AND SERVE.
  4296	
  4297	    V. The directions are vague enough, but one may
  4298	    recognize in them our modern brain sausage.
  4299	
  4300	
  4301	[63] ANOTHER SAUSAGE
  4302	   _ALITER_
  4303	
  4304	WORK COOKED SPELT AND FINELY CHOPPED FRESH PORK TOGETHER, POUND IT
  4305	WITH PEPPER, BROTH AND PIGNOLIA NUTS. FILL THE CASINGS, PARBOIL AND
  4306	FRY WITH SALT, SERVE WITH MUSTARD, OR YOU MAY CUT THE SAUSAGE IN
  4307	SLICES AND SERVE ON A ROUND DISH.
  4308	
  4309	
  4310	[64] ANOTHER SAUSAGE
  4311	   _ALITER_
  4312	
  4313	WASH SPELT AND COOK IT WITH STOCK. CUT THE FAT OF THE INTESTINES OR
  4314	BELLY VERY FINE WITH LEEKS. MIX THIS WITH CHOPPED BACON AND FINELY
  4315	CHOPPED FRESH PORK. CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE AND THREE EGGS AND MIX ALL IN
  4316	THE MORTAR WITH PIGNOLIA NUTS AND WHOLE PEPPER, ADD BROTH, FILL
  4317	CASINGS. PARBOIL SAUSAGE, FRY LIGHTLY, OR SERVE THEM BOILED.
  4318	
  4319	    Tor. and Tac. Serve with pheasant gravy. In the early
  4320	    editions the following formula which thus ends is
  4321	    wanting.
  4322	
  4323	
  4324	[65] ROUND SAUSAGE
  4325	   _CIRELLOS ISICIATOS_
  4326	
  4327	FILL THE CASINGS WITH THE BEST MATERIAL [forcemeat] SHAPE THE SAUSAGE
  4328	INTO SMALL CIRCLES, SMOKE. WHEN THEY HAVE TAKEN ON (VERMILLION) COLOR
  4329	FRY THEM LIGHTLY. DRESS NICELY GARNISHED ON A PHEASANT WINE GRAVY,
  4330	FLAVORED, HOWEVER, WITH CUMIN.
  4331	
  4332	    V. In Tor. and in the earliest edition this formula has
  4333	    been contracted with the preceding and made one formula.
  4334

  4350	{Illustration: ELABORATE THERMOSPODIUM
  4351	
  4352	A heater for the service of hot foods and drinks in the dining room.
  4353	Hot drinks were mixed and foods were served from apparatus of this
  4354	kind. The fuel was charcoal. There were public places, specializing in
  4355	hot drinks, called Thermopolia. This specimen was found at Stabiæ, one
  4356	of the ill-fated towns destroyed by eruption of Mt. Vesuvius. Ntl.
  4357	Mus., Naples, 72986; Field M., 24307.}

  4362	{Illustration: SERVICE PAN
  4363	
  4364	Round, with decorated handle. This and the pan with the Hercules head
  4365	on handle used in connection with the plain Thermospodium to serve hot
  4366	foods in the dining room. Hildesheim Treas.}

  4400	I
  4401	
  4402	
  4403	[66] VEGETABLES, POT HERBS
  4404	   _DE HOLERIBUS_
  4405	
  4406	TO KEEP ALL VEGETABLES GREEN.
  4407	  _UT OMNE HOLUS SMARAGDINUM FIAT._
  4408	
  4409	ALL VEGETABLES WILL REMAIN GREEN IF BOILED WITH COOKING SODA [1].
  4410	
  4411	    [1] _Nitrium._ Method still in use today, considered
  4412	    injurious to health if copper vessel is used, but the
  4413	    amount of copper actually absorbed by the vegetable is
  4414	    infinitesimal, imperceptible even by the taste. Copper,
  4415	    to be actually harmful would have to be present in such
  4416	    quantity as to make enjoyment impossible.
  4417	
  4418	
  4419	
  4420	II
  4421	
  4422	
  4423	[67] VEGETABLE DINNER, EASILY DIGESTED
  4424	   _PULMENTARIUM AD VENTREM_ [1]
  4425	
  4426	ALL GREEN VEGETABLES ARE SUITED FOR THIS PURPOSE [2] VERY YOUNG [3]
  4427	BEETS AND WELL MATURED LEEKS ARE PARBOILED; ARRANGE THEM IN A BAKING
  4428	DISH, GRIND PEPPER AND CUMIN, ADD BROTH AND CONDENSED MUST, OR
  4429	ANYTHING ELSE TO SWEETEN THEM A LITTLE, HEAT AND FINISH THEM ON A SLOW
  4430	FIRE, AND SERVE.
  4431	
  4432	    [1] V. _Ad ventrem_, "for the belly," simple home
  4433	    laxative.
  4434	
  4435	    [2] V. This sentence in Torinus only. Possibly a
  4436	    contraction of the foregoing formula, No. 66.
  4437	
  4438	    [3] V. _minutas_, "small," i.e., young.
  4439	
  4440	
  4441	[68] A SIMILAR DISH
  4442	   _SIMILITER_
  4443	
  4444	PARBOIL POLYPODY [1] ROOT SO AS TO SOFTEN THEM, CUT THEM INTO SMALL
  4445	PIECES, SEASON WITH GROUND PEPPER AND CUMIN, ARRANGE IN A BAKING DISH,
  4446	FINISH ON THE FIRE AND SERVE [2].
  4447	
  4448	    [1] V. Roots of the fern herb.
  4449	
  4450	    [2] V. Although these instructions for vegetable dinners
  4451	    are rather vague, they resemble primitive
  4452	    _chartreuses_--fancy vegetable dishes developed by the
  4453	    Carthusian monks to whom flesh eating was forbidden.
  4454	    Elsewhere in Apicius we shall find the _chartreuse_
  4455	    developed to a remarkable degree.
  4456	
  4457	
  4458	[69] ANOTHER LAXATIVE
  4459	   _ALITER AD VENTREM_ [1]
  4460	
  4461	SCRUB AND WASH BUNDLES OF BEETS BY RUBBING THEM WITH A LITTLE SODA
  4462	[2]. TIE THEM IN INDIVIDUAL BUNDLES, PUT INTO WATER TO BE COOKED, WHEN
  4463	DONE, SEASON WITH REDUCED MUST OR RAISIN WINE AND CUMIN, SPRINKLE
  4464	WITH PEPPER, ADD A LITTLE OIL, AND WHEN HOT, CRUSH POLYPODY AND NUTS
  4465	WITH BROTH, ADD THIS TO THE RED-HOT PAN, INCORPORATING IT WITH THE
  4466	BEETS, TAKE OFF THE FIRE QUICKLY AND SERVE.
  4467	
  4468	    [1] This formula wanting in Tor.
  4469	
  4470	    [2] V. Ingenious method to skin tender root vegetables,
  4471	    still in vogue today. We remove the skin of tender young
  4472	    root vegetables, carrots, beets, etc., by placing them
  4473	    in a towel, sprinkling them with rock salt and shaking
  4474	    them energetically. The modern power vegetable peeler is
  4475	    really built on the same principle, only instead of salt
  4476	    (which soon melts) carborundum or rough concrete
  4477	    surfaces are used, against which surfaces the vegetables
  4478	    are hurled by the rotary motion; often enough, too much
  4479	    of the skin is removed, however.
  4480	
  4481	
  4482	[70] BEETS À LA VARRO
  4483	   _BETACEOS VARRONIS_ [1]
  4484	
  4485	VARRO BEETS, THAT IS, BLACK ONES [2] OF WHICH THE ROOTS MUST BE
  4486	CLEANED WELL, COOK THEM WITH MEAD AND A LITTLE SALT AND OIL; BOIL THEM
  4487	DOWN IN THIS LIQUOR SO THAT THE ROOTS ARE SATURATED THEREBY; THE
  4488	LIQUID ITSELF IS GOOD DRINKING. IT IS ALSO NICE TO COOK A CHICKEN IN
  4489	WITH THEM.
  4490	
  4491	    [1] G.-V. _Betacios_; Tor. _B. Varrones_. Probably named
  4492	    for Varro, the writer on agriculture.
  4493	
  4494	    [2] Roots on the order of parsnips, salsify,
  4495	    oysterplant.
  4496	
  4497	
  4498	[71] ANOTHER LAXATIVE
  4499	   _ALITER AD VENTREM_
  4500	
  4501	ANOTHER VEGETABLE DISH, PROMOTING GOOD HEALTH; WASH CELERY, GREENS AND
  4502	ROOTS, AND DRY IT IN THE SUN: THEN ALSO COOK THE TENDER PART AND HEAD
  4503	OF LEEKS IN A NEW [1] POT, ALLOWING THE WATER TO BOIL DOWN ONE THIRD
  4504	OF ITS VOLUME. THEREUPON GRIND PEPPER WITH BROTH AND HONEY IN EQUAL
  4505	AMOUNTS PROPERLY MEASURED, MIX IT IN THE MORTAR WITH THE WATER OF THE
  4506	COOKED CELERY, STRAIN, BOIL AGAIN AND USE IT TO MASK THE [cooked]
  4507	CELERY WITH. IF DESIRED, ADD [the sliced root of the] CELERY TO IT
  4508	[2].
  4509	
  4510	    [1] V. "new," i.e., cook leeks in a separate sauce pan;
  4511	    NOT together with the celery, which, as the original
  4512	    takes for granted, must be cooked also.
  4513	
  4514	    [2] V. We would leave the honey out, make a cream sauce
  4515	    from the stock, or, adding bouillon, tie same with a
  4516	    little flour and butter, and would call the dish Stewed
  4517	    Celery and Leeks. The ancient method is entirely
  4518	    rational because the mineral salts of the vegetables are
  4519	    preserved and utilized (invariably observed by Apicius)
  4520	    which today are often wasted by inexperienced cooks who
  4521	    discard these precious elements with the water in which
  4522	    vegetables are boiled.
  4523	
  4524	
  4525	
  4526	III
  4527	
  4528	
  4529	[72] ASPARAGUS
  4530	   _ASPARAGOS_
  4531	
  4532	ASPARAGUS [Tor. IN ORDER TO HAVE IT MOST AGREEABLE TO THE PALATE] MUST
  4533	BE [peeled, washed and] DRIED [1] AND IMMERSED IN BOILING WATER
  4534	BACKWARDS [2] [3].
  4535	
  4536	    [1] V. Must be dried before boiling because the cold
  4537	    water clinging to the stalks is likely to chill the
  4538	    boiling water too much in which the asparagus is to be
  4539	    cooked. Apicius here reveals himself as the consummate
  4540	    cook who is familiar with the finest detail of physical
  4541	    and chemical changes which food undergoes at varying
  4542	    temperatures.
  4543	
  4544	    The various editions all agree: _asparagos siccabis_;
  4545	    Schuch, however, says: "For the insane _siccabis_ I
  4546	    substitute _siciabis_, _isiciabis_, prepare with _sicio_
  4547	    [?] and cook." He even goes on to interpret it _cucabis_
  4548	    from the Greek _kouki_, cocoanut milk, and infers that
  4549	    the asparagus was first cooked in cocoanut milk and then
  4550	    put back into water, a method we are tempted to
  4551	    pronounce insane.
  4552	
  4553	    [2] V. Backwards! G.-V. _rursum in calidam_; Tac.
  4554	    _rursus in aquam calidam_; Tor. _ac rursus ..._
  4555	
  4556	    This word has caused us some reflection, but the ensuing
  4557	    discovery made it worth while. _Rursus_ has escaped the
  4558	    attention of the other commentators. In this case
  4559	    _rursus_ means backwards, being a contraction from
  4560	    _revorsum_, h.e. _reversum_. The word is important
  4561	    enough to be observed.
  4562	
  4563	    Apicius evidently has the right way of cooking the fine
  4564	    asparagus. The stalks, after being peeled and washed
  4565	    must be bunched together and tied according to sizes,
  4566	    and the bunches must be set into the boiling water
  4567	    "backwards," that is, they must stand upright with the
  4568	    heads protruding from the water. The heads will be made
  4569	    tender above the water line by rising steam and will be
  4570	    done simultaneously with the harder parts of the stalks.
  4571	    We admit, we have never seen a modern cook observe this
  4572	    method. They usually boil the tender heads to death
  4573	    while the lower stalks are still hard.
  4574	
  4575	    Though this formula is incomplete (it fails to state the
  4576	    sauce to be served, also that the asparagus must be
  4577	    peeled and bunched, that the water must contain salt,
  4578	    etc.) it is one of the neatest formulæ in Apicius. It is
  4579	    amusing to note how the author herein unconsciously
  4580	    reveals what a poor litérateur but what a fine cook he
  4581	    is. This is characteristic of most good practitioners.
  4582	    One may perfectly master the vast subject of cookery,
  4583	    yet one may not be able to give a definition of even a
  4584	    single term, let alone the ability to exactly describe
  4585	    one of the many processes of cookery. Real poets often
  4586	    are in the same predicament; none of them ever explained
  4587	    the art satisfactorily.
  4588	
  4589	    [3] G.-V. add to the formula _callosiores reddes_--give
  4590	    back [eliminate] the harder ones. This sentence belongs
  4591	    to the next article. And Torinus, similar to
  4592	    Humelbergius, renders this sentence _ut reddas ad gustum
  4593	    calliores_--to render the harder ones palatable--the
  4594	    squash and pumpkin namely--and we are inclined to agree
  4595	    with him.
  4596	
  4597	
  4598	
  4599	IV
  4600	
  4601	
  4602	[73] PUMPKIN, SQUASH
  4603	   _CUCURBITAS_
  4604	
  4605	TO HAVE THE HARDER ONES PALATABLE, DO THIS: [1] [Cut the fruit into
  4606	pieces, boil and] SQUEEZE THE WATER OUT OF THE BOILED FRUIT AND
  4607	ARRANGE [the pieces] IN A BAKING DISH. PUT IN THE MORTAR PEPPER, CUMIN
  4608	AND SILPHIUM, THAT IS, A VERY LITTLE OF THE LASER ROOT AND A LITTLE
  4609	RUE, SEASON THIS WITH STOCK, MEASURE A LITTLE VINEGAR AND MIX IN A
  4610	LITTLE CONDENSED WINE, SO THAT IT CAN BE STRAINED [2] AND POUR THIS
  4611	LIQUID OVER THE FRUIT IN THE BAKING DISH; LET IT BOIL THREE TIMES,
  4612	RETIRE FROM THE FIRE AND SPRINKLE WITH VERY LITTLE GROUND PEPPER.
  4613	
  4614	    [1] Cf. note 3 to No. 72.
  4615	
  4616	    [2] List. _Ut coloretur_--to give it color; Tor. _ut ius
  4617	    coletur_--from _colo_--to strain, to filter.
  4618	
  4619	    Cf. also note 2 to No. 55.
  4620	
  4621	
  4622	[74] PUMPKIN LIKE DASHEENS
  4623	   _ALITER CUCURBITAS IURE COLOCASIORUM_ [1]
  4624	
  4625	BOIL THE PUMPKIN IN WATER LIKE COLOCASIA; GRIND PEPPER, CUMIN AND RUE,
  4626	ADD VINEGAR AND MEASURE OUT THE BROTH IN A SAUCEPAN. THE PUMPKIN
  4627	PIECES [nicely cut] WATER PRESSED OUT [are arranged] IN A SAUCEPAN
  4628	WITH THE BROTH AND ARE FINISHED ON THE FIRE WHILE THE JUICE IS BEING
  4629	TIED WITH A LITTLE ROUX. BEFORE SERVING SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER [2].
  4630	
  4631	    [1] V. _Colocasia Antiquorum_ belonging to the dasheen
  4632	    or taro family, a valuable tuber, again mentioned in No.
  4633	    172, 216, 244 and 322. Cf. various notes, principally
  4634	    that to No. 322. Also see U. S. Dept. of Agr. Farmer's
  4635	    Bulletin No. 1396, p. 2. This is a "new" and
  4636	    commercially and gastronomically important root
  4637	    vegetable, the flavor reminding of a combination of
  4638	    chestnuts and potatoes, popularly known as "Chinese
  4639	    potatoes" which has been recently introduced by the
  4640	    U. S. Government from the West Indies where it received
  4641	    the name, Dasheen, derived from _de Chine_--from China.
  4642	
  4643	    [2] Tor. continues without interruption into the next
  4644	    formula.
  4645	
  4646	
  4647	[75] PUMPKIN, ALEXANDRINE STYLE
  4648	   _ALITER CUCURBITAS MORE ALEXANDRINO_
  4649	
  4650	PRESS THE WATER OUT OF THE BOILED PUMPKIN, PLACE IN A BAKING DISH,
  4651	SPRINKLE WITH SALT, GROUND PEPPER, CUMIN, CORIANDER SEED, GREEN MINT
  4652	AND A LITTLE LASER ROOT; SEASON WITH VINEGAR. NOW ADD DATE WINE AND
  4653	PIGNOLIA NUTS GROUND WITH HONEY, VINEGAR AND BROTH, MEASURE OUT
  4654	CONDENSED WINE AND OIL, POUR THIS OVER THE PUMPKIN AND FINISH IN THIS
  4655	LIQUOR AND SERVE, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER BEFORE SERVING.
  4656	
  4657	
  4658	[76] BOILED PUMPKIN
  4659	   _ALITER CUCURBITAS ELIXATAS_
  4660	
  4661	[Boiled Pumpkin] STEWED IN BROTH WITH PURE OIL.
  4662	
  4663	
  4664	[77] FRIED PUMPKIN
  4665	   _ALITER CUCURBITAS FRICTAS_
  4666	
  4667	[Fried pumpkin served with] SIMPLE WINE SAUCE AND PEPPER.
  4668	
  4669	
  4670	[78] ANOTHER WAY, BOILED AND FRIED
  4671	   _ALITER CUCURBITAS ELIXATAS ET FRICTAS_
  4672	
  4673	BOILED PUMPKIN FRIED IS PLACED IN A BAKING PAN. SEASON WITH CUMIN
  4674	WINE, ADD A LITTLE OIL; FINISH ON THE FIRE AND SERVE.
  4675	
  4676	
  4677	[79] ANOTHER WAY, MASHED
  4678	   _CUCURBITAS FRICTAS TRITAS_
  4679	
  4680	FRIED [1] PUMPKIN, SEASONED WITH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, ORIGANY,
  4681	ONION, WINE BROTH AND OIL: STEW THE PUMPKIN [in this] IN A BAKING
  4682	DISH, TIE THE LIQUID WITH ROUX [mash] AND SERVE IN THE DISH.
  4683	
  4684	    [1] V. Baking the fruit reduces the water contents,
  4685	    renders the purée more substantial. G.-V.
  4686	    _Tritas_--mashed. Tor. connects _tritas_ up with pepper,
  4687	    hence it is doubtful whether this dish of pumpkin is
  4688	    mashed pumpkin.
  4689	
  4690	
  4691	[80] PUMPKIN AND CHICKEN
  4692	   _CUCURBITAS CUM GALLINA_
  4693	
  4694	[Stew the pumpkin with a hen, garnish with] HARD-SKINNED PEACHES,
  4695	TRUFFLES; PEPPER, CARRAWAY, AND CUMIN, SILPHIUM AND GREEN HERBS, SUCH
  4696	AS MINT, CELERY, CORIANDER, PENNYROYAL, CRESS, WINE [1] OIL AND
  4697	VINEGAR.
  4698	
  4699	    [1] Tor. _Vinum vel oleum_; List. _vinum_, _mel_,
  4700	    _oleum_.
  4701	
  4702	
  4703	
  4704	V
  4705	
  4706	
  4707	[81] CITRON
  4708	   _CITRIUM_ [1]
  4709	
  4710	FOR THE PREPARATION OF CITRON FRUIT WE TAKE SILER [2] FROM THE
  4711	MOUNTAINS, SILPHIUM, DRY MINT, VINEGAR AND BROTH.
  4712	
  4713	    [1] List. _Citrini_--a lemon or cucumber squash.
  4714	
  4715	    [2] Tor. _Silerem_; List. _sil_, which is hartwort, a
  4716	    kind of cumin or mountain fennel.
  4717	
  4718	
  4719	
  4720	VI
  4721	
  4722	
  4723	[82] CUCUMBERS
  4724	   _CUCUMERES_
  4725	
  4726	[Stew the] PEELED CUCUMBERS EITHER IN BROTH [1] OR IN A WINE SAUCE;
  4727	[and] YOU WILL FIND THEM TO BE TENDER AND NOT CAUSING INDIGESTION.
  4728	
  4729	    [1] Usually cucumbers are parboiled in water and then
  4730	    finished in broth; most often after being parboiled they
  4731	    are stuffed with forcemeat and then finished in broth.
  4732	
  4733	
  4734	[83] CUCUMBERS ANOTHER WAY
  4735	   _ALITER CUCUMERES RASOS_
  4736	
  4737	[Peeled cucumbers are] STEWED WITH BOILED BRAINS, CUMIN AND A LITTLE
  4738	HONEY. ADD SOME CELERY SEED, STOCK AND OIL, BIND THE GRAVY WITH EGGS
  4739	[1] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  4740	
  4741	    [1] Tor. _bis obligabis_--tie twice--for which there is
  4742	    no reason, except in case the sauce should curdle. List.
  4743	    _oleo elixabis_--fry in oil--obviously wrong, as the
  4744	    materials for this stew are already cooked. Sch. _ovis
  4745	    obligabis_--bind with eggs--which is the thing to do in
  4746	    this case.
  4747	
  4748	
  4749	[84] ANOTHER CUCUMBER RECIPE
  4750	   _ALITER CUCUMERES_
  4751	
  4752	CUCUMBERS, PEPPER, PENNYROYAL, HONEY OR CONDENSED MUST, BROTH AND
  4753	VINEGAR; ONCE IN A WHILE ONE ADDS SILPHIUM.
  4754	
  4755	    Sounds like a fancy dressing for raw sliced cucumbers,
  4756	    though there are no directions to this effect.
  4757	
  4758	
  4759	
  4760	VII
  4761	
  4762	
  4763	[85] MELON-GOURD AND MELONS
  4764	   _PEPONES ET MELONES_
  4765	
  4766	PEPPER, PENNYROYAL, HONEY OR CONDENSED MUST, BROTH AND VINEGAR; ONCE
  4767	IN A WHILE ONE ADDS SILPHIUM.
  4768	
  4769	    Same as 84; which confirms above theory. It is quite
  4770	    possible that melons were eaten raw with this fancy
  4771	    dressing. Many people enjoy melons with pepper and salt,
  4772	    or, in salad form with oil and vinegar. Gourds, however,
  4773	    to be palatable, must be boiled and served either hot or
  4774	    cold with this dressing.
  4775	
  4776
