  4778	VIII
  4779	
  4780	
  4781	[86] MALLOWS
  4782	   _MALVAS_
  4783	
  4784	THE SMALLER MALLOWS [are prepared] WITH GARUM [1], STOCK [2] OIL AND
  4785	VINEGAR; THE LARGER MALLOWS [prepare] WITH A WINE SAUCE, PEPPER AND
  4786	STOCK, [adding] CONDENSED WINE OR RAISIN WINE.
  4787	
  4788	    [1] Tor. _Garum_; List. _Oenogarum_.
  4789	
  4790	    [2] _Liquamen_--depending upon the mode of serving the
  4791	    mallows, hot or cold.
  4792	
  4793	
  4794	
  4795	IX
  4796	
  4797	
  4798	[87] YOUNG CABBAGE, SPROUTS [1]
  4799	   _CYMAS ET CAULICULOS_ [2]
  4800	
  4801	[Boil the] SPROUTS; [1] [season with] CUMIN [3], SALT, WINE AND OIL;
  4802	IF YOU LIKE [add] PEPPER, LOVAGE, MINT, RUE, CORIANDER; THE TENDER
  4803	LEAVES OF THE STALKS [stew] IN BROTH; WINE AND OIL BE THE SEASONING.
  4804	
  4805	    [1] Including, perhaps, cauliflower and broccoli.
  4806	
  4807	    [2] List. _Cimæ & Coliculi. Nunc crudi cum condimentis
  4808	    nunc elixati inferentur._ Served sometimes raw with
  4809	    dressing, sometimes boiled.
  4810	
  4811	    [3] Cumin or carraway seed is still used today in the
  4812	    preparation of the delicious "Bavarian" cabbage which
  4813	    also includes wine and other spices.
  4814	
  4815	
  4816	[88] ANOTHER WAY
  4817	   _ALITER_
  4818	
  4819	CUT THE STALKS IN HALF AND BOIL THEM. THE LEAVES ARE MASHED AND
  4820	SEASONED WITH CORIANDER, ONION, CUMIN, PEPPER, RAISIN WINE, OR
  4821	CONDENSED WINE AND A LITTLE OIL.
  4822	
  4823	    Very sensible way of using cabbage stalks that are
  4824	    usually thrown away. Note the almost scientific
  4825	    procedure: the stalks are separated from the leaves,
  4826	    split to facilitate cooking; they are cooked separately
  4827	    because they require more time than the tender greens.
  4828	
  4829	    Our present method appears barbarous in comparison. We
  4830	    quarter the cabbage head, and either boil it or steam
  4831	    it. As a result either the tender leaves are cooked to
  4832	    death or the stems are still hard. The overcooked parts
  4833	    are not palatable, the underdone ones indigestible. Such
  4834	    being the case, our boiled cabbage is a complete loss,
  4835	    unless prepared the Apician way.
  4836	
  4837	
  4838	[89] ANOTHER WAY
  4839	   _ALITER_
  4840	
  4841	THE COOKED [1] STALKS ARE PLACED IN A [baking] DISH; MOISTEN WITH
  4842	STOCK AND PURE OIL, SEASON WITH CUMIN, SPRINKLE [2] WITH PEPPER,
  4843	LEEKS, CUMIN, AND GREEN CORIANDER [all] CHOPPED UP.
  4844	
  4845	    [1] Tor. _Coliculi assati_--_sauté_, fried; (Remember:
  4846	    _Choux de Bruxelles sauté_) List. _elixati_--boiled.
  4847	    G.-V. _Cauliculi elixati_.
  4848	
  4849	    [2] Tor. _Superasperges_; G.-V. _piper asperges_.
  4850	
  4851	    Sounds like a salad of cooked cabbage. The original
  4852	    leaves us in doubt as to the temperature of the dish.
  4853	
  4854	
  4855	[90] ANOTHER WAY
  4856	   _ALITER_
  4857	
  4858	THE VEGETABLE, SEASONED AND PREPARED IN THE ABOVE WAY IS STEWED WITH
  4859	PARBOILED LEEKS.
  4860	
  4861	
  4862	[91] ANOTHER WAY
  4863	   _ALITER_
  4864	
  4865	TO THE SPROUTS OR STALKS, SEASONED AND PREPARED AS ABOVE, ARE ADDED
  4866	GREEN OLIVES WHICH ARE HEATED LIKEWISE.
  4867	
  4868	
  4869	[92] ANOTHER WAY
  4870	   _ALITER_
  4871	
  4872	PREPARE THE SPROUTS IN THE ABOVE WAY, COVER THEM WITH BOILED SPELT AND
  4873	PINE NUTS [1] AND SPRINKLE [2] WITH RAISINS.
  4874	
  4875	    [1] The nuts should not astonish us. The French today
  4876	    have a delicious dish, _Choux de Bruxelles aux
  4877	    Marrons_--Brussels Sprouts with Chestnuts. Sprouts and
  4878	    chestnuts are, of course, cooked separately; the lightly
  4879	    boiled sprouts are _sauté_ in butter; the chestnuts
  4880	    parboiled, peeled, and finished in stock with a little
  4881	    sugar or syrup, tossed in butter and served in the
  4882	    center of the sprouts.
  4883	
  4884	    The Apician formula with cereal and raisins added is too
  4885	    exotic to suit our modern taste, but without a question
  4886	    is a nutritious dish and complete from a dietetic point
  4887	    of view.
  4888	
  4889	    [2] Tor. _Superasperges_; G.-V. _piper asperges_.
  4890	
  4891	
  4892	
  4893	X
  4894	
  4895	
  4896	[93] LEEKS
  4897	   _PORROS_
  4898	
  4899	WELL MATURED LEEKS [1] ARE BOILED WITH A PINCH OF SALT [2] IN
  4900	[combined] WATER AND OIL [3]. THEY ARE THEN STEWED IN OIL AND IN THE
  4901	BEST KIND OF BROTH, AND SERVED.
  4902	
  4903	    [1] Tor. _Poros bene maturos_; G.-V. _maturos fieri_.
  4904	
  4905	    [2] One of the rare instances where Apicius mentions
  4906	    salt in cookery, i.e., salt in a dry form. _Pugnum
  4907	    salis_--a fist of salt--he prescribes here. Usually it
  4908	    is _liquamen_--broth, brine--he uses.
  4909	
  4910	    [3] Tor. is correct in finishing the sentence here.
  4911	    G.-V. continue _et eximes._, which is the opening of the
  4912	    next sentence, and it makes a difference in the formula.
  4913	
  4914	
  4915	[94] ANOTHER WAY TO COOK LEEKS
  4916	   _ALITER PORROS_
  4917	
  4918	WRAP THE LEEKS WELL IN CABBAGE LEAVES, HAVING FIRST COOKED THEM AS
  4919	DIRECTED ABOVE [1] AND THEN FINISH THEM IN THE ABOVE WAY.
  4920	
  4921	    [1] Tor. _in primis_--first; List., G.-V. _in
  4922	    prunis_--hot embers.
  4923	
  4924	
  4925	[95] ANOTHER WAY
  4926	   _ALITER PORROS_
  4927	
  4928	COOK THE LEEKS WITH [laurel] BERRIES [1], [and otherwise treat them]
  4929	AND SERVE AS ABOVE.
  4930	
  4931	    [1] Tor. _Porros in bacca coctos_; List. _in
  4932	    cacabo_--cooked in a casserole; Sch. _bafa
  4933	    embama_--steeped, marinated (in oil); G.-V. _in baca
  4934	    coctos_. Another way to read this: _baca et fabæ_--with
  4935	    beans--is quite within reason. The following formula,
  4936	    96, is perhaps only a variant of the above.
  4937	
  4938	    Brandt: with olives, referring to No. 91 as a precedent.
  4939	
  4940	
  4941	[96] LEEKS AND BEANS
  4942	   _ALITER PORROS_
  4943	
  4944	AFTER HAVING BOILED THE LEEKS IN WATER, [green string] BEANS WHICH
  4945	HAVE NOT YET BEEN PREPARED OTHERWISE, MAY BE BOILED [in the leek
  4946	water] [1] PRINCIPALLY ON ACCOUNT OF THE GOOD TASTE THEY WILL ACQUIRE;
  4947	AND MAY THEN BE SERVED WITH THE LEEKS.
  4948	
  4949	    [1] Apicius needed no modern science of nutrition to
  4950	    remind him of the value of the mineral salts in
  4951	    vegetables.
  4952	
  4953	
  4954	
  4955	XI
  4956	
  4957	
  4958	[97] BEETS
  4959	   _BETAS_
  4960	
  4961	TO MAKE A DISH OF BEETS THAT WILL APPEAL TO YOUR TASTE [1] SLICE [the
  4962	beets, [2] with] LEEKS AND CRUSH CORIANDER AND CUMIN; ADD RAISIN WINE
  4963	[3], BOIL ALL DOWN TO PERFECTION: BIND IT, SERVE [the beets] SEPARATE
  4964	FROM THE BROTH, WITH OIL AND VINEGAR.
  4965	
  4966	    [1] Sentence in Tor.; wanting in List. _et al._
  4967	
  4968	    [2] List. No mention of beets is made in this formula;
  4969	    therefore, it may belong to the foregoing leek recipes.
  4970	    V. This is not so. Here the noun is made subject to the
  4971	    first verb, as is practiced frequently. Moreover, the
  4972	    mode of preparation fits beets nicely, except for the
  4973	    flour to which we object in note 3, below. To cook beets
  4974	    with leeks, spices and wine and serve them (cold) with
  4975	    oil and vinegar is indeed a method that cannot be
  4976	    improved upon.
  4977	
  4978	    [3] Tac., Tor., List., G.-V. _uvam passam_,
  4979	    _Farinam_--raisins and flour--for which there is no
  4980	    reason. Sch. _varianam_--raisin wine of the Varianian
  4981	    variety; Bas. _Phariam_. V. inclined to agree with Sch.
  4982	    and Bas.
  4983	
  4984	
  4985	[98] ANOTHER WAY
  4986	   _ALITER BETAS ELIXAS_
  4987	
  4988	COOK THE BEETS WITH MUSTARD [seed] AND SERVE THEM WELL PICKLED IN A
  4989	LITTLE OIL AND VINEGAR.
  4990	
  4991	    V. Add bay leaves, cloves, pepper grains, sliced onion
  4992	    and a little sugar, and you have our modern pickled
  4993	    beets.
  4994	
  4995	
  4996	
  4997	XII
  4998	
  4999	
  5000	[99] GREEN VEGETABLES, POT HERBS
  5001	   _OLISERA_ [1]
  5002	
  5003	[The greens] TIED IN HANDY BUNDLES, COOKED AND SERVED WITH PURE OIL;
  5004	ALSO PROPER WITH FRIED FISH.
  5005	
  5006	    [1] Tac. _Olisera_; Tor. _Olifera_ (_sev mauis olyra_)
  5007	    Tor. is mistaken. Hum., List. _Olisatra_; (old Ms. note
  5008	    in our Hum. copy: "_Alessandrina uulgò_") from
  5009	    _olusatrum_--_olus_--pot herbs, cabbage, turnips. G.-V.
  5010	    _Holisera_, from _holus_, i.e. _olus_ and from _olitor_
  5011	    one who raises pot herbs.
  5012	
  5013	
  5014	
  5015	XIII
  5016	
  5017	
  5018	[100] TURNIPS OR NAVEWS
  5019	    _RAPAS SIVE NAPOS_
  5020	
  5021	[Turnips are] COOKED [soft, the water is] SQUEEZED [out; then] CRUSH A
  5022	GOOD AMOUNT OF CUMIN AND A LITTLE RUE, ADD PARTHICAN [1] LASER OR [2]
  5023	VINEGAR, STOCK, CONDENSED WINE AND OIL [3] HEAT MODERATELY AND SERVE.
  5024	
  5025	    [1] i.e. Persian laser; List. _laser, Parthicum_; (the
  5026	    comma makes a difference!) Sch. _particum_--a part.
  5027	
  5028	    [2] Tac., Tor. _vel acetum_; List. G.-V. _mel, acetum_.
  5029	    Another comma; and "honey" instead of "or." V. We doubt
  5030	    this: the vinegar is an alternative, for it takes the
  5031	    place of the more expensive Persian _laser_ (which was
  5032	    an essence of the _laser_ root, often diluted with
  5033	    vinegar).
  5034	
  5035	    [3] List., G.-V. _oleum modice_: _fervere_; Tor. _&
  5036	    oleum, quæ modice fervere facias_. Again note Lister's
  5037	    punctuation here and in the foregoing notes. The
  5038	    misplaced commas and colons raise havoc with the formulæ
  5039	    everywhere. Torinus, who in his preface complains that
  5040	    his authority has no punctuation whatsoever and thereby
  5041	    indicates that it must have been a very ancient copy,
  5042	    (at least prior to the 1503 Tac. ed.) is generally not
  5043	    far from the mark. It is also doubtful that the variants
  5044	    are by him, as is claimed by List. In this instance,
  5045	    indeed, Tor. is again correct.
  5046	
  5047	
  5048	[101] ANOTHER WAY [1]
  5049	    _ALITER RAPAS SIVE NAPOS_
  5050	
  5051	[The turnips are] BOILED, SERVED DRESSED WITH OIL, TO WHICH, IF
  5052	DESIRED, YOU MAY ADD VINEGAR [2].
  5053	
  5054	    [1] Tor. _ad delitias_--delightful.
  5055	
  5056	    [2] V. Presumably served cold, as a salad; cf. No. 122.
  5057	
  5058	
  5059	
  5060	XIV
  5061	
  5062	
  5063	[102] RADISHES
  5064	    _RAPHANOS_
  5065	
  5066	PEPPER THE RADISHES WELL; OR, EQUALLY WELL: GRATE IT WITH PEPPER AND
  5067	BRINE.
  5068	
  5069	    Sch., G.-V. _Rafanos_; _Raphanos agria_,--a kind of
  5070	    horseradish; Plinius: h.e. _raphanus sylvestris_.
  5071	
  5072	
  5073	
  5074	XV
  5075	
  5076	
  5077	[103] SOFT CABBAGE
  5078	    _OLUS MOLLE_
  5079	
  5080	THE CABBAGE IS COOKED WITH POT HERBS IN SODA WATER; PRESS [the water
  5081	out] CHOP IT VERY FINE: [now] CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, DRY SATURY WITH
  5082	DRY ONIONS, ADD STOCK, OIL AND WINE.
  5083	
  5084	
  5085	[104] ANOTHER MASHED GREEN VEGETABLE
  5086	    _ALTER OLUS MOLLE_ [_EX APIO_]
  5087	
  5088	COOK CELERY IN SODA WATER, SQUEEZE [water out] CHOP FINE. IN THE
  5089	MORTAR CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, ONION [and mix with] WINE AND
  5090	STOCK, ADDING SOME OIL. COOK THIS IN THE BOILER [1] AND MIX THE
  5091	CELERY WITH THIS PREPARATION.
  5092	
  5093	    [1] _in pultario_. The _pultarius_ is a pot in which
  5094	    cereals were boiled; from _puls_--porridge, pap.
  5095	
  5096	
  5097	[105] ANOTHER MASHED VEGETABLE
  5098	    _ALITER OLUS MOLLE_ [_EX LACTUCIS_]
  5099	
  5100	COOK THE LETTUCE LEAVES WITH ONION IN SODA WATER, SQUEEZE [the water
  5101	out] CHOP VERY FINE; IN THE MORTAR CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CELERY SEED,
  5102	DRY MINT, ONION; ADD STOCK, OIL AND WINE.
  5103	
  5104	
  5105	[106] TO PREVENT MASHED VEGETABLES FROM TURNING
  5106	    _OLUS MOLLE NE ARESCAT_ [1]
  5107	
  5108	IT WILL BE REQUIRED ABOVE ALL TO CLEAN THE VEGETABLES WELL, TO CUT OFF
  5109	ALL DECAYED PARTS AND TO COVER [the cooked vegetables] WITH WORMWOOD
  5110	WATER.
  5111	
  5112	    [1] Tor. _ne ... exarescat_, the difference in the
  5113	    meaning is immaterial.
  5114	
  5115	
  5116	
  5117	XVI
  5118	
  5119	
  5120	[107] FIELD HERBS
  5121	    _HERBÆ RUSTICÆ_
  5122	
  5123	FIELD AND FOREST [1] HERBS ARE PREPARED [2] [either raw] WITH STOCK
  5124	[3] OIL AND VINEGAR [as a salad, [4]] OR AS A COOKED DISH [5] BY
  5125	ADDING PEPPER, CUMIN AND MASTICH BERRIES.
  5126	
  5127	    [1] Tor. _ac sylvestres_; V. German, _Feldsalat_.
  5128	
  5129	    [2] Tor. _parantur_; wanting in other editions.
  5130	
  5131	    [3] _Liquamine_, here interpreted as brine.
  5132	
  5133	    [4] Tac., Sch., _et al._ _a manu_; Tor. _vel
  5134	    manu_--because eaten with the hand.
  5135	
  5136	    [5] Tor. _vel in patina_.
  5137	
  5138	
  5139	
  5140	XVII
  5141	
  5142	
  5143	[108] NETTLES
  5144	    _URTICÆ_
  5145	
  5146	THE FEMALE NETTLES, WHEN THE SUN IS IN THE POSITION OF THE ARIES, IS
  5147	SUPPOSED TO RENDER VALUABLE SERVICES AGAINST AILMENTS OF VARIOUS KINDS
  5148	[1].
  5149	
  5150	    [1] Tac., List., Sch., _et al._ _adversus ægritudinem_.
  5151	
  5152	    Barthius: _Quam ægritudinem?_ etc., etc.
  5153	
  5154	    Tor. _plurifarias_!
  5155	
  5156	    Reinsenius: _ad arcendum morbum_, etc., etc.
  5157	
  5158	    Hum. _scilicet quamcunque hoc est ..._ etc., etc., etc.
  5159	
  5160	    G.-V. _si voles_.
  5161	
  5162	    V. This innocent little superstition about the curative
  5163	    qualities of the female nettle causes the savants to
  5164	    engage in various speculations.
  5165	
  5166	    Nettles are occasionally eaten as vegetables on the
  5167	    Continent.
  5168	
  5169	
  5170	
  5171	XVIII
  5172	
  5173	
  5174	[109] ENDIVES AND LETTUCE
  5175	    _INTUBA ET LACTUCÆ_
  5176	
  5177	ENDIVES [are dressed] WITH BRINE, A LITTLE OIL AND CHOPPED ONION,
  5178	INSTEAD OF THE REAL LETTUCE [1] IN WINTER TIME THE ENDIVES ARE TAKEN
  5179	OUT OF THE PICKLE [2] [and are dressed] WITH HONEY OR VINEGAR.
  5180	
  5181	    [1] Hum. _pro lactucis uere_; Tor. _p. l. accipint_;
  5182	    G.-V. _p. l. vero_ (separated by period)--all indicating
  5183	    that endives are a substitute for lettuce when this is
  5184	    not available.
  5185	
  5186	    [2] Cf. ℞ No. 27, also Nos. 22 and 23.
  5187	
  5188	
  5189	[110] LETTUCE SALAD, FIELD SALAD
  5190	    _AGRESTES LACTUCÆ_ [1]
  5191	
  5192	[Dress it] WITH VINEGAR DRESSING AND A LITTLE BRINE STOCK; WHICH HELPS
  5193	DIGESTION AND IS TAKEN TO COUNTERACT INFLATION [2].
  5194	
  5195	    [1] Tor. _sic_; Hum. _agri l._; Tac. _id._; Sch. and
  5196	    G.-V. have _acri_ as an adjective to vinegar, the last
  5197	    word in the preceding formula.
  5198	
  5199	    [2] List. and Hum. continuing: "And this salad will not
  5200	    hurt you"; but Tor., Sch. and G.-V. use this as a
  5201	    heading for the following formula.
  5202	
  5203	
  5204	[111] A HARMLESS SALAD
  5205	    _NE LACTUCÆ LÆDANT_
  5206	
  5207	[And in order that the lettuce may not hurt you take (with it or after
  5208	it) the following preparation] [1] 2 OUNCES OF GINGER, 1 OUNCE OF
  5209	GREEN RUE, 1 OUNCE OF MEATY DATES, 12 SCRUPLES OF GROUND PEPPER, 1
  5210	OUNCE OF GOOD HONEY, AND 8 OUNCES OF EITHER ÆTHIOPIAN OR SYRIAN CUMIN.
  5211	MAKE AN INFUSION OF THIS IN VINEGAR, THE CUMIN CRUSHED, AND STRAIN. OF
  5212	THIS LIQUOR USE A SMALL SPOONFUL MIX IT WITH STOCK AND A LITTLE
  5213	VINEGAR: YOU MAY TAKE A SMALL SPOONFUL AFTER THE MEAL [2].
  5214	
  5215	    [1] Tac. and Tor. _Ne lactucæ lædant_ [take it] _cum
  5216	    zingiberis uncijs duabus_, etc. Hum., List., G.-V.
  5217	    _cumini unc. II._ They and Sch. read the _cum_ of Tac.
  5218	    and Tor. for _cumini_, overlooking the fact that the
  5219	    recipe later calls for Aethopian or Syrian cumin as
  5220	    well. This shifts the weights of the various ingredients
  5221	    from the one to the other, completely upsetting the
  5222	    sense of the formula.
  5223	
  5224	    [2] Goll. ignores this passage completely.
  5225	
  5226	    V. This is another of the medical formulæ that have
  5227	    suffered much by experimentation and interpretation
  5228	    through the ages. It seems to be an aromatic vinegar for
  5229	    a salad dressing, and, as such, a very interesting
  5230	    article, reminding of our present tarragon, etc.,
  5231	    vinegars. To be used judiciously in salads.
  5232	
  5233	    Again, as might be expected, the medicinal character of
  5234	    the formula inspires the medieval doctors to profound
  5235	    meditation and lively debate.
  5236	
  5237	    Cf. ℞ Nos. 34 and 108.
  5238	
  5239	
  5240	
  5241	XIX
  5242	
  5243	
  5244	[112] CARDOONS
  5245	    _CARDUI_
  5246	
  5247	CARDOONS [are eaten with a dressing of] BRINY BROTH, OIL, AND CHOPPED
  5248	[hard] EGGS.
  5249	
  5250	    V. Precisely as we do today: French dressing and hard
  5251	    boiled eggs. We do not forget pepper, of course. Perhaps
  5252	    the ancient "briny broth" contained enough of this and
  5253	    of other ingredients, such as fine condiments and spices
  5254	    to make the dressing perfect.
  5255	
  5256	
  5257	[113] ANOTHER [Dressing for] CARDOONS
  5258	    _ALITER CARDUOS_
  5259	
  5260	RUE, MINT, CORIANDER, FENNEL--ALL GREEN--FINELY CRUSHED; ADD PEPPER,
  5261	LOVAGE, AND [1] BRINE AND OIL [2].
  5262	
  5263	    [1] Tac. and Tor. _vel._; List., Sch., G.-V.
  5264	    _mel_--honey--which would spoil this fine _vinaigrette_
  5265	    or cold _fines herbes dressing_. However, even nowadays,
  5266	    sugar is quite frequently added to salad dressings.
  5267	
  5268	    [2] Gollmer claims that this dressing is served with
  5269	    cooked cardoons, the recipe for which follows below.
  5270	    This is wanting in Tor.
  5271	
  5272	
  5273	[114] BOILED CARDOONS
  5274	    _ALITER CARDUOS ELIXOS_
  5275	
  5276	[Are served with] PEPPER, CUMIN, BROTH AND OIL.
  5277	
  5278	
  5279	
  5280	XX
  5281	
  5282	
  5283	[115] (COW-) PARSNIPS [?]
  5284	    _SPONDYLI VEL FONDULI_ [1]
  5285	
  5286	COW-PARSNIPS ARE FRIED [and eaten] WITH A SIMPLE WINE SAUCE.
  5287	
  5288	    [1] Tac. _Spondili uel fonduli_ and _Sphon ..._; Tor. as
  5289	    above; Hum. _Spongioli uel funguli_; List., _id._; Sch.
  5290	    _Sfondili uel funguli_; G.-V. _Sphondyli uel funduli_.
  5291	
  5292	    Cf. note to Nos. 46, 121, 122.
  5293	
  5294	
  5295	[116] ANOTHER WAY
  5296	    _ALITER_
  5297	
  5298	BOIL THE PARSNIPS IN SALT WATER [and season them] WITH PURE OIL [1],
  5299	CHOPPED GREEN CORIANDER AND WHOLE PEPPER.
  5300	
  5301	    [1] Tac. _Oleo mero_; Other editors: _Oleo, mero_. V.
  5302	    The comma is misplaced.
  5303	
  5304	
  5305	[117] ANOTHER WAY
  5306	    _ALITER_
  5307	
  5308	PREPARE THE BOILED PARSNIPS WITH THE FOLLOWING SAUCE: CELERY SEED,
  5309	RUE, HONEY, GROUND PEPPER, MIXED WITH RAISIN WINE, STOCK AND A LITTLE
  5310	OIL; BIND THIS WITH ROUX [bring to a boiling point, immerse parsnips]
  5311	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  5312	
  5313	
  5314	[118] ANOTHER WAY [Purée of Parsnips] [1]
  5315	    _ALITER_
  5316	
  5317	MASH THE PARSNIPS, [add] CUMIN, RUE, STOCK, A LITTLE CONDENSED
  5318	WINE, OIL, GREEN CORIANDER [and] LEEKS AND SERVE; GOES WELL WITH
  5319	SALT PORK [2].
  5320	
  5321	    [1] Again faulty punctuation obscures the text.
  5322	    Carefully compare the following: Tac. and Tor.
  5323	    _Spondylos teres, cuminum_, etc. Hum., List. and G.-V.
  5324	    _S. teres cuminum_, i.e. crush the cumin. Sch. _S.
  5325	    tores_--dry, parch!
  5326	
  5327	    [2] _Inferes pro salso_--serve with salt pork or bacon,
  5328	    or, instead of--_Salsum_--salt pork. Dann. Well seasoned
  5329	    with salt! Sch. _infares pro salsa_. For further
  5330	    confirmation of _salsum_ cf. ℞ Nos. 148-152.
  5331	
  5332	
  5333	[119] ANOTHER WAY
  5334	    _ALITER_
  5335	
  5336	BOIL THE PARSNIPS [sufficiently, if] HARD [1] [then] PUT THEM IN A
  5337	SAUCE PAN AND STEW WITH OIL, STOCK, PEPPER, RAISIN WINE, STRAIN [2]
  5338	AND BIND WITH ROUX.
  5339	
  5340	    [1] Tor. _præduratos_; List. _prædurabis_. How can they
  5341	    be hardened? It may perhaps stand for "parboil." We
  5342	    agree with Tor. that the hard ones (_præduratos_) must
  5343	    be cooked soft.
  5344	
  5345	    [2] Tor. and Tac. _Colabis_--strain; List. and G.-V.
  5346	    _Colorabis_--color. No necessity for coloring the gravy,
  5347	    but straining after the binding with roux is important
  5348	    which proves Tor. correct again. Cf. note 1 to ℞ No.
  5349	    73 and note 2 to ℞ No. 55.
  5350	
  5351	
  5352	[120] ANOTHER WAY
  5353	    _ALITER_ [1]
  5354	
  5355	FINISH [marinate] THE PARSNIPS IN OIL AND BROTH, OR FRY THEM IN OIL,
  5356	SPRINKLE WITH SALT AND PEPPER, AND SERVE.
  5357	
  5358	    [1] Ex G.-V. wanting in Tor. and List. Found in Sch.
  5359	    also. V. Procedure quite in accordance with modern
  5360	    practice. We envelope the p. in flour or frying batter.
  5361	
  5362	
  5363	[121] ANOTHER WAY
  5364	    _ALITER_ [1]
  5365	
  5366	BRUISE THE BOILED PARSNIPS [scallops, muscular part of shellfish]
  5367	ELIMINATE THE HARD STRINGS; ADD BOILED SPELT AND CHOPPED HARD EGGS,
  5368	STOCK AND PEPPER. MAKE CROQUETTES OR SAUSAGE FROM THIS, ADDING
  5369	PIGNOLIA NUT AND PEPPER, WRAP IN CAUL [or fill in casings] FRY AND
  5370	SERVE THEM AS AN ENTRÉE DISH IN A WINE SAUCE.
  5371	
  5372	    [1] V. This formula is virtually a repetition of ℞ No.
  5373	    46, all the more bewildering because of the divergence
  5374	    of the term (Cf. ℞ No. 115), which stands for
  5375	    "scallops" or the muscular part of any bivalve, at least
  5376	    in the above formula.
  5377	
  5378	    The Græco-Latin word for cow-parsnip is _spondylium_,
  5379	    _sphondylium_, _spondylion_. It is almost certain that
  5380	    the preceding parsnips formulæ are in the right place
  5381	    here. They are in direct line with the other vegetables
  5382	    here treated--the shellfish--_spondylus_--would be out
  5383	    of place in this chapter, Book III, The Gardener. All
  5384	    the recipes, with the exception of the above, fit a
  5385	    vegetable like parsnips. Even Lister's and Humelberg's
  5386	    interpretation of the term, who read
  5387	    _spongioli_--mushrooms--could be questioned under this
  5388	    heading, Book III.
  5389	
  5390	    It is barely possible that this entire series of
  5391	    formulæ, _Spondyli uel fonduli_ (℞ Nos. 115-121) does
  5392	    belong to Book II among the scallop _hysitia_, though we
  5393	    are little inclined to accept this theory.
  5394	
  5395	    Cf. ℞ No. 122 which appears to be a confirmation of
  5396	    the view expressed above.
  5397	
  5398	
  5399	
  5400	XXI
  5401	
  5402	
  5403	[122] CARROTS AND PARSNIPS
  5404	    _CAROTÆ ET PASTINACÆ_
  5405	
  5406	CARROTS OR PARSNIPS ARE FRIED [and served] WITH A WINE SAUCE.
  5407	
  5408	    V. Exactly like ℞ No. 115, which may be a
  5409	    confirmation that _spondyli_ stands for cow-parsnips.
  5410	
  5411	
  5412	[123] ANOTHER WAY
  5413	    _ALITER_
  5414	
  5415	THE CARROTS [are cooked] SALTED [and served] WITH PURE OIL AND
  5416	VINEGAR.
  5417	
  5418	    V. As a salad. "Italian Salad" consists of a variety of
  5419	    such cooked vegetables, nicely dressed with oil and
  5420	    vinegar, or with mayonnaise. Cf. ℞ No. 102.
  5421	
  5422	
  5423	[124] ANOTHER WAY
  5424	    _ALITER_
  5425	
  5426	THE CARROTS [are] BOILED [and] SLICED, STEWED WITH CUMIN AND A LITTLE
  5427	OIL AND ARE SERVED. AT THE SAME TIME [1] [here is your opportunity]
  5428	MAKE A CUMIN SAUCE [from the carrot juice] FOR THOSE WHO HAVE THE
  5429	COLIC [2].
  5430	
  5431	    [1] Ex Tor. wanting elsewhere.
  5432	
  5433	    [2] Tac. _coliorum_; Tor. _cuminatum colicorum_; List.
  5434	    _c. coloratum_--colored; G.-V. _c. colorium_.
  5435	
  5436	
  5437	END OF BOOK III
  5438	
  5439	_EXPLICIT APICII CEPURICA DE OLERIBUS LIBER TERTIUS_ [Tac.]

  5441	
  5442	
  5443	
  5444	{Illustration: THERMOSPODIUM OF PLAIN DESIGN
  5445	
  5446	Water and food heater for everyday purposes. Charcoal fuel. Foods were
  5447	kept on top in pans, dishes or pots, and were thus carried from the
  5448	kitchen into the dining room. They were also used for food service in
  5449	hotel rooms, supplied from adjacent tavern kitchens, as some hotels
  5450	had no food preparation facilities. This handy apparatus was designed
  5451	for general utility, as it also served as a portable stove on chilly
  5452	days in living rooms that were not heated from the central heating
  5453	plant found in larger houses. Ntl. Mus. Naples, 73882; Field M.
  5454	24179.}

  5480	BOOK IV. MISCELLANEA
  5481	
  5482	_Lib. IV. Pandecter_ [1]
  5483	
  5484	
  5485	    CHAP.   I. BOILED DINNERS.
  5486	    CHAP.  II. DISHES OF FISH, VEGETABLES, FRUITS, AND SO FORTH.
  5487	    CHAP. III. FINELY MINCED DISHES, OR _ISICIA_.
  5488	    CHAP.  IV. PORRIDGE, GRUEL.
  5489	    CHAP.   V. APPETIZING DISHES.
  5490	
  5491	
  5492	
  5493	I
  5494	
  5495	
  5496	[125] BOILED DINNER
  5497	    _SALACATTABIA_ [2]
  5498	
  5499	PEPPER, FRESH MINT, CELERY, DRY PENNYROYAL, CHEESE [3], PIGNOLIA NUTS,
  5500	HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH, YOLKS OF EGG, FRESH WATER, SOAKED BREAD AND THE
  5501	LIQUID PRESSED OUT, COW'S CHEESE AND CUCUMBERS ARE ARRANGED IN A DISH,
  5502	ALTERNATELY, WITH THE NUTS; [also add] FINELY CHOPPED CAPERS [4],
  5503	CHICKEN LIVERS [5]; COVER COMPLETELY WITH [a lukewarm, congealing]
  5504	BROTH, PLACE ON ICE [and when congealed unmould and] SERVE UP [6].
  5505	
  5506	    [1] Read: _Pandectes_--embracing the whole science.
  5507	
  5508	    [2] Read: _Salacaccabia_--from _salsa_ and
  5509	    _caccabus_--salt meat boiled in the pot. Sch. _Sala
  5510	    cottabia_; G.-V. _cattabia_.
  5511	
  5512	    [3] Sch. _casiam_ instead of _caseum_.
  5513	
  5514	    [4] Sch. _Copadiis porcinis_--small bits of pork; List.
  5515	    _cepas aridas puto_--"shallots, I believe"; Lan.
  5516	    _capparis_; Vat., G.-V. _id._
  5517	
  5518	    [5] Dann. Chicken meat.
  5519	
  5520	    [6] This dish if pork were added (cf. Sch. in note 4
  5521	    above) would resemble our modern "headcheese"; the
  5522	    presence of cheese in this formula and in our word
  5523	    "headcheese" is perhaps not accidental; the cheese has
  5524	    been eliminated in the course of time from dishes of
  5525	    this sort while the name has remained with us. "Cheese"
  5526	    also appears in the German equivalent for
  5527	    custard--_Eierkäse_.
  5528	
  5529	
  5530	[126] APICIAN JELLY
  5531	    _SALACATTABIA APICIANA_
  5532	
  5533	PUT IN THE MORTAR CELERY SEED, DRY PENNYROYAL, DRY MINT, GINGER, FRESH
  5534	CORIANDER, SEEDLESS RAISINS, HONEY, VINEGAR, OIL AND WINE; CRUSH IT
  5535	TOGETHER [in order to make a dressing of it]. [Now] PLACE 3 PIECES OF
  5536	PICENTIAN BREAD IN A MOULD, INTERLINED WITH PIECES OF [cooked]
  5537	CHICKEN, [cooked] SWEETBREADS OF CALF OR LAMB, CHEESE [1], PIGNOLIA
  5538	NUTS, CUCUMBERS [pickles] FINELY CHOPPED DRY ONIONS [shallots]
  5539	COVERING THE WHOLE WITH [jellified] BROTH. BURY THE MOULD IN SNOW UP
  5540	TO THE RIM; [unmould] SPRINKLE [with the above dressing] AND SERVE
  5541	[2].
  5542	
  5543	    [1] List. _caseum Vestinum_--a certain cheese from the
  5544	    Adriatic coast.
  5545	
  5546	    [2] The nature of the first passage of this formula
  5547	    indicates a dressing for a cold dish. The dish was
  5548	    probably unmoulded when firm, and the jelly covered with
  5549	    this dressing, though the original does not state this
  5550	    procedure. In that case it would resemble a highly
  5551	    complicated chicken salad, such as we make
  5552	    today--_mayonnaise de volaille en aspic_, for instance.
  5553	    We recall the artistic molds for puddings and other
  5554	    dishes which the ancients had which were nicely suited
  5555	    for dishes such as the above.
  5556	
  5557	    The Picentian bread--made of spelt--was a celebrated
  5558	    product of the bakeries of Picentia, a town of lower
  5559	    Italy, near the Tuscan sea, according to Pliny.
  5560	
  5561	    Cf. ℞ No. 141.
  5562	
  5563	
  5564	[127] OTHER SALACACCABIA
  5565	    _ALITER_
  5566	
  5567	HOLLOW OUT AN ALEXANDRINE LOAF OF BREAD, SOAK THE CRUMBS WITH POSCA [a
  5568	mixture of water, wine, vinegar or lemon juice] AND MAKE A PASTE OF
  5569	IT. PUT IN THE MORTAR PEPPER, HONEY [1] MINT, GARLIC, FRESH CORIANDER,
  5570	SALTED COW'S CHEESE, WATER AND OIL. WINE [2] POURED OVER BEFORE
  5571	SERVING [3].
  5572	
  5573	    [1] Wanting in Tor.
  5574	
  5575	    [2] G.-V. _insuper nivem_--chilled on snow (like the
  5576	    preceding formula). Tac. _insuper vinum_; Sch. _id._
  5577	
  5578	    [3] A panada as is found in every old cookery book.
  5579	    Today it remains as a dressing for roast fowl, etc.
  5580	    Quoting from "A Collection of Receipts in Cookery,
  5581	    Physick and Surgery," London, 1724:
  5582	
  5583	    "Panada for a Sick or Weak Stomach. Put the crumbs of a
  5584	    Penny White-Loaf grated into a Quart of cold Water, set
  5585	    both on the Fire together with a blade of Mace: When
  5586	    'tis boil'd smooth, take it off the fire and put in a
  5587	    bit of Lemon-peel, the juice of a Lemon, a glass of Sack
  5588	    [Spanish Wine] and Sugar to your Taste. This is very
  5589	    Nourishing and never offends the Stomach. Some season
  5590	    with butter and Sugar, adding Currants which on some
  5591	    occasions are proper; but the first is the most grateful
  5592	    and innocent."
  5593	
  5594	    Mrs. Glasse, a quarter century later, in her famous book
  5595	    [The Art of Cookery Made Plain and Easy, London, 1747,
  5596	    1st ed.] omits the wine, but Mrs. Mason, at about the
  5597	    same time, insists on having it with panada.
  5598	
  5599	    The imaginary or real relation between the sciences of
  5600	    cookery and medicine is illustrated here.
  5601	
  5602	
  5603	
  5604	II
  5605	
  5606	DISHES OF FISH, VEGETABLES, FRUITS AND SO FORTH
  5607	  _PATINÆ PISCIUM, HOLERUM & POMORUM_
  5608	
  5609	
  5610	[128] EVERYDAY DISH
  5611	    _PATINA QUOTIDIANA_ [1]
  5612	
  5613	MAKE A PASTE OF STEWED BRAINS [calf's, pig's, etc.] SEASON WITH
  5614	PEPPER, CUMIN, LASER, BROTH, THICKENED WINE, MILK AND EGGS [2] POACH
  5615	IT OVER A WEAK FIRE OR IN A HOT WATER [BATH].
  5616	
  5617	    [1] Tac. _quottidiana_; List. _cottidiana_.
  5618	
  5619	    [2] List. _ovis_--with eggs, which is correct. Tor.
  5620	    _holus_; Lan. _olus_--herbs, cabbage.
  5621	
  5622	    Cf. ℞ No. 142.
  5623	
  5624	
  5625	[129] ANOTHER DISH, WHICH CAN BE TURNED OVER [A Nut Custard]
  5626	    _ALITER PATINA VERSATILIS_
  5627	
  5628	THE DISH, CALLED TURN-OVER, IS THUS MADE [1] CRUSH VERY FINE WALNUTS
  5629	AND HAZELNUTS [2] TOAST THEM AND CRUSH WITH HONEY, MIX IN PEPPER,
  5630	BROTH, MILK AND EGGS AND A LITTLE OIL [3].
  5631	
  5632	    [1] Tor.
  5633	
  5634	    [2] List. _torres eas_--toast them (wanting in Tor.)
  5635	    which is the thing to do. Cf. No. 143, practically a
  5636	    repetition of this. Cf. 301.
  5637	
  5638	    [3] This laconic formula indicates a custard poached,
  5639	    like in the preceding, in a mould, which, when cooled
  5640	    off, is unmoulded in the usual way. This _patina
  5641	    versatilis_ is in fact the modern _crême renversée_,
  5642	    with nuts.
  5643	
  5644	    It is characteristic of Apicius for incompleteness and
  5645	    want of precise directions, without which the experiment
  5646	    in the hands of an inexperienced operator would result
  5647	    in failure.
  5648	
  5649	
  5650	[130] ANOTHER
  5651	    _ALITER PATINA_
  5652	
  5653	ANOTHER DISH IS MADE OF THE [1] STRUNKS OF LETTUCE CRUSHED WITH
  5654	PEPPER, BROTH, THICKENED WINE, [add] WATER AND OIL, AND COOK THIS;
  5655	BIND WITH EGGS, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE [2].
  5656	
  5657	    [1] Tor.
  5658	
  5659	    [2] Very much like a modern soup, purée of lettuce.
  5660	
  5661	
  5662	[131] VEGETABLE AND BRAIN PUDDING
  5663	    _PATINA FRISILIS_ [1]
  5664	
  5665	TAKE VEGETABLES, CLEAN AND WASH, SHRED [2] AND COOK THEM [3] COOL THEM
  5666	OFF AND DRAIN THEM. TAKE 4 [calf's] BRAINS, REMOVE [the skin and]
  5667	STRINGS AND COOK THEM [4] IN THE MORTAR PUT 6 SCRUPLES OF PEPPER,
  5668	MOISTEN WITH BROTH AND CRUSH FINE; THEN ADD THE BRAINS, RUB AGAIN AND
  5669	MEANWHILE ADD THE VEGETABLES, RUBBING ALL THE WHILE, AND MAKE A FINE
  5670	PASTE OF IT. THEREUPON BREAK AND ADD 8 EGGS. NOW ADD A GLASSFUL [5] OF
  5671	BROTH, A GLASSFUL OF WINE, A GLASSFUL OF RAISIN WINE, TASTE THIS
  5672	PREPARATION. OIL THE BAKING DISH THOROUGHLY [put the mixture in the
  5673	dish] AND PLACE IT IN THE HOT PLATE, (THAT IS ABOVE THE HOT ASHES) [6]
  5674	AND WHEN IT IS DONE [unmould it] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE [7].
  5675	
  5676	    [1] List. _frictilis_; Vat. Ms. _fusilis_; G.-V. _id._;
  5677	    Lan. _frisilis_.
  5678	
  5679	    _Patina frisilis_ remains unexplained. None of the
  5680	    various readings can be satisfactorily rendered. If the
  5681	    vegetables had remained whole the dish might be compared
  5682	    to a _chartreuse_, those delightful creations by the
  5683	    Carthusian monks who compelled by the strictest rules of
  5684	    vegetarianism evolved a number of fine vegetable dishes.
  5685	    On the other hand, the poached mixture of eggs and
  5686	    brains is akin to our _farces_ and _quenelles_; but in
  5687	    modern cookery we have nothing just like this _patina
  5688	    frisilis_.
  5689	
  5690	    [2] Wanting in List.
  5691	
  5692	    [3] and [4] Wanting in Tor.
  5693	
  5694	    [5] _Cyathum._
  5695	
  5696	    [6] Sentence in () ex Tor.
  5697	
  5698	    [7] This and some of the following recipes are
  5699	    remarkable for their preciseness and completeness.
  5700	
  5701	
  5702	[132] ANOTHER COLD ASPARAGUS [and Figpecker] DISH
  5703	    _ALITER PATINA DE ASPARAGIS FRIGIDA_
  5704	
  5705	COLD ASPARAGUS PIE IS MADE IN THIS MANNER [1] TAKE WELL CLEANED
  5706	[cooked] ASPARAGUS, CRUSH IT IN THE MORTAR, DILUTE WITH WATER AND
  5707	PRESENTLY STRAIN IT THROUGH THE COLANDER. NOW TRIM, PREPARE [i.e. cook
  5708	or roast] FIGPECKERS [2] [and hold them in readiness]. 3 [3] SCRUPLES
  5709	OF PEPPER ARE CRUSHED IN THE MORTAR, ADD BROTH, A GLASS OF WINE, PUT
  5710	THIS IN A SAUCEPAN WITH 3 OUNCES OF OIL, HEAT THOROUGHLY. MEANWHILE
  5711	OIL YOUR PIE MOULD, AND WITH 6 EGGS, FLAVORED WITH ŒNOGARUM, AND
  5712	THE ASPARAGUS PREPARATION AS DESCRIBED ABOVE; THICKEN THE MIXTURE ON
  5713	THE HOT ASHES. THEREUPON ARRANGE THE FIGPECKERS IN THE MOULD, COVER
  5714	THEM WITH THIS PURÉE, BAKE THE DISH. [When cold, unmould it] SPRINKLE
  5715	WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  5716	
  5717	    [1] Tor.
  5718	
  5719	    [2] Lan. and Tac. _ficedulas curtas tres_; Tor. _curtas_
  5720	    f.--three figpeckers cut fine. G.-V. _F. curatas. Teres
  5721	    in ..._ (etc.)--Prepared _F._
  5722	
  5723	    [3] List. six; G.-V. _id._
  5724	
  5725	
  5726	[133] ANOTHER ASPARAGUS CUSTARD
  5727	    _ALIA PATINA DE ASPARAGIS_
  5728	
  5729	ASPARAGUS PIE IS MADE LIKE THIS [1] PUT IN THE MORTAR ASPARAGUS TIPS
  5730	[2] CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, GREEN CORIANDER, SAVORY AND ONIONS; CRUSH,
  5731	DILUTE WITH WINE, BROTH AND OIL. PUT THIS IN A WELL-GREASED PAN, AND,
  5732	IF YOU LIKE, ADD WHILE ON THE FIRE SOME BEATEN EGGS TO IT TO THICKEN
  5733	IT, COOK [without boiling the eggs] AND SPRINKLE WITH VERY FINE
  5734	PEPPER.
  5735	
  5736	    [1] Tor.
  5737	
  5738	    [2] Reference to wine wanting in Tor. We add that the
  5739	    asparagus should be cooked before crushing.
  5740	
  5741	
  5742	[134] A DISH OF FIELD VEGETABLES
  5743	    _PATINA EX RUSTICIS_ [1]
  5744	
  5745	BY FOLLOWING THE ABOVE INSTRUCTIONS YOU MAY MAKE [2] A PIE OF FIELD
  5746	VEGETABLES, OR OF THYME [3] OR OF GREEN PEPPERS [4] OR OF CUCUMBERS OR
  5747	OF SMALL TENDER SPROUTS [5] SAME AS ABOVE, OR, IF YOU LIKE, MAKE ONE
  5748	UNDERLAID WITH BONELESS PIECES OF FISH OR OF CHICKEN [combined with
  5749	any of the above vegetables] [6].
  5750	
  5751	    [1] Tor. _Patina ex oleribus agrestibus_.
  5752	
  5753	    [2] Tor. wanting in other texts.
  5754	
  5755	    [3] Sch., G.-V. _tamnis_--wild wine; List. _cymis
  5756	    cuminis_; Lan., Tac. _tinis_; Vat. Ms. _tannis_. Thyme
  5757	    is hardly likely to be the chief ingredient of such a
  5758	    dish; the chances are it was used for flavoring and that
  5759	    the above enumerated vegetables were combined in one
  5760	    dish.
  5761	
  5762	    [4] List., G.-V., Goll.--mustard; Dann. green mustard.
  5763	    Tor. _sive pipere viridi_--green peppers, which we
  5764	    accept as correct, gastronomically at least.
  5765	
  5766	    [5] Goll., Dann. cabbage, the originals have
  5767	    _coliculis_--small tender sprouts on the order of
  5768	    Brussels sprouts or broccoli, all belonging to the
  5769	    cabbage family.
  5770	
  5771	    [6] _Pulpa_--boneless pieces of meat, also fruit purée;
  5772	    _pulpamentum_--dainty bits of meat.
  5773	
  5774	
  5775	[135] ELDERBERRY CUSTARD OR PIE
  5776	    _PATINA DE SAMBUCO_ [1]
  5777	
  5778	A DISH OF ELDERBERRIES, EITHER HOT OR COLD, IS MADE IN THIS MANNER [2]
  5779	TAKE ELDERBERRIES [3] WASH THEM; COOK IN WATER, SKIM AND STRAIN.
  5780	PREPARE A DISH IN WHICH TO COOK THE CUSTARD [4] CRUSH 6 SCRUPLES OF
  5781	PEPPER WITH A LITTLE BROTH; ADD THIS TO THE ELDERBERRY PULP WITH
  5782	ANOTHER GLASS OF BROTH, A GLASS OF WINE, A GLASS OF RAISIN WINE AND AS
  5783	MUCH AS 4 OUNCES OF OIL. PUT THE DISH IN THE HOT BATH AND STIR THE
  5784	CONTENTS. AS SOON AS IT IS GETTING WARM, QUICKLY BREAK 6 EGGS AND
  5785	WHIPPING THEM, INCORPORATE THEM, IN ORDER TO THICKEN THE FLUID. WHEN
  5786	THICK ENOUGH SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE UP.
  5787	
  5788	    [1] G.-V. _Sabuco_.
  5789	
  5790	    [2] Tor. wanting in other texts.
  5791	
  5792	    [3] Hum. _semen de sambuco_--E. seed.
  5793	
  5794	    [4] List. Place the berries in a dish; to their juice
  5795	    add pepper, (etc.).
  5796	
  5797	
  5798	[136] ROSE PIE, ROSE CUSTARD OR PUDDING
  5799	    _PATINA DE ROSIS_
  5800	
  5801	TAKE ROSES FRESH FROM THE FLOWER BED, STRIP OFF THE LEAVES, REMOVE THE
  5802	WHITE [from the petals and] PUT THEM IN THE MORTAR; POUR OVER SOME
  5803	BROTH [and] RUB FINE. ADD A GLASS OF BROTH AND STRAIN THE JUICE
  5804	THROUGH THE COLANDER. [This done] TAKE 4 [cooked calf's] BRAINS, SKIN
  5805	THEM AND REMOVE THE NERVES; CRUSH 8 SCRUPLES OF PEPPER MOISTENED WITH
  5806	THE JUICE AND RUB [with the brains]; THEREUPON BREAK 8 EGGS, ADD 1 [1]
  5807	GLASS OF WINE, 1 GLASS OF RAISIN WINE AND A LITTLE OIL. MEANWHILE
  5808	GREASE A PAN, PLACE IT ON THE HOT ASHES [or in the hot bath] IN WHICH
  5809	POUR THE ABOVE DESCRIBED MATERIAL; WHEN THE MIXTURE IS COOKED IN THE
  5810	_BAIN MARIS_ [2] SPRINKLE IT WITH PULVERIZED PEPPER AND SERVE [3].
  5811	
  5812	    [1] List., G.-V. 1-1/2 glass.
  5813	
  5814	    [2] Hot water bath.
  5815	
  5816	    [3] Tor. continues ℞ No. 135 without interruption or
  5817	    caption, and describes the above recipe. He reads: _De
  5818	    thoris accipies rosas_, but List. insists that _de
  5819	    thoris_ be read _de rosis_; Lan., Tac. _de toris_; V.
  5820	    _de thoris_ may be read "fresh from the flower bed."
  5821	
  5822	    Cf. ℞ Nos. 167 and 171 in which case the "rose" may
  5823	    stand for rosy apple, or "Roman Beauty" apple. "Rose
  5824	    apple" also is a small pimento, size of a plum.
  5825	
  5826	
  5827	[137] PUMPKIN PIE
  5828	    _PATINA DE CUCURBITIS_ [1]
  5829	
  5830	AND PUMPKIN PIE IS MADE THUS [2] STEWED AND MASHED PUMPKIN IS PLACED
  5831	IN THE PAN [or pie dish] SEASONED WITH A LITTLE CUMIN ESSENCE. ADD A
  5832	LITTLE OIL; HEAT [bake] AND SERVE [3].
  5833	
  5834	    [1] Dann. Cucumber Dish.
  5835	
  5836	    [2] Tor. Wanting in other texts.
  5837	
  5838	    [3] Modern English recipes for stewed pumpkin resemble
  5839	    this Apician precept, but America has made a really
  5840	    palatable dish from pumpkin by the addition of eggs,
  5841	    cinnamon, nutmeg, ginger--spices which the insipid
  5842	    pumpkin needs. The ancient original may have omitted the
  5843	    eggs because Apicius probably expected his formula to be
  5844	    carried out in accordance with the preceding formulæ.
  5845	    Perhaps this is proven by the fact that Tor. continues
  5846	    the Rose Pie recipe with _et cucurbita patina sic fiet_.
  5847	
  5848	
  5849	[138] SPRATS OR SMELTS AU VIN BLANC
  5850	    _PATINA DE APUA_ [1]
  5851	
  5852	CLEAN THE SMELTS [or other small fish, filets of sole, etc. of white
  5853	meat] MARINATE [i.e. impregnate with] IN OIL, PLACE IN A SHALLOW PAN,
  5854	ADD OIL, BROTH [2] AND WINE. BUNCH [3] [fresh] RUE AND MARJORAM AND
  5855	COOK WITH THE FISH. WHEN DONE REMOVE THE HERBS, SEASON THE FISH WITH
  5856	PEPPER AND SERVE [4].
  5857	
  5858	    [1] Ex List. and G.-V. wanting in Tor.
  5859	
  5860	    [2] _Liquamen_, which in this case corresponds to _court
  5861	    bouillon_, a broth prepared from the trimmings of the
  5862	    fish, herbs, and wine, well-seasoned and reduced.
  5863	
  5864	    [3] Our very own _bouquet garni_, a bunch of various
  5865	    aromatic herbs, inserted during coction and retired
  5866	    before serving.
  5867	
  5868	    [4] Excellent formula for fish in white wine, resembling
  5869	    our ways of making this fine dish.
  5870	
  5871	    This again illustrates the laconic style of the ancient
  5872	    author. He omitted to say that the fish, when cooked,
  5873	    was placed on the service platter and that the juices
  5874	    remaining in the sauce pan were tied with one or two egg
  5875	    yolks, diluted with cream, or wine, or _court bouillon_,
  5876	    strained and poured over the fish at the moment of
  5877	    serving. This is perhaps the best method of preparing
  5878	    fish with white meat of a fine texture. Pink or darker
  5879	    fish do not lend themselves to this method of
  5880	    preparation.
  5881	
  5882	
  5883	[139] SMELT PIE, OR, SPRAT CUSTARD
  5884	    _PATINA DE ABUA SIVE APUA_ [1]
  5885	
  5886	BONELESS PIECES OF ANCHOVIES OR [other small] FISH, EITHER ROAST
  5887	[fried] BOILED, CHOP VERY FINE. FILL A CASSEROLE GENEROUSLY WITH THE
  5888	SAME [season with] CRUSHED PEPPER AND A LITTLE RUE, ADD SUFFICIENT
  5889	BROTH AND SOME OIL, AND MIX IN, ALSO ADD ENOUGH RAW EGGS SO THAT THE
  5890	WHOLE FORMS ONE SOLID MASS. NOW CAREFULLY ADD SOME SEA-NETTLES BUT
  5891	TAKE PAIN THAT THEY ARE NOT MIXED WITH THE EGGS. NOW PUT THE DISH INTO
  5892	THE STEAM SO THAT IT MAY CONGEAL [but avoid boiling] [2]. WHEN DONE
  5893	SPRINKLE WITH GROUND PEPPER AND CARRY INTO THE DINING ROOM. NOBODY
  5894	WILL BE ABLE TO TELL WHAT HE IS ENJOYING [3].
  5895	
  5896	    [1] Tac., Tor. _sic_. List., G.-V. _p. de apua sine
  5897	    apua_--a dish of anchovies (or smelts) without
  5898	    anchovies. Tor. formula bears the title _patina de
  5899	    apua_, and his article opens with the following
  5900	    sentence: _patin de abua sive apua sic facies_. He is
  5901	    therefore quite emphatic that the dish is to be made
  5902	    with the _abua_ or _apua_ (an anchovy) and not without
  5903	    _apua_, as List. has it. Lan. calls the dish: P. _de
  5904	    apabadiade_, not identified.
  5905	
  5906	    [2] Tor. _impones ad uaporem ut cum ouis meare
  5907	    possint_--warning, get along with the eggs, i.e. beware
  5908	    of boiling them for they will curdle, and the experiment
  5909	    is hopelessly lost. List. however, reads _meare possint_
  5910	    thus: _bullire p._--boil (!) It is quite plain that Tor.
  5911	    has the correct formula.
  5912	
  5913	    [3] _et ex esu nemo agnoscet quid manducet._ Dann.
  5914	    renders this sentence thus: "Nobody can value this dish
  5915	    unless he has partaken of it himself." He is too
  5916	    lenient. We would rather translate it literally as we
  5917	    did above, or say broadly, "And nobody will be any the
  5918	    wiser." List. dwells at length upon this sentence; his
  5919	    erudite commentary upon the _cena dubia_, the doubtful
  5920	    meal, will be found under the heading of _cena_ in our
  5921	    vocabulary. List. pp. 126-7. List. undoubtedly made the
  5922	    mistake of reading _sine_ for _sive_. He therefore
  5923	    omitted the _apua_ from his formula. The above boastful
  5924	    sentence may have induced him to do so.
  5925	
  5926	    The above is a fish forcemeat, now seldom used as an
  5927	    integral dish, but still popular as a dressing for fish
  5928	    or as quenelles. The modern fish forcemeat is usually
  5929	    made of raw fish, cream and eggs, with the necessary
  5930	    seasoning. The material is poached or cooked much in the
  5931	    same manner as prescribed by the ancient recipe.
  5932	
  5933	
  5934	[140] A RICH ENTRÉE OF FISH, POULTRY AND SAUSAGE IN CREAM
  5935	    _PATINA EX LACTE_
  5936	
  5937	SOAK [pignolia] NUTS, DRY THEM, AND ALSO HAVE FRESH SEA-URCHINS [1]
  5938	READY. TAKE A DEEP DISH [casserole] IN WHICH ARRANGE THE FOLLOWING
  5939	THINGS [in layers]: MEDIUM-SIZED MALLOWS AND BEETS, MATURE LEEKS,
  5940	CELERY, STEWED TENDER GREEN CABBAGE, AND OTHER BOILED GREEN VEGETABLES
  5941	[2], A DISJOINTED [3] CHICKEN STEWED IN ITS OWN GRAVY, COOKED [calf's
  5942	or pig's] BRAINS, LUCANIAN SAUSAGE, HARD BOILED EGGS CUT INTO HALVES,
  5943	BIG TARENTINIAN SAUSAGE [4] SLICED AND BROILED IN THE ASHES, CHICKEN
  5944	GIBLETS OR PIECES OF CHICKEN MEAT. BITS OF FRIED FISH, SEA NETTLES,
  5945	PIECES OF [stewed] OYSTERS AND FRESH CHEESE ARE ALTERNATELY PUT
  5946	TOGETHER; SPRINKLE IN BETWEEN THE NUTS AND WHOLE PEPPER, AND THE JUICE
  5947	AS IS COOKED FROM PEPPER, LOVAGE, CELERY SEED AND SILPHIUM. THIS
  5948	ESSENCE, WHEN DONE, MIX WITH MILK TO WHICH RAW EGGS HAVE BEEN ADDED
  5949	[pour this over the pieces of food in the dish] SO THAT THE WHOLE IS
  5950	THOROUGHLY COMBINED, STIFFEN IT [in the hot water bath] AND WHEN DONE
  5951	[garnish with] FRESH MUSSELS [sea-urchins, poached and chopped fine]
  5952	SPRINKLE PEPPER OVER AND SERVE.
  5953	
  5954	    [1] Sea-urchins, wanting in Tor.
  5955	
  5956	    [2] Sentence wanting in G.-V.
  5957	
  5958	    [3] _Pullum raptum_, in most texts; G.-V. _p.
  5959	    carptum_--plucked. Of course! Should _raptum_ be
  5960	    translated literally? A most atrocious way of killing
  5961	    fowl, to be sure, but anyone familiar with the habits of
  5962	    the ancients, particularly with those of the less
  5963	    educated element, should not wonder at this most bestial
  5964	    fashion, which was supposed to improve the flavor of the
  5965	    meat, a fashion which, as a matter of fact still
  5966	    survives in the Orient, particularly in China.
  5967	
  5968	    [4] Vat. Ms. _Tarentino farsos_; Tor. cooks the sausage
  5969	    in the ashes--_coctos in cinere_; List. _in cinere
  5970	    legendum jecinora_--chicken giblets. Lister's
  5971	    explanation of the Tarentinian sausage is found in the
  5972	    vocabulary, _v. Longano_.
  5973	
  5974	
  5975	[141] APICIAN DISH
  5976	    _PATINA APICIANA_ [1]
  5977	
  5978	THE APICIAN DISH IS MADE THUS: TAKE SMALL PIECES OF COOKED SOW'S BELLY
  5979	[with the paps on it] PIECES OF FISH, PIECES OF CHICKEN, THE BREASTS
  5980	OF FIGPECKERS OR OF THRUSHES [slightly] COOKED, [and] WHICHEVER IS
  5981	BEST. MINCE ALL THIS VERY CAREFULLY, PARTICULARLY THE FIGPECKERS [the
  5982	meat of which is very tender]. DISSOLVE IN OIL STRICTLY FRESH EGGS;
  5983	CRUSH PEPPER AND LOVAGE, POUR OVER SOME BROTH AND RAISIN WINE, PUT IT
  5984	IN A SAUCEPAN TO HEAT AND BIND WITH ROUX. AFTER YOU HAVE CUT ALL IN
  5985	REGULAR PIECES, LET IT COME TO THE BOILING POINT. WHEN DONE, RETIRE
  5986	[from the fire] WITH ITS JUICE OF WHICH YOU PUT SOME IN ANOTHER DEEP
  5987	PAN WITH WHOLE PEPPER AND PIGNOLIA NUTS. SPREAD [the ragout] OUT IN
  5988	SINGLE LAYERS WITH THIN PANCAKES IN BETWEEN; PUT IN AS MANY PANCAKES
  5989	AND LAYERS OF MEAT AS IS REQUIRED TO FILL THE DISH; PUT A FINAL COVER
  5990	OF PANCAKE ON TOP AND SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AFTER THOSE EGGS HAVE BEEN
  5991	ADDED [which serve] TO TIE THE DISH. NOW PUT THIS [mould or dish] IN A
  5992	BOILER [steamer, hot water bath, allow to congeal] AND DISH IT OUT [by
  5993	unmoulding it]. AN EXPENSIVE SILVER PLATTER WOULD ENHANCE THE
  5994	APPEARANCE OF THIS DISH MATERIALLY.
  5995	
  5996	    [1] Cf. ℞ No. 126.
  5997	
  5998	
  5999	[142] AN EVERY-DAY DISH
  6000	    _PATINA QUOTIDIANA_ [1]
  6001	
  6002	PIECES OF COOKED SOW'S UDDER, PIECES OF COOKED FISH, CHICKEN MEAT AND
  6003	SIMILAR BITS, MINCE UNIFORMLY, SEASON WELL AND CAREFULLY [2]. TAKE A
  6004	METAL DISH [for a mould]. BREAK EGGS [in another bowl] AND BEAT THEM.
  6005	IN A MORTAR PUT PEPPER, LOVAGE AND ORIGANY [3], WHICH CRUSH; MOISTEN
  6006	[this] WITH BROTH, WINE, RAISIN WINE AND A LITTLE OIL; EMPTY IT INTO
  6007	THE BOWL [with the beaten eggs, mix] AND HEAT IT [in the hot water
  6008	bath]. THEREUPON WHEN [this is] THICKENED MIX IT WITH THE PIECES OF
  6009	MEAT. NOW PREPARE [alternately] LAYERS OF STEW AND PANCAKES,
  6010	INTERSPERSED WITH OIL [in the metal mould reserved for this purpose]
  6011	UNTIL FULL, COVER WITH ONE REAL GOOD PANCAKE [4], CUT INTO IT A VENT
  6012	HOLE FOR CHIMNEY ON THE SURFACE [bake in hot water bath and when done]
  6013	TURN OUT UPSIDE DOWN INTO ANOTHER DISH. SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND
  6014	SERVE.
  6015	
  6016	    [1] List. _cottidiana_; G.-V. _cotidiana_. Everyday
  6017	    Dish, in contrast to the foregoing Apician dish which is
  6018	    more sumptuous on account of the figpeckers or thrushes.
  6019	    In the originals these two formulæ are rolled into one.
  6020	    Cf. ℞ No. 128.
  6021	
  6022	    [2] G.-V. _Hæc omnia concides_; Tor. _condies_; List.
  6023	    _condies lege concides_ which we dispute.
  6024	    _Condies_--season, flavor--is more correct in this
  6025	    place; _concides_--mince--is a repetition of what has
  6026	    been said already.
  6027	
  6028	    [3] Origany wanting in G.-V.
  6029	
  6030	    [4] List. _superficie versas in discum insuper in
  6031	    superficium pones_; Sch. _a superficie versas indusium
  6032	    super focum pones_; G.-V. _in discum_; Tor. _unum uerò
  6033	    laganum fistula percuties à superficie uersas in discum
  6034	    in superficiem præterea pones_--which we have translated
  6035	    literally above, as we believe Tor. to be correct in
  6036	    this important matter of having a chimney on top of such
  6037	    a pie.
  6038	
  6039	
  6040	[143] NUT CUSTARD TURN-OVER [1]
  6041	    _PATINA VERSATILIS VICE DULCIS_
  6042	
  6043	PIGNOLIA NUTS, CHOPPED OR BROKEN NUTS [other varieties] ARE CLEANED
  6044	AND ROASTED AND CRUSHED WITH HONEY. MIX IN [beat well] PEPPER, BROTH,
  6045	MILK, EGGS, A LITTLE HONEY [2] AND OIL. [Thicken slowly on fire
  6046	without boiling, fill in moulds, taking care that the nuts do not sink
  6047	to the bottom, bake in hot water bath, when cold unmould].
  6048	
  6049	    [1] Practically the only recipe in Apicius fairly
  6050	    resembling a modern "dessert." This is practically a
  6051	    repetition of ℞ No. 129, which see.
  6052	
  6053	    [2] Tor. _modico melle_; List. _m. mero_--pure wine and
  6054	    also pure honey, i.e. thick honey for sweetening. Wine
  6055	    would be out of place here. This is an excellent example
  6056	    of nut custard, if the "pepper" and the "broth"
  6057	    (_liquamen_), of the original, in other words spices and
  6058	    brine, or salt, be used very sparingly. For "pepper"
  6059	    nutmeg or allspice may be substituted, as is used today
  6060	    in such preparations. The oil seems superfluous, but it
  6061	    is taking the place of our butter. This very incomplete
  6062	    formula is characteristic because of the absence of
  6063	    weights and measures and other vital information as to
  6064	    the manipulation of the materials. None but an
  6065	    experienced practitioner could make use of this formula
  6066	    in its original state.
  6067	
  6068	    Goll. adds toasted raisins, for which there is no
  6069	    authority.
  6070	
  6071	    The text now proceeds without interruption to the next
  6072	    formula.
  6073	
  6074	
  6075	[144] TYROTARICA [1]
  6076	    _PATELLA THIROTARICA_ [2]
  6077	
  6078	TAKE ANY KIND OF SALT FISH [3] COOK [fry or broil it] IN OIL, TAKE THE
  6079	BONES OUT, SHRED IT [and add] PIECES OF COOKED BRAINS, PIECES OF
  6080	[other, fresh (?)] FISH, MINCED CHICKEN LIVERS [4] AND [cover with]
  6081	HOT SOFT [i.e. liquefied] CHEESE. HEAT ALL THIS IN A DISH; [meanwhile]
  6082	GRIND PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, SEEDS OF RUE WITH WINE, HONEY WINE AND
  6083	OIL; COOK ALL ON A SLOW FIRE; BIND [this sauce] WITH RAW EGGS; ARRANGE
  6084	[the fish, etc.]. PROPERLY [incorporate with the sauce] SPRINKLE WITH
  6085	CRUSHED CUMIN AND SERVE [5].
  6086	
  6087	    [1] G.-V., List., Vat. Ms. _Thyrotarnica_; cf. notes to
  6088	    ℞ Nos. 427, 428.
  6089	
  6090	    [2] Tor.
  6091	
  6092	    [3] Tor. Wanting in other texts.
  6093	
  6094	    [4] List., G.-V. here add hard boiled eggs, which is
  6095	    permissible, gastronomically.
  6096	
  6097	    [5] Modern fish _au gratin_ is made in a similar way.
  6098	    Instead of this wine sauce a spiced cream sauce and
  6099	    grated cheese are mixed with the bits of cooked fish,
  6100	    which is then baked in the dish.
  6101	
  6102	    Brains, chicken, etc., too, are served _au gratin_, but
  6103	    a combination of the three in one dish is no longer
  6104	    practiced. However, the Italian method of baking fish,
  6105	    etc., _au gratin à l'Italienne_ contains even more herbs
  6106	    and wine reduction than the above formula.
  6107	
  6108	
  6109	[145] SALT FISH BALLS IN WINE SAUCE [1]
  6110	    _PATELLA ARIDA_ [2]
  6111	
  6112	DRY PIECES OF SALT TURSIO [3] ARE BONED, CLEANED [soaked in water,
  6113	cooked] SHREDDED FINE AND SEASONED WITH GROUND PEPPER, LOVAGE,
  6114	ORIGANY, PARSLEY, CORIANDER, CUMIN, RUE SEEDS AND DRY MINT. MAKE FISH
  6115	BALLS OUT OF THIS MATERIAL AND POACH THE SAME IN WINE, BROTH AND OIL;
  6116	AND WHEN COOKED, ARRANGE THEM IN A DISH. THEN MAKE A SAUCE [utilizing
  6117	the broth, the _court bouillon_ in which the balls were cooked] SEASON
  6118	WITH PEPPER, LOVAGE, SATURY, ONIONS AND WINE AND VINEGAR, ALSO ADD
  6119	BROTH AND OIL AS NEEDED, BIND WITH ROUX [4] [pour over the balls]
  6120	SPRINKLE WITH THYME AND GROUND PEPPER [5].
  6121	
  6122	    [1] Reminding us of the Norwegian _fiske boller_ in wine
  6123	    sauce, a popular commercial article found canned in
  6124	    delicatessen stores.
  6125	
  6126	    [2] List. _patella sicca_--dry, perhaps because made of
  6127	    dried fish.
  6128	
  6129	    [3] List. _isicia de Tursione_; G.-V. _Thursione_.
  6130	    Probably a common sturgeon, or porpoise, or dolphin.
  6131	    List. describes it as "a kind of salt fish from the
  6132	    Black Sea; a malicious fish with a mouth similar to a
  6133	    rabbit"; Dann. thinks it is a sturgeon, but in Goll. it
  6134	    appears as tunny. The ancients called the sturgeon
  6135	    _acipenser_; but this name was gradually changed into
  6136	    _styrio_, _stirio_ and _sturio_, which is similar to
  6137	    _tursio_ (cf. _styrio_ in the vocabulary). The fish in
  6138	    question therefore may have been sturgeon for which the
  6139	    Black Sea is famous.
  6140	
  6141	    [4] List., G.-V. _ovis obligabis_--tie with
  6142	    eggs--certainly preferable to the Tor. version.
  6143	
  6144	    [5] Tor. thyme.
  6145	
  6146	    The above is an excellent way of making fish balls, it
  6147	    being taken for granted, of course, that the salt fish
  6148	    be thoroughly soaked and cooked in milk before shaping
  6149	    into balls. The many spices should be used very
  6150	    moderately, some to be omitted entirely. We read between
  6151	    the lines of the old formula that the _Tursio_ had a
  6152	    long journey from Pontus to Rome; fish however dry
  6153	    acquires a notorious flavor upon such journeys which
  6154	    must be offset by herbs and spices.
  6155	
  6156	    It is quite possible that the ancients made a
  6157	    _réduction_ of the herbs and spices mentioned in this
  6158	    formula; in fact, the presence of vinegar leads us to
  6159	    believe this, in which case this formula would be
  6160	    nothing but a very modern sauce. The herbs and spices in
  6161	    a _réduction_ are crushed and boiled down in vinegar and
  6162	    wine, and strained off, they leave their finest flavor
  6163	    in the sauce.
  6164	
  6165	
  6166	[146] VEGETABLE DINNER
  6167	    _PATELLA EX OLISATRO_ [1]
  6168	
  6169	[Any kind of vegetables or herbs] BLANCHED OFF IN WATER WITH [a
  6170	little] SODA; SQUEEZE [out the water] ARRANGE IN A SAUCEPAN. GRIND
  6171	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CORIANDER, SATURY, ONION WITH WINE, BROTH, VINEGAR AND
  6172	OIL; ADD [this] TO THE VEGETABLES, STEW [all until nearly done] AND
  6173	TIE WITH ROUX. SPRINKLE WITH THYME, FINELY GROUND PEPPER AND SERVE.
  6174	ANY KIND OF VEGETABLE [2] MAY BE PREPARED IN THE ABOVE MANNER, IF YOU
  6175	WISH.
  6176	
  6177	    [1] Wanting in Tac. and Tor. G.-V. _patellam ex
  6178	    holisatro_.
  6179	
  6180	    [2] It is worth noting that Tor. and Tac. omit this
  6181	    recipe entirely and that Tor. concludes the preceding
  6182	    formula with the last sentence of the above formula,
  6183	    except for the difference in one word. Tor. _et de
  6184	    quacunque libra_ [List. _et al._ _herba_] _si volueris
  6185	    facies ut demonstratum est suprà_. This might mean that
  6186	    it is optional (in the preceding formula) to shape the
  6187	    fish into one pound loaves instead of the small fish
  6188	    balls, which is often done in the case of forcemeats, as
  6189	    in veal, beef, ham loaves, or fish pie.
  6190	
  6191	    We are inclined to accept the reading of Torinus, for
  6192	    the above way of preparing "any kind of vegetables or
  6193	    herbs" is somewhat farfetched. Furthermore, the
  6194	    vegetable dish would more properly belong in Book III.
  6195	
  6196	    Just another example of where readings by various
  6197	    editors are different because of the interpretations of
  6198	    one word. In this case one group reads _libra_ whereas
  6199	    the other reads _herba_.
  6200	
  6201	
  6202	[147] A DISH OF SARDINES
  6203	    _PATELLA DE APUA_ [1]
  6204	
  6205	SARDINE LOAF (OR OMELETTE) IS MADE IN THIS MANNER [2] CLEAN THE
  6206	SARDINES [of skin and bones]; BREAK [and beat] EGGS AND MIX WITH [half
  6207	of the] FISH [3]; ADD TO THIS SOME STOCK, WINE AND OIL, AND FINISH
  6208	[the composition] BY HEATING IT. WHEN DONE TO A POINT, ADD [the
  6209	remaining part of the] SARDINES TO IT, LET IT STAND A WHILE [over a
  6210	slow fire to congeal] CAREFULLY TURN OVER [dish it up] MASK WITH A
  6211	WARM [4] WINE SAUCE, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  6212	
  6213	    [1] G.-V. _Patina de apua fricta_--same as _aphya_,
  6214	    fried fresh small fish of the kind of anchovies,
  6215	    sardines, sprats.
  6216	
  6217	    In experimenting with this formula we would advise to
  6218	    use salt and oil judiciously if any at all. We have no
  6219	    knowledge of the ancient _apua fricta_ other than our
  6220	    making of modern sardines which is to fry them in oil as
  6221	    quickly as possible after the fish has left the water,
  6222	    for its meat is very delicate. For an omelette, our
  6223	    modern sardines, including kippered smelts, sprotten,
  6224	    and similar smoked and processed fish, contain
  6225	    sufficient salt and fat to season the eggs of an
  6226	    omelette.
  6227	
  6228	    [2] Tor. Sentence wanting in other texts.
  6229	
  6230	    [3] Tor. _cum aqua_; List., G.-V. _cum apua_. Perhaps a
  6231	    typographical error in Tor. A little water is used to
  6232	    dilute the eggs of an omelette, but Apicius already
  6233	    prescribes sufficient liquids (stock or brine, wine) for
  6234	    that purpose.
  6235	
  6236	    [4] Tor. _et in calore œnogarum perfundes_; List.,
  6237	    G.-V. _ut coloret_--to keep the omelette in the pan long
  6238	    enough to give it "color." We prefer the Torinus version
  6239	    because an omelette should have no or very little color
  6240	    from the fire (the eggs thus browned are indigestible)
  6241	    and because hot _œnogarum_ (wine-fish sauce, not in
  6242	    List.) is accompanying this dish, to give additional
  6243	    savour and a finishing touch.
  6244	
  6245	
  6246	[148] FINE RAGOUT OF BRAINS AND BACON
  6247	    _PATINA EX LARIDIS _[1]_ ET CEREBELLIS_
  6248	
  6249	THE DISH OF BACON AND BRAINS IS MADE IN THIS MANNER [2] STRAIN [or
  6250	chop fine] HARD BOILED EGGS [3] WITH PARBOILED BRAINS [calf's or
  6251	pig's] THE SKIN AND NERVES OF WHICH HAVE BEEN REMOVED; ALSO COOK
  6252	CHICKEN GIBLETS, ALL IN PROPORTION TO THE FISH [4] PUT THIS AFORESAID
  6253	MIXTURE IN A SAUCEPAN, PLACE THE COOKED BACON IN THE CENTER, GRIND
  6254	PEPPER AND LOVAGE AND TO SWEETEN ADD A DASH OF MEAD, HEAT, WHEN HOT
  6255	STIR BRISKLY WITH A RUE WHIP AND BIND WITH ROUX.
  6256	
  6257	    [1] G.-V. _lagitis_; Tor. _laridis_ and _largitis_; Vat.
  6258	    Ms. _lagatis_; List. _pro lagitis ... legendum
  6259	    Lacertis_. The _lacertus_, according to List., is a much
  6260	    esteemed salt fish; not identified. List. _et al._ seem
  6261	    to be mistaken in their reading of _lacertis_ for
  6262	    _laridis_. This work stands for salt pork, from
  6263	    _laridum_ and _lardum_ (French, _lard_; the English
  6264	    _lard_ is applied to the rendered fat of pork in
  6265	    general). Cf. notes to ℞ No. 41.
  6266	
  6267	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  6268	
  6269	    [3] _oua dura_; Sch. _o. dua_--two eggs.
  6270	
  6271	    [4] This formula would be intelligible and even
  6272	    gastronomically correct were it not for this word
  6273	    "fish." However, we cannot accept Lister's reading
  6274	    _lacertis_. We prefer the reading, _laridis_, bacon. The
  6275	    French have another term for this--_petits salés_. Both
  6276	    this and the Torinus term are in the plural. They are
  6277	    simply small strips of bacon to which Torinus again
  6278	    refers in the above formula, _salsum, coctum in media
  6279	    pones_--put the bacon, when done, in the center (of the
  6280	    dish). Regarding _salsum_ also see note to ℞ No. 41.
  6281	
  6282	    The above dish resembles _ragoût fin en coquille_, a
  6283	    popular Continental dish, although its principal
  6284	    ingredients are sweetbreads instead of brains.
  6285	
  6286	
  6287	[149] BROILED MULLET
  6288	    _PATINA EX PISCIBUS MULLIS_ [1]
  6289	
  6290	A DISH OF MULLET CONSISTS OF [2] SCALED SALT MULLET PLACED IN A CLEAN
  6291	PAN WITH ENOUGH OIL [3] AS IS NECESSARY FOR COOKING; WHEN DONE ADD [a
  6292	dash of honey-] WINE OR RAISIN WINE, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  6293	
  6294	    [1] List., G.-V. _mullorum loco salsi_--salt mullet.
  6295	
  6296	    [2] Tor. wanting in other texts.
  6297	
  6298	    [3] List. _liquamen_--broth, brine, which would be worse
  6299	    than carrying owls to Athens. As a matter of fact, the
  6300	    mullet if it be what List. says, _loco salsi_--salted on
  6301	    the spot, i.e. as caught, near the sea shore, requires
  6302	    soaking to extract the salt.
  6303	
  6304	
  6305	[150] A DISH OF ANY KIND OF SALT FISH
  6306	    _PATINA EX PISCIBUS QUIBUSLIBET_ [1]
  6307	
  6308	ANOTHER FISH DISH IS THUS MADE [2] FRY ANY KIND OF CURED [3] FISH,
  6309	CAREFULLY TREATED [soaked and cleaned] PLACE IN A PAN, COVER WITH
  6310	SUFFICIENT OIL, LAY [strips of] COOKED SALT [4] [pork or
  6311	bacon--_petits salés_] OVER THE CENTER, KEEP IT HOT, WHEN REAL HOT,
  6312	ADD A DASH OF HONEY WINE TO THE GRAVY AND STIR IT UP [5].
  6313	
  6314	    [1] Ex Tor.; G.-V. _P. piscium loco salsi_.
  6315	
  6316	    [2] Tor.; sentence wanting in other texts.
  6317	
  6318	    [3] Tor. _duratos_--_hard_--no sense here, probably a
  6319	    misprint of the d. List. _curatos_--carefully treated,
  6320	    "cured," processed.
  6321	
  6322	    [4] _Salsum coctum_, cf. notes to ℞ No. 148; Goll.,
  6323	    Dann.--sprinkle [the fish] with salt.... Like Lister's
  6324	    error in the preceding formula it would be a great
  6325	    blunder to add salt to a cured fish already saturated
  6326	    with salt to the utmost. Cf. also note 2 to ℞ Nos.
  6327	    41, 148.
  6328	
  6329	    [5] Virtually a repetition of ℞ No. 149, except for
  6330	    the addition of the pork.
  6331	
  6332	
  6333	[151] ANOTHER FISH DISH, WITH ONIONS
  6334	    _ALIA PISCIUM PATINA_
  6335	
  6336	ANOTHER FISH DISH MAKE AS FOLLOWS [1] CLEAN ANY KIND OF FISH AND PLACE
  6337	IT PROPERLY IN A SAUCEPAN WITH SHREDDED DRY ASCALONIAN ONIONS
  6338	[shallots] OR WITH ANY OTHER KIND OF ONIONS, THE FISH ON TOP. ADD
  6339	STOCK AND OIL AND COOK. WHEN DONE, PUT BROILED BACON IN THE CENTER,
  6340	GIVE IT A DASH OF VINEGAR, SPRINKLE WITH [finely chopped] SAVORY AND
  6341	GARNISH WITH [the] ONIONS.
  6342	
  6343	    [1] Tor., sentence wanting in other texts.
  6344	
  6345	
  6346	[152] A LUCRETIAN DISH
  6347	    _PATINA LUCRETIANA_ [1]
  6348	
  6349	CLEAN YOUNG ONIONS, REJECTING THE GREEN TOPS, AND PLACE [2] THEM IN A
  6350	SAUCEPAN WITH A LITTLE BROTH, SOME OIL AND WATER, AND, TO BE COOKED
  6351	[with the onions] PLACE SALT PORK [3] IN THE MIDST [of the scallions].
  6352	WHEN NEARLY DONE, ADD A SPOON OF HONEY [4] A LITTLE VINEGAR AND
  6353	REDUCED MUST, TASTE IT, IF INSIPID ADD MORE BRINE [broth] IF TOO
  6354	SALTY, ADD MORE HONEY, AND SPRINKLE WITH SAVORY [5].
  6355	
  6356	    [1] Dann. Named for Lucretius Epicuræus, a contemporary
  6357	    of Cicero. List. _ab authore cui in usu fuit sic
  6358	    appellata_.
  6359	
  6360	    [2] G.-V. _concides_. Not necessary.
  6361	
  6362	    [3] _salsum crudum_--salt pork, i.e. not smoked or cured
  6363	    bacon. Dann. raw salt; Goll. salt. Impossible, of
  6364	    course! Cf. notes to ℞ Nos. 41, 147, 149.
  6365	
  6366	    [4] To glaze the pork, no doubt; reminding us of our own
  6367	    use of sugar to glaze ham or bacon, and of the molasses
  6368	    added to pork (and beans).
  6369	
  6370	    [5] G.-V. _coronam bubulam_. In experimenting with this
  6371	    formula omit salt completely. Instead of honey we have
  6372	    also added maple syrup once. To make this a perfect
  6373	    luncheon dish a starch is wanting; we have therefore
  6374	    added sliced raw potatoes and cooked with the rest, to
  6375	    make it a balanced meal, by way of improving upon
  6376	    Lucretius. Since the ancients had no potatoes we have,
  6377	    on a different occasion, created another version by
  6378	    added sliced dasheens (_colocasia_, cf. ℞ Nos. 74,
  6379	    216, 244, 322). It is surprising that the ancients who
  6380	    used the _colocasium_ extensively did not combine it
  6381	    with the above dish.
  6382	
  6383	
  6384	[153] STEWED LACERTUS FISH
  6385	    _PATINA DE LACERTIS_ [1]
  6386	
  6387	CLEAN AND WASH [soak] THE FISH [2] [cook and flake it] BREAK AND BEAT
  6388	EGGS, MIX THEM WITH THE FISH, ADD BROTH, WINE AND OIL. PLACE THIS ON
  6389	THE FIRE, WHEN COOKED [scrambled] ADD SIMPLE FISH WINE SAUCE [3] TO
  6390	IT, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE [4].
  6391	
  6392	    [1] Ex List. wanting in Tor. G.-V. _P. de lagitis_; cf.
  6393	    note to ℞ No. 148.
  6394	
  6395	    [2] Remembering that List. reads _lagitis_ for
  6396	    _lacertis_, this formula appears to be an antique
  6397	    "Scrambled Eggs and Bacon." Cf. notes to ℞ Nos. 42,
  6398	    148-150.
  6399	
  6400	    [3] _Oenogarum_, cf. ℞ No. 147, the Sardine Omelette.
  6401	
  6402	    [4] To cook the eggs as described above would be
  6403	    disastrous. The fish, if such was used, was probably
  6404	    first poached in the broth, wine and oil, and when done,
  6405	    removed from the pan. The _fond_, or remaining juice or
  6406	    gravy, was subsequently tied with the egg yolks, and
  6407	    this sauce was strained over the fish dressed on the
  6408	    service platter, the _œnogarum_ sparingly sprinkled
  6409	    over the finished dish. This would closely resemble our
  6410	    modern _au vin blanc_ fish dishes; the _œnogarum_
  6411	    taking the place of our meat glacé.
  6412	
  6413	    Another interpretation of this vexatious formula is that
  6414	    if fish was used, the cooked fish was incorporated with
  6415	    the raw beaten eggs which were then scrambled in the
  6416	    pan. In that event this formula resembles closely the
  6417	    sardine omelette.
  6418	
  6419	
  6420	[154] A FISH STEW
  6421	    _PATINA ZOMORE_ [1]
  6422	
  6423	THE ZOMORE FISH DISH IS MADE AS FOLLOWS [2] TAKE RAW GANONAS [3] AND
  6424	OTHER [fish] WHICHEVER YOU LIKE, PLACE THEM IN A SAUCE PAN, ADDING
  6425	OIL, BROTH, REDUCED WINE, A BUNCH [4] OF LEEKS AND [green] CORIANDER;
  6426	WHILE THIS COOKS, CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE AND A BUNCH OF ORIGANY WHICH
  6427	CRUSH BY ITSELF AND DILUTE WITH THE JUICE [5] OF THE FISH. NOW
  6428	DISSOLVE [break and beat egg yolks for a _liaison_] PREPARE AND TASTE
  6429	THE DISH, BINDING [the sauce with the yolks] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND
  6430	SERVE.
  6431	
  6432	    [1] List. _Zomoteganite_--"a dish of fish boiled in
  6433	    their own liquor"; G.-V. _zomoteganon_; Lan.
  6434	    _zomoreganonas_; Vat. Ms. _zomonam Ganas_.
  6435	
  6436	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  6437	
  6438	    [3] _ganonas crudas_--an unidentified fish.
  6439	
  6440	    [4] "Bouquet garni."
  6441	
  6442	    [5] _ius de suo sibi_--old Plautian latinity. Cf. H. C.
  6443	    Coote, cit. Apiciana; the proof of the antiquity and the
  6444	    genuineness of Apicius.
  6445	
  6446	
  6447	[155] SOLE IN WHITE WINE
  6448	    _PATINA EX SOLEIS_ [1]
  6449	
  6450	A DISH OF SOLE IS THUS MADE [2] BEAT THE SOLE [3] PREPARE [4] AND
  6451	PLACE THEM IN A [shallow] SAUCE PAN, ADD OIL, BROTH AND WINE, AND
  6452	POACH THEM THUS; NOW CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY AND ADD OF THE FISH
  6453	JUICE; THEN BIND THE SAUCE WITH RAW EGGS [yolks] TO MAKE A GOOD CREAMY
  6454	SAUCE OF IT; STRAIN THIS OVER THE SOLE, HEAT ALL ON A SLOW FIRE [to
  6455	fill it with live heat] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE [5].
  6456	
  6457	    [1] G.-V. _P. solearum_.
  6458	
  6459	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  6460	
  6461	    [3] Beat, to make tender, to be able to remove the skin.
  6462	
  6463	    [4] Tor. _curatos_--trim, skin, remove entrails, wash.
  6464	
  6465	    [5] One of the best of Apician accomplishments. Exactly
  6466	    like our modern _sole au vin blanc_, one of the most
  6467	    aristocratic of dishes. Cf. ℞ No. 487, Excerpta, XIX.
  6468

  6474	PEPPER, ONE PINT OF REDUCED WINE, ONE PINT OF SPICED WINE AND TWO

  6480	[156] A DISH OF LITTLE FISH
  6481	    _PATINA DE PISCICULIS_ [1]
  6482	
  6483	TAKE RAISINS, PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, ONIONS, WINE, BROTH AND OIL,
  6484	PLACE THIS IN A PAN; AFTER THIS HAS COOKED ADD TO IT THE COOKED SMALL
  6485	FISH, BIND WITH ROUX AND SERVE.
  6486	
  6487	    [1] Smelts, anchovies, whitebait.
  6488	
  6489	
  6490	[157] A DISH OF TOOTH FISH, DORY OR SEA MULLET AND OYSTERS
  6491	    _PATINA DE PISCIBUS DENTICE, AURATA ET MUGILE_ [1]
  6492	
  6493	TAKE THE FISH, PREPARE [clean, trim, wash] AND HALF BROIL OR FRY THEM;
  6494	THEREUPON SHRED THEM [in good-sized] PIECES: NEXT PREPARE OYSTERS; PUT
  6495	IN A MORTAR 6 SCRUPLES OF PEPPER, MOISTEN WITH BROTH AND CRUSH. ADD A
  6496	SMALL GLASS OF BROTH, ONE OF WINE TO IT; PUT IN A SAUCE PAN 3 OUNCES
  6497	OF OIL AND THE [shelled] OYSTERS AND LET THEM POACH WITH WINE SAUCE.
  6498	WHEN THEY ARE DONE, OIL A DISH ON WHICH PLACE THE ABOVE MENTIONED FISH
  6499	PIECES AND STEWED OYSTERS, HEAT AGAIN, AND WHEN HOT, BREAK 40 [2] EGGS
  6500	[whip them] AND POUR THEM OVER THE OYSTERS, SO THAT THEY CONGEAL.
  6501	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE. [3].
  6502	
  6503	    [1] _dentex_--"tooth-fish"; _aurata_--"gilt"--dory, red
  6504	    snapper; _mugilis_--Sea Mullet, according to some.
  6505	
  6506	    [2] G.-V. _ova XI_--11 eggs. Tac. _ova Xl_, which may be
  6507	    read XL--forty.
  6508	
  6509	    [3] This dish may be allowed to congeal slowly; if done
  6510	    quickly it may become a dish of scrambled eggs with fish
  6511	    and oysters.
  6512	
  6513	
  6514	[158] SEA BASS, OR BARRACUDA
  6515	    _PATINA DE LUPO_ [1]
  6516	
  6517	GRIND PEPPER, CUMIN, PARSLEY, RUE, ONIONS, HONEY, BROTH, RAISIN WINE
  6518	AND DROPS OF OIL [2].
  6519	
  6520	    [1] G.-V. _p. de pisce lupo_--wolf, because of its
  6521	    voracity; a sea fish, sea pike, or sea bass; perhaps
  6522	    akin to our barracuda, wolfish both in appearance and
  6523	    character. Sch. _Perca labrax_ Lin.
  6524	
  6525	    [2] The cleaned fish is cut into convenient portions or
  6526	    fillets, placed in an oiled pan, the ingredients spread
  6527	    over; it is either poached in the oven or cooked under
  6528	    the open fire.
  6529	
  6530	    Schuch here inserts his ℞ Nos. 153 to 166 which more
  6531	    properly belong among the Excerpta of Vinidarius and
  6532	    which are found at the end Book X by Apicius.
  6533	
  6534	
  6535	[159] A DISH OF SORB-APPLE, HOT OR COLD
  6536	    _PATINA DE SORBIS CALIDA ET FRIGIDA_
  6537	
  6538	TAKE MEDLARS, CLEAN THEM; CRUSH THEM IN THE MORTAR AND STRAIN THROUGH
  6539	COLANDER. 4 COOKED [calf's or pork] BRAINS, SKINNED AND FREED FROM
  6540	STRINGY PARTS, PUT IN THE MORTAR WITH 8 SCRUPLES OF PEPPER, DILUTE
  6541	WITH STOCK AND CRUSH, ADDING THE MEDLAR PULP AND COMBINE ALL; NOW
  6542	BREAK 8 EGGS AND ADD A SMALL GLASS OF BROTH. OIL A CLEAN PAN AND PLACE
  6543	IT IN THE HOT BATH OR IN THE HOT ASHES; AFTER YOU HAVE FILLED IT WITH
  6544	THE PREPARATION, MAKE SURE THAT THE PAN GETS ENOUGH HEAT FROM BELOW;
  6545	LET IT CONGEAL, AND WHEN DONE SPRINKLE WITH A LITTLE FINE PEPPER AND
  6546	SERVE.
  6547	
  6548	    Sch. ℞ No. 166.
  6549	
  6550	
  6551	[160] A DISH OF PEACHES [1]
  6552	    _PATINA DE PERSICIS_
  6553	
  6554	CLEAN HARD-SKINNED PEACHES AND SLICE, STEW THEM; ARRANGE IN A DISH,
  6555	SPRINKLE WITH A LITTLE OIL AND SERVE WITH CUMIN-FLAVORED WINE [2].
  6556	
  6557	    [1] Tor. is not sure whether this is a Persian fish or
  6558	    peaches--_persica_.
  6559	
  6560	    [2] Dann. Pepper, for which there is no authority.

  6565	[161] A DISH OF PEARS

  6567	
  6568	A DISH OF PEARS IS MADE THIS WAY: [1] STEW THE PEARS, CLEAN OUT THE
  6569	CENTER [remove core and seeds] CRUSH THEM WITH PEPPER, CUMIN, HONEY,
  6570	RAISIN WINE, BROTH AND A LITTLE OIL; MIX WITH EGGS, MAKE A PIE
  6571	[custard] OF THIS, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.

  6578	[162] A DISH OF SEA-NETTLES
  6579	    _PATINA DE URTICA_ [1]
  6580	
  6581	A DISH OF SEA-NETTLES, EITHER HOT OR COLD, IS MADE THUS: [2] TAKE
  6582	SEA-NETTLES, WASH AND DRAIN THEM ON THE COLANDER, DRY ON THE TABLE AND
  6583	CHOP FINE. CRUSH 10 SCRUPLES OF PEPPER, MOISTEN WITH BROTH, ADD 2
  6584	SMALL GLASSES OF BROTH AND 6 OUNCES OF OIL. HEAT THIS IN A SAUCE PAN
  6585	AND WHEN COOKED TAKE IT OUT AND ALLOW TO COOL OFF. NEXT OIL A CLEAN
  6586	PAN, BREAK 8 EGGS AND BEAT THEM; COMBINE THESE WITH THE ABOVE
  6587	PREPARATIONS, PLACE THE PAN ON HOT ASHES TO GIVE IT HEAT FROM BELOW,
  6588	WHEN DONE [congealed] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  6589	
  6590	    [1] G.-V. _p. urticarum calida et frigida_.
  6591	
  6592	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  6593	
  6594	
  6595	[163] A DISH OF QUINCES
  6596	    _PATINA DE CYDONIIS_ [1]
  6597	
  6598	A DISH OF QUINCES IS MADE AS FOLLOWS: [2] QUINCES ARE COOKED WITH
  6599	LEEKS, HONEY AND BROTH, USING HOT OIL, OR THEY ARE STEWED IN HONEY
  6600	[3].
  6601	
  6602	    [1] G.-V. _p. de Cydoneis_.
  6603	
  6604	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  6605	
  6606	    [3] This latter method would appeal to our modern notion
  6607	    of preparing fruits of this sort; we use sugar syrup to
  6608	    cook them in and flavor with various spices, adding
  6609	    perhaps a little wine or brandy.
