  6612	
  6613	III
  6614	
  6615	OF FINELY CHOPPED, MINCED MEATS
  6616	  _DE MINUT ALIBUS_ [1]
  6617	
  6618	
  6619	[164] A MINCE OF SEA FOOD
  6620	    _MINUTAL MARINUM_
  6621	
  6622	PLACE THE FISH IN SAUCE PAN, ADD BROTH OIL AND WINE [and poach it].
  6623	ALSO FINELY CHOP LEEK HEADS [the white part only of leeks] AND [fresh]
  6624	CORIANDER. [When cool, mince the fish fine] FORM IT INTO SMALL CAKES
  6625	[2] ADDING CAPERS [3] AND SEA-NETTLES WELL CLEANED. THESE FISH CAKES
  6626	COOK IN A LIQUOR OF PEPPER, LOVAGE AND ORIGANY, CRUSHED, DILUTED WITH
  6627	BROTH AND THE ABOVE FISH LIQUOR WHICH SKIM WELL, BIND [with roux or
  6628	eggs] STIR [strain] OVER THE CAKES, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  6629	
  6630	    [1] G.-V. _minutal de piscibus vel Isiciis_.
  6631	
  6632	    [2] Tac. G.-V. _isiciola ... minuta_--resembling our
  6633	    modern _quenelles de poisson_--tiny fish dumplings.
  6634	
  6635	    [3] Tac. _cum caparis_; Tor. _c. capparibus_; Vat. Ms.
  6636	    _concarpis_; List. G.-V. _concerpis_.
  6637	
  6638	
  6639	[165] TARENTINE MINUTAL
  6640	    _MINUTAL TARENTINUM_ [1]
  6641	
  6642	FINELY CHOP THE WHITE PART OF LEEKS AND PLACE IN A SAUCE PAN; ADD OIL
  6643	[fry lightly] AND BROTH; NEXT ADD SMALL SAUSAGE TO BE COOKED LIKEWISE.
  6644	TO HAVE A GOOD TARENTINE DISH, THEY MUST BE TENDER. THE MAKING OF
  6645	THESE SAUSAGE WILL BE FOUND AMONG THE ISICIA [Nos. 60-66] [2]. ALSO
  6646	MAKE A SAUCE IN THE FOLLOWING MANNER: CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE AND
  6647	ORIGANY, MOISTEN WITH BROTH, ADD OF THE ABOVE [sausage] GRAVY, WINE,
  6648	RAISIN WINE; PUT IN A SAUCE PAN TO BE HEATED, WHEN BOILING, SKIM
  6649	CAREFULLY, BIND, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  6650	
  6651	    [1] G.-V. _Terentinum_, for which there is no reason.
  6652	    Tarentum, town of lower Italy, now Taranto, celebrated
  6653	    for its wine and luxurious living.
  6654	
  6655	    [2] Such references to other parts of the book are very
  6656	    infrequent.
  6657	
  6658	
  6659	[166] APICIAN MINUTAL
  6660	    _MINUTAL APICIANUM_
  6661	
  6662	THE APICIAN MINUTAL IS MADE AS FOLLOWS: [1] OIL, BROTH WINE, LEEK
  6663	HEADS, MINT, SMALL FISH, SMALL TIDBITS [2] COCK'S FRIES OR CAPON'S
  6664	KIDNEYS [3] AND PORK SWEETBREADS; ALL OF THESE ARE COOKED TOGETHER [4]
  6665	NOW CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, GREEN CORIANDER, OR SEEDS, MOISTENED WITH
  6666	BROTH; ADD A LITTLE HONEY, AND OF THE OWN LIQUOR [5] OF THE ABOVE
  6667	MORSELS, WINE AND HONEY TO TASTE; BRING THIS TO A BOILING POINT SKIM,
  6668	BIND, STIR WELL [strain, pour over the morsels] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER
  6669	AND SERVE [6].
  6670	
  6671	    [1] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  6672	
  6673	    [2] _isitia_--_quenelles_, dumplings of some kind,
  6674	    mostly fine forcemeats.
  6675	
  6676	    [3] _testiculi caponum_; the capon has no _testiculi_,
  6677	    these organs having been removed by an operation when
  6678	    the cock is young. This operation is said to have been
  6679	    first performed by a Roman surgeon with the intention of
  6680	    beating the _Lex Fannia_, or Fannian law, sponsored by a
  6681	    fanatic named Fannius. It prohibited among other
  6682	    restrictions the serving of any fowl at any time or
  6683	    repast except a hen, and this hen was not to be
  6684	    fattened. Note the cunning of the law: The useful hen
  6685	    and her unlaid eggs could be sacrificed while the
  6686	    unproductive rooster was allowed to thrive to no
  6687	    purpose, immune from the butcher's block. This set the
  6688	    shrewd surgeon to thinking; he transformed a rooster
  6689	    into a capon by his surgical trick. The emasculated bird
  6690	    grew fat without his owner committing any infraction of
  6691	    the Roman law against fattening chickens. Of course the
  6692	    capon, being neither hen nor rooster, was perfectly safe
  6693	    to eat, for he was within the law. Thus he became a huge
  6694	    success as an ancient "bootleg" chicken.
  6695	
  6696	    [4] These integral parts must be prepared and poached
  6697	    separately and merely heated together before the final
  6698	    service.
  6699	
  6700	    [5] Again the Plautian colloquialism _ius de suo sibi_.
  6701	
  6702	    [6] This dish is worthy of Apicius. It is akin to our
  6703	    _Ragoût Financière_, and could pass for _Vol-au-vent à
  6704	    la Financière_ if it were served in a large fluffy crust
  6705	    of puff paste.
  6706	
  6707	
  6708	[167] MINUTAL À LA MATIUS [1]
  6709	    _MINUTAL MATIANUM_
  6710	
  6711	PUT IN A SAUCE PAN OIL, BROTH FINELY CHOPPED LEEKS, CORIANDER, SMALL
  6712	TID-BITS, COOKED PORK SHOULDER, CUT INTO LONG STRIPS INCLUDING THE
  6713	SKIN, HAVE EVERYTHING EQUALLY HALF DONE. ADD MATIAN APPLES [2]
  6714	CLEANED, THE CORE REMOVED, SLICED LENGTHWISE AND COOK THEM TOGETHER:
  6715	MEANWHILE CRUSH PEPPER, CUMIN, GREEN CORIANDER, OR SEEDS, MINT, LASER
  6716	ROOT, MOISTENED WITH VINEGAR, HONEY AND BROTH AND A LITTLE REDUCED
  6717	MUST, ADD TO THIS THE BROTH OF THE ABOVE MORSELS, VINEGAR TO TASTE,
  6718	BOIL, SKIM, BIND [strain over the morsels] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND
  6719	SERVE.
  6720	
  6721	    [1] Named for Matius, ancient author, or because of the
  6722	    Matian apples used in this dish, also named for the same
  6723	    man. Plinius, Nat. Hist. lib. XV, Cap. 14-15, Columella,
  6724	    De re Rustica, lib. XII, Cap. XLIIII.
  6725	
  6726	    This is not the first instance where fruits or
  6727	    vegetables were named for famous men. Beets, a certain
  6728	    kind of them were named for Varro, writer on
  6729	    agriculture. Matius, according to Varro, wrote a book on
  6730	    waiters, cooks, cellar men and food service in general,
  6731	    of which there is no trace today. It was already lost
  6732	    during Varro's days.
  6733	
  6734	    [2] Cf. note 1, above. This illustrates the age-old
  6735	    connection of pork and apples.
  6736	
  6737	
  6738	[168] SWEET MINUTAL
  6739	    _MINUTAL DULCE_ [1]
  6740	
  6741	IN A SAUCE PAN PUT TOGETHER OIL, BROTH, COCTURA [2] FINELY CUT LEEK
  6742	HEADS AND GREEN CORIANDER, COOKED PORK SHOULDER, SMALL TID-BITS. WHILE
  6743	THIS IS BEING COOKED, CRUSH PEPPER, CUMIN, CORIANDER OR [its] SEEDS,
  6744	GREEN RUE, LASER ROOT, MOISTENED WITH VINEGAR, REDUCED MUST AND THE
  6745	GRAVY OF THE ABOVE MORSELS; ADD VINEGAR TO TASTE: WHEN THIS [sauce] IS
  6746	COOKED, HOLLOW OUT CITRON SQUASH [3] CUT IN DICE, BOIL AND PLACE THEM
  6747	TOGETHER WITH THE REST IN THE DISH, SKIM, BIND [strain] THE SAUCE
  6748	[pour it over the morsels] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  6749	
  6750	    [1] G.-V. _m. ex citriis_.
  6751	
  6752	    [2] At this late point Apicius commences to use the term
  6753	    _coctura_ which does not designate any particular
  6754	    ingredient but rather stands for a certain process of
  6755	    cookery, depending upon the ingredients used in the
  6756	    dish. We would here interpret it as the frying of the
  6757	    leeks in oil, etc. In another instance _coctura_ may
  6758	    mean our modern _réduction_.
  6759	
  6760	    [3] The fruit to be used here has not been
  6761	    satisfactorily identified. The texts have _citrium_ and
  6762	    _citrum_--a sweet squash or cucumber--perhaps even a
  6763	    melon, but not the citron, the _mala citrea_ as read by
  6764	    List. This specimen is hard to identify because of the
  6765	    many varieties in the cucumber, squash and the citrus
  6766	    families. _Citrus_, as a matter of fact, is but a
  6767	    corruption of _cedrus_, the cedar tree.
  6768	
  6769	    We are not sure whether this fruit is to be stuffed with
  6770	    the ragout and then baked, as is often the custom to do
  6771	    with such shells; the texts prescribes distinctly to
  6772	    hollow out the fruit.
  6773	
  6774	    The title, implying a "sweet dish" is obviously wrong.
  6775	
  6776	    It may be remarked here that Apicius makes no mention of
  6777	    that marvelous citrus fruit, the lemon, nor of the
  6778	    orange, both of which are indispensable to modern
  6779	    cookery.
  6780	
  6781	
  6782	[169] MINUTAL OF FRUIT
  6783	    _MINUTAL EX PRÆCOQUIS_
  6784	
  6785	IN A SAUCE PAN PUT OIL, BROTH AND WINE, FINELY CUT SHALLOTS, DICED
  6786	COOKED PORK SHOULDER. WHEN THIS IS COOKED, CRUSH PEPPER, CUMIN, DRY
  6787	MINT, DILL, MOISTEN WITH HONEY, BROTH, RAISIN WINE [and] A LITTLE
  6788	VINEGAR, SOME OF THE GRAVY OF THE ABOVE MORSELS, ADD FRUITS THE SEEDS
  6789	OF WHICH HAVE BEEN TAKEN OUT, LET BOIL, WHEN THOROUGHLY COOKED, SKIM,
  6790	BIND, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE [1].
  6791	
  6792	    [1] This, rather than ℞ No. 168, deserves the title,
  6793	    Sweet Minutal, for it is practically the same, with the
  6794	    addition of the fruit.
  6795	
  6796	
  6797	[170] MINUTAL OF HARE'S LIVERS
  6798	    _MINUTAL LEPORINUM_
  6799	
  6800	THE WAY TO MAKE A MINUTAL OF HARE'S GIBLETS MAY BE FOUND AMONG THE
  6801	HARE RECIPES [1].
  6802	
  6803	[170a] IN A SAUCE PAN PUT OIL, BROTH AND WINE, FINELY CUT SHALLOTS,
  6804	DICED COOKED PORK SHOULDER. WHEN THIS IS COOKED, CRUSH PEPPER, CUMIN,
  6805	DRY MINT, DILL, MOISTEN WITH HONEY, BROTH, RAISIN WINE [and] A LITTLE
  6806	VINEGAR, SOME OF THE GRAVY OF THE ABOVE MORSELS, ADD SEEDLESS FRUITS,
  6807	LET BOIL, WHEN THOROUGHLY COOKED, SKIM, BIND, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND
  6808	SERVE.
  6809	
  6810	    [1] ℞ No. 386, Book VIII is one of these recipes.
  6811	    This is one of the few instances where the ancient
  6812	    original makes any reference to any other part of the
  6813	    Apicius book.* After this bare reference, the original
  6814	    proceeds to repeat the text of the preceding formula
  6815	    verbatim.
  6816	
  6817	    * Cf. ℞ No. 165.
  6818	
  6819	    Brandt suggests a new title for [170a] ANOTHER SWEET
  6820	    MINUTAL.
  6821	
  6822	    The G.-V. version differs but little from ℞ No. 169.
  6823	
  6824	
  6825	[171] RED APPLE MINUTAL
  6826	    _MINUTAL EX ROSIS_ [1]
  6827	
  6828	MAKE THIS THE SAME WAY AS DESCRIBED IN THE FOREGOING, ONLY ADD MORE
  6829	RAISIN WINE.
  6830	
  6831	    [1] List. Roses; Tor. _Rosatium_; this term, medieval
  6832	    Latin, does not exist in the ancient language.
  6833	
  6834	    Sch. _mala rosea_--rosy or red apple, most likely to be
  6835	    the correct interpretation. Cf. ℞ Nos. 136 and 167.
  6836	
  6837	    The above title has led to the belief that the ancients
  6838	    made pies, etc., of roses, an idea that was much
  6839	    ridiculed in England after the publication of Lister's
  6840	    work in 1705.
  6841	
  6842	    We concur with Schuch's interpretation that rosy apples
  6843	    were used, remembering, however, that the fruit of the
  6844	    rose tree, the hip, dog-briar, eglantine is also made
  6845	    into dainty confections on the Continent today. It is
  6846	    therefore entirely possible that this recipe calls for
  6847	    the fruit of the rose tree.
  6848	
  6849	
  6850	
  6851	IV
  6852	
  6853	GRUELS
  6854	  _TISANAM VEL SUCUM_
  6855	
  6856	
  6857	[172] BARLEY BROTH, PAP, PORRIDGE, GRUEL
  6858	    _TISANA SIVE CREMORE_ [1]
  6859	
  6860	CRUSH BARLEY, SOAKED THE DAY BEFORE, WELL WASHED, PLACE ON THE FIRE
  6861	TO BE COOKED [in a double boiler] WHEN HOT ADD ENOUGH OIL, A BUNCH OF
  6862	DILL, DRY ONION, SATURY AND COLOCASIUM [2] TO BE COOKED TOGETHER
  6863	BECAUSE FOR THE BETTER JUICE, ADD GREEN CORIANDER AND A LITTLE SALT;
  6864	BRING IT TO A BOILING POINT. WHEN DONE TAKE OUT THE BUNCH [of dill]
  6865	AND TRANSFER THE BARLEY INTO ANOTHER KETTLE TO AVOID STICKING TO THE
  6866	BOTTOM AND BURNING, MAKE IT LIQUID [by addition of water, broth, milk]
  6867	STRAIN INTO A POT, COVERING THE TOPS OF THE COLOCASIA. NEXT CRUSH
  6868	PEPPER, LOVAGE, A LITTLE DRY FLEA-BANE, CUMIN AND SYLPHIUM [3] STIR IT
  6869	WELL AND ADD VINEGAR, REDUCED MUST AND BROTH; PUT IT BACK INTO THE
  6870	POT, THE REMAINING COLOCASIA FINISH ON A GENTLE FIRE [4].
  6871	
  6872	    [1] Tor. _ptisana siue Cremore_.
  6873	
  6874	    [2] G.-V. _Colœfium_; Tor. _colœsium_ and
  6875	    _colesium_ (the different readings perhaps on account of
  6876	    the similarity of the "long" s with the f). Tor. spells
  6877	    this word differently every time he is confronted with
  6878	    it. Tac., Lan. _coledium_--unidentified. List.
  6879	    _colocasium_, which see in notes to ℞ Nos. 74, 200,
  6880	    216, 244, and 322, also Sch. p. 95.
  6881	
  6882	    [3] List. _sil frictum_; Tor. _silphium f._
  6883	
  6884	    [4] Tor. continuing without interruption. This formula
  6885	    is reported in ℞ No. 200.
  6886	
  6887	
  6888	[173] ANOTHER TISANA
  6889	    _TISANA TARICHA_ [1]
  6890	
  6891	THE CEREAL [2] IS SOAKED; CHICKPEAS, LENTILS AND PEAS ARE CRUSHED AND
  6892	BOILED WITH IT; WHEN WELL COOKED, ADD PLENTY OF OIL. NOW CUT GREEN
  6893	HERBS, LEEKS, CORIANDER, DILL, FENNEL, BEETS, MALLOWS, CABBAGE
  6894	STRUNKS, ALL SOFT AND GREEN AND FINELY CUT, AND PUT IN A POT. THE
  6895	CABBAGE COOK [separately. Also] CRUSH FENNEL SEED, ORIGANY, SYLPHIUM
  6896	AND LOVAGE, AND WHEN CRUSHED, ADD BROTH TO TASTE, POUR THIS OVER THE
  6897	PORRIDGE, STIR IT TOGETHER AND USE SOME FINELY CHOPPED CABBAGE STEMS
  6898	TO SPRINKLE ON TOP [2].
  6899	
  6900	    [1] Variants: _barrica_, _farrica_; List. _legendum,
  6901	    puto, Taricam; id. est Salsam_. Cf. ℞ 144, 149,
  6902	    426-8. Lan., Tor., G.-V. _barricam_, not identified.
  6903	    Sch. _farrica_--corn spelt; probably not far from the
  6904	    mark. We would venture to suggest that our "farina" is
  6905	    the thing here used, or any ordinary corn meal.
  6906	
  6907	    [2] This formula is repeated in ℞ No. 201.
  6908	
  6909	
  6910	
  6911	V
  6912	
  6913	HORS D'ŒUVRES, APPETIZERS, RELISHES
  6914	  _GUSTUM_
  6915	
  6916	
  6917	[174] "MOVEABLE" APPETIZERS
  6918	    _GUSTUM VERSATILE_
  6919	
  6920	THE MOVEABLE [1] APPETIZERS ARE THUS MADE: [2] SMALL WHITE BEETS,
  6921	MATURE LEEKS, CELERY ROOTS [3] STEWED COCKLES [4] GINGER [5] CHICKEN
  6922	GIBLETS, SMALL FOWL [6] SMALL MORSELS COOKED IN THEIR OWN LIQUOR [7].
  6923	OIL A PAN, LINE IT WITH MALLOW LEAVES AND A COMPOSITION OF DIFFERENT
  6924	VEGETABLES, AND, IF YOU HAVE ROOM ENOUGH, BULBS, DAMASCUS PLUMS,
  6925	SNAILS, TID-BITS [8] SHORT LUCANIAN SAUSAGE SLICED; ADD BROTH, OIL,
  6926	WINE, VINEGAR PUT ON THE FIRE TO HEAT AND SO COOK THEM. MEANWHILE
  6927	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, GINGER, A LITTLE TARRAGON, MOISTEN IT AND LET IT
  6928	COOK. BREAK SEVERAL EGGS IN A DISH, USE THE REMAINING LIQUOR IN THE
  6929	MORTAR TO MIX IT WITH THE SAUCE IN THE DISH AND TO BIND IT. WHEN THIS
  6930	IS DONE, MAKE A WINE SAUCE FOR IT AS FOLLOWS: CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE,
  6931	MOISTENED WITH BROTH, RAISIN WINE TO TASTE; IN A SMALL SAUCE PAN PUT A
  6932	LITTLE OIL [with the other ingredients] HEAT, AND BIND WITH ROUX WHEN
  6933	HOT. NOW [unmould] UPSET THE DISH ON A PLATTER, REMOVE THE MALLOW
  6934	LEAVES, POUR OVER THE WINE SAUCE, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE [9].
  6935	
  6936	    [1] Moveable, either because it is one show piece that
  6937	    is carried from one guest to another, or, as here
  6938	    indicated, a dish that is to be unmoulded or turned out
  6939	    of its mould or pan before service.
  6940	
  6941	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  6942	
  6943	    [3] Celery roots, i.e. the thick bulbs. G.-V. _apios,
  6944	    bulbos_--celery, onions; note the comma after _apios_.
  6945	
  6946	    [4] Periwinkles, also snails.
  6947	
  6948	    [5] Tac., Lan. _gingibera_; Tor. _zinziber_; Vat. Ms.
  6949	    _gibera_; G.-V. _Gigeria_; Hum. _id._--giblets. Wanting
  6950	    in List.
  6951	
  6952	    [6] List. _avicellas_; Vat. Ms. _aucellare_ and
  6953	    _scellas_; Tac., Lan. _id._; Tor. _pullorum
  6954	    axillas_--chicken wings (?); G.-V. _ascellas_.
  6955	
  6956	    [7] _ex iure._
  6957	
  6958	    [8] _isitia_--quenelles of forcemeat, etc.
  6959	
  6960	    [9] An extremely complicated composition of varied
  6961	    morsels, definite instructions lacking, however. It is
  6962	    not clear whether the dish was served hot (in which case
  6963	    the dish would not stand up long) or whether served
  6964	    cold, jellyfied. Moreover, the title _gustum_--_hors
  6965	    d'œuvres_--is not consistent either with similar
  6966	    creations by Apicius or with our own notions of such
  6967	    dishes. This title may merely suggest that such a dish
  6968	    was to be served at the beginning of a repast. This
  6969	    recipe presents an instance of the difficulty to render
  6970	    the text and its variants in a manner acceptable to our
  6971	    modern palates.
  6972	
  6973	    We are of the opinion that the above recipe is a
  6974	    contraction of two or more formulæ, each of which,
  6975	    separately, might make acceptable hot appetizers.
  6976	
  6977	
  6978	[175] VEGETABLE RELISH [1]
  6979	    _GUSTUM DE OLERIBUS_ [2]
  6980	
  6981	FOR THIS VEGETABLE DISH BOIL BULBS [3] [in] BROTH, OIL, AND WINE; WHEN
  6982	DONE [add] LIVER OF SUCKLING PIG [4] CHICKEN LIVERS AND FEET AND SMALL
  6983	BIRDS [5] CUT IN HALVES, ALL TO BE COOKED WITH THE BULBS. WHEN DONE,
  6984	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, MOISTENED WITH BROTH, WINE, RAISIN WINE TO
  6985	SWEETEN IT. ADD OF THE OWN LIQUOR OF THE MORSELS, RETIRE THE ONIONS,
  6986	WHEN DONE [group the morsels together in the service dish] BIND [the
  6987	sauce] WITH ROUX IN THE LAST MOMENT [strain over the morsels] AND
  6988	SERVE.
  6989	
  6990	    [1] An entremet of fowl and livers.
  6991	
  6992	    [2] a misnomer, as vegetables play the least part in
  6993	    this dish.
  6994	
  6995	    [3] Onions, etc.
  6996	
  6997	    [4] _jecinora porcelli_; Sch. _iscinera porcellum_.
  6998	
  6999	    [5] Tor. _axillas_ and _scellas_; see note 6 to ℞
  7000	    174.
  7001	
  7002	
  7003	[176] STUFFED PUMPKIN FRITTERS
  7004	    _GUSTUM DE CUCURBITIS FARSILIBUS_
  7005	
  7006	A DISH OF STUFFED PUMPKIN [1] IS MADE THUS: [2] PEEL AND CUT THE
  7007	PUMPKIN LENGTHWISE INTO OBLONG PIECES WHICH HOLLOW OUT AND PUT IN A
  7008	COOL PLACE. THE DRESSING FOR THE SAME MAKE IN THIS WAY: CRUSH PEPPER,
  7009	LOVAGE AND ORIGANY, MOISTENED WITH BROTH; MINCE COOKED BRAINS AND BEAT
  7010	RAW EGGS AND MIX ALL TOGETHER TO FORM A PASTE; ADD BROTH AS TASTE
  7011	REQUIRES. STUFF THE ABOVE PREPARED PIECES OF PUMPKIN THAT HAVE NOT
  7012	BEEN FULLY COOKED WITH THE DRESSING; FIT TWO PIECES TOGETHER AND CLOSE
  7013	THEM TIGHT [holding them by means of strings or skewers]. [Now poach
  7014	them and] TAKE THE COOKED ONES OUT AND FRY THEM [3]. [The proper] WINE
  7015	SAUCE [for this dish] MAKE THUS: CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE MOISTENED WITH
  7016	WINE, RAISIN WINE TO TASTE, A LITTLE OIL, PLACE IN PAN TO BE COOKED;
  7017	WHEN DONE BIND WITH ROUX. COVER THE FRIED PUMPKIN WITH THIS SAUCE,
  7018	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE [4].
  7019	
  7020	    [1] Dann. cucumbers, for which there is no authority.
  7021	    Cucumbers lend themselves equally well for a dish of
  7022	    this kind; they are often stuffed with a forcemeat of
  7023	    finely minced meats, mushrooms, eggs, breadcrumbs, or
  7024	    simply with raw sausage meat, cooked as above, and
  7025	    served as a garnish with _entrées_.
  7026	
  7027	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  7028	
  7029	    [3] Presumably in deep fat or oil, a procedure which
  7030	    would require previous breading in bread crumbs or
  7031	    enveloping in frying batter.
  7032	
  7033	    [4] Whether you like pumpkin and brains or not--Apicius
  7034	    in this dish reveals himself as the consummate master of
  7035	    his art that he really is--a cook for cooks; Moreover,
  7036	    the lucidity of his diction in this instance is equally
  7037	    remarkable. It stands out in striking contrast to his
  7038	    many other formulæ which are so obscured. Many of them
  7039	    perhaps were precepts of likewise striking originality
  7040	    as this one just cited.
  7041	
  7042	
  7043	[177] COMPÔTE OF EARLY FRUIT
  7044	    _GUSTUM DE PRÆCOQUIS_
  7045	
  7046	CLEAN HARD-SKINNED EARLY FRUITS [1] REMOVE THE SEEDS AND KEEP THEM
  7047	COLD IN A PAN. CRUSH PEPPER [2] DRY MINT, MOISTENED WITH BROTH, ADDING
  7048	HONEY, RAISIN WINE, WINE AND VINEGAR; POUR THIS OVER THE FRUIT IN THE
  7049	PAN, ADDING A LITTLE OIL. STEW SLOWLY ON A WEAK FIRE, THICKEN [the
  7050	juice] WITH ROUX [rice flour or other starch diluted with water]
  7051	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER [2] AND SERVE [3].
  7052	
  7053	    [1] Lister praises the early green fruit and the use
  7054	    thereof, and, as a physician, recommends imitation of
  7055	    the above as follows: _In aliis plurimis locis hujus
  7056	    fructus mentio fit; ususque mirabilis fuit; & certe
  7057	    propter salubritatem, nostram imitationem meretur._
  7058	
  7059	    [2] We do not like the "pepper" in this connection and
  7060	    we venture to suggest that in this case the term
  7061	    probably stands for some other kind of aromatic seed
  7062	    less pungent than the grain known to us as "pepper" and
  7063	    one more acceptable to the fine flavor of fruit, namely
  7064	    pimiento, allspice for instance, or clove, or nutmeg, or
  7065	    a mixture of these. "Pepper" formerly was a generic term
  7066	    for all of these spices but was gradually confined to
  7067	    the grain pepper of black and white varieties.
  7068	
  7069	    [3] We concur with Lister's idea of the use of early
  7070	    fruits. The use of early and unripe fruit for this and
  7071	    similar purposes is excellent. The above formula is a
  7072	    good example of our own "spiced" peaches, pears, etc.,
  7073	    usually taken as a relish. Of course, we use sugar
  7074	    instead of honey for sweetening, and brandy instead of
  7075	    wine; but the underlying principles are alike.
  7076	
  7077	    This is a good illustration of and speaks well for the
  7078	    economy and the ingenuity of the ancients.
  7079	
  7080	
  7081	END OF BOOK IV

  7088	{Illustration: ROUND TABLE
  7089	
  7090	Claw-footed bronze legs on triangular base, consisting of three molded
  7091	cylindrical supports, connected by cross-bars. Near the top the legs
  7092	take on a greyhound design, with a three-armed brace connecting them.
  7093	The round top is of marble. Pompeii. Ntl. Mus., Naples, 78613; Field
  7094	M., 24281.}

  7098	
  7099	APICIUS
  7100	
  7101	Book V
  7102	
  7103	
  7104	
  7105	
  7106	{Illustration: POMPEII: WINE STOCK ROOM OF A TAVERN
  7107	
  7108	Wine was kept in these great jugs, tightly sealed with plaster and
  7109	pitch, properly dated and labeled, often remaining for many years.
  7110	Some writers mention wine thus kept for a hundred years; the porosity
  7111	of the earthen crocks, often holding fifty gallons or more, allowed
  7112	evaporation, so that the wine in time became as thick as oil or honey,
  7113	which necessitated diluting with water.
  7114	
  7115	Smaller amphoræ, with various vintages readily mixed, were kept cool
  7116	in "bars" very similar to our present ice cream cabinets, ready for
  7117	service for the guests in tavern rooms.
  7118	
  7119	Elaborate dippers (see our illustration) were used to draw the wine
  7120	from the amphoræ.}
  7121	
  7122	
  7123	
  7124	
  7125	{Illustration: FRUIT OR DESSERT DISH, SEA-SHELL SHAPE
  7126	
  7127	The curved handle ends in the head of a griffin. Ntl. Mus., Naples,
  7128	76303; Field M. 24298.}

  7133	BOOK V. LEGUMES
  7134	
  7135	_Lib. V. Osprion_ [1]
  7136

  7138	    CHAP.    I. PULSE, MEAL MUSH, PORRIDGE, ETC.
  7139	    CHAP.   II. LENTILS.
  7140	    CHAP.  III. PEAS.
  7141	    CHAP.   IV. BEANS OR PEAS IN THE POD.
  7142	    CHAP.    V. BARLEY BROTH.
  7143	    CHAP.   VI. GREEN BEANS, BAIÆAN BEANS.
  7144	    CHAP.  VII. FENUGREEK.
  7145	    CHAP. VIII. GREEN STRING BEANS AND CHICK-PEAS.
  7146	
  7147	
  7148	
  7149	I
  7150	
  7151	MEAL MUSH, MUSH, PULSE, PAP, PORRIDGE, POLENTA
  7152	  _DE PULTIBUS_ [2]
  7153	
  7154	
  7155	[178] JULIAN MEAL MUSH
  7156	    _PULTES JULIANÆ_ [3]
  7157	
  7158	JULIAN PULSES ARE COOKED THUS: SOAK WELL-CLEANED SPELT, PUT IT ON THE
  7159	FIRE; WHEN COOKED, ADD OIL. IF IT THREATENS TO BECOME THICK, CAREFULLY
  7160	THIN IT DOWN. TAKE TWO COOKED BRAINS AND HALF A POUND OF MEAT GROUND
  7161	AS FOR FORCEMEAT, CRUSH THIS WITH THE BRAINS AND PUT IN A POT. CRUSH
  7162	PEPPER, LOVAGE AND FENNEL SEED, MOISTENED WITH BROTH, A LITTLE WINE
  7163	AND PUT IT ON TOP OF THE BRAIN AND MEAT. WHEN THIS FORCEMEAT IS HEATED
  7164	SUFFICIENTLY, MIX IT WITH THE SPELT [finish boiling] TRANSFER INTO
  7165	SERVICE DISH, THINNED. THIS MUST HAVE THE CONSISTENCY OF A HEAVY JUICE
  7166	[4].
  7167	
  7168	    [1] List. _Osprios_; G.-V. _Ospreon_--cookery of
  7169	    leguminous plants.
  7170	
  7171	    [2] _Puls_--formerly a simple porridge of various kinds
  7172	    of cereals or legumes, eaten by the Romans before bread
  7173	    came into use. _Puls_ remained in use after the
  7174	    introduction of bread only as a food of the poor. It was
  7175	    also used at sacrifices. The _pultes_ and _pulticulæ_
  7176	    given by Apicius are illustrations of the ever-present
  7177	    desire to improve--to glorify, as it were, a thing which
  7178	    once was or still is of vital importance in the daily
  7179	    life of humans. The _nouveaux-riches_ of the ancient and
  7180	    the modern world cannot find it easy to separate
  7181	    themselves from their traditions nor are they wont to
  7182	    put up with their plainness, hence the fancy trimmings.
  7183	    The development of the American pie is a curious analogy
  7184	    in this respect. We see in this the intricate working of
  7185	    human culture, its eternal strife for perfection. And
  7186	    perfection is synonymous with decay. The fare of the
  7187	    Carthusian monks, professed, stern vegetarians,
  7188	    underwent the same tortuous evolution.
  7189	
  7190	    [3] Named for Didius Julianus, the emperor who was a
  7191	    vegetarian. Of course, his majesty could not live on a
  7192	    plain porridge, hence the Apician artistry. The _pultes_
  7193	    were popular with the many professed vegetarians though
  7194	    the obliging cooks mixed finely ground meat in this and
  7195	    other porridges.
  7196	
  7197	    Our various cream soups and legume purées--those most
  7198	    salubrious creations of modern cookery are no doubt
  7199	    lineal descendants from the Apician _pultes_. They are
  7200	    so scarce comparatively because they require all the
  7201	    ingenuity and resourcefulness of a gifted cook to be
  7202	    perfect.
  7203	
  7204	    [4] Dann. remarks that this formula is wanting in List.
  7205	    Both Lister's first and second editions have it.
  7206	
  7207	
  7208	[179] GRUEL AND WINE
  7209	    _PULTES ŒNOCOCTI_
  7210	
  7211	PORRIDGE AND WINE IS THUS MADE: [1] FLAVOR THE PULSE WELL WITH WINE
  7212	[2] AND IMMERSE IN THE JUICE DAINTY MORSELS [3].
  7213	
  7214	    [1] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  7215	
  7216	    [2] Tor. _Oenogari_; G.-V. _Oenococti_.
  7217	
  7218	    [3] Tor. _cupedias_; _copadia_.
  7219	
  7220	
  7221	[180] SIMILAR
  7222	    _SIMILAM_ [1]
  7223	
  7224	OR FLAVOR COOKED SPELT WITH THE LIQUOR OF DAINTY PIECES OF PORK, OR
  7225	CAPON [2] COOKED IN WINE [3].
  7226	
  7227	    [1] Tac. _inulam_; Tor. _mulam_--misreading.
  7228	
  7229	    [2] Tor.; List. _apponis_.
  7230	
  7231	    [3] For practical reasons we have separated the text of
  7232	    ℞ Nos. 179 and 180 which appears as one in the texts.
  7233	
  7234	
  7235	[181] MILK TOAST
  7236	    _PULTES TRACTOGALATÆ_ [1]
  7237	
  7238	PUT A PINT OF MILK AND SOME WATER ON THE FIRE IN A NEW [clean] POT;
  7239	BREAK ROUND BREAD INTO IT [2] DRY, STIR WELL TO PREVENT BURNING; ADD
  7240	WATER AS NECESSARY [3].
  7241	
  7242	    [1] Tor. _pulticula tractogala_.
  7243	
  7244	    [2] List. _tres orbiculos tractæ_; Tor. _teres
  7245	    sorbiculos tractæ_.
  7246	
  7247	    _Tractum_ is a piece of pastry, a round bread or roll in
  7248	    this case, stale, best suited for this purpose.
  7249	
  7250	    [3] The text continues without interruption.
  7251	
  7252	
  7253	[182] HONEY PAP
  7254	    _SIMILITER_
  7255	
  7256	HONEY AND MEAD ARE TREATED SIMILARLY, MIXED WITH MILK, WITH THE
  7257	ADDITION OF SALT AND A LITTLE OIL.
  7258	
  7259	
  7260	[178-183] PULSE
  7261	        _PULTES_ [1]
  7262	
  7263	    [1] Tor. _Alia pulticula_.
  7264	
  7265	    This is a verbatim repetition of ℞ No. 178.
  7266	
  7267	
  7268	
  7269	II
  7270	
  7271	LENTILS
  7272	  _LENTICULA_ [1]
  7273	
  7274	
  7275	[183] LENTILS AND COW-PARSNIPS
  7276	    _LENTICULA EX SPONDYLIS SIVE FONDYLIS_ [2]
  7277	
  7278	PUT THE LENTILS IN A CLEAN SAUCE PAN [and cook with salt]. IN THE
  7279	MORTAR CRUSH PEPPER, CUMIN, CORIANDER SEED, MINT, RUE, AND FLEA-BANE,
  7280	MOISTENED WITH VINEGAR, ADD HONEY AND BROTH AND REDUCED MUST, VINEGAR
  7281	TO TASTE AND PUT THIS IN A SAUCE PAN. THE COOKED COW-PARSNIPS CRUSH,
  7282	HEAT [mix with the lentils] WHEN THOROUGHLY COOKED, TIE, ADD GREEN
  7283	[fresh olive] OIL AND SERVE IN AN APPROPRIATE DISH [3].
  7284	
  7285	    [1] Tor. _De Lenticula et Castaneis_.
  7286	
  7287	    [2] List. again: _ex spongiolis sive fungulis_. See
  7288	    notes to ℞ Nos. 115-120 and 431.
  7289	
  7290	    [3] _Boletar_--a "mushroom" dish. G.-V. _in boletari_;
  7291	    Tac. _insuper oleum uiridem mittis_; Tor.
  7292	    _inuolutari_--unidentified.
  7293	
  7294	
  7295	[184] LENTILS [1] AND CHESTNUTS
  7296	    _LENTICULAM DE CASTANEIS_ [2]
  7297	
  7298	TAKE A NEW SAUCE PAN, PLACE THEREIN THE CHESTNUTS CAREFULLY CLEANED
  7299	[3] ADD WATER AND A LITTLE SODA AND PLACE ON THE FIRE TO BE COOKED.
  7300	THIS DONE, CRUSH IN THE MORTAR PEPPER, CUMIN, CORIANDER SEED, MINT,
  7301	RUE, LASER ROOT AND FLEA-BANE MOISTENED WITH VINEGAR, HONEY AND BROTH;
  7302	ADD VINEGAR TO TASTE AND POUR THIS OVER THE COOKED CHESTNUTS, ADD OIL
  7303	AND ALLOW TO BOIL. WHEN DONE CRUSH IT IN THE MORTAR [4]. TASTE TO SEE
  7304	IF SOMETHING IS MISSING AND IF SO, PUT IT IN, AND AT LAST ADD GREEN
  7305	[fresh virgin] OIL.
  7306	
  7307	    [1] Lentils are omitted in this formula; therefore see
  7308	    the following formula.
  7309	
  7310	    [2] Thus G.-V.; Tor. Chestnuts.
  7311	
  7312	    [3] i.e. peeled and skinned. To do this easily, boil the
  7313	    chestnuts with the skin, whereupon the outer brown shell
  7314	    and the inner membrane are easily removed.
  7315	
  7316	    [4] To make a purée of the chestnuts which strain
  7317	    through the colander.
  7318	
  7319	
  7320	[184a] ANOTHER WAY [1]
  7321	     _ALITER LENTICULAM_
  7322	
  7323	COOK THE LENTILS, SKIM THEM [strain] ADD LEEKS, GREEN CORIANDER; CRUSH
  7324	CORIANDER SEED, FLEA-BANE, LASER ROOT, MINT SEED AND RUE SEED
  7325	MOISTENED WITH VINEGAR; ADD HONEY, BROTH, VINEGAR, REDUCED MUST TO
  7326	TASTE, THEN OIL, STIRRING [the purée] UNTIL IT IS DONE, BIND WITH
  7327	ROUX, ADD GREEN OIL, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  7328	
  7329	    [1] It is evident that ℞ No. 184 and the above are
  7330	    really one formula, the former dealing with the cooking
  7331	    of the maroons, the latter describing the lentils.
  7332	    Presumably the two purées are to be mixed, or to be
  7333	    served as integral parts of one dish.
  7334	
  7335	
  7336	
  7337	III
  7338	
  7339	
  7340	[185] PEAS
  7341	    _DE PISIS_
  7342	
  7343	COOK THE PEAS, WHEN SKIMMED, LAY LEEKS, CORIANDER AND CUMIN ON TOP.
  7344	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, DILL AND GREEN BASILICA, WINE AND BROTH
  7345	TO TASTE, MAKE IT BOIL; WHEN DONE STIR WELL, PUT IN WHAT PERCHANCE
  7346	SHOULD BE MISSING AND SERVE [1].
  7347	
  7348	    [1] This reminds us of _Petits Pois à la Française_,
  7349	    namely green peas (often very young ones with the pods)
  7350	    cooked in broth, or _bouillon_, with shredded bacon,
  7351	    lettuce, parsley, onions (or leeks, as above) fresh
  7352	    mint, pepper, salt and other fresh herbs such as
  7353	    chervil. Which is a very delectable way of preparing the
  7354	    tender pea. Some of its refreshing green color is
  7355	    sacrificed by this process, but this loss is amply
  7356	    offset by the savour of the dish.
  7357	
  7358	
  7359	[186] PEAS [supreme style]
  7360	    _PISA FARSILIS_ [1]
  7361	
  7362	COOK THE PEAS WITH OIL AND A PIECE OF SOW'S BELLY [2] PUT IN A SAUCE
  7363	PAN BROTH, LEEK HEADS [the lower white part] GREEN CORIANDER AND PUT
  7364	ON THE FIRE TO BE COOKED. OF TID-BITS [3] CUT LITTLE DICE. SIMILARLY
  7365	COOK THRUSHES OR OTHER SMALL [game] BIRDS, OR TAKE SLICED CHICKEN AND
  7366	DICED BRAIN, PROPERLY COOKED. FURTHER COOK, IN THE AVAILABLE LIQUOR OR
  7367	BROTH, LUCANIAN SAUSAGE AND BACON; COOK LEEKS IN WATER; CRUSH A PINT
  7368	OF TOASTED PIGNOLIA NUTS; ALSO CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY AND
  7369	GINGER, DILUTE WITH THE BROTH OF PORK, TIE [4] TAKE A SQUARE BAKING
  7370	DISH SUITABLE FOR TURNING OVER WHICH OIL WELL AND LINE WITH CAUL [5]
  7371	SPRINKLE [on the bottom] A LAYER OF CRUSHED NUTS UPON WHICH PUT SOME
  7372	PEAS, FULLY COVERING THE BOTTOM OF THE SQUASH DISH; ON TOP OF THIS
  7373	ARRANGE SLICES OF THE BACON [6] LEEKS AND SLICED LUCANIAN SAUSAGE;
  7374	AGAIN COVER WITH A LAYER OF PEAS AND ALTERNATE ALL THE REST OF THE
  7375	AVAILABLE EDIBLES IN THE MANNER DESCRIBED UNTIL THE DISH IS FILLED,
  7376	CONCLUDING AT LAST WITH A LAYER OF PEAS, UTILIZING EVERYTHING. BAKE
  7377	THIS DISH IN THE OVEN, OR PUT IT INTO A SLOW FIRE [covering it with
  7378	live coal] SO THAT IT MAY BE BAKED THOROUGHLY. [Next make a sauce of
  7379	the following] PUT YOLKS OF HARD BOILED EGGS IN THE MORTAR WITH WHITE
  7380	PEPPER, NUTS, HONEY, WHITE WINE AND A LITTLE BROTH; MIX AND PUT IT
  7381	INTO A SAUCE PAN TO BE COOKED; WHEN [the sauce is] DONE, TURN OUT THE
  7382	PEAS INTO A LARGE [silver dish] AND MASK THEM WITH THIS SAUCE WHICH IS
  7383	CALLED WHITE SAUCE [7].
  7384	
  7385	    [1] List. _Pisa farsilis_; Tor. _p. farsilia_; Tac.,
  7386	    G.-V. _pisam farsilem_--same as _fartilis_, from
  7387	    _farcio_--fattened, stuffed, or crammed, or as full as
  7388	    it can hold, metaphorically perhaps "supreme style,"
  7389	    "most sumptuous," etc.
  7390	
  7391	    [2] This meat being fat enough, the oil seems
  7392	    superfluous.
  7393	
  7394	    [3] _isicia_, formerly called Greek _hysitia_--any fine
  7395	    forcemeats, cut into or cooked in tiny dumplings.
  7396	
  7397	    [4] _Liaison_ wanting in Tor.
  7398	
  7399	    [5] Tor. makes no mention of the square dish and its
  7400	    caul lining. Caul is the abdominal membrane.
  7401	
  7402	    [6] _petasonis pulpas_; Dann. ham, which is not quite
  7403	    correct. The _petaso_ is the shoulder part of pork,
  7404	    either cured or fresh, generally fresh. The cooked pork
  7405	    shoulder here is cut into small pieces. Nothing is said
  7406	    about the utilization of the sow's belly mentioned at
  7407	    the opening of the formula. We assume that the _petaso_
  7408	    can take its place in the dish.
  7409	
  7410	    [7] There is nothing just like this dish in the history
  7411	    of gastronomy, considering both the comparatively cheap
  7412	    materials and the refinement of the gastronomic idea
  7413	    which it embodies. The _chartreuses_ of Carême are the
  7414	    nearest thing to it. Lister waxes enthusiastic about it.
  7415	
  7416	
  7417	[187] INDIAN PEAS
  7418	    _PISAM INDICAM_ [1]
  7419	
  7420	COOK PEAS; WHEN SKIMMED, PUT IN THE SAUCE PAN FINELY CHOPPED LEEKS AND
  7421	CORIANDER TO BE COOKED [with the peas]. TAKE SMALL CUTTLE FISH, MOST
  7422	DESIRABLE BECAUSE OF THE BLACK LIQUOR AND COOK THEM ALSO. ADD OIL,
  7423	BROTH AND WINE, A BUNCH OF LEEK AND [green] CORIANDER AND MAKE IT
  7424	BOIL. WHEN DONE, CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, A LITTLE WILD CUMIN
  7425	[2] MOISTEN WITH THE JUICE [of the peas] ADD WINE AND RAISIN WINE TO
  7426	TASTE; MINCE THE FISH VERY FINE, INCORPORATE IT WITH THE PEAS, AND
  7427	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER [3].
  7428	
  7429	    [1] Tor. _pisum Indicum_.
  7430	
  7431	    [2] Tor., Tac. _casei modicum_; other texts, _carei_.
  7432	
  7433	    [3] The texts continues without interruption to the next
  7434	    formula.
  7435	
  7436	
  7437	[188] ANOTHER WAY
  7438	    _ALITER_
  7439	
  7440	COOK THE PEAS, WORK WELL [to make a purée] PLACE IN THE COLD, STIRRING
  7441	UNTIL THEY HAVE COOLED OFF. FINELY CHOP ONIONS AND THE WHITES OF HARD
  7442	BOILED EGGS, SEASON WITH SALT AND A LITTLE VINEGAR; THE YOLKS PRESS
  7443	THROUGH A COLANDER INTO AN ENTRÉE DISH, SEASON WITH FRESH OIL AND
  7444	SERVE [1].
  7445	
  7446	    [1] The texts fail to state that the whites, yolks,
  7447	    onions, vinegar and oil must eventually be combined into
  7448	    a dressing very similar to our own modern _vinaigrette_;
  7449	    for decorative and other gastronomic reasons the
  7450	    separate treatment of the whites and the yolks is both
  7451	    ingenious and excellent, and is very often practised in
  7452	    good kitchens today.
  7453	
  7454	
  7455	[189] PEAS OR BEANS À LA VITELLIUS
  7456	    _PISAM VITELLIANAM SIVE FABAM_ [1]
  7457	
  7458	PEAS OR BEANS WITH YOLKS ARE MADE THUS: [2] COOK THE PEAS, SMOOTHEN
  7459	[3] THEM; CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, GINGER, AND ON THE CONDIMENTS PUT HARD
  7460	BOILED YOLKS, 3 OUNCES OF HONEY, ALSO BROTH, WINE AND VINEGAR; [mix
  7461	and] PLACE ALL IN A SAUCE PAN; THE FINELY CHOPPED CONDIMENTS WITH OIL
  7462	ADDED, PUT ON THE STOVE TO BE COOKED; WITH THIS FLAVOR THE PEAS WHICH
  7463	MUST BE SMOOTH; AND IF THEY BE TOO HARSH [in taste] ADD HONEY AND
  7464	SERVE [4].
  7465	
  7466	    [1] List. _Pisa Vitelliana_--named for Vitellius, ninth
  7467	    Roman emperor, notorious glutton, according to Hum. who
  7468	    says that V. invented this dish: _ab auctore Vitellio
  7469	    Imperatore luxui deditissimo_. But Tor. differs; his
  7470	    _pisum uitellinum_ stands for peas with
  7471	    yolks--_vitellum_--yolk, (also calf) dim. _vitellinum_;
  7472	    Tac. _v----am_. Cf. ℞ No. 193.
  7473	
  7474	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  7475	
  7476	    [3] _lias_--to make a purée by crushing and straining.
  7477	    Tor. _lævigabis_, from _levigo_--meaning the same.
  7478	
  7479	    [4] If Vitellius never invented any other dish than this
  7480	    one, his gluttony was overrated. As a gastronomer he may
  7481	    be safely relegated to the vast multitude of ill-advised
  7482	    people whose craving for carbohydrates (which is perhaps
  7483	    pathological) causes them to accumulate a surplus of
  7484	    fat. This was fatal to Vitellius and his faithful court
  7485	    baker who is said to have stuck to his master to the
  7486	    last. The poor emperor's _embonpoint_ proved cumbersome
  7487	    when he fled the infuriated mob. Had he been leaner he
  7488	    might have effected a "getaway." He was dragged through
  7489	    the streets and murdered, Dec. 21 or 22, A.D. 69.
  7490	
  7491	
  7492	[190] ANOTHER WAY
  7493	    _ALITER PISAM SIVE FABAM_
  7494	
  7495	WHEN [the peas or beans are] SKIMMED MIX BROTH, HONEY, MUST, CUMIN,
  7496	RUE, CELERY SEED, OIL AND WINE, STIR [1]. SERVE WITH CRUSHED PEPPER
  7497	AND SAUSAGE [2].
  7498	
  7499	    [1] G.-V. _tudiclabis_; Tor. _misceas_.
  7500	
  7501	    [2] _cum isiciis_--bits of forcemeat.
  7502	
  7503	
  7504	[191] ANOTHER WAY
  7505	    _ALITER PISAM SIVE FABAM_
  7506	
  7507	WHEN [the peas or beans are] SKIMMED FLAVOR THEM WITH CRUSHED PERSIAN
  7508	[1] LASER, BROTH AND MUST; POUR A LITTLE OIL OVER AND SERVE.
  7509	
  7510	    [1] Parthian, from _Parthia_, a country of Asia.
  7511	
  7512	
  7513	[192] A TEMPTING DISH OF PEAS
  7514	    _PISAM ADULTERAM _[1]_ VERSATILEM_
  7515	
  7516	THIS ADROIT, TEMPTING DISH OF PEAS IS PREPARED IN THIS MANNER: [2]
  7517	COOK PEAS; BRAINS OR SMALL BIRDS, OR BONED THRUSHES, LUCANIAN SAUSAGE,
  7518	CHICKEN LIVERS AND GIBLETS--ALL OF WHICH ARE PUT IN A SAUCE PAN;
  7519	BROTH, OIL AND A BUNCH OF LEEKS, GREEN CORIANDER FINELY CHOPPED, COOK
  7520	WITH THE BRAINS; CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE AND BROTH [3].
  7521	
  7522	    [1] Sch., Dann. crafty, i.e. not genuine. _Adulteram_
  7523	    cannot here be used in its most accepted sense, because
  7524	    the peas are genuine, and no attempt is made to
  7525	    adulterate or "fake" this dish in any way, shape or
  7526	    form. Never before have we applied the term "seductive"
  7527	    to any dish, but this is just what _adultera_ means.
  7528	    "Tempting" of course is quite common.
  7529	
  7530	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  7531	
  7532	    [3] This formula is incomplete or mutilated, the last
  7533	    sentence breaks off in the middle--very likely a
  7534	    description of the sauce or condiments belonging to the
  7535	    peas.
  7536	
  7537	    Each and every component of this (really tempting) dish
  7538	    must be cooked separately; they are then composed in a
  7539	    dish, nicely arranged, with the peas in the center,
  7540	    surrounded by the several morsels, with an appropriate
  7541	    gravy made from the natural liquor or juices of the
  7542	    component parts poured over the dish.
  7543	
  7544	
  7545	[193] PEAS À LA VITELLIUS
  7546	    _PISAM SIVE FABAM VITELLIANAM_ [1]
  7547	
  7548	PEAS OR BEANS IN THE STYLE OF VITELLIUS PREPARE THUS: [2] [The peas or
  7549	beans] ARE COOKED, WHEN CAREFULLY SKIMMED, ADD LEEKS, CORIANDER AND
  7550	MALLOW FLOWERS [3]: WHEN DONE, CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, AND
  7551	FENNEL SEED MOISTENED WITH BROTH [and put it] INTO A SAUCE PAN WITH
  7552	WINE [4], ADDING OIL, HEAT THOROUGHLY AND WHEN BOILING STIR WELL; PUT
  7553	GREEN OIL ON TOP AND SERVE.
  7554	
  7555	    [1] Named for the inventor, Emperor Vitellius; cf. notes
  7556	    to ℞ No. 189. Tor. _Vitellianum_.
  7557	
  7558	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  7559	
  7560	    [3] Wanting in Dann.
  7561	
  7562	    [4] Tor.
  7563	
  7564	
  7565	
  7566	IV
  7567	
  7568	
  7569	[194] BEANS IN THE POD
  7570	    _CONCHICLA_ [1]
  7571	
  7572	COOK THE BEANS [2]; MEANWHILE CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, GREEN
  7573	CORIANDER, MOISTENED WITH BROTH AND WINE, AND ADD [more] BROTH TO
  7574	TASTE, PUT INTO THE SAUCE PAN [with the beans] ADDING OIL; HEAT ON A
  7575	SLOW FIRE AND SERVE.
  7576	
  7577	    [1] Tor. _Concicla_--_conchis_--_conchicula_--young,
  7578	    immature beans, string or wax, boiled in the shell or
  7579	    pod.
  7580	
  7581	    [2] _conchiclam cum faba_--young string beans and (dry,
  7582	    white or kidney) beans, cooked separately of course and
  7583	    mixed when done, ready for service.
  7584	
  7585	
  7586	[195] PEAS IN THE POD APICIAN STYLE
  7587	    _CONCHICLAM APICIANAM_
  7588	
  7589	FOR PEAS IN THE POD [1] APICIAN STYLE TAKE: [2] A CLEAN EARTHEN POT IN
  7590	WHICH TO COOK THE PEAS; TO THE PEAS ADD FINELY CUT LUCANIAN SAUSAGE,
  7591	LITTLE PORK CAKES [3], PIECES OF MEAT [4] AND PORK SHOULDER [5]. CRUSH
  7592	PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, DILL, DRY ONIONS [6] GREEN CORIANDER
  7593	MOISTENED WITH BROTH, WINE, AND ADD [more] BROTH TO TASTE; UNITE THIS
  7594	WITH THE PEAS IN THE EARTHEN POT TO WHICH ADD OIL IN SUFFICIENT
  7595	QUANTITY TO BE ABSORBED BY THE PEAS; FINISH ON A SLOW FIRE TO GIVE IT
  7596	LIVE HEAT AND SERVE.
  7597	
  7598	    [1] Peas in the pod are likewise called _conchicla_;
  7599	    hence perhaps any legumes cooked in the shells.
  7600	
  7601	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  7602	
  7603	    [3] _isiciola porcina._
  7604	
  7605	    [4] _pulpas_--in this case no specific meat.
  7606	
  7607	    [5] _petaso_; Dann. pieces of ham
  7608	
  7609	    [6] _cepam siccam_--ordinary dry onions, not shallots.
  7610	
  7611	
  7612	[196] SIMPLE DISH OF PEAS IN THE POD
  7613	    _CONCHICLA DE PISA SIMPLICI_ [1]
  7614	
  7615	COOK THE PEAS [in the pods] WHEN SKIMMED ADD A BUNCH [2] OF LEEKS AND
  7616	GREEN CORIANDER. WHILE BEING COOKED CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, AND
  7617	[the above] BUNCH [of herbs] [3] MOISTEN WITH ITS OWN JUICE, WINE [4]
  7618	ENOUGH TO SUIT YOUR TASTE, THEN ADD OIL AND FINISH ON A SLOW FIRE [5].
  7619	
  7620	    [1] Thus G.-V.; Tor. _Concicla Pisorum_.
  7621	
  7622	    [2] Sch. _feniculum_ instead of _fasciculum_.
  7623	
  7624	    [3] G.-V. _de suo sibi fricabis_; Tor. _seorsim f._
  7625	
  7626	    [4] G.-V. wine wanting in Tor.
  7627	
  7628	    [5] Brandt, referring to ℞ No. 154, suggests that the
  7629	    things crushed in a mortar be placed on top of the peas.
  7630	
  7631	
  7632	[197] PEAS IN THE POD À LA COMMODUS [1]
  7633	    _CONCHICLA COMMODIANA_
  7634	
  7635	MAKE PEAS COMMODIAN STYLE THUS: [2] COOK THE PEAS, WHEN SKIMMED, CRUSH
  7636	PEPPER, LOVAGE, DILL, SHALLOTS MOISTENED WITH BROTH; ADD WINE AND
  7637	BROTH TO TASTE: STIR IN A SAUCE PAN [with the peas] TO COMBINE; FOR
  7638	EACH SEXTARIUS OF PEAS BEAT 4 EGGS, AND COMBINE THEM WITH THE PEAS,
  7639	PLACE ON THE FIRE TO THICKEN [avoiding ebullition] AND SERVE.
  7640	
  7641	    [1] Hum. Named for Commodus, the emperor; List. for
  7642	    Commodus Antonius, son of the philosopher Marcus.
  7643	
  7644	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  7645	
  7646	
  7647	[198] ANOTHER STYLE
  7648	    _ALITER CONCHICLAM SIC FACIES_ [1]
  7649	
  7650	CUT [raw] CHICKEN INTO SMALL PIECES, ADD BROTH, OIL AND WINE, AND STEW
  7651	IT. CHOP ONIONS AND CORIANDER FINE AND ADD BRAINS [calf's or pork,
  7652	parboiled] THE SKIN AND NERVES REMOVED, TO THE CHICKEN. WHEN THIS IS
  7653	COOKED TAKE [the chicken] OUT AND BONE IT. THE PEAS COOK SEPARATELY,
  7654	WITHOUT SEASONING, ONLY USING CHOPPED ONIONS AND CORIANDER AND THE
  7655	BROTH OF THE CHICKEN; STRAIN [part of] THE PEAS AND ARRANGE THEM
  7656	ALTERNATELY [in a dish with the pieces of chicken, brains and the
  7657	unstrained peas] THEN CRUSH PEPPER AND CUMIN, MOISTENED WITH CHICKEN
  7658	BROTH. IN THE MORTAR BEAT 2 EGGS WITH BROTH TO TASTE, POUR THIS OVER
  7659	THE CHICKEN AND PEAS, FINISH ON A SLOW FIRE [1], DISH OUT ON A HEAP OF
  7660	PEAS, GARNISH WITH PINE NUTS AND SERVE.
  7661	
  7662	    [1] By congealing in a mould, which is unmoulded on a
  7663	    heap of peas. Danneil directs to stuff the whole chicken
  7664	    with the pea preparation, brains, etc., and to poach it
  7665	    in a square pan.
  7666	
  7667	
  7668	[199] STUFFED CHICKEN OR SUCKLING PIG
  7669	    _CONCHICLATUS PULLUS VEL PORCELLUS_ [1]
  7670	
  7671	BONE [either] CHICKEN [or suckling pig] FROM THE CHICKEN REMOVE THE
  7672	BREAST BONE AND THE [upper joint bones of the] LEGS; HOLD IT TOGETHER
  7673	BY MEANS OF WOODEN SKEWERS, AND MEANWHILE [2] PREPARE [the following
  7674	dressing in this manner]: ALTERNATE [inside of the chicken or pig]
  7675	PEAS WITH THE PODS [washed and cooked], BRAINS, LUCANIAN SAUSAGE, ETC.
  7676	NOW CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY AND GINGER, MOISTENED WITH BROTH,
  7677	RAISIN WINE AND WINE TO TASTE, MAKE IT BOIL, WHEN DONE, USE IT
  7678	MODERATELY FOR SEASONING AND ALTERNATELY WITH THE OTHER DRESSING; WRAP
  7679	[the chicken, or pig] IN CAUL, PLACE IT IN A BAKING DISH AND PUT IT IN
  7680	THE OVEN TO BE COOKED SLOWLY, AND SERVE.
  7681	
  7682	    [1] G.-V., Tor. _Concicla farsilis_.
  7683	
  7684	    [2] Tor. here splits the formula, using the above title.
  7685	
  7686	
  7687	
  7688	V
  7689	
  7690	GRUELS
  7691	  _TISANAM ET ALICAM_ [1]
  7692	
  7693	
  7694	[200] BARLEY BROTH
  7695	    _ALICAM VEL SUCCUM TISANÆ SIC FACIES_ [2]
  7696	
  7697	CRUSH WELL WASHED BARLEY, SOAKED THE DAY BEFORE, PLACE ON THE FIRE TO
  7698	BE COOKED. WHEN HOT ADD PLENTY OIL, A SMALL BUNCH OF DILL, DRY ONION,
  7699	SATURY AND COLOCASIUM, TO BE COOKED TOGETHER BECAUSE THIS GIVES A
  7700	BETTER JUICE; ADD GREEN CORIANDER AND A LITTLE SALT; BRING IT TO A
  7701	BOILING POINT. WHEN WELL HEATED TAKE OUT THE BUNCH [dill] AND TRANSFER
  7702	THE BARLEY INTO ANOTHER VESSEL TO AVOID BURNING ON THE BOTTOM OF THE
  7703	POT; THIN IT OUT [with water, broth, milk] AND STRAIN INTO A POT,
  7704	COVERING THE TIPS OF THE COLOCASIA [2]. NEXT CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, A
  7705	LITTLE DRY FLEA-BANE, CUMIN AND SYLPHIUM, STIR WELL, ADD VINEGAR,
  7706	REDUCED MUST AND BROTH; PUT IT BACK IN THE POT; THE REMAINING
  7707	COLOCASIA FINISH ON A GENTLE FIRE.
  7708	
  7709	    [1] A repetition of Book IV, Chap. IV, _Tisanam vel
  7710	    sucum_, our ℞ No. 172
  7711	
  7712	    [2] Tor. still has difficulties with the vegetable
  7713	    called by Lister _colocasium_. He reads here _colonium_
  7714	    and _colosium_. G.-V. _colœfium_. Cf. Note 1 to ℞
  7715	    No. 172 and Note to Nos. 74, 216, 244 and 322.
  7716	
  7717	
  7718	[201] ANOTHER GRUEL
  7719	    _ALITER TISANAM_ [1]
  7720	
  7721	SOAK CHICK-PEAS, LENTILS AND PEAS, CRUSH BARLEY AND COOK WITH THE
  7722	LEGUMES, WHEN WELL COOKED ADD PLENTY OF OIL. NOW CUT GREENS, LEEKS,
  7723	CORIANDER, DILL, FENNEL, BEETS, MALLOWS, CABBAGE STRUNKS, ALL SOFT AND
  7724	GREEN AND VERY FINELY CUT, AND PUT IN A POT. THE CABBAGE COOK
  7725	[separately; also] CRUSH FENNEL SEED, PLENTY OF IT, ORIGANY, SILPHIUM,
  7726	AND LOVAGE, AND WHEN GROUND, ADD BROTH TO TASTE, POUR THIS OVER THE
  7727	PORRIDGE, STIR, AND USE SOME FINELY CHOPPED CABBAGE STEMS TO SPRINKLE
  7728	ON TOP.
  7729	
  7730	    [1] A repetition of ℞ No. 173.
  7731	
  7732	
  7733	
  7734	VI
  7735	
  7736	GREEN BEANS
  7737	  _FABACIÆ VIRIDES ET BAIANÆ_ [1]
  7738	
  7739	
  7740	[202] GREEN BEANS
  7741	    _FABACIÆ VIRIDES_
  7742	
  7743	GREEN BEANS ARE COOKED IN BROTH, WITH OIL, GREEN CORIANDER, CUMIN AND
  7744	CHOPPED LEEKS, AND SERVED.
  7745	
  7746	    [1] Beans grown in Baiæ, also called _bajanas_ or
  7747	    _bacanas_; beans without skin or pods.
  7748	
  7749	
  7750	[203] BEANS SAUTÉ
  7751	    _ALITER: FABACIÆ FRICTÆ_
  7752	
  7753	FRIED BEANS ARE SERVED IN BROTH.
  7754	
  7755	
  7756	[204] MUSTARD BEANS
  7757	    _ALITER: FABACIÆ EX SINAPI_
  7758	
  7759	[The beans previously cooked are seasoned with] CRUSHED MUSTARD SEED,
  7760	HONEY, NUTS, RUE, CUMIN, AND SERVED WITH VINEGAR.
  7761	
  7762	
  7763	[205] BAIÆAN BEANS
  7764	    _BAIANAS_ [1]
  7765	
  7766	COOKED BEANS FROM BAIÆ ARE CUT FINE [and finished with] RUE, GREEN
  7767	CELERY, LEEKS, VINEGAR [2] A LITTLE MUST OR RAISIN WINE AND SERVED
  7768	[3].
  7769	
  7770	    [1] Named for Baiæ, a town of Campania, noted for its
  7771	    warm baths; a favorite resort of the Romans.
  7772	
  7773	    [2] Wanting in Tor.
  7774	
  7775	    [3] These apparently outlandish ways of cooking beans
  7776	    compel us to draw a modern parallel in a cookery book,
  7777	    specializing in Jewish dishes. To prove that Apicius is
  7778	    not dead "by a long shot," we shall quote from Wolf,
  7779	    Rebekka: Kochbuch für Israelitische Frauen, Frankfurt,
  7780	    1896, 11th edition. As a matter of fact, Rebekka Wolf is
  7781	    outdoing Apicius in strangeness--a case of _Apicium in
  7782	    ipso Apicio_, as Lister sarcastically remarks of
  7783	    Torinus.
  7784	
  7785	    Rebekka Wolf: ℞ No. 211--wash and boil the young
  7786	    beans in fat _bouillon_ (Apicius: _oleum et liquamen_)
  7787	    adding a handful of chopped pepperwort (A.: _piper,
  7788	    ligusticum_) and later chopped parsley (A.:
  7789	    _petroselinum_) some sugar (A.: _mel pavo_--little
  7790	    honey) and pepper. Beans later in the season are cooked
  7791	    with potatoes. The young beans are tied with flour
  7792	    dissolved in water, or with roux.
  7793	
  7794	    _Id. ibid._, ℞ No. 212, Beans Sweet-Sour. Boil in
  7795	    water, fat, salt, add vinegar, sugar or syrup, "English
  7796	    aromatics" and spices, lemon peel, and a little pepper;
  7797	    bind with roux.
  7798	
  7799	    _Id. ibid._, ℞ No. 213, Cut Pickled Beans
  7800	    (_Schneidebohnen_) prepare as ℞ No. 212, but if you
  7801	    would have them more delicious, take instead of the roux
  7802	    grated chocolate, sugar, cinnamon, lemon peel and lemon
  7803	    juice, and some claret. If not sour enough, add vinegar,
  7804	    but right here you must add more fat; you may lay on top
  7805	    of this dish a bouquet of sliced apples.
  7806	
  7807	    _Id. ibid._, ℞ No. 214, Beans and Pears. Take cut and
  7808	    pickled beans and prepare as above. To this add peeled
  7809	    fresh pears, cut into quarters; then sugar, lemon peel
  7810	    cut thin, cinnamon, "English" mixed spices, and at last
  7811	    the roux, thinned with broth. This dish must be sweet
  7812	    and very fat.
  7813	
  7814	    As for exotic combinations, Apicius surely survives
  7815	    here, is even surpassed by this Jewish cookery book
  7816	    where, no doubt, very ancient traditions have been
  7817	    stored away.
  7818	
  7819	
  7820	
  7821	VII
  7822	
  7823	
  7824	[206] THE HERB FENUGREEK
  7825	    _FŒNUM GRÆCUM_ [1]
  7826	
  7827	FENUGREEK [is prepared] IN BROTH, OIL AND WINE.
  7828	
  7829	    [1] Tor. or _fenum_; G.-V. _Fænum_.
  7830	
  7831	
  7832	
  7833	VIII
  7834	
  7835	
  7836	[207] GREEN STRING BEANS AND CHICK-PEAS
  7837	    _PHASEOLI _[1]_ VIRIDES ET CICER_
  7838	
  7839	ARE SERVED WITH SALT, CUMIN, OIL, AND A LITTLE PURE WINE.
  7840	
  7841	    [1] Tor. _Faseolus_, the bean with a long, sabre-like
  7842	    pod; a phasel, kidney bean, when ripened.
  7843	
  7844	
  7845	[208] ANOTHER WAY
  7846	    _ALITER FASEOLUS ET CICER_
  7847	
  7848	[Beans or chick-peas] ARE COOKED IN A WINE SAUCE AND SEASONED WITH
  7849	PEPPER [1].
  7850	
  7851	    [1] Dann. and Goll.: "roasted" beans.
  7852	
  7853	
  7854	[209] BOILED, SUMPTUOUSLY
  7855	    _ET ELIXATI, SUMPTO_ [1]
  7856	
  7857	AND COOK THE BEANS, IN A RICH MANNER, REMOVE THE SEEDS AND SERVE [as a
  7858	Salad [2]], WITH HARD EGGS, GREEN FENNEL, PEPPER, BROTH, A LITTLE
  7859	REDUCED WINE AND A LITTLE SALT, OR SERVE THEM IN SIMPLER WAYS, AS YOU
  7860	MAY SEE FIT.
  7861	
  7862	    [1] The original continues with the preceding formula.
  7863	
  7864	    [2] For a salad we would add finely chopped onion,
  7865	    pepper, and some lemon juice.
  7866	
  7867	    The purpose of removing the seeds is obscure. G.-V.
  7868	    reads _semine cum ovis_; Tac. _semie_; Hum. _s. cum
  7869	    lobis_. The passage may mean to sprinkle (sow) with hard
  7870	    boiled (and finely chopped) eggs, which is often done on
  7871	    a salad and other dishes.
  7872	
  7873	
  7874	END OF BOOK V
  7875	
  7876	_EXPLICIT APICII OSPRION LIBER QUINTUS_ [Tac.]
  7877	
  7878	
  7879	
  7880	
  7881	{Illustration: ADJUSTABLE TABLE
  7882	
  7883	Polychrome marble in bronze frame. Four elaborately designed bronze
  7884	legs, braced and hinged, so that the table may be raised or lowered.
  7885	The legs end in claw feet resting on a molded base. Above they are
  7886	encircled with leaves, from which emerge young satyrs, each holding a
  7887	rabbit under the left arm. The legs below the acanthus leaves are
  7888	ornamented with elaborate floral patterns, inlaid, with other inlaid
  7889	patterns on the connecting braces and around the frame of the marble
  7890	top. Bronze and marble tables that could be folded and taken down
  7891	after banquets were used by the Babylonians centuries before this
  7892	table was designed in Pompeii. Ntl. Mus., Naples, 72994; Field M.
  7893	24290.}
