  8833	    in the text of Book VII, namely Chap. X, Fresh Ham and
  8834	    Chap. XI, To Cook Salt Pork; these being inserted after
  8835	    Chap. IX, Pork Shoulder, making a total of XIX
  8836	    Chapters.]
  8837	
  8838	
  8839	
  8840	I
  8841	
  8842	SOW'S WOMB, CRACKLINGS, UDDER, TENDERLOIN, TAILS AND FEET
  8843	  _VULVÆ STERILES, CALLUM LUMBELLI COTICULÆ ET UNGELLÆ_
  8844	
  8845	
  8846	[251] SPAYED SOW'S WOMB [1]
  8847	    _VULVÆ STERILES_
  8848	
  8849	STERILE SOW'S WOMB (ALSO UDDER AND BELLY) IS PREPARED IN THIS MANNER:
  8850	TAKE [2] LASER FROM CYRENE OR PARTHIA, VINEGAR AND BROTH.
  8851	
  8852	    [1] The vulva of a sow was a favorite dish with the
  8853	    ancients, considered a great delicacy. Sows were
  8854	    slaughtered before they had a litter, or were spayed for
  8855	    the purpose of obtaining the sterile womb.
  8856	
  8857	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  8858	
  8859	
  8860	[252] ANOTHER WAY
  8861	    _ALITER_
  8862	
  8863	TAKE PEPPER, CELERY SEED, DRY MINT, LASER ROOT, HONEY, VINEGAR AND
  8864	BROTH.
  8865	
  8866	
  8867	[253] SPAYED SOW'S WOMB
  8868	    _VULVÆ STERILES_
  8869	
  8870	WITH PEPPER, BROTH AND PARTHIAN LASER.
  8871	
  8872	
  8873	[254] ANOTHER WAY
  8874	    _ALITER_
  8875	
  8876	WITH PEPPER, LOVAGE [1] AND BROTH AND A LITTLE CONDIMENT.
  8877	
  8878	    [1] Wanting in Lister.
  8879	
  8880	
  8881	[255] CRACKLINGS, PORK SKIN, TENDERLOIN, TAILS AND FEET
  8882	    _CALLUM, LUMBELLI _[1]_ COTICULÆ, UNGELLÆ_
  8883	
  8884	SERVE WITH PEPPER, BROTH AND LASER (WHICH THE GREEKS CALL "SILPHION")
  8885	[2].
  8886	
  8887	    [1] Tor., G.-V. _libelli_.
  8888	
  8889	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  8890	
  8891	
  8892	[256] GRILLED SOW'S WOMB
  8893	    _VULVAM UT TOSTAM FACIAS_
  8894	
  8895	ENVELOPE IN BRAN, AFTERWARDS [1] PUT IN BRINE AND THEN COOK IT.
  8896	
  8897	    [1] We would reverse the process: first pickle the
  8898	    vulva, then coat it with bran (or with bread crumbs) and
  8899	    fry.
  8900	
  8901	
  8902	
  8903	II
  8904	
  8905	
  8906	[257] SOW'S BELLY
  8907	    _SUMEN_
  8908	
  8909	SOW'S UDDER OR BELLY WITH THE PAPS ON IT IS PREPARED IN THIS MANNER
  8910	[1] THE BELLY BOIL, TIE IT TOGETHER WITH REEDS, SPRINKLE WITH SALT AND
  8911	PLACE IT IN THE OVEN, OR, START ROASTING ON THE GRIDIRON. CRUSH
  8912	PEPPER, LOVAGE, WITH BROTH, PURE WINE, ADDING RAISIN WINE TO TASTE,
  8913	THICKEN [the sauce] WITH ROUX AND POUR IT OVER THE ROAST.
  8914	
  8915	    [1] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  8916	
  8917	
  8918	[258] STUFFED SOW'S BELLY
  8919	    _SUMEN PLENUM_
  8920	
  8921	FULL [1] SOW'S BELLY IS STUFFED WITH [2] CRUSHED PEPPER, CARRAWAY,
  8922	SALT MUSSELS; SEW THE BELLY TIGHT AND ROAST. ENJOY THIS WITH A BRINE
  8923	SAUCE AND MUSTARD.
  8924	
  8925	    [1] Full grown, also stuffed with forcemeat.
  8926	
  8927	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  8928	
  8929	
  8930	
  8931	III
  8932	
  8933	FIG-FED PORK
  8934	  _FICATUM_ [1]
  8935	
  8936	    [1] Tor. _De Sycoto, id est, Ficato_.
  8937	
  8938	
  8939	[259] WINE SAUCE FOR FIG-FED PORK
  8940	    _IN FICATO ŒNOGARUM_ [1]
  8941	
  8942	FIG-FED PORK LIVER (THAT IS, LIVER CRAMMED WITH FIGS) IS PREPARED IN A
  8943	WINE SAUCE WITH [2] PEPPER, THYME, LOVAGE, BROTH, A LITTLE WINE AND
  8944	OIL [3].
  8945	
  8946	    [1] Tor. _Ficatum, iecur suillum_.
  8947	
  8948	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  8949	
  8950	    [3] Reinsenius, _ficatum_ [_or sicatum_] _projecore_.
  8951	
  8952	    According to the invention of Marcus Apicius, pigs were
  8953	    starved, and the hungry pigs were crammed with dry figs
  8954	    and then suddenly given all the mead they wanted to
  8955	    drink. The violent expansion of the figs in the
  8956	    stomachs, or the fermentation caused acute indigestion
  8957	    which killed the pigs. The livers were very much
  8958	    enlarged, similar to the cramming of geese for the sake
  8959	    of obtaining abnormally large livers. This latter method
  8960	    prevailed in the Strassburg District until recently when
  8961	    it was prohibited by law.
  8962	
  8963	
  8964	[260] ANOTHER WAY
  8965	    _ALITER_
  8966	
  8967	TRIM [the liver] MARINATE IN BROTH, WITH PEPPER, LOVAGE, TWO LAUREL
  8968	BERRIES, WRAP IN CAUL, GRILL ON THE GRIDIRON AND SERVE.
  8969	
  8970	    Goll. Stick figs into the liver by making apertures with
  8971	    the knife or with a needle.
  8972	
  8973	    It is by no means clear that the liver is meant.
  8974	
  8975	
  8976	
  8977	IV
  8978	
  8979	TID-BITS, CHOPS, CUTLETS
  8980	  _OFFELLÆ_ [1]
  8981	
  8982	
  8983	[261] OSTIAN [2] MEAT BALLS
  8984	    _OFFELLÆ OSTIENSES_
  8985	
  8986	PREPARE THE MEAT IN THIS MANNER [3] CLEAN THE MEAT [of bones, sinews,
  8987	etc.] SCRAPE IT AS THIN AS A SKIN [and shape it]. CRUSH PEPPER,
  8988	LOVAGE, CUMIN, CARRAWAY, SILPHIUM, ONE LAUREL BERRY, MOISTENED WITH
  8989	BROTH; IN A SQUARE DISH PLACE THE MEAT BALLS AND THE SPICES WHERE THEY
  8990	REMAIN IN PICKLING FOR TWO OR THREE DAYS, COVERED CROSSWISE WITH
  8991	TWIGS. THEN PLACE THEM IN THE OVEN [to be roasted], WHEN DONE TAKE THE
  8992	FINISHED MEAT BALLS OUT. CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, WITH THE BROTH, ADD A
  8993	LITTLE RAISIN WINE TO SWEETEN. COOK IT, THICKEN WITH ROUX, IMMERSE THE
  8994	BALLS IN THE SAUCE AND SERVE.
  8995	
  8996	    [1] G.-V. _Ofellæ_; apparently the old Roman "Hamburger
  8997	    Steak." The term covers different small meat pieces,
  8998	    chops, steaks, etc.
  8999	
  9000	    [2] Ostia, town at the mouth of the river Tiber, Rome's
  9001	    harbour.
  9002	
  9003	    [3] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  9004	
  9005	
  9006	[262] APICIAN ROULADES
  9007	    _OFFELLAS APICIANAS_
  9008	
  9009	BONE THE MEAT FOR THE [roulades--a pork loin, roll it, tie it] OVEN,
  9010	SHAPE ROUND, COVER WITH OR WRAP IN RUSHES. [Roast] WHEN DONE, RETIRE,
  9011	ALLOW TO DRIP AND DRY ON THE GRIDIRON BUT SO THAT THE MEAT DOES NOT
  9012	HARDEN. CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, RUSH [1], CUMIN, ADDING BROTH AND RAISIN
  9013	WINE TO TASTE. PLACE THE ROULADES WITH THIS SAUCE TOGETHER IN A SAUCE
  9014	PAN [finish by braising] WHEN DONE, RETIRE THE ROULADES AND DRY THEM.
  9015	SERVE WITHOUT THE GRAVY SPRINKLED WITH PEPPER. IF TOO FAT REMOVE THE
  9016	OUTER SKIN [2].
  9017	
  9018	    [1] _Cyperis_, _--os_, _--um_, _cypirus_, variants for a
  9019	    sort of rush; probably "Cyprian Grass."
  9020	
  9021	    [2] Dann. Dumplings; but this formula appears to deal
  9022	    with boneless pork chops, pork roulades or "_filets
  9023	    mignons_."
  9024	
  9025	
  9026	[263] PORK CUTLETS, HUNTER STYLE
  9027	    _OFFELLÆ APRUGNEO _[1]_ MORE_
  9028	
  9029	IN THE SAME MANNER YOU CAN MAKE TIDBITS OF SOW'S BELLY [2] PORK CHOPS
  9030	PREPARED IN A MANNER TO RESEMBLE WILD BOAR ARE [3] PICKLED IN OIL AND
  9031	BROTH AND PLACED IN SPICES. WHEN THE CUTLETS ARE DONE [marinated] THE
  9032	PICKLE IS PLACED ON THE FIRE AND BOILED; THE CUTLETS ARE PUT BACK INTO
  9033	THIS GRAVY AND ARE FINISHED WITH CRUSHED PEPPER, SPICES, HONEY, BROTH,
  9034	AND ROUX. WHEN THIS IS DONE SERVE THE CUTLETS WITHOUT THE BROTH AND
  9035	OIL, SPRINKLED WITH PEPPER.
  9036	
  9037	    [1] G.-V. _Aprugineo_; List. _Offellæ Aprugneæ_, i.e.
  9038	    wild boar chops or cutlets. Vat. Ms. _aprogneo more_;
  9039	    Tor. _pro genuino more_; Tac. _aprogeneo_--from
  9040	    _aprugnus_, wild boar.
  9041	
  9042	    Mutton today is prepared in a similar way, marinated
  9043	    with spices, etc., to resemble venison, and is called
  9044	    _Mouton à la Chasseur_, hunter style.
  9045	
  9046	    [2] This sentence, probably belonging to the preceding
  9047	    formula, carried over by Torinus.
  9048	
  9049	    [3] This sentence only in Torinus.
  9050	
  9051	
  9052	[264] TIDBITS ANOTHER WAY
  9053	    _ALITER OFFELLÆ_
  9054	
  9055	THE BALLS OR CUTLETS ARE [1] PROPERLY FRIED IN THE PAN, NEARLY DONE.
  9056	[Next prepare the following] ONE WHOLE [2] GLASS BROTH, A GLASS OF
  9057	WATER, A GLASS OF VINEGAR AND A GLASS OF OIL, PROPERLY MIXED; PUT THIS
  9058	IN AN EARTHEN BAKING DISH [immerse meat pieces] FINISH ON THE FIRE AND
  9059	SERVE.
  9060	
  9061	    [1] Tor.
  9062	
  9063	    [2] Tor. _Summi_; List. _sumis_, i.e. broth of the pork.
  9064	
  9065	
  9066	[265] TIDBITS IN ANOTHER STYLE
  9067	    _ALITER OFFELLAS_
  9068	
  9069	ALSO FRY THE CUTLETS THIS WAY: [1] IN A PAN WITH PLENTY OF WINE SAUCE,
  9070	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE. [ANOTHER WAY] [2] THE CUTLETS
  9071	PREVIOUSLY SALT AND PICKLED IN A BROTH OF CUMIN, ARE PROPERLY FRIED
  9072	[3].
  9073	
  9074	    [1] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  9075	
  9076	    [2] The texts have two formulæ; by the transposition of
  9077	    the two sentences the formula appears as a whole and
  9078	    one that is intelligible from a culinary point of view.
  9079	
  9080	    [3] The texts have: _in aqua recte friguntur_; the
  9081	    _acqua_ presumably belongs to the cumin pickle. To fry
  9082	    in water is not possible.
  9083	
  9084	
  9085	
  9086	V
  9087	
  9088	CHOICE ROASTS [1]
  9089	  _ASSATURÆ_
  9090	
  9091	
  9092	[266] ROASTING, PLAIN
  9093	    _ASSATURAM SIMPLICEM_ [2]
  9094	
  9095	SIMPLY PUT THE MEATS TO BE ROASTED IN THE OVEN, GENEROUSLY SPRINKLED
  9096	WITH SALT, AND SERVE [it glazed] WITH HONEY [3].
  9097	
  9098	    [1] Tor. _De assaturæ exquisitæ apparatu_.
  9099	
  9100	    [2] Brandt adds "plain."
  9101	
  9102	    [3] Corresponding to our present method of roasting;
  9103	    fresh and processed ham is glazed with sugar.
  9104	
  9105	    Roasting in the oven is not as desirable as roasting on
  9106	    the spit, universally practised during the middle ages.
  9107	    The spit seems to have been unknown to the Romans. It is
  9108	    seldom used today, although we have improved it by
  9109	    turning it with electrical machinery.
  9110	
  9111	
  9112	[267] ANOTHER STYLE FOR ROASTS
  9113	    _ALITER ASSATURAS_
  9114	
  9115	TAKE 6 SCRUPLES OF PARSLEY, OF LASER [1] JUST AS MANY, 6 OF GINGER, 5
  9116	LAUREL BERRIES, 6 SCRUPLES OF PRESERVED LASER ROOT, CYPRIAN RUSH 6, 6
  9117	OF ORIGANY, A LITTLE COSTMARY, 3 SCRUPLES OF CHAMOMILE [or pellitory],
  9118	6 SCRUPLES OF CELERY SEED, 12 SCRUPLES OF PEPPER, AND BROTH AND OIL AS
  9119	MUCH AS IT WILL TAKE UP [2].
  9120	
  9121	    [1] G.-V. _asareos_ [?] _Asarum_, the herb foalbit, wild
  9122	    spikenard.
  9123	
  9124	    [2] No directions are given for the making of this
  9125	    compound which are essential to insure success of this
  9126	    formula. Outwardly it resembles some of the commercial
  9127	    sauces made principally in England (Worcestershire,
  9128	    etc.), which are served with every roast.
  9129	
  9130	
  9131	[268] ANOTHER [Condiment for] ROAST
  9132	    _ALITER ASSATURAS_
  9133	
  9134	CRUSH DRY MYRTLE BERRIES WITH CUMIN AND PEPPER, ADDING HONEY ALSO
  9135	BROTH, REDUCED MUST AND OIL. HEAT AND BIND WITH ROUX. POUR THIS OVER
  9136	THE ROAST THAT IS MEDIUM DONE, WITH SALT; SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND
  9137	SERVE.
  9138	
  9139	
  9140	[269] ANOTHER ROAST [Sauce]
  9141	    _ALITER ASSATURAS_
  9142	
  9143	6 SCRUPLES PEPPER, 6 SCRUPLES LOVAGE, 6 SCRUPLES PARSLEY, 6 SCRUPLES
  9144	CELERY SEED, 6 SCRUPLES DILL, 6 SCRUPLES LASER ROOT, 6 SCRUPLES WILD
  9145	SPIKENARD [1], 6 SCRUPLES CYPRIAN RUSH, 6 SCRUPLES CARRAWAY, 6
  9146	SCRUPLES CUMIN, 6 SCRUPLES GINGER, A PINT OF BROTH AND A SPOONFUL OIL.
  9147	
  9148	    [1] Tor. _assareos_; cf. note 1 to ℞ No. 267.
  9149	
  9150	
  9151	[270] ROAST NECK [1]
  9152	    _ASSATURAS IN COLLARI_
  9153	
  9154	PUT IN A BRAISIÈRE [2] AND BOIL PEPPER, SPICES, HONEY, BROTH; AND HEAT
  9155	THIS WITH THE MEAT IN THE OVEN. THE NECK PIECE ITSELF, IF YOU LIKE, IS
  9156	ALSO ROASTED WITH SPICES AND THE HOT GRAVY IS SIMPLY POURED OVER AT
  9157	THE MOMENT OF SERVING [3].
  9158	
  9159	    [1] A piece of meat from the neck of a food animal,
  9160	    beef, veal, pork; a muscular hard piece, requiring much
  9161	    care to make it palatable, a "pot roast."
  9162	
  9163	    [2] A roasting pan especially adapted for braising tough
  9164	    meats, with closefitting cover to hold the vapors.
  9165	
  9166	    [3] Tor. combines this and the foregoing formula. G.-V.
  9167	    _siccum calidum_, for hot gravy. Perhaps a typographical
  9168	    error for _succum_.
  9169	
  9170	
  9171	
  9172	VI
  9173	
  9174	BOILED, STEWED MEATS, AND DAINTY FOOD
  9175	  _IN ELIXAM ET COPADIA_
  9176	
  9177	
  9178	[271] SAUCE FOR ALL BOILED DISHES
  9179	    _JUS IN ELIXAM OMNEM_
  9180	
  9181	PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, RUE, SILPHIUM, DRY ONION, WINE, REDUCED WINE,
  9182	HONEY, VINEGAR, A LITTLE OIL, BOILED DOWN, STRAINED THROUGH A CLOTH
  9183	AND POURED UNDER THE HOT COOKED MEATS [1].
  9184	
  9185	    [1] A very complicated sauce for boiled viands. Most of
  9186	    the ingredients are found in the Worcestershire Sauce.
  9187	
  9188	
  9189	[272] SAUCE FOR BOILED VIANDS
  9190	    _JUS IN ELIXAM_
  9191	
  9192	MAKE IT THUS: [Tor.] PEPPER, PARSLEY, BROTH, VINEGAR, FIG-DATES,
  9193	ONIONS, LITTLE OIL, POURED UNDER VERY HOT.
  9194	
  9195	
  9196	[273] ANOTHER
  9197	    _JUS IN ELIXAM_
  9198	
  9199	CRUSH PEPPER, DRY RUE, FENNEL SEED, ONION, FIGDATES, WITH BROTH AND
  9200	OIL.
  9201	
  9202	
  9203	[274] WHITE [bread] [1] SAUCE FOR BOILED VIANDS
  9204	    _JUS CANDIDUM IN ELIXAM_
  9205	
  9206	WHITE SAUCE FOR BOILED DISHES IS MADE THUS: [2] PEPPER, BROTH, WINE,
  9207	RUE, ONIONS, NUTS, A LITTLE SPICE, BREAD SOAKED TO THE SATURATION
  9208	POINT, OIL, WHICH IS COOKED AND SPREAD UNDER [the meat].
  9209	
  9210	    [1] Our present bread sauce, somewhat simpler, but
  9211	    essentially the same as the Apician sauce, is very
  9212	    popular with roast partridge, pheasant and other game in
  9213	    England.
  9214	
  9215	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
  9216	
  9217	
  9218	[275] ANOTHER WHITE SAUCE FOR BOILED VIANDS
  9219	    _ALITER JUS CANDIDUM IN ELIXAM_
  9220	
  9221	ANOTHER WHITE SAUCE FOR BOILED DISHES CONTAINS: [1] PEPPER, CARRAWAY,
  9222	LOVAGE, THYME, ORIGANY, SHALLOTS, DATES, HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH AND
  9223	OIL.
  9224	
  9225	
  9226	[276] WHITE SAUCE FOR DAINTY FOOD
  9227	    _IN COPADIIS _[1]_ JUS ALBUM_
  9228	
  9229	TAKE CUMIN, LOVAGE, RUE SEED, PLUMS FROM DAMASCUS [2] SOAK IN WINE,
  9230	ADD HONEY MEAD AND VINEGAR, THYME AND ORIGANY TO TASTE [3].
  9231	
  9232	    [1] Lacking definite description of the _copadia_ it is
  9233	    hard to differentiate between them and the
  9234	    _offelæ_.--_Cupedia_ (Plaut. and Goll.), nice dainty
  9235	    dishes, from _cupiditas_, appetite, desire for dainty
  9236	    fare. Hence _cupedinarius_ (Terent.) and _cupediarius_
  9237	    (Lamprid.) a seller or maker of dainties, a
  9238	    confectioner.
  9239	
  9240	    [2] _Damascena_; they correspond apparently to our
  9241	    present stewed (dried) prunes. It is inconceivable how
  9242	    this sauce can be white in color, but, as a condiment
  9243	    and if taken in small quantity, it has our full
  9244	    approval.
  9245	
  9246	    [3] G.-V. _agitabis_, i.e. stir the sauce with a whip of
  9247	    thyme and origany twigs. Cf. note to following.
  9248	
  9249	
  9250	[277] ANOTHER WHITE SAUCE FOR APPETIZERS
  9251	    _ALITER JUS CANDIDUM IN COPADIIS_
  9252	
  9253	IS MADE THUS [1] PEPPER, THYME, CUMIN, CELERY SEED, FENNEL, RUE, MINT
  9254	[2], MYRTLE BERRIES, RAISINS, RAISIN WINE, AND MEAD TO TASTE; STIR IT
  9255	WITH A TWIG OF SATURY [3].
  9256	
  9257	    [1] Tor.
  9258	
  9259	    [2] G.-V., rue wanting.
  9260	
  9261	    [3] An ingenious way to impart a very subtle flavor. The
  9262	    sporadic discoveries of such very subtle and refined
  9263	    methods (cf. notes to ℞ No. 15) should dispell once
  9264	    and for all time the old theories that the ancients were
  9265	    using spices to excess. They simply used a greater
  9266	    variety of flavors and aromas than we do today, but
  9267	    there is no proof that spices were used excessively. The
  9268	    great variety of flavors at the disposal of the ancients
  9269	    speaks well for the refinement of the olfactory sense
  9270	    and the desire to bring variety into their fare. Cf.
  9271	    ℞ Nos. 345, 369 and 385.
  9272	
  9273	
  9274	[278] SAUCE FOR TIDBITS
  9275	    _JUS IN COPADIIS_
  9276	
  9277	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY, MINT, LEAVES OF SPIKENARD (WHICH THE GREEKS
  9278	CALL "NARDOSACHIOM") [_sic!_] [1] YOLKS, HONEY, MEAD, VINEGAR, BROTH
  9279	AND OIL. STIR WELL WITH SATURY AND LEEKS [2] AND TIE WITH ROUX.
  9280	
  9281	    [1] Tor. [_sic!_] _spicam nardi_--sentence wanting in
  9282	    other texts. G.-V. _nardostachyum_, spikenard.
  9283	
  9284	    [2] A fagot of satury and leeks! Cf. notes to ℞ Nos.
  9285	    276 and 277.
  9286	
  9287	
  9288	[279] WHITE SAUCE FOR TIDBITS
  9289	    _JUS ALBUM IN COPADIIS_
  9290	
  9291	IS MADE THUS: [1] PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, CELERY SEED, THYME, NUTS,
  9292	WHICH SOAK AND CLEAN, HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH AND OIL TO BE ADDED [2].
  9293	
  9294	    [1, 2] First three and last three words in Tor.
  9295	
  9296	
  9297	[280] SAUCE FOR TIDBITS
  9298	    _JUS IN COPADIIS_
  9299	
  9300	PEPPER, CELERY SEED, CARRAWAY, SATURY, SAFFRON, SHALLOTS, TOASTED
  9301	ALMONDS, FIGDATES, BROTH, OIL AND A LITTLE MUSTARD; COLOR WITH REDUCED
  9302	MUST.
  9303	
  9304	
  9305	[281] SAUCE FOR TIDBITS
  9306	    _JUS IN COPADIIS_
  9307	
  9308	PEPPER, LOVAGE, PARSLEY, SHALLOTS, TOASTED ALMONDS, DATES, HONEY,
  9309	VINEGAR, BROTH, REDUCED MUST AND OIL.
  9310	
  9311	
  9312	[282] SAUCE FOR TIDBITS
  9313	    _JUS IN COPADIIS_
  9314	
  9315	CHOP HARD EGGS, PEPPER, CUMIN, PARSLEY, COOKED LEEKS, MYRTLE BERRIES,
  9316	SOMEWHAT MORE HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH AND OIL.
  9317	
  9318	
  9319	[283] RAW DILL SAUCE FOR BOILED DISH
  9320	    _IN ELIXAM ANETHATUM CRUDUM_
  9321	
  9322	PEPPER, DILL SEED, DRY MINT, LASER ROOT, POUR UNDER: VINEGAR, DATE
  9323	WINE, HONEY, BROTH, AND A LITTLE MUSTARD, REDUCED MUST AND OIL TO
  9324	TASTE; AND SERVE IT WITH ROAST PORK SHOULDER.
  9325	
  9326	
  9327	[284] BRINY SAUCE FOR BOILED DISH
  9328	    _JUS IN ELIXAM ALLECATUM_
  9329	
  9330	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY, CELERY SEED, THYME, SHALLOTS, DATES, FISH
  9331	BRINE [1] STRAINED HONEY, AND WINE TO TASTE; SPRINKLE WITH CHOPPED
  9332	GREEN CELERY AND OIL AND SERVE.
  9333	
  9334	    [1] G.-V. _allecem_; Tor. _Halecem_.
  9335	
  9336	
  9337	
  9338	VII
  9339	
  9340	PAUNCH
  9341	  _VENTRICULA_
  9342	
  9343	
  9344	[285] PIG'S PAUNCH
  9345	    _VENTREM PORCINUM_
  9346	
  9347	CLEAN THE PAUNCH OF A SUCKLING PIG WELL WITH SALT AND VINEGAR AND
  9348	PRESENTLY WASH WITH WATER. THEN FILL IT WITH THE FOLLOWING DRESSING:
  9349	PIECES OF PORK POUNDED IN THE MORTAR, THREE BRAINS--THE NERVES
  9350	REMOVED--MIX WITH RAW EGGS, ADD NUTS, WHOLE PEPPER, AND SAUCE TO
  9351	TASTE. CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, SILPHIUM, ANISE, GINGER, A LITTLE RUE;
  9352	FILL THE PAUNCH WITH IT, NOT TOO MUCH, THOUGH, LEAVING PLENTY OF ROOM
  9353	FOR EXPANSION LEST IT BURSTS WHILE BEING COOKED. PUT IT IN A POT WITH
  9354	BOILING WATER, RETIRE AND PRICK WITH A NEEDLE SO THAT IT DOES NOT
  9355	BURST. WHEN HALF DONE, TAKE IT OUT AND HANG IT INTO THE SMOKE TO TAKE
  9356	ON COLOR; NOW BOIL IT OVER AGAIN AND FINISH IT LEISURELY. NEXT TAKE
  9357	THE BROTH, SOME PURE WINE AND A LITTLE OIL, OPEN THE PAUNCH WITH A
  9358	SMALL KNIFE. SPRINKLE WITH THE BROTH AND LOVAGE; PLACE THE PIG NEAR
  9359	THE FIRE TO HEAT IT, TURN IT AROUND IN BRAN [or bread crumbs] IMMERSE
  9360	IN [sprinkle with] BRINE AND FINISH [the outer crust to a golden
  9361	brown] [1].
  9362	
  9363	    [1] The good old English way of finishing a roast joint
  9364	    called dredging.
  9365	
  9366	    Lister has this formula divided into two; Danneil and Schuch
  9367	    make three different formulas out of it.
  9368	
  9369	
  9370	
  9371	VIII
  9372	
  9373	LOINS AND KIDNEYS
  9374	  _LUMBI ET RENES_
  9375	
  9376	
  9377	[286] ROAST LOINS MADE THUS
  9378	    _LUMBULI ASSI ITA FIUNT_
  9379	
  9380	SPLIT THEM INTO TWO PARTS SO THAT THEY ARE SPREAD OUT [1] SPRINKLE THE
  9381	OPENING WITH CRUSHED PEPPER AND [ditto] NUTS, FINELY CHOPPED CORIANDER
  9382	AND CRUSHED FENNEL SEED. THE TENDERLOINS ARE THEN ROLLED UP TO BE
  9383	ROASTED; TIE TOGETHER, WRAP IN CAUL, PARBOIL IN OIL [2] AND BROTH, AND
  9384	THEN ROAST IN THE OVEN OR BROIL ON THE GRIDIRON.
  9385	
  9386	    [1] "Frenched," the meat here being pork tenderloin.
  9387	
  9388	    [2] G.-V. best broth and a little oil, which is more
  9389	    acceptable.
  9390	
  9391	
  9392	
  9393	IX
  9394	
  9395	HAM
  9396	  _PERNA_
  9397	
  9398	
  9399	[287] [Baked Picnic] HAM [Pork Shoulder, fresh or cured]
  9400	    _PERNAM_
  9401	
  9402	THE HAM SHOULD BE BRAISED WITH A GOOD NUMBER OF FIGS AND SOME THREE
  9403	LAUREL LEAVES; THE SKIN IS THEN PULLED OFF AND CUT INTO SQUARE PIECES;
  9404	THESE ARE MACERATED WITH HONEY. THEREUPON MAKE DOUGH CRUMBS OF FLOUR
  9405	AND OIL [1] LAY THE DOUGH OVER OR AROUND THE HAM, STUD THE TOP WITH
  9406	THE PIECES OF THE SKIN SO THAT THEY WILL BE BAKED WITH THE DOUGH [bake
  9407	slowly] AND WHEN DONE, RETIRE FROM THE OVEN AND SERVE [2].
  9408	
  9409	    [1] Ordinary pie or pastry dough, or perhaps a
  9410	    preparation similar to streusel, unsweetened.
  9411	
  9412	    [2] Experimenting with this formula, we have adhered to
  9413	    the instructions as closely as possible, using regular
  9414	    pie dough to envelop the parboiled meat. The figs were
  9415	    retired from the sauce pan long before the meat was done
  9416	    and they were served around the ham as a garnish. As a
  9417	    consequence we partook of a grand dish that no inmate of
  9418	    Olympus would have sneezed at.
  9419	
  9420	    In Pompeii an inn-keeper had written the following on
  9421	    the wall of his establishment: _Ubi perna cocta est si
  9422	    convivæ apponitur non gustat pernam linguit ollam aut
  9423	    caccabum._
  9424	
  9425	    When we first beheld this message we took the inn-keeper
  9426	    for a humorist and clever advertiser; but now we are
  9427	    convinced that he was in earnest when he said that his
  9428	    guests would lick the sauce pan in which his hams were
  9429	    cooked.
  9430	
  9431	
  9432	[288] TO COOK PORK SHOULDER
  9433	    _PERNÆ _[1]_ COCTURAM_
  9434	
  9435	HAM SIMPLY COOKED IN WATER WITH FIGS IS USUALLY DRESSED ON A PLATTER
  9436	[baking pan] SPRINKLED WITH CRUMBS AND REDUCED WINE, OR, STILL BETTER,
  9437	WITH SPICED WINE [and is glazed under the open flame, or with a shovel
  9438	containing red-hot embers].
  9439	
  9440	    [1] _Perna_ is usually applied to shoulder of pork,
  9441	    fresh, also cured.
  9442	
  9443	    _Coxa_ is the hind leg, or haunch of pork, or fresh ham.
  9444	    Cf. note 1 to ℞ No. 289.
  9445	
  9446	
  9447	
  9448	X
  9449	
  9450	
  9451	[289] FRESH HAM
  9452	    _MUSTEIS _[1]_ PETASONEM_ [2]
  9453	
  9454	A FRESH HAM IS COOKED WITH 2 POUNDS OF BARLEY AND 25 FIGS. WHEN DONE
  9455	SKIN, GLAZE THE SURFACE WITH A FIRE SHOVEL FULL OF GLOWING COALS,
  9456	SPREAD HONEY OVER IT, OR, WHAT'S BETTER: PUT IT IN THE OVEN COVERED
  9457	WITH HONEY. WHEN IT HAS A NICE COLOR, PUT IN A SAUCE PAN RAISIN WINE,
  9458	PEPPER, A BUNCH OF RUE AND PURE WINE TO TASTE. WHEN THIS [sauce] IS
  9459	DONE, POUR HALF OF IT OVER THE HAM AND IN THE OTHER HALF SOAK
  9460	SPECIALLY MADE GINGER BREAD [3] THE REMNANT OF THE SAUCE AFTER MOST OF
  9461	IT IS THOROUGHLY SOAKED INTO THE BREAD, ADD TO THE HAM [4].
  9462	
  9463	    [1] _Musteus_, fresh, young, new; _vinum mustum_, new
  9464	    wine, must. Properly perhaps, _Petasonem ex mustaceis_;
  9465	    cf. note 3.
  9466	
  9467	    [2] Hum. _verum petaso coxa cum crure_ [shank] _esse
  9468	    dicitur...._
  9469	
  9470	    Plainly, we are dealing here with fresh, uncured ham.
  9471	
  9472	    [3] A certain biscuit or cake made of must, spices and
  9473	    pepper, perhaps baked on laurel leaves. _Mustaceus_ was
  9474	    a kind of cake, the flour of which had been kneaded with
  9475	    must, cheese, anise, etc., the cake was baked upon
  9476	    laurel leaves.
  9477	
  9478	    [4] Tor. continues without interruption. He has the
  9479	    three foregoing formulæ thrown into one.
  9480	
  9481	
  9482	
  9483	XI
  9484	
  9485	
  9486	[290] BACON, SALT PORK
  9487	    _LARIDI _[1]_ COCTURA_
  9488	
  9489	COVER WITH WATER AND COOK WITH PLENTY OF DILL; SPRINKLE WITH A LITTLE
  9490	OIL AND A TRIFLE OF SALT.
  9491	
  9492	    [1] Lister, at this point, has forgotten his explanation
  9493	    of _laridum_, and now accepts the word in its proper
  9494	    sense. This rather belated correction by Lister
  9495	    confirms the correctness of our own earlier
  9496	    observations. Cf. note to ℞ Nos. 41 and 148.
  9497	
  9498	
  9499	
  9500	XII
  9501	
  9502	LIVERS AND LUNGS
  9503	  _JECINORA SIVE PULMONES_
  9504	
  9505	
  9506	[291] SHEEP LIVER
  9507	    _JECINORA HŒDINA VEL AGNINA_ [1]
  9508	
  9509	COOK THUS: MAKE A MIXTURE OF WATER, MEAD, EGGS AND MILK IN WHICH
  9510	THOROUGHLY SOAK THE SLICED LIVER. STEW THE LIVER IN WINE SAUCE,
  9511	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  9512	
  9513	    [1] G.-V. _Iecinera hœdina_.
  9514	
  9515	
  9516	[292] ANOTHER WAY TO COOK LUNG
  9517	    _ALITER IN PULMONIBUS_
  9518	
  9519	LIVER AND LUNG ARE ALSO COOKED THIS WAY: [1] SOAK WELL IN MILK, STRAIN
  9520	IT OFF IF OFFENSIVE IN TASTE [2] BREAK 2 EGGS AND ADD A LITTLE SALT,
  9521	MIX IN A SPOONFUL HONEY AND FILL THE LUNG WITH IT, BOIL AND SLICE [3].
  9522	
  9523	    [1] Tor.
  9524	
  9525	    [2] Lungs of slaughtered animals are little used
  9526	    nowadays. The soaking of livers in milk is quite common;
  9527	    it removes the offensive taste of the gall.
  9528	
  9529	    [3] G.-V. continue without interruption.
  9530	
  9531	
  9532	[293] A HASH OF LIVER
  9533	    _ALITER_
  9534	
  9535	CRUSH PEPPER, MOISTEN WITH BROTH, RAISIN WINE, PURE OIL, CHOP THE
  9536	LIGHTS [1] FINE AND ADD WINE SAUCE [2].
  9537	
  9538	    [1] Edible intestines, livers, lung, kidney, etc., are
  9539	    thus named.
  9540	
  9541	    [2] List., Tor., G.-V. have both recipes in one. Dann.
  9542	    is in doubt whether to separate them or not.
  9543	
  9544	
  9545	
  9546	XIII
  9547	
  9548	HOME-MADE SWEET DISHES AND HONEY SWEET-MEATS
  9549	  _DULCIA DOMESTICA _[1]_ ET MELCÆ_
  9550	
  9551	
  9552	[294] HOME-MADE SWEETS
  9553	    _DULCIA DOMESTICA_
  9554	
  9555	LITTLE HOME CONFECTIONS (WHICH ARE CALLED DULCIARIA) ARE MADE THUS:
  9556	[2] LITTLE PALMS OR (AS THEY ARE ORDINARILY CALLED) [3] DATES ARE
  9557	STUFFED--AFTER THE SEEDS HAVE BEEN REMOVED--WITH A NUT OR WITH NUTS
  9558	AND GROUND PEPPER, SPRINKLED WITH SALT ON THE OUTSIDE AND ARE CANDIED
  9559	IN HONEY AND SERVED [4].
  9560	
  9561	    [1] _Dulcia_, sweetmeats, cakes; hence _dulciarius_, a
  9562	    pastry cook or confectioner.
  9563	
  9564	    The fact that here attention is drawn to home-made sweet
  9565	    dishes may clear up the absence of regular baking and
  9566	    dessert formulæ in Apicius. The trade of the
  9567	    _dulciarius_ was so highly developed at that time that
  9568	    the professional bakers and confectioners supplied the
  9569	    entire home market with their wares, making it
  9570	    convenient and unprofitable for the domestic cook to
  9571	    compete with their organized business, a condition which
  9572	    largely exists in our modern highly civilized centers of
  9573	    population today. Cf. "Cooks."
  9574	
  9575	    [2 + 3] Tor.
  9576	
  9577	    [4] Still being done today in the same manner.
  9578	
  9579	
  9580	[295] ANOTHER SWEETMEAT
  9581	    _ALITER DULCIA_
  9582	
  9583	GRATE [scrape, peel] SOME VERY BEST FRESH APHROS [1] AND IMMERSE IN
  9584	MILK. WHEN SATURATED PLACE IN THE OVEN TO HEAT BUT NOT TO DRY OUT;
  9585	WHEN THOROUGHLY HOT RETIRE FROM OVEN, POUR OVER SOME HONEY, STIPPLE
  9586	[the fruit] SO THAT THE HONEY MAY PENETRATE, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER [2]
  9587	AND SERVE.
  9588	
  9589	    [1] Tor., Tac., Lan. _musteos aphros_; Vat. Ms., G.-V.
  9590	    _afros_; List. _apios_, i.e. celery, which is farthest
  9591	    from the mark. Goll. interprets this a "cider apple,"
  9592	    reminiscent, probably, of _musteos_, which is fresh,
  9593	    new, young, and which has here nothing to do with cider.
  9594	
  9595	    _Aphros_ is not identified. Perhaps the term stood for
  9596	    Apricots (Old English: Aphricocks) or some other African
  9597	    fruit or plant; Lister's celery is to be rejected on
  9598	    gastronomical grounds.
  9599	
  9600	    The above treatment would correspond to that which is
  9601	    given apricots and peaches today. They are peeled,
  9602	    immersed in cream and sweetened with sugar. Apicius'
  9603	    heating of the fruit in milk is new to us; it sounds
  9604	    good, for it has a tendency to parboil any hard fruit,
  9605	    make it more digestible and reduce the fluid to a creamy
  9606	    consistency.
  9607	
  9608	    [2] The "pepper" again, as pointed out in several other
  9609	    places, here is some spice of agreeable taste as are
  9610	    used in desserts today.
  9611	
  9612	
  9613	[296] ANOTHER SWEET DISH
  9614	    _ALITER DULCIA_
  9615	
  9616	BREAK [slice] FINE WHITE BREAD, CRUST REMOVED, INTO RATHER LARGE
  9617	PIECES WHICH SOAK IN MILK [and beaten eggs] FRY IN OIL, COVER WITH
  9618	HONEY AND SERVE [1].
  9619	
  9620	    [1] "French" Toast, indeed!--_Sapienti sat!_
  9621	
  9622	
  9623	[297] ANOTHER SWEET
  9624	    _ALITER DULCIA_
  9625	
  9626	IN A CHAFING-DISH PUT [1] HONEY, PURE WINE, RAISIN WINE, RUE, PINE
  9627	NUTS, NUTS, COOKED SPELT, ADD CRUSHED AND TOASTED HAZELNUTS [2] AND
  9628	SERVE.
  9629	
  9630	    [1] G.-V. _Piperato mittis_. _Piperatum_ is a dish
  9631	    prepared with pepper, any spicy dish; the term may here
  9632	    be applied to the bowl in which the porridge is served.
  9633	    Tac. _Dulcia piperata mittis_.
  9634	
  9635	    [2] Dann. Almonds.
  9636	
  9637	
  9638	[298] ANOTHER SWEET
  9639	    _ALITER DULCIA_
  9640	
  9641	CRUSH PEPPER, NUTS, HONEY, RUE, AND RAISIN WINE WITH MILK, AND COOK
  9642	THE MIXTURE [1] WITH A FEW EGGS WELL WORKED IN, COVER WITH HONEY,
  9643	SPRINKLE WITH [crushed nuts, etc.] AND SERVE.
  9644	
  9645	    [1] _Tractam_, probably with a starch added, or else it
  9646	    is a nut custard, practically a repetition of ℞ Nos.
  9647	    129 and 143.
  9648	
  9649	
  9650	[299] ANOTHER SWEET
  9651	    _ALITER DULCIA_
  9652	
  9653	TAKE A PREPARATION SIMILAR [1] [to the above] AND IN THE HOT WATER
  9654	[bath or double boiler] MAKE A VERY HARD PORRIDGE OF IT. THEREUPON
  9655	SPREAD IT OUT ON A PAN AND WHEN COOL CUT IT INTO HANDY PIECES LIKE
  9656	SMALL COOKIES. FRY THESE IN THE BEST OIL, TAKE THEM OUT, DIP INTO
  9657	[hot] HONEY, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER [2] AND SERVE.
  9658	
  9659	    [1] This confirms the assumption that some flour or meal
  9660	    is used in ℞ No. 298 also without which this present
  9661	    preparation would not "stand up."
  9662	
  9663	    [2] It is freely admitted that the word "pepper" not
  9664	    always stands for the spice that we know by this name.
  9665	    Cf. note 2 to ℞ No. 295 _et al._
  9666	
  9667	
  9668	[300] A STILL BETTER WAY
  9669	    _ALITER_
  9670	
  9671	IS TO PREPARE THIS WITH MILK INSTEAD OF WATER.
  9672	
  9673	
  9674	[301] CUSTARD
  9675	    _TYROPATINAM_
  9676	
  9677	ESTIMATE THE AMOUNT OF MILK NECESSARY FOR THIS DISH AND SWEETEN IT
  9678	WITH HONEY TO TASTE; TO A PINT [1] OF FLUID TAKE 5 EGGS; FOR HALF A
  9679	PINT [2] DISSOLVE 3 EGGS IN MILK AND BEAT WELL TO INCORPORATE
  9680	THOROUGHLY, STRAIN THROUGH A COLANDER INTO AN EARTHEN DISH AND COOK ON
  9681	A SLOW FIRE [in hot water bath in oven]. WHEN CONGEALED SPRINKLE WITH
  9682	PEPPER AND SERVE [3].
  9683	
  9684	    [1] _Sextarium._
  9685	
  9686	    [2] _ad heminam._
  9687	
  9688	    [3] Dann. calls this a cheese cake, which is a
  9689	    far-fetched conclusion, although standard dictionaries
  9690	    say that the _tyropatina_ is a kind of cheese cake. It
  9691	    must be borne in mind, however, that the ancient
  9692	    definition of "custard" is "egg cheese," probably
  9693	    because of the similarity in appearance and texture.
  9694	
  9695	    Cf. ℞ Nos. 129 and 143.
  9696	
  9697	
  9698	[302] OMELETTE SOUFFLÉE [1]
  9699	    _OVA SPHONGIA EX LACTE_
  9700	
  9701	FOUR EGGS IN HALF A PINT OF MILK AND AN OUNCE OF OIL WELL BEATEN, TO
  9702	MAKE A FLUFFY MIXTURE; IN A PAN PUT A LITTLE OIL, AND CAREFULLY ADD
  9703	THE EGG PREPARATION, WITHOUT LETTING IT BOIL [2] HOWEVER. [Place it in
  9704	the oven to let it rise] AND WHEN ONE SIDE IS DONE, TURN IT OUT INTO A
  9705	SERVICE PLATTER [fold it] POUR OVER HONEY, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER [3]
  9706	AND SERVE [4].
  9707	
  9708	    [1] Dann. misled by the title, interprets this dish as
  9709	    "Floating Island"; he, the chef, has completely
  9710	    misunderstood the ancient formula.
  9711	
  9712	    [2] Tor. _sinas bullire_--which is correct. List.
  9713	    _facies ut bulliat_--which is monstrous.
  9714	
  9715	    [3] G.-V.
  9716	
  9717	    [4] Tor. continues without interruption.
  9718	
  9719	
  9720	[303] CHEESE AND HONEY
  9721	    _MEL ET CASEUM_ [1]
  9722	
  9723	PREPARE [cottage] CHEESE EITHER WITH HONEY AND BROTH [brine] OR WITH
  9724	SALT, OIL AND [chopped] CORIANDER [2].
  9725	
  9726	    [1] G.-V. _Melca ... stum_; List. _mel castum_, refined
  9727	    honey; Tac. _Mel caseum_; Tor. _mel, caseum_. Cf. ℞
  9728	    No. 294.
  9729	
  9730	    [2] To season cottage (fresh curd) cheese today we use
  9731	    salt, pepper, cream, carraway or chopped chives;
  9732	    sometimes a little sugar.
  9733	
  9734	
  9735	
  9736	XIV
  9737	
  9738	
  9739	[304] BULBS [1]
  9740	    _BULBOS_
  9741	
  9742	SERVE WITH OIL, BROTH AND VINEGAR, WITH A LITTLE CUMIN SPRINKLED OVER.
  9743	
  9744	    [1] Onions, roots of tulips, narcissus. Served raw
  9745	    sliced, with the above dressing, or cooked. Cf. notes to
  9746	    ℞ No. 307.
  9747	
  9748	
  9749	[305] ANOTHER WAY
  9750	    _ALITER_
  9751	
  9752	SOAK [1] THE BULBS AND PARBOIL THEM IN WATER; THEREUPON FRY THEM IN
  9753	OIL. THE DRESSING MAKE THUS: TAKE THYME, FLEA-BANE, PEPPER, ORIGANY,
  9754	HONEY, VINEGAR, REDUCED WINE, DATE WINE, IF YOU LIKE [2] BROTH AND A
  9755	LITTLE OIL. SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
  9756	
  9757	    [1] Tor. _tundes_; probably a typographical error, as
  9758	    this should read _fundis_, i.e. _infundis_. Wanting in
  9759	    the other texts.
  9760	
  9761	
  9762	[306] ANOTHER WAY
  9763	    _ALITER_
  9764	
  9765	COOK THE BULBS INTO A THICK PURÉE [1] AND SEASON WITH THYME, ORIGANY,
  9766	HONEY, VINEGAR, REDUCED WINE, DATE WINE, BROTH AND A LITTLE OIL.
  9767	
  9768	    [1] _Tundes_, i.e. mash. Practically a correction of
  9769	    ℞ No. 305, repeated by Tor.
  9770	
  9771	
  9772	[307] VARRO SAYS OF BULBS [1]
  9773	    _VARRO SI QUID DE BULBIS DIXIT_
  9774	
  9775	COOKED IN WATER THEY ARE CONDUCIVE TO LOVE [2] AND ARE THEREFORE ALSO
  9776	SERVED AT WEDDING FEASTS, BUT ALSO SEASONED WITH PIGNOLIA NUT OR WITH
  9777	THE JUICE OF COLEWORT, OR MUSTARD, AND PEPPER.
  9778	
  9779	    [1] The first instance in Apicius where the monotony and
  9780	    business-like recital of recipes is broken by some
  9781	    interesting quotation or remark.
  9782	
  9783	    Brandt is of the opinion that this remark was added by a
  9784	    posterior reader.
  9785	
  9786	    [2] The texts: _qui Veneris ostium quærunt_--"seek the
  9787	    mouth of Venus."
  9788	
  9789	    This favorite superstition of the ancients leads many
  9790	    writers, as might be expected, into fanciful
  9791	    speculations. Humelberg, quoting Martial, says: _Veneram
  9792	    mirè stimulant, unde et salaces à Martiali vocantur._ 1.
  9793	    XIII, Ep. 34:
  9794	
  9795	    _Cum sit anus conjunx, cum sint tibi mortua membra
  9796	    Nil aliud, bulbis quam satur esse potes._
  9797	
  9798	    We fail to find this quotation from Varro in his works,
  9799	    M. Teren. Varronis De Re Rustica, Lugduni, 1541, but we
  9800	    read in Columella and Pliny that the buds or shoots of
  9801	    reeds were called by some "bulbs," by others "eyes,"
  9802	    and, remembering that these shoots make very desirable
  9803	    vegetables when properly cooked, we feel inclined to
  9804	    include these among the term "bulbs." Platina also adds
  9805	    the squill or sea onion to this category. Nonnus, p. 84,
  9806	    Diæteticon, Antwerp, 1645, quotes Columella as saying:
  9807	    _Jam Magaris veniant genitalia semina Bulbi._
  9808	
  9809	
  9810	[308] FRIED BULBS
  9811	    _BULBOS FRICTOS_
  9812	
  9813	ARE SERVED WITH WINE SAUCE [Oenogarum].
  9814	
  9815	
  9816	
  9817	XV
  9818	
  9819	MUSHROOMS OR MORELS [1]
  9820	  _FUNGI FARNEI VEL BOLETI_
  9821	
  9822	
  9823	[309] MORELS [2]
  9824	    _FUNGI FARNEI_
  9825	
  9826	MORELS ARE COOKED QUICKLY IN GARUM AND PEPPER, TAKEN OUT, ALLOWED TO
  9827	DRIP; ALSO BROTH WITH CRUSHED PEPPER MAY BE USED [to cook the
  9828	mushrooms in].
  9829	
  9830	    [1] It is noteworthy that the term _spongiolus_ which
  9831	    creates so much misunderstanding in Book II is not used
  9832	    here in connection with mushrooms. Cf. ℞ No. 115.
  9833	
  9834	    [2] "Ashtree-Mushrooms."
  9835	
  9836	
  9837	[310] FOR MORELS
  9838	    _IN FUNGIS FARNEIS_
  9839	
  9840	PEPPER, REDUCED WINE, VINEGAR AND OIL.
  9841	
  9842	
  9843	[311] ANOTHER WAY OF COOKING MORELS
  9844	    _ALITER FUNGI FARNEI_
  9845	
  9846	IN SALT WATER, WITH OIL, PURE WINE, AND SERVE WITH CHOPPED CORIANDER.
  9847	
  9848	
  9849	[312] MUSHROOMS
  9850	    _BOLETOS FUNGOS_
  9851	
  9852	FRESH MUSHROOMS ARE STEWED [1] IN REDUCED WINE WITH A BUNCH OF GREEN
  9853	CORIANDER, WHICH REMOVE BEFORE SERVING.
  9854	
  9855	    [1] Tor.
  9856	
  9857	
  9858	[313] ANOTHER STYLE OF MUSHROOMS
  9859	    _BOLETOS ALITER_ [1]
  9860	
  9861	MUSHROOM STEMS [or buds, very small mushrooms] ARE COOKED IN BROTH.
  9862	SERVE SPRINKLED WITH SALT.
  9863	
  9864	    [1] Tor. _Boletorum coliculi_; G.-V. _calyculos_.
  9865	
  9866	
  9867	[314] ANOTHER WAY OF COOKING MUSHROOMS
  9868	    _BOLETOS ALITER_
  9869	
  9870	SLICE THE MUSHROOM STEMS [1] [stew them as directed above] AND FINISH
  9871	BY COVERING THEM WITH EGGS [2] ADDING PEPPER, LOVAGE, A LITTLE HONEY,
  9872	BROTH AND OIL TO TASTE.
  9873	
  9874	    [1] _Thyrsos._
  9875	
  9876	    [2] G.-V. _in patellam novam_; nothing said about eggs.
  9877	    Tor. _concisos in patellam; ovaque perfundes_; Tac. _ova
  9878	    perfundis_.
  9879	
  9880	    A mushroom omelette.
  9881	
  9882	
  9883	
  9884	XVI
  9885	
  9886	
  9887	[315] TRUFFLES
  9888	    _TUBERA_
  9889	
  9890	SCRAPE [brush] THE TRUFFLES, PARBOIL, SPRINKLE WITH SALT, PUT SEVERAL
  9891	OF THEM ON A SKEWER, HALF FRY THEM; THEN PLACE THEM IN A SAUCE PAN
  9892	WITH OIL, BROTH, REDUCED WINE, WINE, PEPPER, AND HONEY. WHEN DONE
  9893	[retire the truffles] BIND [the liquor] WITH ROUX, DECORATE THE
  9894	TRUFFLES NICELY AND SERVE [1].
  9895	
  9896	    [1] This formula clearly shows up the master Apicius.
  9897	    Truffles, among all earthly things, are the most
  9898	    delicate and most subtle in flavor. Only a master cook
  9899	    is privileged to handle them and to do them justice.
  9900	
  9901	    Today, whenever we are fortunate enough to obtain the
  9902	    best fresh truffles, we are pursuing almost the same
  9903	    methods of preparation as described by Apicius.
  9904	
  9905	    The commercially canned truffles bear not even a
  9906	    resemblance of their former selves.
  9907	
  9908	
  9909	[316] ANOTHER WAY TO PREPARE TRUFFLES
  9910	    _ALITER TUBERA_
  9911	
  9912	[Par]BOIL THE TRUFFLES, SPRINKLE WITH SALT AND FASTEN THEM ON SKEWERS,
  9913	HALF FRY THEM AND THEN PLACE THEM IN A SAUCE PAN WITH BROTH, VIRGIN
  9914	OIL, REDUCED WINE, A LITTLE PURE WINE [1] CRUSHED PEPPER AND A LITTLE
  9915	HONEY; ALLOW THEM TO FINISH [gently and well covered] WHEN DONE, BIND
  9916	THE LIQUOR WITH ROUX, PRICK THE TRUFFLES SO THEY MAY BECOME SATURATED
  9917	WITH THE JUICE, DRESS THEM NICELY, AND WHEN REAL HOT, SERVE.
  9918	
  9919	    [1] Preferably Sherry or Madeira.
  9920	
  9921	
  9922	[317] ANOTHER WAY
  9923	    _ALITER_
  9924	
  9925	IF YOU WISH YOU MAY ALSO WRAP THE TRUFFLES IN CAUL OF PORK, BRAISE AND
  9926	SO SERVE THEM.
  9927	
  9928	
  9929	[318] ANOTHER TRUFFLE
  9930	    _ALITER TUBERA_
  9931	
  9932	STEW THE TRUFFLES IN WINE SAUCE, WITH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CORIANDER, RUE,
  9933	BROTH, HONEY, WINE, AND A LITTLE OIL.
  9934	
  9935	
  9936	[319] ANOTHER WAY FOR TRUFFLES
  9937	    _ALITER TUBERA_
  9938	
  9939	BRAISE THE TRUFFLES WITH PEPPER, MINT, RUE, HONEY, OIL, AND A LITTLE
  9940	WINE. HEAT AND SERVE.
  9941	
  9942	
  9943	[320] ANOTHER WAY FOR TRUFFLES
  9944	    _ALITER TUBERA_ [1]
  9945	
  9946	PEPPER, CUMIN, SILPHIUM, MINT, CELERY, RUE, HONEY, VINEGAR, OR WINE,
  9947	SALT OR BROTH, A LITTLE OIL.
  9948	
  9949	    [1] Wanting in G.-V.
  9950	
  9951	
  9952	[321] ANOTHER WAY FOR TRUFFLES
  9953	    _ALITER TUBERA_ [1]
  9954	
  9955	COOK THE TRUFFLES WITH LEEKS, SALT, PEPPER, CHOPPED CORIANDER, THE
  9956	VERY BEST WINE AND A LITTLE OIL.
  9957	
  9958	    [1] Wanting in Tor.
  9959	
  9960	    This, to our notion of eating truffles, is the best
  9961	    formula, save ℞ Nos. 315 and 316.
  9962	
  9963	
  9964	
  9965	XVII
  9966	
  9967	TARO, DASHEEN
  9968	  _IN COLOCASIO_
  9969	
  9970	
  9971	[322] COLOCASIUM [1] TARO, DASHEEN
  9972	    _COLOCASIUM_
  9973	
  9974	FOR THE COLOCASIUM (WHICH IS REALLY THE COLOCASIA PLANT, ALSO CALLED
  9975	"EGYPTIAN BEAN") USE [2] PEPPER, CUMIN, RUE, HONEY, OR BROTH, AND A
  9976	LITTLE OIL; WHEN DONE BIND WITH ROUX [3] COLOCASIUM IS THE ROOT OF THE
  9977	EGYPTIAN BEAN WHICH IS USED EXCLUSIVELY [4].
  9978	
  9979	    [1] Cf. notes to ℞ Nos. 74, 172, 216, 244; also the
  9980	    copious explanations by Humelberg, fol. III.
  9981	
  9982	    [2] Tor. who is trying hard to explain the _colocasium_.
  9983	    His name, "Egyptian Bean" may be due to the mealiness
  9984	    and bean-like texture of the _colocasium_ tuber;
  9985	    otherwise there is no resemblance to a bean, except,
  9986	    perhaps, the seed pod which is not used for food. This
  9987	    simile has led other commentators to believe that the
  9988	    _colocasium_ in reality was a bean.
  9989	
  9990	    The U.S. Department of Agriculture has in recent years
  9991	    imported various specimens of that taro species
  9992	    (belonging to the _colocasia_), and the plants are now
  9993	    successfully being farmed in the southern parts of the
  9994	    United States, with fair prospects of becoming an
  9995	    important article of daily diet. The Department has
  9996	    favored us repeatedly with samples of the taro, or
  9997	    dasheen, (_Colocasium Antiquorum_) and we have made many
  9998	    different experiments with this agreeable, delightful
  9999	    and important "new" vegetable. It can be prepared in
 10000	    every way like a potato, and possesses advantages over
 10001	    the potato as far as value of nutrition, flavor, culture
 10002	    and keeping qualities are concerned. As a commercial
 10003	    article, it is not any more expensive than any good kind
 10004	    of potato. It grows where the potato will not thrive,
 10005	    and vice versa. It thus saves much in freight to parts
 10006	    where the potato does not grow.
 10007	
 10008	    The ancient _colocasium_ is no doubt a close relative of
 10009	    the modern dasheen or taro. The Apician _colocasium_ was
 10010	    perhaps very similar to the ordinary Elephant-Ear,
 10011	    _colocasium Antiquorum Schott_, often called _caladium
 10012	    esculentum_, or _tanyah_, more recently called the
 10013	    "Dasheen" which is a corruption of the French "de
 10014	    Chine"--from China--indicating the supposed origin of
 10015	    this variety of taro. The dasheen is a broad-leaved
 10016	    member of the _arum_ family. The name dasheen originated
 10017	    in the West Indies whence it was imported into the
 10018	    United States around 1910, and the name is now
 10019	    officially adopted.
 10020	
 10021	    Mark Catesby, in his Natural History of Carolina,
 10022	    Florida and the Bahama Islands, London, 1781, describes
 10023	    briefly under the name of _arum maximum Aegypticum_ a
 10024	    plant which was doubtless one of the tanyahs or taros.
 10025	    He says: "This was a welcome improvement among the
 10026	    negroes and was esteemed a blessing; they being
 10027	    delighted with all their African food, particularly
 10028	    this, which a great part of Africa subsists much on."
 10029	
 10030	    Torinus, groping for the right name, calls it variously
 10031	    _colosium_, _coledium_, _coloesium_, till he finally
 10032	    gets it right, _colocasium_.
 10033	
 10034	    [3] The root or tubers of this plant was used by the
 10035	    ancients as a vegetable. They probably boiled and then
 10036	    peeled and sliced the tubers, seasoning the pieces with
 10037	    the above ingredients, heated them in bouillon stock and
 10038	    thickened the gravy in the usual way. Since the tuber is
 10039	    very starchy, little roux is required for binding.
 10040	
 10041	    [4] Afterthought by Tor. printed in italics on the
 10042	    margin of his book.
 10043	
 10044	
 10045	
 10046	XVIII
 10047	
 10048	SNAILS
 10049	  _COCHLEAS_
 10050	
 10051	
 10052	[323] MILK-FED SNAILS
 10053	    _COCHLEAS LACTE PASTAS_
 10054	
 10055	TAKE SNAILS AND SPONGE THEM; PULL THEM OUT OF THE SHELLS BY THE
 10056	MEMBRANE AND PLACE THEM FOR A DAY IN A VESSEL WITH MILK AND SALT [1]
 10057	RENEW THE MILK DAILY. HOURLY [2] CLEAN THE SNAILS OF ALL REFUSE, AND
 10058	WHEN THEY ARE SO FAT THAT THEY CAN NO LONGER RETIRE [to their shells]
 10059	FRY THEM IN OIL AND SERVE THEM WITH WINE SAUCE. IN A SIMILAR WAY THEY
 10060	MAY BE FED ON A MILK PORRIDGE [3].
 10061	
 10062	    [1] Just enough so they do not drown.
 10063	
 10064	    [2] Wanting in Tor.
 10065	
 10066	    [3] The Romans raised snails for the table in special
 10067	    places called _cochlearia_. Fluvius Hirpinus is credited
 10068	    with having popularized the snail in Rome a little
 10069	    before the civil wars between Cæsar and Pompey. If we
 10070	    could believe Varro, snails grew to enormous
 10071	    proportions. A supper of the younger Pliny consisted of
 10072	    a head of lettuce, three snails, two eggs, a barley
 10073	    cake, sweet wine, refrigerated in snow.
 10074	
 10075	    Snails as a food are not sufficiently appreciated by the
 10076	    Germanic races who do not hesitate to eat similar
 10077	    animals and are very fond of such food as oysters,
 10078	    clams, mussels, cocles, etc., much of which they even
 10079	    eat in the raw state.
 10080	
 10081	
 10082	[324] ANOTHER WAY
 10083	    _ALITER_
 10084	
 10085	THE SNAILS ARE FRIED WITH PURE SALT AND OIL AND [a sauce of] LASER,
 10086	BROTH, PEPPER AND OIL IS UNDERLAID; OR THE FRIED SNAILS ARE FULLY
 10087	COVERED WITH BROTH, PEPPER AND CUMIN.
 10088	
 10089	    Tor. divides this into three articles.
 10090	
 10091	
 10092	[325] ANOTHER WAY FOR SNAILS
 10093	    _ALITER COCHLEAS_
 10094	
 10095	THE LIVE SNAILS ARE SPRINKLED WITH MILK MIXED WITH THE FINEST WHEAT
 10096	FLOUR, WHEN FAT AND NICE AND PLUMP THEY ARE COOKED.
 10097	
 10098	
 10099	
 10100	XIX
 10101	
 10102	EGGS
 10103	  _OVA_
 10104	
 10105	
 10106	[326] FRIED EGGS
 10107	    _OVA FRIXA_
 10108	
 10109	FRIED EGGS ARE FINISHED IN WINE SAUCE.
 10110	
 10111	
 10112	[327] BOILED EGGS
 10113	    _OVA ELIXA_
 10114	
 10115	ARE SEASONED WITH BROTH, OIL, PURE WINE, OR ARE SERVED WITH BROTH,
 10116	PEPPER AND LASER.
 10117	
 10118	
 10119	[328] WITH POACHED EGGS
 10120	    _IN OVIS HAPALIS_
 10121	
 10122	SERVE PEPPER, LOVAGE, SOAKED NUTS, HONEY, VINEGAR AND BROTH.
 10123

 10138	
 10139	{Illustration: CRATICULA
 10140	
 10141	Combination broiler and stove; charcoal fuel. The sliding rods are
 10142	adjustable to the size of food to be cooked thereon. Pans of various
 10143	sizes would rest on these rods. In the rear two openings to hold the
 10144	caccabus, or stewpot, of which we have four different illustrations.
 10145	The craticula usually rested on top of a stationary brick oven or
 10146	range. The apparatus, being moveable, is very ingenious. The roughness
 10147	of the surface of this specimen is caused by corrosion and lava
 10148	adhering to its metal frame. Found in Pompeii. Ntl. Mus., Naples,
 10149	121321; Field M., 26145.}
 10150	
 10151	
 10152	
 10153	
 10154	{Illustration: CACCABUS
 10155	
 10156	A stewpot, marmite, kettle. The cover, rising from the circumference
 10157	to the center in a succession of steps, fits inside the mouth of the
 10158	kettle. Ntl. Mus., Naples 72766; Field M., 24178.}

 10179	
 10180	I
 10181	
 10182	
 10183	[329] WILD BOAR IS PREPARED THUS
 10184	    _APER ITA CONDITUR_
 10185	
 10186	IT IS CLEANED; SPRINKLED WITH SALT AND CRUSHED CUMIN AND THUS LEFT.
 10187	THE NEXT DAY IT IS PUT INTO THE OVEN; WHEN DONE SEASON WITH CRUSHED
 10188	PEPPER. A SAUCE FOR BOAR: HONEY [1] BROTH, REDUCED WINE, RAISIN WINE.
 10189	
 10190	    [1] Lan., Tor. _vel_ instead of _mel_.
 10191	
 10192	
 10193	[330] ANOTHER WAY TO PREPARE BOAR
 10194	    _ALITER IN APRO_
 10195	
 10196	YOU BOIL THE BOAR IN SEA WATER WITH SPRIGS OF LAUREL; WHEN DONE NICE
 10197	AND SOFT, REMOVE THE SKIN, SERVE WITH SALT, MUSTARD, VINEGAR.
 10198	
 10199	
 10200	[331] ANOTHER WAY TO COOK [sauce for] BOAR
 10201	    _ALITER IN APRO_
 10202	
 10203	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, SEEDLESS MYRTLE BERRIES, CORIANDER,
 10204	ONIONS; ADD HONEY, WINE, BROTH AND A LITTLE OIL; HEAT AND TIE WITH
 10205	ROUX. THE BOAR ROASTED IN THE OVEN, IS MASKED WITH THIS SAUCE, WHICH
 10206	YOU MAY USE FOR ANY KIND OF ROAST GAME [1].
 10207	
 10208	    [1] Tor. continues without interruption.
 10209	
 10210	
 10211	[332] MAKE A HOT SAUCE FOR ROAST BOAR THUS
 10212	    _JURA FERVENTIA IN APRUM ASSUM FACIES SIC_ [1]
 10213	
 10214	CRUSH PEPPER, CUMIN, CELERY SEED, MINT, THYME, SATURY, SAFFRON,
 10215	TOASTED NUTS, OR TOASTED ALMONDS, HONEY, WINE, BROTH, VINEGAR AND A
 10216	LITTLE OIL.
 10217	
 10218	    [1] Tor. _In aprum uerò assum_, indicating, perhaps,
 10219	    that ordinary pork also was prepared "boar style." Cf.
 10220	    ℞ No. 362.
 10221	
 10222	
 10223	[333] ANOTHER HOT SAUCE FOR BOAR
 10224	    _ALITER IN APRUM ASSUM IURA FERVENTIA_
 10225	
 10226	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CELERY SEED, MINT, THYME, TOASTED NUTS, WINE, VINEGAR,
 10227	BROTH, AND A LITTLE OIL. WHEN THE SIMPLE BROTH [1] IS BOILING
 10228	INCORPORATE THE CRUSHED THINGS AND STIR WITH AN AROMATIC BOUQUET OF
 10229	ONIONS AND RUE. IF YOU DESIRE TO MAKE THIS A RICHER SAUCE, TIE IT WITH
 10230	WHITES OF EGG, STIRRING THE LIQUID EGG IN GENTLY. SPRINKLE WITH A
 10231	LITTLE PEPPER AND SERVE.
 10232	
 10233	    [1] Presumably the broth or stock in which the meat was
 10234	    roasted or braised.
 10235	
 10236	
 10237	[334] SAUCE FOR BOILED BOAR
 10238	    _IUS IN APRUM ELIXUM_
 10239	
 10240	REAL SAUCE FOR BOILED BOAR IS COMPOSED IN THIS MANNER [1] PEPPER,
 10241	LOVAGE, CUMIN, SILPHIUM, ORIGANY, NUTS, FIGDATES, MUSTARD, VINEGAR,
 10242	BROTH AND OIL.
 10243	
 10244	    [1] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
 10245	
 10246	
 10247	[335] COLD SAUCE FOR BOILED BOAR [1]
 10248	    _IUS FRIGIDUM IN APRUM ELIXUM_
 10249	
 10250	PEPPER, CUMIN, LOVAGE, CRUSHED CORIANDER SEED, DILL SEED, CELERY
 10251	SEED, THYME, ORIGANY, LITTLE ONION, HONEY, VINEGAR, MUSTARD, BROTH AND
 10252	OIL.
 10253	
 10254	    [1] ℞ No. 336 precedes this formula in Tor.
 10255	
 10256	
 10257	[336] ANOTHER COLD SAUCE FOR BOILED BOAR
 10258	    _ALITER IUS FRIGIDUM IN APRUM ELIXUM_
 10259	
 10260	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, DILL SEED, THYME, ORIGANY, LITTLE SILPHIUM,
 10261	RATHER MORE MUSTARD SEED, ADD PURE WINE, SOME GREEN HERBS, A LITTLE
 10262	ONION, CRUSHED NUTS FROM THE PONTUS, OR ALMONDS, DATES, HONEY,
 10263	VINEGAR, SOME MORE PURE WINE, COLOR WITH REDUCED MUST [and add] BROTH
 10264	AND OIL [1].
 10265	
 10266	    [1] Strongly resembling our _vinaigrette_.
 10267	
 10268	
 10269	[337] ANOTHER [sauce] FOR BOAR
 10270	    _ALITER [ius] IN APRO_
 10271	
 10272	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, CELERY SEED, LASER ROOT, CUMIN, FENNEL
 10273	SEED, RUE, BROTH, WINE, RAISIN WINE; HEAT, WHEN DONE TIE WITH ROUX;
 10274	COVER THE MEAT WITH THIS SAUCE SO AS TO PENETRATE THE MEAT AND SERVE.
 10275	
 10276	
 10277	[338] SHOULDER OF BOAR IS STUFFED IN THIS MANNER
 10278	    _PERNA APRUNA ITA IMPLETUR_ [1]
 10279	
 10280	LOOSEN THE MEAT FROM THE BONES BY MEANS OF A WOODEN STICK IN ORDER TO
 10281	FILL THE CAVITY LEFT BY THE BONES WITH DRESSING WHICH IS INTRODUCED
 10282	THROUGH A FUNNEL. [The dressing season with] CRUSHED PEPPER, LAUREL
 10283	BERRIES AND RUE; IF YOU LIKE, ADD LASER, THE BEST KIND OF BROTH,
 10284	REDUCED MUST AND SPRINKLE WITH FRESH OIL. WHEN THE FILLING IS DONE,
 10285	TIE THE PARTS THUS STUFFED IN LINEN, PLACE THEM IN THE STOCK POT IN
 10286	WHICH THEY ARE TO BE COOKED AND BOIL THEM IN SEA WATER, WITH A SPRIG
 10287	OF LAUREL AND DILL [2].
 10288	
 10289	    [1] G.-V. _Terentina_, referring to a place in the
 10290	    Campus Martius, where the _ludi seculares_ were
 10291	    celebrated. Tor. _recentia_, fresh.
 10292	
 10293	    [2] The dressing consisted principally of pork or veal
 10294	    pounded fine, seasoned as directed above, and tied with
 10295	    eggs, as is often prescribed by Apicius.
 10296	
 10297	    To verify how little high class cookery methods have
 10298	    changed consult one of the foremost of modern
 10299	    authorities, Auguste Escoffier, of the Carlton and Ritz
 10300	    hotels, London and Paris, who in his "Guide Culinaire"
 10301	    presents this dish under its ancient Italian name of
 10302	    _Zampino_.
 10303	
 10304	
 10305	
 10306	II
 10307	
 10308	VENISON [Stag]
 10309	  _IN CERVO_
 10310	
 10311	
 10312	[339] SAUCE FOR STAG
 10313	    _IUS IN CERVUM_
 10314	
 10315	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY [1] ORIGANY, CELERY SEED, LASER ROOT,
 10316	FENNEL SEED, MOISTEN WITH BROTH, WINE [2] RAISIN WINE AND A LITTLE
 10317	OIL. WHEN BOILING BIND WITH ROUX; THE COOKED MEAT IMMERSE IN THIS
 10318	SAUCE [braise] TO PENETRATE AND TO SOFTEN, AND SERVE. FOR BROAD HORN
 10319	DEER AS WELL AS FOR OTHER VENISON FOLLOW SIMILAR METHODS AND USE THE
 10320	SAME CONDIMENTS.
 10321	
 10322	    [1] Tor. _carenum_; Hum. _legendum: careum_.
 10323	
 10324	    [2] Wanting in Tor.
 10325	
 10326	
 10327	[340] ANOTHER WAY [1]
 10328	    _ALITER_
 10329	
 10330	PARBOIL AND BRAISE THE VENISON. CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY, CELERY
 10331	SEED, MOISTEN WITH HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH AND OIL; HEAT, BIND WITH ROUX
 10332	AND POUR OVER THE ROAST.
 10333	
 10334	    [1] Tor. Another little sauce for venison.
 10335	
 10336	
 10337	[341] VENISON SAUCE
 10338	    _IUS IN CERVO_
 10339	
 10340	MIX PEPPER, LOVAGE, ONION, ORIGANY, NUTS, FIGDATES, HONEY, BROTH,
 10341	MUSTARD, VINEGAR, OIL [1].
 10342	
 10343	    [1] Resembling a _vinaigrette_, except for the nuts and
 10344	    dates.
 10345	
 10346	
 10347	[342] PREPARATION OF VENISON
 10348	    _CERVINÆ CONDITURA_
 10349	
 10350	PEPPER, CUMIN, CONDIMENTS, PARSLEY, ONION, RUE, HONEY, BROTH, MINT,
 10351	RAISIN WINE, REDUCED WINE, AND A LITTLE OIL; BIND WITH ROUX WHEN
 10352	BOILING.
 10353	
 10354	
 10355	[343] HOT SAUCE FOR VENISON
 10356	    _IURA FERVENTIA IN CERVO_
 10357	
 10358	PEPPER, LOVAGE, PARSLEY, CUMIN, TOASTED NUTS OR ALMONDS, HONEY,
 10359	VINEGAR, WINE, A LITTLE OIL; ADD BROTH AND STIR WELL.
 10360	
 10361	
 10362	[344] MARINADE FOR ROAST VENISON
 10363	    _EMBAMMA [1] IN CERVINAM ASSAM_
 10364	
 10365	PEPPER, NARD LEAVES, CELERY SEED, DRY ONIONS, GREEN RUE, HONEY,
 10366	VINEGAR, BROTH, ADD DATES, RAISINS AND OIL.
 10367	
 10368	    [1] Tor. _Intinctus_, same; a _marinade_, a pickle or
 10369	    sauce in which to preserve or to flavor raw meat or
 10370	    fish.
 10371	
 10372	
 10373	[345] ANOTHER HOT SAUCE FOR VENISON
 10374	    _ALITER IN CERVUM ASSUM IURA FERVENTIA_
 10375	
 10376	PEPPER, LOVAGE, PARSLEY, STEWED DAMASCUS PRUNES, WINE, HONEY, VINEGAR,
 10377	BROTH, A LITTLE OIL; STIR WITH A FAGOT OF LEEKS AND SATURY [1].
 10378	
 10379	    [1] A fagot of herbs; regarding this method of
 10380	    flavoring. Cf. notes to ℞ No. 277 _seq._
 10381	
 10382	    A sauce resembling our Cumberland, very popular with
 10383	    venison which is sweetened with currant jelly instead of
 10384	    the above prunes.
 10385

 10396	
 10397	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY, CUMIN, PARSLEY, RUE SEED, HONEY, MUSTARD,
 10398	VINEGAR, BROTH AND OIL.
 10399	
 10400	
 10401	[347] SAUCE FOR ROAST WILD GOAT
 10402	    _IUS IN CAPREA ASSA_
 10403	
 10404	PEPPER, HERBS, RUE, ONION, HONEY, BROTH, RAISIN WINE, A LITTLE OIL,
 10405	BIND WITH ROUX.
 10406	
 10407	
 10408	[347a] STILL ANOTHER
 10409	     _ALITER_
 10410	
 10411	AS ABOVE IS MADE WITH PARSLEY AND MARJORAM [1].
 10412	
 10413	    [1] Wanting in G.-V.
 10414	
 10415	
 10416	[347b] ANOTHER SAUCE FOR WILD GOAT
 10417	     _ALITER IUS IN CAPREA_
 10418	
 10419	PEPPER, SPICES, PARSLEY, A LITTLE ORIGANY, RUE, BROTH, HONEY, RAISIN
 10420	WINE, AND A LITTLE OIL; BIND WITH ROUX [1].
 10421	
 10422	    [1] Wanting in Tor.
