 10424	
 10425	
 10426	IV
 10427	
 10428	WILD SHEEP
 10429	  _IN OVIFERO (HOC EST OVIS SILVATICA)_ [1]
 10430	
 10431	
 10432	[348] SAUCE FOR MOUNTAIN SHEEP
 10433	    _IUS IN OVIFERO FERVENS_
 10434	
 10435	[THAT IS, (ROAST) THE MEAT, PREPARE A SAUCE OF] [2] PEPPER, LOVAGE,
 10436	CUMIN, DRY MINT [3], THYME, SILPHIUM, MOISTEN WITH WINE, ADD STEWED
 10437	DAMASCUS PRUNES, HONEY, WINE, BROTH, VINEGAR, RAISIN WINE,--ENOUGH TO
 10438	COLOR--AND STIR WITH A WHIP OF ORIGANY AND DRY MINT [3].
 10439	
 10440	    [1] G.-V., List. _in ovi fero_; Dann. "wild eggs," i.e.,
 10441	    the eggs of game birds, and he comes to the conclusion
 10442	    that game birds themselves are meant to be used in this
 10443	    formula, as no reference to "eggs" is made.
 10444	
 10445	    There can be no doubt but what this formula deals with
 10446	    the preparation of sheep; Torinus says expressly:
 10447	    _oviferum, hoc est, carnem ovis sylvestris_--the meat of
 10448	    sheep from the woods, mountain sheep. _Ferum_ is "wild,"
 10449	    "game," but it also means "pregnant." For this double
 10450	    sense the formula may be interpreted as dealing with
 10451	    either wild sheep, or with pregnant sheep, or, more
 10452	    probably, with unborn baby lamb, which in antiquity as
 10453	    today is often killed principally for its skin.
 10454	
 10455	    [2] Tor.
 10456	
 10457	    [3] Mint is still associated with lamb; the above sauce
 10458	    appears to be merely an elaborate Roman ancestor of our
 10459	    modern mint sauce, served with lamb, the chief
 10460	    ingredients of which are mint, vinegar and sugar, served
 10461	    both hot and cold.
 10462	
 10463	
 10464	[349] SAUCE FOR ALL KINDS OF GAME, BOILED OR ROAST
 10465	    _IUS IN VENATIONIBUS OMNIBUS ELIXIS ET ASSIS_ [1]
 10466	
 10467	8 SCRUPLES OF PEPPER, RUE, LOVAGE, CELERY SEED, JUNIPER, THYME, DRY
 10468	MINT, 6 SCRUPLES IN WEIGHT [each] 3 SCRUPLES OF FLEA-BANE; REDUCE ALL
 10469	THIS TO THE FINEST POWDER, PUT IT TOGETHER IN A VESSEL WITH SUFFICIENT
 10470	HONEY AND USE IT WITH VINEGAR AND GARUM.
 10471	
 10472	    [1] Tor. _Jusculum omni venationi competens_.
 10473	
 10474	
 10475	[350] COLD SAUCE FOR WILD SHEEP
 10476	    _IUS FRIGIDUM IN OVIFERO_ [1]
 10477	
 10478	PEPPER, LOVAGE, THYME, CUMIN, CRUSHED TOASTED NUTS, HONEY, VINEGAR,
 10479	BROTH, AND OIL; SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER.
 10480	
 10481	    [1] List. _omni fero_; which Dann. interprets, "All kind
 10482	    of game." Cf. note 1 to ℞ No. 348.
 10483	
 10484	
 10485	
 10486	V
 10487	
 10488	BEEF OR VEAL
 10489	  _BUBULA SIVE VITELLINA_
 10490	
 10491	
 10492	[351] VEAL STEAK
 10493	    _VITELLINA FRICTA_ [1]
 10494	
 10495	[FOR A SAUCE WITH FRIED BEEF OR VEAL TAKE] [2] PEPPER, LOVAGE, CELERY
 10496	SEED, CUMIN, ORIGANY, DRY ONION, RAISINS, HONEY, VINEGAR, WINE, BROTH,
 10497	OIL, AND REDUCED MUST.
 10498	
 10499	    [1] Evidently a beef or veal steak _sauté_. Beef did not
 10500	    figure very heavily on the dietary of the ancients in
 10501	    contrasts to present modes which make beef the most
 10502	    important meat, culinarily speaking. The above sauce,
 10503	    save for the raisins and the honey, resembles the modern
 10504	    _Bordelaise_, often served with beef steaks _sauté_, in
 10505	    contrast to the grilled steaks which are served with
 10506	    _maître d'hôtel_ butter.
 10507	
 10508	
 10509	[352] VEAL OR BEEF WITH LEEKS
 10510	    _VITULINAM [1] SIVE BULULAM CUM PORRIS_
 10511	
 10512	[or] WITH QUINCES [2] OR WITH ONIONS, OR WITH DASHEENS [3] [use]
 10513	BROTH, PEPPER, LASER AND A LITTLE OIL.
 10514	
 10515	    [1] G.-V. same as _vitellinam_.
 10516	
 10517	    [2] Tor. _cydoniis_; List. _succidaneis_.
 10518	
 10519	    [3] Cf. ℞ No. 332 _et al._
 10520	
 10521	
 10522	[353] FRICASSÉE OF VEAL
 10523	    _IN VITULINAM ELIXAM_
 10524	
 10525	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY, CELERY SEED, MOISTEN WITH HONEY,
 10526	VINEGAR, BROTH AND OIL; HEAT, BIND WITH ROUX AND COVER THE MEAT.
 10527	
 10528	
 10529	[354] ANOTHER VEAL FRICASSÉE
 10530	    _ALITER IN VITULINA EXLIXA_
 10531	
 10532	PEPPER, LOVAGE, FENNEL SEED, ORIGANY, NUTS, FIGDATES, HONEY, VINEGAR,
 10533	BROTH, MUSTARD AND OIL.
 10534	
 10535	
 10536	
 10537	VI
 10538	
 10539	KID OR LAMB
 10540	  _IN HÆDO VEL AGNO_
 10541	
 10542	
 10543	[355] DAINTY DISHES OF KID OR OF LAMB
 10544	    _COPADIA HÆDINA SIVE AGNINA_
 10545	
 10546	COOK WITH PEPPER AND BROTH, ALSO WITH VARIOUS ORDINARY BEANS [1]
 10547	BROTH, PEPPER AND LASER, CUMIN, DUMPLINGS [2] AND A LITTLE OIL [3].
 10548	
 10549	    [1] _cum faseolis_, green string beans.
 10550	
 10551	    [2] Tor. _imbrato_; G.-V. _inbracto_, broken bread,
 10552	    regular dumplings.
 10553	
 10554	    [3] Lamb and beans is a favorite combination, as in the
 10555	    French _haricot_, made with white beans, or boiled lamb
 10556	    with fresh string beans, quite a modern dish. Torinus
 10557	    omits the cumin, which is quite characteristic.
 10558	
 10559	
 10560	[356] ANOTHER LAMB STEW
 10561	    _ALITER HÆDINAM SIVE AGNINAM EXCALDATAM_
 10562	
 10563	PUT [pieces of] KID OR LAMB IN THE STEW POT WITH CHOPPED ONION AND
 10564	CORIANDER. CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, AND COOK WITH BROTH OIL AND
 10565	WINE. PUT IN A DISH AND TIE WITH ROUX [1].
 10566	
 10567	    [1] It appears that the binding should be done before
 10568	    the stew is dished out; but this sentence illustrates
 10569	    the consummate art of Apicius. The good cook carefully
 10570	    separates the meat (as it is cooked) from the sauce,
 10571	    eliminates impurities, binds and strains it and puts the
 10572	    meat back into the finished sauce. This is the ideal way
 10573	    of making a stew which evidently was known to Apicius.
 10574	
 10575	
 10576	[357] ANOTHER LAMB STEW
 10577	    _ALITER HÆDINAM SIVE AGNINAM EXCALDATAM_
 10578	
 10579	ADD TO THE PARBOILED MEAT THE RAW HERBS THAT HAVE BEEN CRUSHED IN THE
 10580	MORTAR AND COOK IT. GOAT MEAT IS COOKED LIKEWISE.
 10581	
 10582	
 10583	[358] BROILED KID OR LAMB STEAK
 10584	    _HÆDUM SIVE AGNUM ASSUM_
 10585	
 10586	KID AFTER BEING COOKED IN BROTH AND OIL IS SLICED AND MARINATED [1]
 10587	WITH CRUSHED PEPPER, LASER, BROTH AND A LITTLE OIL. IT IS THEN GRILLED
 10588	ON THE BROILER AND SERVED WITH GRAVY. SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE
 10589	UP.
 10590	
 10591	    [1] The marinade is used to make the gravy.
 10592	
 10593	
 10594	[359] ROAST KID OR LAMB
 10595	    _ALITER HÆDUM SIVE AGNUM ASSUM_
 10596	
 10597	[LET US ROAST THE KID OR LAMB, ADDING] [1] HALF AN OUNCE OF PEPPER, 6
 10598	SCRUPLES OF FOALBIT [2] A LITTLE GINGER, 6 SCRUPLES OF PARSLEY, A
 10599	LITTLE LASER, A PINT OF BEST BROTH AND A SPOONFUL OIL [3].
 10600	
 10601	    [1] Tor.
 10602	
 10603	    [2] _Asarum_; Tor. _aseros_; List. _asareos_--the herb
 10604	    foalbit, foalfoot, wild spikenard.
 10605	
 10606	    [3] Tor. continues without interruption.
 10607	
 10608	
 10609	[360] STUFFED BONED KID OR LAMB
 10610	    _HÆDUS SIVE AGNUS SYRINGIATUS_ [1]
 10611	
 10612	MILK-FED [2] KID OR LAMB IS CAREFULLY BONED THROUGH THE THROAT SO AS
 10613	TO CREATE A PAUNCH OR BAG; THE INTESTINES ARE PRESERVED WHOLE IN A
 10614	MANNER THAT ONE CAN BLOW OR INFLATE THEM AT THE HEAD IN ORDER TO EXPEL
 10615	THE EXCREMENTS AT THE OTHER END; THE BODY IS WASHED CAREFULLY AND IS
 10616	FILLED WITH A LIQUID DRESSING. THEREUPON TIE IT CAREFULLY AT THE
 10617	SHOULDERS, PUT IT INTO THE ROASTING PAN, BASTE WELL. WHEN DONE, BOIL
 10618	THE GRAVY WITH MILK AND PEPPER, PREVIOUSLY CRUSHED, AND BROTH, REDUCED
 10619	WINE, A LITTLE REDUCED MUST AND ALSO OIL; AND TO THE BOILING GRAVY ADD
 10620	ROUX. TO PLAY SAFE PUT THE ROAST IN A NETTING, BAG OR LITTLE BASKET
 10621	AND CAREFULLY TIE TOGETHER, ADD A LITTLE SALT TO THE BOILING GRAVY.
 10622	AFTER THIS HAS BOILED WELL THREE TIMES, TAKE THE MEAT OUT, BOIL THE
 10623	BROTH OVER AGAIN [to reduce it] INCORPORATE WITH THE ABOVE DESCRIBED
 10624	LIQUOR, ADDING THE NECESSARY SEASONING.
 10625	
 10626	    [1] "Hollowed out like a pipe."
 10627	
 10628	    [2] G.-V. _syringiatus_ (_id est mammotestus_). Tor.
 10629	    _mammocestis_. We are guessing.
 10630	
 10631	    [3] We would call this a galantine of lamb if such a
 10632	    dish were made of lamb today.
 10633	
 10634	    This article, like the following appears to be a
 10635	    contraction of two different formulæ.
 10636	
 10637	
 10638	[361] STUFFED KID OR LAMB ANOTHER WAY
 10639	    _ALITER HÆDUS SIVE AGNUS SYRINGIATUS_
 10640	
 10641	KID OR LAMB IS THUS PREPARED AND SEASONED: TAKE [1] 1 PINT MILK, 4
 10642	OUNCES HONEY, 1 OUNCE PEPPER, A LITTLE SALT, A LITTLE LASER, GRAVY [of
 10643	the lamb] 8 OUNCES CRUSHED DATES, A SPOONFUL OIL, A LITTLE BROTH, A
 10644	SPOONFUL HONEY [2] A PINT OF GOOD WINE AND A LITTLE ROUX.
 10645	
 10646	    [1] Tor.
 10647	
 10648	    [2] G.-V.
 10649	
 10650	
 10651	[362] THE RAW KID OR LAMB [1]
 10652	    _HÆDUS SIVE AGNUS CRUDUS_
 10653	
 10654	IS RUBBED WITH OIL AND PEPPER AND SPRINKLED WITH PLENTY OF CLEAN SALT
 10655	AND CORIANDER SEED, PLACED IN THE OVEN, SERVED ROAST.
 10656	
 10657	    [1] It is quite evident that this sentence belongs to
 10658	    the preceding formula; but all the texts make a distinct
 10659	    separation.
 10660	
 10661	
 10662	[363] KID OR LAMB À LA TARPEIUS [1]
 10663	    _HÆDUM SIVE AGNUM TARPEIANUM_
 10664	
 10665	BEFORE COOKING THE LAMB TRUSS IT PROPERLY AND [marinate it in] PEPPER,
 10666	RUE, SATURY, ONIONS, AND A LITTLE THYME AND BROTH. PLACE THE ROAST IN
 10667	A PAN WITH OIL, BASTE WELL WHILE IN THE OVEN, WHEN COOKED THOROUGHLY,
 10668	FILL THE PAN WITH CRUSHED SATURY, ONIONS, RUE, DATES, BROTH, WINE,
 10669	REDUCED WINE, AND OIL; WHEN THIS GRAVY IS WELL COOKED [strain] PUT IT
 10670	UP IN A DISH, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
 10671	
 10672	    [1] Tor. _Tatarpeianum_. Tarpeius, family name of
 10673	    Romans. Humelberg thinks this dish is named for the
 10674	    people who dwelled on Mount Tarpeius. This was the
 10675	    Tarpeian Rock from which malefactors were thrown.
 10676	
 10677	
 10678	[364] KID OR LAMB PARTHIAN STYLE
 10679	    _HÆDUM SIVE AGNUM PARTHICUM_
 10680	
 10681	PUT [the roast] IN THE OVEN; CRUSH PEPPER, RUE, ONION, SATURY, STONED
 10682	DAMASCUS PLUMS, A LITTLE LASER, WINE, BROTH AND OIL. HOT WINE IS
 10683	SERVED ON THE SIDE AND TAKEN WITH VINEGAR.
 10684	
 10685	
 10686	[365] CREAMED KID FLAVORED WITH LAUREL [1]
 10687	    _HÆDUM LAUREATUM EX LACTE_
 10688	
 10689	[The kid] DRESS AND PREPARE, BONE, REMOVE THE INTESTINES WITH THE
 10690	RENNET AND WASH. PUT IN THE MORTAR PEPPER, LOVAGE, LASER ROOT, 2
 10691	LAUREL BERRIES, A LITTLE CHAMOMILE AND 2 OR 3 BRAINS, ALL OF WHICH
 10692	CRUSH. MOISTEN WITH BROTH AND SEASON WITH SALT. OVER THIS MIXTURE
 10693	STRAIN 2 PINTS [2] OF MILK, 2 LITTLE SPOONS OF HONEY. WITH THIS
 10694	FORCEMEAT STUFF THE INTESTINES AND WRAP THEM AROUND THE KID. COVER THE
 10695	ROAST WITH CAUL AND PARCHMENT PAPER TIGHTENED WITH SKEWERS, AND PLACE
 10696	IT IN THE ROASTING PAN, ADDING BROTH, OIL AND WINE. WHEN HALF DONE,
 10697	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, MOISTEN WITH THE ROAST'S OWN GRAVY AND A LITTLE
 10698	REDUCED MUST; PUT THIS BACK INTO THE PAN AND WHEN THE ROAST IS DONE
 10699	COMPLETELY GARNISH IT AND BIND [the gravy] WITH ROUX AND SERVE.
 10700	
 10701	    [1] Dann. thinks _laureatus_ stands for the best, the
 10702	    prize-winning meat, but the laurel may refer to the
 10703	    flavor used.
 10704	
 10705	    List. remarks that cow's milk was very scarce in Italy;
 10706	    likewise was goat's and sheep's milk; hence it is
 10707	    possible that the kid was cooked with its mother's own
 10708	    milk.
 10709	
 10710	    [2] pints--_sextarii_.
 10711	
 10712	
 10713	
 10714	VII
 10715	
 10716	PIG
 10717	  _IN PORCELLO_
 10718	
 10719	
 10720	[366] SUCKLING PIG STUFFED TWO WAYS
 10721	    _PORCELLUM FARSILEM DUOBUS GENERIBUS_
 10722	
 10723	PREPARE, REMOVE THE ENTRAILS BY THE THROAT BEFORE THE CARCASS HARDENS
 10724	[immediately after killing]. MAKE AN OPENING UNDER THE EAR, FILL AN OX
 10725	BLADDER WITH TARENTINE [1] SAUSAGE MEAT AND ATTACH A TUBE SUCH AS THE
 10726	BIRD KEEPER USES TO THE NECK OF THE BLADDER AND SQUEEZE THE DRESSING
 10727	INTO THE EAR AS MUCH AS IT WILL TAKE TO FILL THE BODY. THEN SEAL THE
 10728	OPENING WITH PARCHMENT, CLOSE SECURELY [with skewers] AND PREPARE [the
 10729	roast for the oven].
 10730	
 10731	    [1] Tor. _impensam Tarentinam_; G.-V. _Terentinam_.
 10732	
 10733	    The birdkeeper's tube may be an instrument for the
 10734	    cramming of fowl.
 10735	
 10736	[366a] THE OTHER DRESSING IS MADE THUS:
 10737	
 10738	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, LASER ROOT, MOISTEN WITH A LITTLE
 10739	BROTH, ADD COOKED BRAINS, RAW EGGS, COOKED SPELT, GRAVY OF THE PIG,
 10740	SMALL BIRDS (IF ANY) NUTS, WHOLE PEPPER, AND SEASON WITH BROTH. STUFF
 10741	THE PIG, CLOSE THE OPENING WITH PARCHMENT AND SKEWERS AND PUT IT IN
 10742	THE OVEN. WHEN DONE, DRESS AND GARNISH VERY NICELY, GLAZE THE BODY
 10743	AND SERVE.
 10744	
 10745	
 10746	[367] ANOTHER SUCKLING PIG
 10747	    _ALITER PORCELLUM_
 10748	
 10749	SALT, CUMIN, LASER; ADD SAUSAGE MEAT. DILUTE WITH BROTH [1] REMOVE THE
 10750	WOMB OF THE PIG SO THAT NO PART OF IT REMAINS INSIDE. CRUSH PEPPER,
 10751	LOVAGE, ORIGANY, MOISTEN WITH BROTH, ADD WINE [2] BRAINS, MIX IN 2
 10752	EGGS, FILL THE [previously] PARBOILED PIG WITH THIS FORCEMEAT, CLOSE
 10753	TIGHT, PLACE IN A BASKET AND IMMERSE IN THE BOILING STOCK POT. WHEN
 10754	DONE REMOVE THE SKEWERS BUT IN A MANNER THAT THE GRAVY REMAINS INSIDE.
 10755	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER, SERVE.
 10756	
 10757	    [1] G.-V. treats the following as a separate article
 10758	    under the heading of _porcellum liquaminatum_.
 10759	
 10760	    [2] G.-V. _unum_ (one brain) instead of _uinum_.
 10761	
 10762	
 10763	[368] STUFFED BOILED SUCKLING PIG
 10764	    _PORCELLUM ELIXUM FARSILEM_
 10765	
 10766	REMOVE THE WOMB OF THE PIG. PARBOIL. CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY,
 10767	MOISTEN WITH BROTH. ADD COOKED BRAINS, AS MUCH AS IS NEEDED [1]
 10768	LIKEWISE DISSOLVE EGGS, [add] BROTH TO TASTE, MAKE A SAUSAGE [of this
 10769	forcemeat] FILL THE PIG WHICH HAS BEEN PARBOILED AND RINSED WITH
 10770	BROTH. TIE THE PIG SECURELY IN A BASKET, IMMERSE IN THE BOILING STOCK
 10771	POT. REMOVE WHEN DONE, WIPE CLEAN CAREFULLY, SERVE WITHOUT PEPPER.
 10772	
 10773	    [1] To have a forcemeat of the right consistency.
 10774	
 10775	
 10776	[369] ROAST SUCKLING PIG WITH HONEY
 10777	    _PORCELLUM ASSUM TRACTOMELINUM_ [1]
 10778	
 10779	EMPTY THE PIG BY THE NECK, CLEAN AND DRY, CRUSH ONE OUNCE PEPPER,
 10780	HONEY AND WINE, PLACE [this in a sauce pan and] HEAT; NEXT BREAK DRY
 10781	TOAST [2] AND MIX WITH THE THINGS IN THE SAUCE PAN; STIR WITH A WHIP
 10782	OF FRESH LAUREL TWIGS [3] SO THAT THE PASTE IS NICE AND SMOOTH UNTIL
 10783	SUFFICIENTLY COOKED. THIS DRESSING FILL INTO THE PIG, WRAP IN
 10784	PARCHMENT, PLACE IN THE OVEN [roast slowly, when done, glaze with
 10785	honey] GARNISH NICELY AND SERVE.
 10786	
 10787	    [1] treated with honey.
 10788	
 10789	    [2] Tor. _tactam siccatam_ for _tractam_.
 10790	
 10791	    [3] Again this very subtle method of flavoring, so often
 10792	    referred to. This time it is a laurel whip. Cf. ℞
 10793	    Nos. 277 _seq._, 345, 369, 385.
 10794	
 10795	
 10796	[370] MILK-FED PIG, COLD, APICIAN SAUCE
 10797	    _PORCELLUM LACTE PASTUM ELIXUM CALIDUM IURE FRIGIDO CRUDO APICIANO_
 10798	
 10799	SERVE BOILED MILK-FED PIG EITHER HOT OR COLD WITH THIS SAUCE [1] IN A
 10800	MORTAR, PUT PEPPER, LOVAGE, CORIANDER SEED, MINT, RUE, AND CRUSH IT.
 10801	MOISTEN WITH BROTH. ADD HONEY, WINE AND BROTH. THE BOILED PIG IS WIPED
 10802	OFF HOT WITH A CLEAN TOWEL, [cooled off] COVERED WITH THE SAUCE AND
 10803	SERVED [2].
 10804	
 10805	    [1] Tor.
 10806	
 10807	    [2] This sentence wanting in Tor.
 10808	
 10809	
 10810	[371] SUCKLING PIG À LA VITELLIUS [1]
 10811	    _PORCELLUM VITELLIANUM_
 10812	
 10813	SUCKLING PIG CALLED VITELLIAN STYLE IS PREPARED THUS [2] GARNISH THE
 10814	PIG LIKE WILD BOAR [3] SPRINKLE WITH SALT, ROAST IN OVEN. IN THE
 10815	MORTAR PUT PEPPER, LOVAGE, MOISTEN WITH BROTH, WINE AND RAISIN WINE TO
 10816	TASTE, PUT THIS IN A SAUCE PAN, ADDING VERY LITTLE OIL, HEAT; THE
 10817	ROASTING PIG BASTE WITH THIS IN A MANNER SO THAT [the aroma] WILL
 10818	PENETRATE THE SKIN.
 10819	
 10820	    [1] Named for Vitellius, Roman emperor.
 10821	
 10822	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
 10823	
 10824	    [3] i.e. marinated with raw vegetables, wine, spices,
 10825	    etc. Cf. ℞ Nos. 329-30.
 10826	
 10827	
 10828	[372] SUCKLING PIG À LA FLACCUS
 10829	    _PORCELLUM FLACCIANUM_ [1]
 10830	
 10831	THE PIG IS GARNISHED LIKE WILD BOAR [2] SPRINKLE WITH SALT, PLACE IN
 10832	THE OVEN. WHILE BEING DONE PUT IN THE MORTAR PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY,
 10833	CELERY SEED, LASER ROOT, GREEN RUE, AND CRUSH IT, MOISTEN WITH BROTH,
 10834	WINE AND RAISIN WINE TO TASTE, PUT THIS IN A SAUCE PAN, ADDING A
 10835	LITTLE OIL, HEAT, BIND WITH ROUX. THE ROAST PIG, FREE FROM BONES,
 10836	SPRINKLE WITH POWDERED CELERY SEED AND SERVE.
 10837	
 10838	    [1] List. named for Flaccus Hordeonius, (_puto_).
 10839	    Flaccus was a rather common Roman family name.
 10840	
 10841	    [2] Cf. note 3 to ℞ No. 371, also ℞ Nos. 329-30.
 10842	    Lister is thoroughly puzzled by this procedure, but the
 10843	    problem is very simple: just treat the pig like wild
 10844	    boar.
 10845	
 10846	
 10847	[373] SUCKLING PIG, LAUREL FLAVOR
 10848	    _PORCELLUM LAUREATUM_
 10849	
 10850	THE PIG IS BONED AND GARNISHED WITH A LITTLE WINE SAUCE [1] PARBOIL
 10851	WITH GREEN LAUREL IN THE CENTER [2] AND PLACE IT IN THE OVEN TO BE
 10852	ROASTED SUFFICIENTLY. MEANWHILE PUT IN THE MORTAR PEPPER, LOVAGE,
 10853	CARRAWAY, CELERY SEED, LASER ROOT, AND LAUREL BERRIES, CRUSH THEM,
 10854	MOISTEN WITH BROTH, WINE AND RAISIN WINE TO TASTE. [Put this in a
 10855	sauce pan and heat] BIND [with roux; untie the pig] REMOVE THE LAUREL
 10856	LEAVES; INCORPORATE THE JUICE OF THE BONES [from which a gravy has
 10857	been made in the meantime] AND SERVE.
 10858	
 10859	    [1] marinate in the ordinary way with _œnogarum_ as
 10860	    the dominant flavor.
 10861	
 10862	    [2] It is presumed that the boned pig is rolled and
 10863	    tied, with the leaves in the center.
 10864	
 10865	
 10866	[374] SUCKLING PIG À LA FRONTO [1]
 10867	    _PORCELLUM FRONTINIANUM_
 10868	
 10869	BONE THE PIG, PARBOIL, GARNISH; IN A SAUCE PAN. ADD BROTH, WINE, BIND.
 10870	WHEN HALF DONE, ADD A BUNCH OF LEEKS AND DILL, SOME REDUCED MUST. WHEN
 10871	COOKED WIPE THE PIG CLEAN, LET IT DRIP OFF; SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER,
 10872	SERVE.
 10873	
 10874	    [1] List. Probably named for Julius Fronto, _prætor
 10875	    urbanus_ under Vitellius. Cornelius Fronto was an orator
 10876	    and author at the time of emperor Hadrian. Cf. ℞ No.
 10877	    246. G.-V. Frontinianus.
 10878	
 10879	
 10880	[375] SUCKLING PIG STEWED IN WINE
 10881	    _PORCELLUM ŒNOCOCTUM_ [1]
 10882	
 10883	SCALD [parboil] THE PIG [and] MARINATE [2] PLACE IN A SAUCE PAN [with]
 10884	OIL, BROTH, WINE AND WATER, TIE A BUNCH OF LEEKS AND CORIANDER; [cook
 10885	(in the oven)] WHEN HALF DONE COLOR WITH REDUCED MUST. IN THE MORTAR
 10886	PUT PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY, ORIGANY, CELERY SEED, LASER ROOT AND
 10887	CRUSH THEM, MOISTEN WITH BROTH, ADD THE PIG'S OWN GRAVY AND RAISIN
 10888	WINE TO TASTE. ADD THIS [to the meat in the sauce pan] AND LET IT
 10889	BOIL. WHEN BOILING BIND WITH ROUX. THE PIG, PLACED ON A PLATTER, MASK
 10890	[with the sauce] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
 10891	
 10892	    [1] Tor. _vino elixatus_; G.-V. _œnococtum_.
 10893	
 10894	    [2] It is presumed that the pig is prepared for coction
 10895	    as in the foregoing, namely cleaned, washed, boned, etc.
 10896	    This also applies to the succeeding recipes of pig.
 10897	
 10898	
 10899	[376] PIG À LA CELSINUS [1]
 10900	    _PORCELLUM CELSINIANUM_
 10901	
 10902	PREPARE [as above] INJECT [the following dressing made of] PEPPER,
 10903	RUE, ONIONS, SATURY, THE PIG'S OWN GRAVY [and] EGGS THROUGH THE EAR
 10904	[2] AND OF PEPPER, BROTH AND A LITTLE WINE [make a sauce which is
 10905	served] IN THE SAUCE BOAT [3]; AND ENJOY IT.
 10906	
 10907	    [1] Tor. _Cæsianus_; Tac. _cesinianum_; G.-V.
 10908	    _Celsinianum_. Lister goes far out of his way to prove
 10909	    that the man for whom this dish was named was Celsinus.
 10910	    He cites a very amusing bit of ancient humor by Petrus
 10911	    Lambecius, given below.
 10912	
 10913	    [2] Really a dressing in a liquid state when raw, a
 10914	    custard syringed into the carcass, which congeals during
 10915	    coction. Eggs must be in proper proportion to the other
 10916	    liquids. The pig thus filled is either steamed, roasted
 10917	    or baked, well protected by buttered or oiled paper--all
 10918	    of which the ancient author failed to state, as a matter
 10919	    of course.
 10920	
 10921	    [3] _acetabulum._
 10922	
 10923	       *       *       *       *       *
 10924	
 10925	    "The Porker's Last Will and Testament"
 10926	    by Petrus Lambecius
 10927	
 10928	    (V. Barnab. Brissonium de Formulis lib. VII, p. 677)
 10929	    [ex Lister, 1705, p. 196; Lister, 1709, p. 236].
 10930	
 10931	    "I, M. Grunter Corocotta Porker, do hereby make my last
 10932	    will and testament. Incapable of writing in my own hand,
 10933	    I have dictated what is to be set down:
 10934	
 10935	    "The Chief Cook sayeth: 'Come here, you--who has upset
 10936	    this house, you nuissance, you porker! I'll deprive you
 10937	    of your life this day!'
 10938	
 10939	    "Corocotta Porker sayeth: 'What, perchance, have I done?
 10940	    In what way, please, have I sinned? Have I with my feet
 10941	    perhaps smashed your crockery? I beg of you, Mr. Cook, I
 10942	    entreat you, if such be the case, kindly grant the
 10943	    supplicant a reprieve.'
 10944	
 10945	    "The Chief Cook sayeth: 'Go over there, boy! Fetch me
 10946	    from the kitchen that slaughtering-knife. I'm just
 10947	    itching to give this porker a blood-bath!'
 10948	
 10949	    "Mr. Porker, realizing that this is the season when
 10950	    cabbage sprouts are abundant, and visualizing himself
 10951	    potted and peppered, and furthermore seeing that death
 10952	    is inevitable, asks for time and begs of the cook
 10953	    whether it was possible to make a will. This granted, he
 10954	    calls out with a loud voice to his parents to save for
 10955	    them the food that was to have been his own in the
 10956	    future, to wit:
 10957	
 10958	    "To my father, Mr. Genuine Bacon-Fat, appointed by me
 10959	    in my last will I give and bequeath: thirty measures of
 10960	    acorns; and to my mother, Mrs. Old-Timer Sow, appointed
 10961	    by me in my last will, I give and bequeath: forty
 10962	    measures of Spartan wheat; and to my sister, Cry-Baby,
 10963	    appointed by me in my last will, whose wedding, alas! I
 10964	    cannot attend, I give and bequeath: thirty measures of
 10965	    barley; and of my nobler parts and property I give and
 10966	    bequeath, to the cobbler: my bristles; to the brawlers,
 10967	    my jaw-bones; to the deaf, my ears; to the shyster
 10968	    lawyers, my tongue; to the cow-herds, my intestines; to
 10969	    the sausage makers, my thighs; to the ladies, my
 10970	    tenderloins; to the boys, my bladder; to the girls, my
 10971	    little pig's tail; to the dancers, my muscles; to the
 10972	    runners and hunters, my knuckles; to the hired man, my
 10973	    hoofs; and to the cook--though not to be named--I give
 10974	    and bequeath and transmit my belly and appendage which I
 10975	    have dragged with me from the rotten oak bottoms to the
 10976	    pig's sty, for him to tie around his neck and to hang
 10977	    himself with.
 10978	
 10979	    "I wish to erect a monument to myself, inscribed with
 10980	    golden letters: 'M. Grunter Corocotta Porker lived
 10981	    nine-hundred-and-ninety-nine years, and had he lived
 10982	    another half year, a thousand years would have been
 10983	    nearly completed.'
 10984	
 10985	    "I ask of you who love me best, you who live like me, I
 10986	    ask you: will not my name remain to be eulogized in all
 10987	    eternity? if you only will prepare my body properly and
 10988	    flavor it well with good condiments, nuts, pepper and
 10989	    honey!
 10990	
 10991	    "My master and my relatives, all of you who have
 10992	    witnessed this execution of my last will and testament,
 10993	    you are requested to sign.
 10994	
 10995	    "(Signed) Hard Sausage
 10996	              Match Maker
 10997	              Fat Bacon
 10998	              Bacon Rind
 10999	              Celsinus
 11000	              Meat Ball
 11001	              Sprout Cabbage."
 11002	
 11003	       *       *       *       *       *
 11004	
 11005	Thus far the story by Petrus Lambecius. The fifth of the signatories of
 11006	the Porker's Testament is Celsinus; and since the other names are
 11007	fictitious it is quite possible that Lambecius had a special purpose in
 11008	pointing out the man for whom the dish, Porcellus Celsinianus,--Suckling
 11009	Pig à la Celsinus--was named.
 11010	
 11011	Celsinus was counsellor for Aurelianus, the emperor.
 11012	
 11013	
 11014	[377] ROAST PIG
 11015	    _PORCELLUM ASSUM_
 11016	
 11017	CRUSH PEPPER, RUE, SATURY, ONIONS, HARD YOLKS OF EGG, BROTH, WINE,
 11018	OIL, SPICES; BOIL THESE INGREDIENTS, POUR OVER THE [roast] PIG IN THE
 11019	SAUCE PAN AND SERVE.
 11020	
 11021	
 11022	[378] PIG À LA JARDINIÈRE
 11023	    _PORCELLUM HORTOLANUM_ [1]
 11024	
 11025	THE PIG IS BONED THROUGH THE THROAT AND FILLED WITH QUENELLES OF
 11026	CHICKEN FORCEMEAT, FINELY CUT [roast] THRUSHES, FIG-PECKERS, LITTLE
 11027	SAUSAGE CAKES, MADE OF THE PIG'S MEAT, LUCANIAN SAUSAGE, STONED DATES,
 11028	EDIBLE BULBS [glazed onions] SNAILS TAKEN OUT OF THE SHELL [and
 11029	poached] MALLOWS, LEEKS, BEETS, CELERY, COOKED SPROUTS, CORIANDER,
 11030	WHOLE PEPPER, NUTS, 15 EGGS POURED OVER, BROTH, WHICH IS SPICED WITH
 11031	PEPPER, AND DILUTED WITH 3 EGGS; THEREUPON SEW IT TIGHT, STIFFEN, AND
 11032	ROAST IN THE OVEN. WHEN DONE, OPEN THE BACK [of the pig] AND POUR OVER
 11033	THE FOLLOWING SAUCE: CRUSHED PEPPER, RUE, BROTH, RAISIN WINE, HONEY
 11034	AND A LITTLE OIL, WHICH WHEN BOILING IS TIED WITH ROUX [2].
 11035	
 11036	    [1] Tor. _Hortulanus_; Gardener's style, the French
 11037	    equivalent _Jardinière_, a very common name for all
 11038	    dishes containing young vegetables. However, in the
 11039	    above rich formula there is very little to remind us of
 11040	    the gardener's style, excepting the last part of the
 11041	    formula, enumerating a number of fresh vegetables. It is
 11042	    unthinkable for any gourmet to incorporate these with
 11043	    the rich dressing. The vegetables should be used as a
 11044	    garnish for the finished roast. This leads us to believe
 11045	    that the above is really two distinct formulæ, or that
 11046	    the vegetables were intended for garniture.
 11047	
 11048	    [2] This extraordinary and rich dressing, perfectly
 11049	    feasible and admirable when compared with our own
 11050	    "Toulouse," "Financière," "Chipolata," can be palatable
 11051	    only when each component part is cooked separately
 11052	    before being put into the pig. The eggs must be whipped
 11053	    and diluted with broth and poured over the filling to
 11054	    serve as binder. The pig must be parboiled before
 11055	    filling, and the final cooking or roasting must be done
 11056	    very slowly and carefully--procedure not stated by the
 11057	    original which it takes for granted.
 11058	
 11059	
 11060	[379] COLD SAUCE FOR BOILED SUCKLING PIG
 11061	    _JUS PORRO _[1]_ FRIGIDUM IN PORCELLUM ELIXUM_
 11062	
 11063	CRUSH PEPPER, CARRAWAY, DILL, LITTLE ORIGANY, PINE NUTS, MOISTEN WITH
 11064	VINEGAR, BROTH [2], DATE WINE, HONEY, PREPARED MUSTARD; SPRINKLE WITH
 11065	A LITTLE OIL, PEPPER, AND SERVE.
 11066	
 11067	    [1] Tor. only; _porrò_ indicating that the sauce may
 11068	    also be served with the foregoing. Wanting in List. _et
 11069	    al._
 11070	
 11071	    [2] Wanting in Tor.
 11072	
 11073	
 11074	[380] SMOKED PIG À LA TRAJANUS
 11075	    _PORCELLUM TRAIANUM_ [1]
 11076	
 11077	MAKE THUS: BONE THE PIG, TREAT IT AS FOR STEWING IN WINE [℞ No.
 11078	375, i.e. marinate for some time in spices, herbs and wine] THEREUPON
 11079	HANG IT IN THE SMOKE HOUSE [2] NEXT BOIL IT IN SALT WATER AND SERVE
 11080	THUS [3] ON A LARGE PLATTER [4].
 11081	
 11082	    [1] Tor. and Tac. _traganum_.
 11083	
 11084	    [2] _ad fumum suspendes_; G.-V. _et adpendeas, et
 11085	    quantum adpendeas, tantum salis in ollam
 11086	    mittes_--passage wanting in other texts, meaning,
 11087	    probably, that the more pigs are used for smoking the
 11088	    more salt must be used for pickling which is a matter of
 11089	    course, or, the heavier the pig, ...
 11090	
 11091	    [3] Tor. _atque ita in lance efferes_; Tac. & _sic eum
 11092	    ..._; G.-V. _et siccum in lance inferes_.
 11093	
 11094	    [4] Hum. _salso recente_, with fresh salt pork. Tor.
 11095	    _cum salsamento istoc recenti_ and Tor. continues
 11096	    without interruption, indicating, perhaps, that the
 11097	    following formula is to be served, or treated (boiled)
 11098	    like the above.
 11099	
 11100	
 11101	[381] MILK-FED PIG
 11102	    _IN PORCELLO LACTANTE_ [1]
 11103	
 11104	ONE OUNCE OF PEPPER, A PINT OF WINE, A RATHER LARGE GLASS OF THE BEST
 11105	OIL, A GLASS OF BROTH [2], AND RATHER LESS THAN A GLASS OF VINEGAR
 11106	[3].
 11107	
 11108	    [1] G.-V. _lactans_, suckling, milk-fed; other texts:
 11109	    _lactente_: Dann. wild boar.
 11110	
 11111	    [2] wanting in Tac. and Tor.
 11112	
 11113	    [3] a variant of the foregoing, a mild pickling solution
 11114	    for extremely young suckling pigs, prior to their
 11115	    smoking or boiling, or both, which the original does not
 11116	    state.
 11117	
 11118	    Schuch and his disciple Danneil, have inserted here
 11119	    seven more pork formulæ (Sch. p. 179, ℞ Nos. 388-394)
 11120	    taken from the Excerpts of Vinidarius, found at the
 11121	    conclusion of the Apicius formulæ.
 11122	
 11123	
 11124	
 11125	VIII
 11126	
 11127	HARE
 11128	  _LEPOREM_
 11129	
 11130	
 11131	[382] BRAISED HARE
 11132	    _LEPOREM MADIDUM_
 11133	
 11134	IS PARBOILED A LITTLE IN WATER, THEREUPON PLACE IT ON A ROASTING PAN
 11135	WITH OIL, TO BE ROASTED IN THE OVEN. AND WHEN PROPERLY DONE, WITH A
 11136	CHANGE OF OIL, IMMERSE IT IN THE FOLLOWING GRAVY: CRUSH PEPPER,
 11137	SATURY, ONION, RUE, CELERY SEED; MOISTEN WITH BROTH, LASER, WINE, AND
 11138	A LITTLE OIL. WHILE THE ROASTING [of the hare] IS BEING COMPLETED IT
 11139	IS SEVERAL TIMES BASTED WITH THE GRAVY.
 11140	
 11141	    Wanting in Goll.
 11142	
 11143	    A difference in the literary style from the foregoing is
 11144	    quite noticeable.
 11145	
 11146	
 11147	[383] THE SAME, WITH A DIFFERENT DRESSING
 11148	    _ITEM ALIA AD EUM IMPENSAM_
 11149	
 11150	[The hare] MUST BE PROPERLY KEPT [i.e. aged for a few days after
 11151	killing]. CRUSH PEPPER, DATES, LASER, RAISINS, REDUCED WINE, BROTH AND
 11152	OIL; DEPOSIT [the hare in this preparation to be cooked] WHEN DONE,
 11153	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
 11154	
 11155	    Wanting in Goll. Tor. continuing without interruption.
 11156	
 11157	
 11158	[384] STUFFED HARE
 11159	    _LEPOREM FARSUM_
 11160	
 11161	WHOLE [pine] NUTS, ALMONDS, CHOPPED NUTS OR BEECHNUTS, WHOLE PEPPER
 11162	ARE MIXED WITH THE [force] MEAT OF HARE THICKENED WITH EGGS AND
 11163	WRAPPED IN PIG'S CAUL TO BE ROASTED IN THE OVEN [1]. ANOTHER FORCEMEAT
 11164	IS MADE WITH RUE, PLENTY OF PEPPER, ONION, SATURY, DATES, BROTH,
 11165	REDUCED WINE, OR SPICED WINE. THIS IS REDUCED TO THE PROPER
 11166	CONSISTENCY AND IS LAID UNDER; BUT THE HARE REMAINS IN THE BROTH
 11167	FLAVORED WITH LASER.
 11168	
 11169	    [1] Reminding of the popular meat loaf, made of
 11170	    remnants: _Falscher Hase_, "Imitation Hare," as it is
 11171	    known on the Continent.
 11172	
 11173	    The ancients probably used the trimmings of hare and
 11174	    other meat for this forcemeat, or meat loaf, either to
 11175	    stuff the hare with, or to make a meal of the
 11176	    preparation itself, as indicated above.
 11177	
 11178	    We also recall that the ancients had ingenious baking
 11179	    moulds of metal in the shape of hares and other animals.
 11180	    These moulds, no doubt, were used for baking or the
 11181	    serving of preparations of this sort. The absence of
 11182	    table forks and cutlery as is used today made such
 11183	    preparations very appropriate and convenient in
 11184	    leisurely dining.
 11185	
 11186	
 11187	[385] WHITE SAUCE FOR HARE
 11188	    _IUS ALBUM IN ASSUM LEPOREM_
 11189	
 11190	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, CELERY SEED, HARD BOILED YOLKS, PROPERLY
 11191	POUNDED, MADE INTO A PASTE. IN A SAUCE PAN BOIL BROTH, WINE, OIL, A
 11192	LITTLE VINEGAR AND CHOPPED ONIONS. WHILE BOILING ADD THE PASTE OF
 11193	SPICES, STIRRING WITH A FAGOT OF ORIGANY OR SATURY [1] AND WHEN THE
 11194	WORK IS DONE, BIND IT WITH ROUX.
 11195	
 11196	    [1] Fagots, or whips made of different herbs and brushes
 11197	    are often employed by Apicius, a very subtle device to
 11198	    impart faint flavors to sauces. The custom has been in
 11199	    use for ages. With the return of mixed drinks in America
 11200	    it was revived by the use of cinnamon sticks with which
 11201	    to stir the drinks.
 11202	
 11203	    The above hare formulæ are wanting in Goll.
 11204	
 11205	
 11206	[386] LIGHTS OF HARE [1]
 11207	    _ALITER IN LEPOREM_ [2]
 11208	
 11209	A FINE HASH OF HARE'S BLOOD, LIVER AND LUNGS. PUT INTO A SAUCE PAN
 11210	BROTH AND OIL, AND LET IT BOIL WITH FINELY CHOPPED LEEKS AND
 11211	CORIANDER; NOW ADD THE LIVERS AND LUNGS, AND, WHEN DONE, CRUSH PEPPER,
 11212	CUMIN, CORIANDER, LASER ROOT, MINT, RUE, FLEA-BANE, MOISTENED WITH
 11213	VINEGAR [3].
 11214	
 11215	    [1] Wanting in Goll.
 11216	
 11217	    [2] Tor. _Condimentum ex visceribus leporinis_.
 11218	
 11219	    [3] The various texts combine the above and the
 11220	    following formula; but we are of the opinion that they
 11221	    are two distinct preparations.
 11222	
 11223	
 11224	[387] LIGHTS OF HARE, ANOTHER WAY
 11225	    _ALITER_
 11226	
 11227	TO THE HARE'S LIVER ADD THE BLOOD AND POUND IT WITH HONEY AND SOME OF
 11228	THE HARE'S OWN GRAVY; ADD VINEGAR TO TASTE AND PUT IN A SAUCE PAN, ADD
 11229	THE LUNGS CHOPPED FINE, MAKE IT BOIL: WHEN DONE BIND WITH ROUX,
 11230	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
 11231	
 11232	    This and the preceding formula resemble closely our
 11233	    purées or forcemeats of livers of game and fowl, which
 11234	    are spread on croutons to accompany the roast.
 11235	
 11236	
 11237	[388] HARE IN ITS OWN BROTH [1]
 11238	    _ALITER LEPOREM EX SUO IURE_
 11239	
 11240	PREPARE THE HARE, BONE IT, GARNISH [2] PUT IT IN A STEW POT [3] AND
 11241	WHEN HALF DONE ADD A SMALL BUNCH OF LEEKS, CORIANDER, DILL; WHILE THIS
 11242	IS BEING DONE, PUT IN THE MORTAR PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, CORIANDER
 11243	SEED, LASER ROOT, DRY ONION, MINT, RUE, CELERY SEED; CRUSH, MOISTEN
 11244	WITH BROTH, ADD HONEY, THE HARE'S OWN GRAVY, REDUCED MUST AND VINEGAR
 11245	TO TASTE; LET IT BOIL, TIE WITH ROUX, DRESS, GARNISH THE ROAST ON A
 11246	PLATTER, UNDERLAY THE SAUCE, SPRINKLE AND SERVE.
 11247	
 11248	    [1] Cf. Goll. ℞ No. 381.
 11249	
 11250	    [2] with vegetables for braising, possibly larding.
 11251	
 11252	    [3] _braisière_, for this is plainly a "potroast" of
 11253	    hare. The boned carcass should be tied; this is perhaps
 11254	    meant by or is included in _ornas_--garnish, i.e.
 11255	    getting ready for braising.
 11256	
 11257	
 11258	[389] HARE À LA PASSENIANUS [1]
 11259	    _LEPOREM PASSENIANUM_
 11260	
 11261	THE HARE IS DRESSED, BONED, THE BODY SPREAD OUT [2] GARNISHED [with
 11262	pickling herbs and spices] AND HUNG INTO THE SMOKE STACK [3] WHEN IT
 11263	HAS TAKEN ON COLOR, COOK IT HALF DONE, WASH IT, SPRINKLE WITH SALT AND
 11264	IMMERSE IT IN WINE SAUCE. IN THE MORTAR PUT PEPPER, LOVAGE, AND CRUSH:
 11265	MOISTEN WITH BROTH, WINE AND A LITTLE OIL, HEAT; WHEN BOILING, BIND
 11266	WITH ROUX. NOW DETACH THE SADDLE OF THE ROAST HARE, SPRINKLE WITH
 11267	PEPPER AND SERVE.
 11268	
 11269	    [1] This personage, Passenius, or Passenianus, is not
 11270	    identified.
 11271	
 11272	    [2] To bone the carcass, it usually is opened in the
 11273	    back, flattened out and all the bones are easily
 11274	    removed. In that state it is easily pickled and
 11275	    thoroughly smoked.
 11276	
 11277	    [3] Lan., Tac., and Tor. _suspendes ad furnum_; Hum.,
 11278	    List., and G.-V. _... ad fumum_. We accept the latter
 11279	    reading, "in the smoke," assuming that _furnum_ is a
 11280	    typographical error in Lan. and his successors, Tac. and
 11281	    Tor. Still, roasts have for ages been "hung on chains
 11282	    close to or above the open fire"; Torinus may not be
 11283	    wrong, after all, in this essential direction. However,
 11284	    a boned and flattened-out hare would be better broiled
 11285	    on the grill than hung up over the open fire.
 11286	
 11287	
 11288	[390] KROMESKIS OF HARE
 11289	    _LEPOREM ISICIATUM_
 11290	
 11291	THE HARE IS COOKED AND FLAVORED IN THE SAME [above] MANNER; SMALL BITS
 11292	OF MEAT ARE MIXED WITH SOAKED NUTS; THIS [salpicon] [1] IS WRAPPED IN
 11293	CAUL OR PARCHMENT, THE ENDS BEING CLOSED BY MEANS OF SKEWERS [and
 11294	fried].
 11295	
 11296	    [1] We call this preparation a salpicon because it
 11297	    closely resembles to our modern salpicons--a fine mince
 11298	    of meats, mushrooms, etc., although the ancient formula
 11299	    fails to state the binder of this mince--either eggs or
 11300	    a thickened sauce, or both.
 11301	
 11302	
 11303	[391] STUFFED HARE
 11304	    _LEPOREM FARSILEM_
 11305	
 11306	DRESS THE HARE [as usual] GARNISH [marinate] IT, PLACE IN A SQUARE PAN
 11307	[1]. IN THE MORTAR PUT PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, MOISTEN WITH BROTH,
 11308	ADD CHICKEN LIVERS [sauté] COOKED BRAINS, FINELY CUT MEAT [2] 3 RAW
 11309	EGGS, BROTH TO TASTE. WRAP IT IN CAUL OR PARCHMENT, FASTEN WITH
 11310	SKEWERS. HALF ROAST ON A SLOW FIRE. [Meanwhile] PUT IN THE MORTAR
 11311	PEPPER, LOVAGE: CRUSH AND MOISTEN WITH BROTH, WINE, SEASON, MAKE IT
 11312	HOT, WHEN BOILING BIND WITH ROUX; THE HALF-DONE HARE IMMERSE [finish
 11313	its cooking in this broth] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
 11314	
 11315	    [1] _Quadratum imponis_, which is plain enough. The hare
 11316	    is to be roast therein. Dann. Cut in dice; Goll. Spread
 11317	    it out. Cf. illustration of square roast pan.
 11318	
 11319	    [2] Presumably the trimmings of the hare or of pork.
 11320	    This forcemeat is supposed to be used for the stuffing
 11321	    of the hare; it, being boned, is rolled up, the
 11322	    forcemeat inside, the outside covered with caul or
 11323	    paper, fastened with skewers. Danneil's interpretation
 11324	    suggests the thought that the raw hare's meat is cut
 11325	    into squares which are filled with forcemeat, rolled,
 11326	    wrapped, and roast--a roulade of hare in the regular
 11327	    term.
 11328	
 11329	
 11330	[392] BOILED HARE
 11331	    _ALITER LEPOREM ELIXUM_
 11332	
 11333	DRESS THE HARE; [boil it]. IN A FLAT SAUCE PAN POUR OIL, BROTH,
 11334	VINEGAR, RAISIN WINE, SLICED ONION, GREEN RUE AND CHOPPED THYME [a
 11335	sauce which is served on the side] AND SO SERVE IT.
 11336	
 11337	    Tor. continuing without interruption.
 11338	
 11339	
 11340	[393] SPICED SAUCE FOR HARE
 11341	    _LEPORIS CONDITURA_
 11342	
 11343	CRUSH PEPPER, RUE, ONIONS, THE HARE'S LIVER, BROTH, REDUCED WINE,
 11344	RAISIN WINE, A LITTLE OIL; BIND WITH RUE WHEN BOILING.
 11345	
 11346	    Tor. _id._
 11347	
 11348	
 11349	[394] SPRINKLED HARE
 11350	    _LEPOREM (PIPERE) SICCO SPARSUM_ [1]
 11351	
 11352	DRESS THE HARE AS FOR KID À LA TARPEIUS [℞ No. 363]. BEFORE COOKING
 11353	DECORATE IT NICELY [2]. SEASON WITH PEPPER, RUE, SATURY, ONION, LITTLE
 11354	THYME, MOISTEN WITH BROTH, ROAST IN THE OVEN; AND ALL OVER SPRINKLE
 11355	HALF AN OUNCE OF PEPPER, RUE, ONIONS, SATURY, 4 DATES, AND RAISINS.
 11356	THE GRAVY IS GIVEN PLENTY OF COLOR OVER THE OPEN FIRE, AND IS SEASONED
 11357	WITH WINE, OIL, BROTH, REDUCED WINE, FREQUENTLY STIRRING IT [basting
 11358	the hare] SO THAT IT MAY ABSORB ALL THE FLAVOR. AFTER THAT SERVE IT
 11359	IN A ROUND DISH WITH DRY PEPPER.
 11360	
 11361	    [1] Tac., Tor. _succo sparsum_.
 11362	
 11363	    [2] We have no proof that the ancients used the larding
 11364	    needle as we do (or did) in our days. "Decorate" may,
 11365	    therefore, also mean "garnish," i.e. marinate the meat
 11366	    in a generous variety of spices, herbs, roots and wine.
 11367	    It is noteworthy that this term, "garnish," used here
 11368	    and in the preceding formulæ has survived in the
 11369	    terminology of the kitchen to this day, in that very
 11370	    sense.
 11371	
 11372	
 11373	[395] SPICED HARE
 11374	    _ALITER LEPOREM CONDITUM_
 11375	
 11376	[The well-prepared hare] COOK IN WINE, BROTH, WATER, WITH A LITTLE
 11377	MUSTARD [seed], DILL AND LEEKS WITH THE ROOTS. WHEN ALL IS DONE,
 11378	SEASON WITH PEPPER, SATURY, ROUND ONIONS, DAMASCUS PLUMS, WINE, BROTH,
 11379	REDUCED WINE AND A LITTLE OIL; TIE WITH ROUX, LET BOIL A LITTLE LONGER
 11380	[baste] SO THAT THE HARE IS PENETRATED BY THE FLAVOR, AND SERVE IT ON
 11381	A PLATTER MASKED WITH SAUCE.
 11382	
 11383	
 11384	
 11385	IX
 11386	
 11387	DORMICE
 11388	  _GLIRES_
 11389	
 11390	
 11391	[396] STUFFED DORMOUSE [1]
 11392	    _GLIRES_
 11393	
 11394	IS STUFFED WITH A FORCEMEAT OF PORK AND SMALL PIECES OF DORMOUSE MEAT
 11395	TRIMMINGS, ALL POUNDED WITH PEPPER, NUTS, LASER, BROTH. PUT THE
 11396	DORMOUSE THUS STUFFED IN AN EARTHEN CASSEROLE, ROAST IT IN THE OVEN,
 11397	OR BOIL IT IN THE STOCK POT.
 11398	
 11399	    [1] _Glis_, dormouse, a special favorite of the
 11400	    ancients, has nothing to do with mice. The fat dormouse
 11401	    of the South of Europe is the size of a rat, arboreal
 11402	    rodent, living in trees.
 11403	
 11404	    Galen, III, de Alim.; Plinius, VIII, 57/82; Varro, III,
 11405	    describing the _glirarium_, place where the dormouse was
 11406	    raised for the table.
 11407	
 11408	    Petronius, Cap. 31, describes another way of preparing
 11409	    dormouse. Nonnus, Diæteticon, p. 194/5, says that
 11410	    Fluvius Hirpinus was the first man to raise dormouse in
 11411	    the _glirarium_.
 11412	
 11413	    Dormouse, as an article of diet, should not astonish
 11414	    Americans who relish squirrel, opossum, muskrat, "coon,"
 11415	    etc.
 11416	
 11417

 11455	form of a goddess--Scylla, or Diana with two hounds--ending in
 11456	acanthus leaves below the waist. On the curved back of handle is a

 11472	BOOK IX. SEAFOOD
 11473	
 11474	_Lib. IX. Thalassa_
 11475	
 11476	
 11477	    CHAP.    I. SHELLFISH.
 11478	    CHAP.   II. RAY.
 11479	    CHAP.  III. CALAMARY.
 11480	    CHAP.   IV. CUTTLEFISH.
 11481	    CHAP.    V. POLYPUS.
 11482	    CHAP.   VI. OYSTERS.
 11483	    CHAP.  VII. ALL KINDS OF BIVALVES.
 11484	    CHAP. VIII. SEA URCHIN.
 11485	    CHAP.   IX. MUSSELS.
 11486	    CHAP.    X. SARDINES.
 11487	    CHAP.   XI. FISH SAUCES.
 11488	    CHAP.  XII. BAIAN SEAFOOD STEW.
 11489	
 11490	
 11491	
 11492	I
 11493	
 11494	SHELLFISH
 11495	  _IN LOCUSTA_
 11496	
 11497	
 11498	[397] SAUCE FOR SHELLFISH
 11499	    _IUS IN LOCUSTA ET CAPPARI_ [1]
 11500	
 11501	CHOPPED SCALLIONS FRIED LIGHTLY, CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY,
 11502	CUMIN, FIGDATES, HONEY, VINEGAR, WINE, BROTH, OIL, REDUCED MUST; WHILE
 11503	BOILING ADD MUSTARD.
 11504	
 11505	    [1] _locusta_, spiny lobster; Fr. _langouste_; G.-V.
 11506	    _capparus_; not clear, (_cammarus_, a crab); List.
 11507	    _carabus_--long-tailed lobster or crab, the _cancer
 11508	    cursor_ of Linnæus, according to Beckmann; mentioned by
 11509	    Plinius.
 11510	
 11511	
 11512	[398] BROILED LOBSTER
 11513	    _LOCUSTAS ASSAS_
 11514	
 11515	MAKES THUS: IF BROILED, THEY SHOULD APPEAR IN THEIR SHELL; [which is
 11516	opened by splitting the live lobster in two] SEASON WITH PEPPER SAUCE
 11517	AND CORIANDER SAUCE [moisten with oil] AND BROIL THEM ON THE GRILL.
 11518	WHEN THEY ARE DRY [1] KEEP ON BASTING THEM MORE AND MORE [with oil or
 11519	butter] UNTIL THEY ARE PROPERLY BROILED [2].
 11520	
 11521	    [1] i.e. when the soft jelly-like meat has congealed.
 11522	
 11523	    [2] Same procedure as today.
 11524	
 11525	
 11526	[399] BOILED LOBSTER WITH CUMIN SAUCE [1]
 11527	    _LOCUSTAM ELIXAM CUM CUMINATO_
 11528	
 11529	REAL BOILED LOBSTER IS COOKED WITH CUMIN SAUCE [essence] AND, BY
 11530	RIGHT, THROW IN SOME [whole] [2] PEPPER, LOVAGE, PARSLEY, DRY MINT, A
 11531	LITTLE MORE WHOLE CUMIN, HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH, AND, IF YOU LIKE, ADD
 11532	SOME [bay] LEAVES AND MALOBATHRON [3].
 11533	
 11534	    [1] Cumin, mustard and other spices similar to the above
 11535	    are used for cooking crawfish today.
 11536	
 11537	    [2] Sentence ex Tor. wanting in other texts.
 11538	
 11539	    [3] Malabathrum, aromatic leaves of an Indian tree;
 11540	    according to Plinius the _laurus cassia_--wild cinnamon.
 11541	
 11542	
 11543	[400] ANOTHER LOBSTER DISH--MINCE OF THE TAIL MEAT
 11544	    _ALITER LOCUSTAM--ISICIA DE CAUDA EIUS SIC FACIES_
 11545	
 11546	HAVE LEAVES READY [in which to wrap the mince croquettes] BOIL [the
 11547	lobster] TAKE THE CLUSTER OF SPAWN [from under the female's tail, and
 11548	the coral of the male] THEREUPON CUT FINE THE [boiled] MEAT OF THE
 11549	TAIL, AND WITH BROTH AND PEPPER AND THE EGGS MAKE THE CROQUETTES [and
 11550	fry].
 11551	
 11552	    It is understood that hen eggs are added to bind the
 11553	    mince.
 11554	
 11555	
 11556	[401] BOILED LOBSTER
 11557	    _IN LOCUSTA ELIXA_
 11558	
 11559	PEPPER, CUMIN, RUE, HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH AND OIL.
 11560	
 11561	
 11562	[402] ANOTHER LOBSTER PREPARATION
 11563	    _ALITER IN LOCUSTA_
 11564	
 11565	FOR LOBSTER LET US PROPERLY EMPLOY [1] PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, MINT,
 11566	RUE, NUTS, HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH, AND WINE.
 11567	
 11568	    [1] Tor. _rectè adhibemus_, sentence not in the other
 11569	    texts.
 11570	
 11571	
 11572	
 11573	II
 11574	
 11575	RAY, SKATE
 11576	  _IN TORPEDINE_ [1]
 11577	
 11578	
 11579	[403] [A Sauce for] RAY
 11580	    _IN TORPEDINE_
 11581	
 11582	CRUSH PEPPER, RUE, SHALLOTS, [adding] HONEY, BROTH, RAISIN WINE, A
 11583	LITTLE WINE, ALSO A FEW DROPS OF OIL; WHEN IT COMMENCES TO BOIL, BIND
 11584	WITH ROUX.
 11585	
 11586	    [1] _torpedo_; the _raia torpedo_ of Linnæus; a ray or
 11587	    skate.
 11588	
 11589	
 11590	[404] BOILED RAY
 11591	    _IN TORPEDINE ELIXA_
 11592	
 11593	PEPPER, LOVAGE, PARSLEY, MINT, ORIGANY, YOLKS OF EGG, HONEY, BROTH,
 11594	RAISIN WINE. WINE, AND OIL. IF YOU WISH, ADD MUSTARD AND VINEGAR, OR,
 11595	IF DESIRED RICHER, ADD RAISINS.
 11596	
 11597	    This appears to be a sauce to be poured over the boiled
 11598	    ray.
 11599	
 11600	    Today the ray is boiled in water seasoned strongly and
 11601	    with similar ingredients. When done, the fish is allowed
 11602	    to cool in this water; the edible parts are then
 11603	    removed, the water drained from the meat, which is
 11604	    tossed in sizzling brown butter with lemon juice,
 11605	    vinegar and capers. This is _raie au beurre noir_, much
 11606	    esteemed on the French seaboards.
 11607

 11611	
 11612	CALAMARY
 11613	  _IN LOLIGINE_ [1]
 11614	
 11615	
 11616	[405] CALAMARY IN THE PAN
 11617	    _IN LOLIGINE IN PATINA_
 11618	
 11619	CRUSH PEPPER, RUE, A LITTLE HONEY, BROTH, REDUCED WINE, AND OIL TO
 11620	TASTE. WHEN COMMENCING TO BOIL, BIND WITH ROUX.
 11621	
 11622	    [1] Calamary, ink-fish, cuttlefish. Cf. Chap. IV. G.-V.
 11623	    _Lolligine_.
 11624	
 11625	
 11626	[405a] STUFFED CALAMARY [1]
 11627	     _IN LOLIGINE FARSILI_
 11628	
 11629	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CORIANDER, CELERY SEED, YOLKS, HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH,
 11630	WINE, OIL, AND BIND [2].
 11631	
 11632	    [1] Ex List., Sch., and G.-V. Evidently a sauce or
 11633	    dressing. The formula for the forcemeat of the fish is
 11634	    not given here but is found in ℞ No. 406--stuffed
 11635	    Sepia, a fish akin to the calamary.

 11639	IV
 11640	
 11641	SEPIA, CUTTLEFISH
 11642	  _IN SEPIIS_
 11643	
 11644	
 11645	[406] STUFFED SEPIA
 11646	    _IN SEPIA FARSILI_
 11647	
 11648	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CELERY SEED, CARRAWAY, HONEY, BROTH, WINE, BASIC
 11649	CONDIMENTS [1] HEAT [in water] THROW IN THE CUTTLEFISH; [when done]
 11650	SPLIT, THEN STUFF THE CUTTLEFISH [2] WITH [the following forcemeat]
 11651	BOILED BRAINS, THE STRINGS AND SKIN REMOVED, POUND WITH PEPPER, MIX IN
 11652	RAW EGGS UNTIL IT IS PLENTY. WHOLE PEPPER [to be added]. TIE [the
 11653	filled dish] INTO LITTLE BUNDLES [of linen] AND IMMERSE IN THE BOILING
 11654	STOCK POT UNTIL THE FORCEMEAT IS PROPERLY COOKED.
 11655	
 11656	    [1] _Condimenta coctiva_--salt, herbs, roots.
 11657	
 11658	    [2] G.-V. treat this as a separate formula.
 11659	
 11660	
 11661	[407] BOILED CUTTLEFISH [1]
 11662	    _SEPIAS ELIXAS AB AHENO_ [2]
 11663	
 11664	ARE PLACED IN A COPPER KETTLE WITH COLD [WATER] AND PEPPER, LASER,
 11665	BROTH, NUTS, EGGS, AND [any other] SEASONING YOU MAY WISH.
 11666	
 11667	    [1] List. connects this article with the foregoing.
 11668	
 11669	    [2] Tor. _aheno_ for copper kettle; List. _amylo_.
 11670	
 11671	
 11672	[408] ANOTHER WAY TO COOK CUTTLEFISH
 11673	    _ALITER SEPIAS_
 11674	
 11675	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, GREEN CORIANDER, DRY MINT, YOLKS, HONEY, BROTH,
 11676	WINE, VINEGAR, AND A LITTLE OIL. WHEN BOILING BIND WITH ROUX.

 11680	V
 11681	
 11682	POLYPUS [1]
 11683	  _IN POLYPO_
 11684	
 11685	
 11686	[409] POLYPUS
 11687	    _IN POLYPO_
 11688	
 11689	[cook with] PEPPER, LOVAGE, BROTH, LASER, GINGER [2] AND SERVE.
 11690	
 11691	    [1] The polypus, or eight-armed sepia, has been
 11692	    described by Plinius, Galen, Cicero, Diocles, Athenæus
 11693	    and other ancient writers. The ancients praise it as a
 11694	    food and attribute to the polypus the power of restoring
 11695	    lost vitality: _molli carne pisces, & suaves gustu sunt,
 11696	    & ad venerem conferunt_--Diocles.
 11697	
 11698	    Wanting in the Vat. Ms.
 11699	
 11700	    [2] Wanting in List. and G.-V. Ex Tor. p. 100.
 11701

 11704	VI
 11705	
 11706	OYSTERS
 11707	  _IN OSTREIS_
 11708	
 11709	
 11710	[410] OYSTERS [1]
 11711	    _IN OSTREIS_
 11712	
 11713	TO OYSTERS WHICH WANT TO BE WELL SEASONED ADD [2] PEPPER, LOVAGE,
 11714	YOLKS, VINEGAR, BROTH, OIL, AND WINE; IF YOU WISH ALSO ADD HONEY [3].
 11715	
 11716	    [1] Wanting in the Vat. Ms.
 11717	
 11718	    [2] Tor. sentence wanting in the other texts.
 11719	
 11720	    [3] Cf. No. 14 for the keeping of oysters. It is not
 11721	    likely that the oysters brought from Great Britain to
 11722	    Rome were in a condition to be enjoyed from the
 11723	    shell--raw.
 11724	
 11725	    The above formula appears to be a sort of oyster stew.

 11732	[411] ALL KINDS OF BIVALVES
 11733	    _IN OMNE GENUS CONCHYLIORUM_ [1]
 11734	
 11735	FOR ALL KINDS OF SHELLFISH USE PEPPER, LOVAGE, PARSLEY, DRY MINT, A
 11736	LITTLE MORE OF CUMIN, HONEY, AND BROTH; IF YOU WISH, ADD [bay] LEAVES
 11737	AND MALOBATHRON [2].

 11740	
 11741	    [2] Cf. note to ℞ No. 399.
 11742	
 11743	    The shellfish is cooked or steamed with the above
 11744	    ingredients.

 11748	VIII
 11749	
 11750	SEA URCHINS
 11751	  _IN ECHINO_
 11752	
 11753	
 11754	[412] SEA URCHIN
 11755	    _IN ECHINO_
 11756	
 11757	TO PREPARE SEA URCHIN TAKE A NEW EARTHEN POT, A LITTLE OIL, BROTH,
 11758	SWEET WINE, GROUND PEPPER, AND SET IT TO HEAT; WHEN BOILING PUT THE
 11759	URCHINS IN SINGLY. SHAKE THEM WELL, LET THEM STEW, AND WHEN DONE
 11760	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
 11761	
 11762	    Plinius states that only a few small parts of the sea
 11763	    urchin are edible.
 11764	
 11765	
 11766	[413] ANOTHER METHOD
 11767	    _ALITER [IN] ECHINO_
 11768	
 11769	PEPPER, A LITTLE COSTMARY, DRY MINT, MEAD, BROTH, INDIAN SPIKENARD,
 11770	AND [bay or nard] LEAVES.
 11771	
 11772	
 11773	[414] PLAIN BOILED
 11774	    _ALITER_
 11775	
 11776	PUT THE SEA URCHINS SINGLY IN BOILING WATER, COOK, RETIRE, AND PLACE
 11777	ON A PLATTER.
 11778	
 11779	
 11780	[415] IN CHAFING DISH
 11781	    _IN THERMOSPODIO_ [1]
 11782	
 11783	[To the meat of sea urchins, cooked as above, add a sauce made of bay]
 11784	LEAVES, PEPPER, HONEY, BROTH, A LITTLE OIL, BIND WITH EGGS IN THE HOT
 11785	WATER BATH [2] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
 11786	
 11787	    [1] This formula is combined with the preceding in the
 11788	    original.
 11789	
 11790	    [2] Thermospodium; in this respect resembling seafood à
 11791	    la Newburgh. The thermospodium is an elaborate food and
 11792	    drink heater, used both in the kitchen and in the dining
 11793	    room. Our drawing illustrates an elaborate specimen
 11794	    which was used to prepare dishes such as this one in
 11795	    front of the guests.
 11796	
 11797	
 11798	[416] SALT SEA URCHIN
 11799	    _IN ECHINO SALSO_
 11800	
 11801	[The cooked meat of] SALT SEA URCHIN IS SERVED UP WITH THE BEST [fish]
 11802	BROTH, REDUCED WINE AND PEPPER TO TASTE.
 11803	
 11804	    Undoubtedly a commercial article like crabmeat today.
 11805	    The sea urchins were cooked at the fisheries, picked,
 11806	    shells, refuse discarded, the meat salted and marketed.
 11807	    The fish was also salted in the shell as seen in the
 11808	    following:
 11809	
 11810	
 11811	[417] ANOTHER WAY
 11812	    _ALITER_
 11813	
 11814	TAKE SALT SEA URCHINS, ADD THE BEST BROTH AND TREAT THEM IN A MANNER
 11815	AS TO LOOK LIKE FRESH THAT HAVE JUST COME OUT OF THE WATER.
 11816

 11819	IX
 11820	
 11821	MUSSELS
 11822	  _IN MITULIS_ [1]
 11823	
 11824	
 11825	[418] MUSSELS
 11826	    _IN MITULIS_
 11827	
 11828	BEST [2] BROTH, FINELY CUT LEEKS, CUMIN, RAISIN WINE, MUST [3] AND ADD
 11829	WATER TO MAKE A MIXTURE IN WHICH TO COOK THE MUSSELS.
 11830	
 11831	    [1] Variously spelled _mytilus_, _mitylus_, _mutulus_,
 11832	    an edible mussel.
 11833	
 11834	    Tor. and List. _merula_, merling, whiting, Fr. _merlan_.
 11835	    _Merula_ also is a blackbird, which is out of place here.
 11836	    The Vat. Ms. reads _in metulis_.
 11837	
 11838	    [2] Tor.
 11839	
 11840	    [3] Tor. _vinum mustum_; List. _v. mixtum_.
 11841

 11844	X
 11845	
 11846	SARDINES, BABY TUNNY, MULLET
 11847	  _IN SARDA _[1]_ CORDULA _[2]_ MUGILE_ [3]
 11848	
 11849	
 11850	[419] STUFFED SARDINE
 11851	    _SARDAM FARSILEM_
 11852	
 11853	PROPERLY, OUGHT TO BE TREATED IN THIS MANNER: THE SARDINE IS BONED AND
 11854	FILLED WITH CRUSHED FLEA-BANE, SEVERAL GRAINS OF PEPPER, MINT, NUTS,
 11855	DILUTED WITH HONEY, TIED OR SEWED, WRAPPED IN PARCHMENT AND PLACED IN
 11856	A FLAT DISH ABOVE THE STEAM RISING FROM THE STOVE; SEASON WITH OIL,
 11857	REDUCED MUST AND ORIGANY [4].
 11858	
 11859	    [1] The freshly caught sardine.
 11860	
 11861	    [2] _Cordyla_, _cordilla_, the young or the fry of
 11862	    tunny.
 11863	
 11864	    [3] _Mugil_, sea-mullet.
 11865	
 11866	    [4] Tor. origany; List. _alece_, with brine.
 11867	
 11868	
 11869	[420] ANOTHER PREPARATION OF SARDINES
 11870	    _SARDA ITA FIT_
 11871	
 11872	COOK AND BONE THE SARDINES; FILL WITH CRUSHED PEPPER, LOVAGE, THYME,
 11873	ORIGANY, RUE, MOISTENED WITH DATE WINE, HONEY; PLACE ON A DISH,
 11874	GARNISH WITH CUT HARD EGGS. POUR OVER A LITTLE WINE, VINEGAR, REDUCED
 11875	MUST, AND VIRGIN OIL.
 11876	
 11877	
 11878	[421] SAUCE FOR SARDINES
 11879	    _IUS IN SARDA_
 11880	
 11881	PEPPER, ORIGANY, MINT, ONIONS, A LITTLE VINEGAR, AND OIL.
 11882	
 11883	    Resembling our _vinaigrette_.
 11884	
 11885	
 11886	[422] ANOTHER SAUCE FOR SARDINES [1]
 11887	    _IUS ALIUD IN SARDA_
 11888	
 11889	PEPPER, LOVAGE, DRY MINT [2] COOKED, ONION [chopped], HONEY, VINEGAR,
 11890	DILUTE WITH OIL, SPRINKLE WITH CHOPPED HARD EGGS.
 11891	
 11892	    [1] Another _Vinaigrette_.
 11893	
 11894	    [2] Tac. and Tor. _mentam aridam coctam_, dry mint
 11895	    cooked, which is reasonable, to soften it. Hum., G.-V.
 11896	    dry mint, cooked onion; there is no necessity to cook
 11897	    the onion. As a matter of fact, it should be chopped raw
 11898	    in this dressing. The onion is wanting in Tac. and Tor.
 11899	
 11900	
 11901	[423] SAUCE FOR BROILED BABY TUNNY
 11902	    _IUS IN CORDULA ASSA_
 11903	
 11904	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CELERY SEED, MINT, RUE, FIGDATE [or its wine] HONEY,
 11905	VINEGAR, WINE. ALSO SUITABLE FOR SARDINES.
 11906	
 11907	
 11908	[424] SAUCE FOR SALT SEA-MULLET
 11909	    _IUS IN MUGILE SALSO_
 11910	
 11911	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, ONION, MINT, RUE, SAGE [1], DATE WINE, HONEY,
 11912	VINEGAR, MUSTARD AND OIL.
 11913	
 11914	    [1] Tor. _calva_; G.-V. _calvam_. Does not exist. Hum.
 11915	    _calva legendum puto salvia_.
 11916	
 11917	
 11918	[425] ANOTHER SAUCE FOR SALT SEA-MULLET
 11919	    _ALITER IUS IN MUGILE SALSO_
 11920	
 11921	PEPPER, ORIGANY, ROCKET, MINT, RUE, SAGE [1], DATE WINE, HONEY, OIL,
 11922	VINEGAR AND MUSTARD.
 11923	
 11924	    [1] Same as above.
 11925	
 11926	
 11927	
 11928	XI [1]
 11929	
 11930	
 11931	[426] SAUCE FOR CATFISH, BABY TUNNY AND TUNNY
 11932	    _IUS IN SILURO _[2]_ IN PELAMYDE _[3]_ ET IN THYNNO_ [4]
 11933	
 11934	TO MAKE THEM MORE TASTY USE [5] PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, ONIONS, MINT,
 11935	RUE, SAGE [6] DATE WINE, HONEY, VINEGAR, MUSTARD AND OIL.
 11936	
 11937	    [1] The twelve chapters of Book IX, as shown in the
 11938	    beginning of the text are here increased to fourteen by
 11939	    G.-V., to wit, XII, _IUS IN MULLO TARICHO_ and XIII,
 11940	    _SALSUM SINE SALSO_, but these are more properly
 11941	    included in the above chapter XI, as does Tor. All of
 11942	    the above fish were salt, and probably were important
 11943	    commercial articles. The _silurus_, for instance, is
 11944	    best in the river Danube in the Balkans, while the red
 11945	    mullet, as seen in ℞ No. 427 came from the sea of
 11946	    Galilee. Cf. ℞ Nos. 144, 149.
 11947	
 11948	    [2] _Silurus_, probably the sly silurus, or sheatfish,
 11949	    in the U. S. called horn-pout--a large catfish.
 11950	
 11951	    [3] _Pelamis_, a tunny before it is a year old.
 11952	
 11953	    [4] Tunny, Tunafish.
 11954	
 11955	    [5] Tor. wanting in the others.
 11956	
 11957	    [6] Cf. note 1 to ℞ No. 424.
 11958	
 11959	
 11960	
 11961	XII
 11962	
 11963	
 11964	[427] SAUCE FOR SALT RED MULLET
 11965	    _IUS IN MULLO _[1]_ TARICHO_ [2]
 11966	
 11967	IF IN NEED OF CONDIMENTS USE [3] PEPPER, RUE, ONIONS, DATES, GROUND
 11968	MUSTARD; MIX ALL WITH [flaked meat of] SEA URCHINS, MOISTEN WITH OIL,
 11969	AND POUR OVER THE FISH WHICH IS EITHER FRIED OR BROILED, OMITTING SALT
 11970	[4].
 11971	
 11972	    [1] Tor. _mulo_, the red sur-mullet--a very esteemed
 11973	    fish.
 11974	
 11975	    [2] Tarichea, town of Galilee, on the sea of Galilee.
 11976	    Salt mullet as prepared at Tarichea was known as
 11977	    _Tarichus_. This became finally a generic name for all
 11978	    kinds of salt fish, whether coming from Tarichea or from
 11979	    elsewhere. We have an interesting analogy in "Finnan
 11980	    Haddie," smoked Haddock from Findon, Scotland, corrupted
 11981	    into "Finnan," and now used for any kind of smoked
 11982	    Haddock. Cf. ℞ Nos. 144, 149.
 11983	
 11984	    [3] Tor. Quite correctly, he questions the need of
 11985	    condiments for salt fish.
 11986	
 11987	    [4] List. uses this last sentence as the title for the
 11988	    next formula, implying that more salt be added to the
 11989	    salt fish; Tor. is explicit in saying that no salt be
 11990	    added which of course, is correct.
 11991	
 11992	
 11993	
 11994	XIII
 11995	
 11996	ANOTHER WAY, WITHOUT SALT [PORK?]
 11997	  _ALITER, SINE SALSO_ [1]
 11998	
 11999	
 12000	[428] FISH LIVER PUDDING
 12001	    _SALSUM, SINE SALSO_ [2]
 12002	
 12003	COOK THE LIVER [of the mullet] CRUSH [3] AND ADD PEPPER, EITHER BROTH
 12004	OR SALT [4] ADD OIL, LIVER OF HARE, OR OF LAMB [5] OR OF CHICKEN, AND,
 12005	IF YOU LIKE, PRESS INTO A FISH MOULD [6] [unmould, after baking]
 12006	SPRINKLE WITH VIRGIN OIL [7].
 12007	
 12008	    [1] Tor.
 12009	
 12010	    [2] G.-V. plainly, a contradiction. The possible meaning
 12011	    may be, "Salt Fish, without salt pork" as salt fish is
 12012	    frequently served with bacon.
 12013	
 12014	    [3] Dann. Crush the liver, which is probably correct. A
 12015	    paste or forcemeat of the livers and fish were made.
 12016	
 12017	    [4] The addition of salt would be superfluous if the
 12018	    liver of salt meat is used, excepting if the liver of
 12019	    hare, etc., predominated.
 12020	
 12021	    [5] G.-V. or liver of kid, wanting in Tor.
 12022	
 12023	    [6] Such fish-shape moulds existed, made of bronze,
 12024	    artistically finished, same as we possess them today;
 12025	    such moulds were made in various styles and shapes. Cf.
 12026	    ℞ No. 384.
 12027	
 12028	    [7] This is an attempt to make a "fish" of livers, not
 12029	    so much with the intention to deceive as to utilize the
 12030	    livers in an attractive way. A very nutritious dish and
 12031	    a most ingenious device, requiring much skill.
 12032	
 12033	    This is another good example of Roman cookery, far from
 12034	    being extravagant as it is reputed to be, it is
 12035	    economical and clever, and shows ingenuity in the
 12036	    utilization of good things which are often discarded as
 12037	    worthless.
 12038	
 12039	
 12040	[429] ANOTHER WAY, FOR A CHANGE!
 12041	    _ALITER VICEM GERENS SALSI_ [1]
 12042	
 12043	CUMIN, PEPPER, BROTH, WHICH CRUSH, ADDING A LITTLE RAISIN WINE, OR
 12044	REDUCED WINE, AND A QUANTITY OF CRUSHED NUTS. MIX EVERYTHING WELL,
 12045	INCORPORATE WITH THE SALT [2] [fish]; MIX IN A LITTLE OIL AND SERVE.
 12046	
 12047	    [1] G.-V. _Alter vice salsi_.
 12048	
 12049	    [2] Tor. _& salibus imbue_; List. _& salsa redde_. There
 12050	    is no sense to Lister's version, nor can we accept G.-V.
 12051	    who have _et salari defundes_.
 12052	
 12053	
 12054	[430] ANOTHER WAY
 12055	    _ALITER SALSUM IN _[1]_ SALSO_
 12056	
 12057	TAKE AS MUCH CUMIN AS YOUR FIVE FINGERS WILL HOLD; CRUSH HALF OF THAT
 12058	QUANTITY OF PEPPER AND ONE PIECE OF PEELED GARLIC, MOISTEN WITH BROTH
 12059	AND MIX IN A LITTLE OIL. THIS WILL CORRECT AND BENEFIT A SOUR STOMACH
 12060	AND PROMOTE DIGESTION [2].
 12061	
 12062	    [1] Tor., G.-V. _sine_.
 12063	
 12064	    [2] The title has reference to salt fish or salt pork;
 12065	    but the formula obviously is of a medicinal character
 12066	    and has no place here.
 12067	
 12068	
 12069	
 12070	XII [XIV]
 12071	
 12072	
 12073	[431] BAIAN SEAFOOD STEW
 12074	    _EMBRACTUM _[1]_ BAIANUM_ [2]
 12075	
 12076	MINCED [poached] OYSTERS, MUSSELS [3] [or scallops] AND SEA NETTLES
 12077	PUT IN A SAUCE PAN WITH TOASTED NUTS, RUE, CELERY, PEPPER, CORIANDER,
 12078	CUMIN, RAISIN WINE, BROTH, REDUCED WINE AND OIL.
 12079	
 12080	    [1] List. _emphractum_--a caudle, a stew. Seafood stews
 12081	    of this sort are very popular in the South of Europe,
 12082	    the most famous among them being the _Bouillabaisse_ of
 12083	    Marseilles.
 12084	
 12085	    [2] Baiæ, a very popular seaside resort of the ancients
 12086	    located in the bay of Naples. The stew was named after
 12087	    the place. Horace liked the place but Seneca warned
 12088	    against it.
 12089	
 12090	    [3] Tor. _spondylos_; List. _sphondylos_--scallops.
 12091	    Both terms, if used in connection with the shellfish are
 12092	    correct. Lister in several places confuses this term
 12093	    with _spongiolus_--mushroom. This instance is the final
 12094	    vindication of Torinus, whose correctness was maintained
 12095	    in ℞ Nos. 41, 47, 115, _seq._; 120, 121, 183, 309,
 12096	    _seq._
 12097	
 12098	
 12099	END OF BOOK IX [1]
 12100	
 12101	_EXPLICIT APICII THALASSA LIBER NONUS_ [2]
 12102	
 12103	
 12104	    [1] It appears to us that Book IX and the following,
 12105	    Book X, judging from its recipes, phraseology and from
 12106	    other appearances is by a different author than the
 12107	    preceding books. (Long after having made this
 12108	    observation, we learn from Vollmer, Studien, that Books
 12109	    IX and X were missing in the Archetypus Fuldensis.)
 12110	
 12111	    [2]. Tac.

 12173	{Illustration: SHALLOW SAUCE PAN
 12174	
 12175	The plain bowl is molded, the fluted handle ends in a head of the
 12176	young Hercules in a lion's skin, with the paws tied under the neck.
 12177	This corresponds somewhat to our modern chafing dish pan both in size
 12178	and in utility. This pan was used in connection with the plain
 12179	thermospodium for the service of hot foods in the dining room. Ntl.
 12180	Mus., Naples, 73438; Field M., 24032.}

 12195	BOOK X. THE FISHERMAN [1]
 12196	
 12197	_Lib. X. Halieus_
 12198	
 12199	
 12200	    CHAP.   I. DIFFERENT KINDS OF FISH.
 12201	    CHAP.  II. MURENAS.
 12202	    CHAP. III. EEL.
 12203	
 12204	    The numbers of the chapters differ in the various texts.
 12205	
 12206	
 12207	
 12208	I
 12209	
 12210	
 12211	[432] A SAUCE FINES HERBES FOR FRIED FISH
 12212	    _IUS DIABOTANON _[2]_ PRO _[3]_ PISCE FRIXO_
 12213	
 12214	USE ANY KIND OF FISH. PREPARE [clean, salt, turn in flour] SALT [4]
 12215	AND FRY IT. CRUSH PEPPER, CUMIN, CORIANDER SEED, LASER ROOT, ORIGANY,
 12216	AND RUE, ALL CRUSHED FINE, MOISTENED WITH VINEGAR, DATE WINE, HONEY,
 12217	REDUCED MUST, OIL AND BROTH. POUR IN A SAUCE PAN, PLACE ON FIRE, WHEN
 12218	SIMMERING POUR OVER THE FRIED FISH, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
 12219	
 12220	    [1] This chapter principally deals with fish sauces.
 12221	    Apparently it is by a different author than Books
 12222	    I-VIII, which have many formulæ for fish. While we have
 12223	    no direct proof, we are inclined to believe that Book X
 12224	    is a Roman version of a Greek treatise on fish sauces, a
 12225	    monograph, of which there existed many, according to
 12226	    Athenæus, which specialized on the various departments
 12227	    of cookery.
 12228	
 12229	    [2] Tor. _Diabotom_ (in Greek characters); Greek,
 12230	    relating to herbs.
 12231	
 12232	    [3] Tor. G.-V. _in_.
 12233	
 12234	    [4] G.-V. _salsas_.
 12235	
 12236	
 12237	[433] SAUCE FOR BOILED FISH
 12238	    _IUS IN PISCE ELIXO_
 12239	
 12240	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CUMIN, SMALL ONIONS, ORIGANY, NUTS, FIGDATES, HONEY,
 12241	VINEGAR, BROTH, MUSTARD, A LITTLE OIL; HEAT THIS SAUCE, AND IF YOU
 12242	WISH [it to be richer, add] RAISINS.
 12243	
 12244	
 12245	[434] ANOTHER SAUCE FOR BOILED FISH
 12246	    _ALITER IN PISCE ELIXO_ [1]
 12247	
 12248	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, GREEN CORIANDER, SATURY, ONION, [hard] BOILED
 12249	YOLKS, RAISIN WINE, VINEGAR, OIL AND BROTH.
 12250	
 12251	    [1] Tor. _frixo_--fried fish, although his heading reads
 12252	    _elixo_.
 12253	
 12254	
 12255	[435] ANOTHER SAUCE FOR BOILED FISH
 12256	    _ALITER IUS IN PISCE ELIXO_
 12257	
 12258	PREPARE THE FISH CAREFULLY; IN THE MORTAR PUT SALT, CORIANDER SEED,
 12259	CRUSH AND MIX WELL; TURN THE FISH THEREIN, PUT IT IN A PAN, COVER IT
 12260	AND SEAL IT WITH PLASTER [1] COOK IT IN THE OVEN. WHEN DONE RETIRE
 12261	[the fish from the pan] SPRINKLE WITH STRONG VINEGAR AND SERVE.
 12262	
 12263	    [1] Remarkable culinary ingenuity, resembling in
 12264	    principle the North American Indian method of cooking
 12265	    whitefish wrapped in clay. Today we use flour and water
 12266	    made into a stiff paste to seal a pan hermetically if no
 12267	    "pressure cooker" is available.
 12268	
 12269	    This formula cannot be classified under "Sauce for
 12270	    Boiled Fish."
 12271	
 12272	
 12273	[436] ANOTHER SAUCE FOR BOILED FISH
 12274	    _ALITER IUS IN PISCE ELIXO_
 12275	
 12276	WHEN THE FISH IS PREPARED, PUT THE SAME IN A FLAT PAN WITH CORIANDER
 12277	SEED, WATER AND GREEN DILL; WHEN COOKED SPRINKLE WITH VINEGAR AND
 12278	SERVE [1].
 12279	
 12280	    [1] Another fair example of the incompleteness, on the
 12281	    one hand, of the directions, and of the superfluity, on
 12282	    the other hand, of words such as the initial and the
 12283	    closing words, which characterizes so many of the
 12284	    formulæ. This is characteristic of ever so many culinary
 12285	    authors of all ages, who, lacking literary training,
 12286	    assume that the reader is thoroughly versed with the
 12287	    methods indicated. A versatile modern author would have
 12288	    said: "Poach the filleted fish in small water seasoned
 12289	    with coriander seed and green dill; sprinkle with
 12290	    vinegar before serving." He mentioned neither the salt
 12291	    nor the oil which he undoubtedly used.
 12292	
 12293	
 12294	[437] ALEXANDRINE [1] SAUCE FOR BROILED FISH
 12295	    _IUS ALEXANDRINUM IN PISCE ASSO_
 12296	
 12297	PEPPER, DRY ONIONS [shallots] LOVAGE, CUMIN, ORIGANY, CELERY SEED,
 12298	STONED DAMASCUS PRUNES [pounded in the mortar] FILLED UP [2] WITH
 12299	VINEGAR, BROTH, REDUCED MUST, AND OIL, AND COOK IT.
 12300	
 12301	    [1] Alexandria, Egyptian city, at the mouth of the river
 12302	    Nile, third of the three great cities of antiquity
 12303	    excepting Carthage during Apicius' time a rival of Rome
 12304	    and Athens in splendor and commerce. Most important as a
 12305	    Mediterranean port, where fishing and fish eating was
 12306	    (and still is) good.
 12307	
 12308	    [2] G.-V. _mulsum_, mead.
 12309	
 12310	
 12311	[438] ANOTHER ALEXANDRINE SAUCE FOR BROILED FISH
 12312	    _ALITER IUS ALEXANDRINUM IN PISCE ASSO_
 12313	
 12314	PEPPER, LOVAGE, GREEN CORIANDER, SEEDLESS RAISINS, WINE, RAISIN WINE,
 12315	BROTH, OIL, COOKED TOGETHER.
 12316	
 12317	
 12318	[439] ANOTHER ALEXANDRINE SAUCE FOR BROILED FISH
 12319	    _ALITER IUS ALEXANDRINUM IN PISCE ASSO_
 12320	
 12321	PEPPER, LOVAGE, GREEN CORIANDER, ONIONS, STONED DAMASCUS PRUNES,
 12322	RAISIN WINE, BROTH, OIL AND VINEGAR, AND COOK.
 12323	
 12324	
 12325	[440] SAUCE FOR BROILED CONGER
 12326	    _IUS IN CONGRO ASSO_

 12329	MEAD, VINEGAR, BROTH, REDUCED MUST, AND COOK.
 12330

 12338	VINEGAR, BROTH, OIL; AND COOK IT [2]

 12341	    unidentified sea fish.
 12342

 12344	    into one.

 12351	HEAT AND POUR OVER [1].

 12354	    while salt mullet was treated in the preceding formulæ.

 12361	HONEY, BROTH, AND A LITTLE OIL.

 12365	    _IUS IN PELAMYDE ASSA_
 12366	
 12367	PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, GREEN CORIANDER, ONION, SEEDLESS RAISINS [1],
 12368	RAISIN WINE, VINEGAR, BROTH, REDUCED MUST, OIL, AND COOK.
 12369	
 12370	    [1] Wanting in Tor.

 12378	[446] SAUCE FOR PERCH
 12379	    _IUS IN PERCAM_ [1]
 12380	
 12381	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CRUSHED CUMIN, ONIONS, STONED DAMASCUS PRUNES, WINE,
 12382	MEAD, VINEGAR, OIL, REDUCED MUST; COOK IT.
 12383	
 12384	    [1] _Perca_, perch--sea perch or sea bass.
 12385	
 12386	
 12387	[447] SEASONING FOR REDSNAPPER
 12388	    _CONDIMENTUM IN RUBELLIONEM_ [1]
 12389	
 12390	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY, WILD THYME, CELERY SEED, DRY ONIONS, WINE,
 12391	RAISIN WINE, VINEGAR, BROTH AND OIL; BIND WITH ROUX.
 12392	
 12393	    [1] _Rubellio_--a "reddish" fish; perhaps a species of
 12394	    the red-mullet or red-snapper. Hum. says the Latins
 12395	    called the fish _rubelliones_, _rubellos_ and _rubros_;
 12396	    the Greeks _erythrinos_ or _erythricos_, because of
 12397	    their reddish color. A fish, according to Athenæus
 12398	    similar to the _pager_ or _pagrus_, _phager_ or
 12399	    _phagrus_, also called _pagur_, which is not quite
 12400	    identified.
 12401

 12403	
 12404	II
 12405	
 12406	
 12407	[448] SAUCE FOR [BROILED] MURENA
 12408	    _IUS IN MURENA [ASSA]_ [1]
 12409	
 12410	PEPPER, LOVAGE, SATURY, SAFFRON [2], ONIONS, STONED DAMASCUS PRUNES,
 12411	WINE, MEAD, VINEGAR, REDUCED MUST AND OIL; COOK IT [3].
 12412	
 12413	    [1] V. doubting that this is broiled.
 12414	
 12415	    [2] Tor. _Crocomagma_; List. _crocum magnum_, still used
 12416	    today in some fish preparations, particularly in the
 12417	    Bouillabaisse.
 12418	
 12419	    [3] The laconic style in which all these fish
 12420	    preparations are given, is very confusing to the
 12421	    uninitiated. We assume that most of these ingredients
 12422	    were used to season the water in which to boil fish; or,
 12423	    to make a _court-bouillon_, a fish-essence of the bones
 12424	    and the trimmings of the fish, in which to poach the
 12425	    sliced fish. The liquor thus gained was reduced and in
 12426	    the moment of serving was bound with roux or with yolks,
 12427	    and the fish was masked with this sauce. The exceptions
 12428	    from this rule are, of course, in cases where the fish
 12429	    was broiled or fried.
 12430	
 12431	
 12432	[449] SAUCE FOR BROILED MURENA
 12433	    _IUS IN MURENA ASSA_
 12434	
 12435	PEPPER, LOVAGE, [stoned] DAMASCUS PRUNES, WINE, MEAD, VINEGAR, BROTH,
 12436	REDUCED MUST, OIL; COOK IT.
 12437	
 12438	
 12439	[450] ANOTHER SAUCE FOR BROILED MURENA
 12440	    _ALITER IUS IN MURENA ASSA_
 12441	
 12442	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CATMINT [1] CORIANDER SEED, ONIONS, PINE NUTS, HONEY,
 12443	VINEGAR, BROTH, OIL; COOK IT.
 12444	
 12445	    [1] _Nepeta montana_--nep.
 12446	
 12447	
 12448	[451] ANOTHER SAUCE FOR BOILED MURENA [1]
 12449	    _ALITER IUS IN MURENA ELIXA_
 12450	
 12451	PEPPER, LOVAGE, DILL, CELERY SEED, CORIANDER, DRY MINT, PINE NUTS,
 12452	RUE, HONEY, VINEGAR, WINE [2] BROTH, A LITTLE OIL, HEAT AND BIND WITH
 12453	ROUX.
 12454	
 12455	    [1] Ex Tac. and Tor.; wanting in List. and G.-V.
 12456	
 12457	    [2] Tac.; wanting in Tor.
 12458	
 12459	
 12460	[452] ANOTHER SAUCE FOR BOILED MURENA
 12461	    _ALITER IUS IN MURENA ELIXA_
 12462	
 12463	PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY, CELERY SEED [1] CORIANDER, FIGDATES,
 12464	MUSTARD, HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH, OIL, REDUCED WINE.
 12465	
 12466	    [1] List., Sch., Dann. add here which is wanting in Tor.
 12467	    _rhus Syriacum_--Syrian Sumach.
 12468	
 12469	    The originals are considerably confused on the above and
 12470	    the following formulæ.
 12471

 12473	[453] ANOTHER SAUCE FOR BOILED MURENA
 12474	    _ALITER IUS IN MURENA ELIXA_
 12475	
 12476	PEPPER, LOVAGE, VINEGAR, CELERY SEED, SYRIAN SUMACH [1] FIGDATE WINE,
 12477	HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH, OIL, MUSTARD, AND REDUCED MUST. SERVE [2].
 12478	
 12479	    [1] See note to ℞ No. 452.
 12480	
 12481	    [2] Ex Tor. It appears that this formula is a correction
 12482	    of ℞ No. 452, as this is wanting in the other
 12483	    editions. Tor. also lacks the following formula.
 12484	
 12485	    In Tac. the above formula follows the next.
 12486	
 12487	
 12488	[454] SAUCE FOR BOILED FISH
 12489	    _IUS IN PISCE ELIXO_
 12490	
 12491	PEPPER, LOVAGE, PARSLEY, ORIGANY, DRY ONIONS, HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH,
 12492	WINE, A LITTLE OIL, WHEN BOILING, TIE WITH ROUX AND SERVE IN A SMALL
 12493	SAUCE BOAT [1].
 12494	
 12495	    [1] _in lance_; _lanx_ may also mean a large oblong
 12496	    platter on which fish would be served. Cf. illustration
 12497	    Oval Dish with Handles.
 12498	
 12499	    Horace II Sat. 8--_in patina porrecta_--a special dish
 12500	    to hold the cooked _murena_ and to display it to
 12501	    advantage.
 12502	
 12503	    Such special dishes are found in any good table service,
 12504	    to serve special purposes. Not so long ago special forks
 12505	    and knives were used for fish service which have been
 12506	    gradually discarded.
 12507

 12513	LITTLE OIL; WHEN BOILING TIE WITH ROUX [2].

 12536	CUMIN, THYME, CORIANDER, ONIONS, RAISINS, VINEGAR, HONEY, WINE, AND

 12547	wine] HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH, OIL, MUSTARD AND TIE [2].

 12617	[464] WINE SAUCE FOR FISH
 12618	    _IN PISCE ŒNOGARUM_
 12619	
 12620	CRUSH PEPPER, RUE, AND HONEY; MIX IN RAISIN WINE, BROTH, REDUCED WINE;
 12621	HEAT ON A VERY SLOW FIRE.
 12622	
 12623	
 12624	[465] ANOTHER WAY
 12625	    _ALITER_
 12626	
 12627	THE ABOVE, WHEN BOILING, MAY BE TIED WITH ROUX.
