 12629	
 12630	
 12631	III
 12632	
 12633	EEL
 12634	
 12635	
 12636	[466] SAUCE FOR EEL
 12637	    _IUS IN ANGUILLAM_
 12638	
 12639	EEL WILL BE MADE MORE PALATABLE BY A SAUCE WHICH HAS [1] PEPPER,
 12640	CELERY SEED, LOVAGE [2], ANISE, SYRIAN SUMACH [3], FIGDATE WINE [4],
 12641	HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH, OIL, MUSTARD, REDUCED MUST.
 12642	
 12643	    [1] Tor. sentence wanting in other texts.
 12644	
 12645	    [2] Note the position of lovage in this formula. Usually
 12646	    it follows pepper. We have finally accounted for this
 12647	    peculiarity. Torinus, throughout the original, treats
 12648	    "pepper" and "lovage" as one spice, whereas we have kept
 12649	    the two separate. He believed it to be a certain kind of
 12650	    pepper--_piper Ligusticum_. _Piper_, as a matter of
 12651	    fact, stands for pepper, and _Ligusticum_ is the herb,
 12652	    Lovage, an umbelliferous plant, also called
 12653	    _Levisticum_. The fact that the two words are here
 12654	    separated plainly shows that Torinus has been in the
 12655	    dark about this matter almost to the end.
 12656	
 12657	    One wonders why he did not change or correct this error
 12658	    in the preceding books. His marginal errata prove that
 12659	    his work was being printed as he wrote it, or furnished
 12660	    copy therefor--namely in installments. Since the
 12661	    printer's type was limited, each sheet was printed in
 12662	    the complete edition, and the type was then used over
 12663	    again for the next sheet.
 12664	
 12665	    [3] Tor. _thun_.
 12666	
 12667	    [4] Wanting in Tor.
 12668	
 12669	
 12670	[467] ANOTHER SAUCE FOR EEL
 12671	    _ALITER IUS IN ANGUILLAM_
 12672	
 12673	PEPPER, LOVAGE, SYRIAN SUMACH, DRY MINT, RUE BERRIES, HARD YOLKS,
 12674	MEAD, VINEGAR, BROTH, OIL; COOK IT.
 12675

 12696	From the Lancilotus edition, printed by Tacuinus in Venice in 1503.
 12697	Identical with the two previous editions except for very minor
 12698	variants. The rubrication is not completed here. Fine initials were
 12699	painted in the vacant spaces by hand; the small letter in the center
 12700	of the square being the cue for the rubricator. This practice, a
 12701	remnant from the manuscript books, was very soon abandoned after the

 12800	
 12801	
 12802	
 12803	    THE EXCERPTS FROM APICIUS
 12804	    BY VINIDARIUS
 12805	    THE ILLUSTRIOUS MAN
 12806	
 12807	_Apici Excerpta A Vinidario Viro Inlustri_
 12808	
 12809	FIFTH CENTURY
 12810	
 12811	
 12812	Vinidarius, a Goth, of noble birth or a scientist, living in Italy.
 12813	Vinithaharjis is the native name. Of his time and life very little is
 12814	known. It appears that he was a student of Apicius and that he made
 12815	certain excerpts from that book which are preserved in the uncial
 12816	codex of Salmasius, sæc. VIII, Paris, lat. 10318.
 12817	
 12818	Vollmer in his Apicius commentary says that Salmasius and his
 12819	predecessors have accepted them as genuine. Schuch incorporated these
 12820	recipes in the Apicius text of his editions, in appropriate places, as
 12821	he thought. This course cannot be recommended, although the recipes
 12822	should form an integral part of any Apicius edition.
 12823	
 12824	M. Ihm, who faithfully reprinted the excerpta in the Archiv f. lat.
 12825	Lex. XV, 64, ff. says distinctly: "These excerpts have nothing to do
 12826	with the ten books of Apicius, even if some recipes resemble each
 12827	other ..." and other researchers have expressed the same opinion.
 12828	Vollmer, however, does not share this view.
 12829	
 12830	If I may be permitted to concur with Vollmer, I would say that the
 12831	excerpts are quite Apician in character, and that in a sense they fill
 12832	certain gaps in the Apicius text, although the language is strongly
 12833	vulgarized which may be readily expected to be the case in the age of
 12834	Vinidarius.
 12835	
 12836	The recipes of Anthimus, written around A.D. 511 also confirm the
 12837	close relation existing between Vinidarius and Apicius. Anthimus was
 12838	the Greek physician to Theodoric I, (The Great), Frankish king living
 12839	in Italy. He was not acquainted with Apicius.
 12840	
 12841	
 12842	SUMMARY OF SPICES
 12843	  _BREVIS PIMENTORUM_ [1]
 12844	
 12845	WHICH SHOULD BE IN THE HOUSE ON HAND SO THAT THERE MAY BE NOTHING
 12846	WANTING [in the line of condiments]: SAFFRON, PEPPER, GINGER, LASER,
 12847	LEAVES [laurel-bay-nard], MYRTLE BERRIES, COSTMARY, CHERVIL [2],
 12848	INDIAN SPIKENARD, ADDENA [3], CARDAMOM, SPIKENARD.
 12849	
 12850	    [1] _Pigmentorum_--_specierum_--spices. The old
 12851	    _pigmentum_ is really any coloring matter; the word,
 12852	    corrupted to pimento and pimiento is now used for sweet
 12853	    red pepper and also for allspice.
 12854	
 12855	    [2] _Cariofilu_--_cærefolium_--_Chærephyllon_; Fr.
 12856	    _Cerfeuille_; Ger. _Kerbel_. This should be among the
 12857	    herbs.
 12858	
 12859	    [3] Not identified.

 12986	    rendered with "Menu,"--something minute, short,--but
 12987	    this list is not a menu in our modern sense. It is an
 12988	    enumeration of recipe names, a summary of dishes
 12989	    contained in the excerpts.
 12990	
 12991	    There is considerable variation in the spelling of the
 12992	    names here and in the following. Syllables ending with
 12993	    "u" are invariably abbreviations of "um."
 12994	
 12995	
 12996	
 12997	I
 12998	
 12999	
 13000	[468] A CASSEROLE [1] OF VEGETABLE AND CHICKEN
 13001	    _CACCABINAM MINOREM_
 13002	
 13003	ARRANGE DIFFERENT KINDS OF COOKED VEGETABLES IN A CASSEROLE WITH
 13004	[cooked] CHICKEN INTERSPERSED, IF YOU LIKE; SEASON WITH BROTH AND OIL,
 13005	SET TO BOIL. NEXT CRUSH A LITTLE PEPPER AND LEAVES, AND MIX AN EGG IN
 13006	WITH THE DRESSING [add this to the vegetables] PRESS [into the
 13007	casserole, eliminating the juice] [2].
 13008	
 13009	    [1] The dish resembles a chartreuse.
 13010	
 13011	    [2] Juice should be extracted before the addition of the
 13012	    egg, if the dish is to be unmoulded.
 13013	
 13014	
 13015	
 13016	Ia
 13017	
 13018	
 13019	[469] THE SAME, WITH ANOTHER DRESSING, A CABBAGE _CHARTREUSE_
 13020	    _ALIAS: TRITURA UNDE PERFUNDES CACCABINAM_
 13021	
 13022	CRUSH WHATEVER QUANTITY OF LEAVES IS REQUIRED WITH CHERVIL AND ONE AND
 13023	A QUARTER PART OF LAUREL BERRIES, A MEDIUM-SIZED BOILED CABBAGE,
 13024	CORIANDER LEAVES, DISSOLVE WITH ITS OWN JUICE, STEAM IN THE HOT ASHES,
 13025	BUT FIRST PLACE IN A MOULD [when stiff unmould on a platter] DECORATE,
 13026	POUR UNDER A WELL-SEASONED SAUCE, AND SO SERVE [1].
 13027	
 13028	    [1] Either the vegetables and chicken of ℞ No. 468
 13029	    are combined with this dressing or a purée of the above
 13030	    cabbage, etc., is made, which will make this an integral
 13031	    dish. The instructions are vague enough to leave room
 13032	    for this choice; but there can be no doubt but what we
 13033	    have here a formula for a vegetable purée or a pudding,
 13034	    a genuine "Chartreuse," such as were prepared in the
 13035	    fancy moulds so popular in old Rome. The "Chartreuse,"
 13036	    then, is not original with the vegetarian monks of the
 13037	    monastery by that name, the Carthusians.
 13038	
 13039	
 13040	
 13041	II
 13042	
 13043	
 13044	[470] A STUFFED CHARTREUSE
 13045	    _CACCABINAM _[1]_ FUSILEM_
 13046	
 13047	[Take cooked] MALLOWS, LEEKS, BEETS, OR COOKED CABBAGE SPROUTS [shoots
 13048	or tender strunks] THRUSHES [roast] AND QUENELLES OF CHICKEN, TIDBITS
 13049	OF PORK OR SQUAB CHICKEN AND OTHER SIMILAR SHREDS OF FINE MEATS THAT
 13050	MAY BE AVAILABLE; ARRANGE EVERYTHING ALTERNATELY IN LAYERS [in a mould
 13051	or in a casserole]. CRUSH PEPPER AND LOVAGE WITH 2 PARTS OF OLD WINE,
 13052	1 PART BROTH, 1 PART HONEY AND A LITTLE OIL. TASTE IT; AND WHEN WELL
 13053	MIXED AND IN DUE PROPORTIONS PUT IN A SAUCE PAN AND ALLOW TO HEAT
 13054	MODERATELY; WHEN BOILING ADD A PINT OF MILK IN WHICH [about eight]
 13055	EGGS HAVE BEEN DISSOLVED; [next] POUR [this spiced custard] OVER [the
 13056	layers of vegetables and meats, heat slowly without allowing to boil]
 13057	AND WHEN CONGEALED SERVE [either in the casserole, or carefully
 13058	unmould the dish on a service platter] [2].
 13059	
 13060	    [1] It is interesting to note how the generic terms,
 13061	    _salacaccabia_ and _caccabina_ have degenerated here. In
 13062	    these formulas the terms have lost all resemblance to
 13063	    the former meaning, the original "salt meat boiled in a
 13064	    pot." Such changes are very often observed in the
 13065	    terminology of our modern kitchens, in every language.
 13066	    They make the definition of terms and the classification
 13067	    of subjects extremely difficult. They add much to the
 13068	    confusion among cooks and guests in public dining places
 13069	    and create misunderstandings that only an expert can
 13070	    explain.
 13071	
 13072	    [2] This dish affords an opportunity for a decorative
 13073	    scheme by the arrangement of the various vegetables and
 13074	    meats in a pleasing and artistic manner, utilizing the
 13075	    various colors and shapes of the bits of food as one
 13076	    would use pieces of stone in a mosaic. Of course, such a
 13077	    design can be appreciated only if the chartreuse is
 13078	    served unmoulded, i.e. if the cook succeeds in
 13079	    unmoulding it without damaging the structure.
 13080	
 13081	
 13082	
 13083	III
 13084	
 13085	
 13086	[471] BRAISED CUTLETS
 13087	    _OFELLAS GARATAS_ [1]
 13088	
 13089	PLACE THE MEAT IN A STEW PAN, ADD ONE POUND [2] OF BROTH, A LIKE
 13090	QUANTITY OF OIL, A TRIFLE OF HONEY, AND THUS BRAISE [3].
 13091	
 13092	    [1] Derived from _garum_ or _œnogarum_, the wine
 13093	    sauce. These are supposed to be meat balls or cutlets
 13094	    prepared with garum, but the _garum_ is not mentioned in
 13095	    the formula. This also illustrates the interesting
 13096	    etymology of the word. It is not recognized in every-day
 13097	    ancient language because it is a typical technical term,
 13098	    the much complained-of _lingua culinaria_. We find,
 13099	    therefore, that--at least in this instance--_garum_ no
 13100	    longer stands for a sauce made from the fish, _garus_,
 13101	    but that _garum_ has become a generic term for certain
 13102	    kinds of sauces. Danneil renders _garatus_ with
 13103	    _lasaratus_, which is clearly out of place.
 13104	
 13105	    [2] In this instance, and in several others, and also
 13106	    according to Sueton. Cæs. fluids were weighed. What idea
 13107	    could be more practical, useful and more "modern" than
 13108	    this? Sheer commercial greed, stubbornness, indolence
 13109	    have thus far made futile all efforts towards more
 13110	    progressive methods in handling food stuffs,
 13111	    particularly in the weighing of them and in selling them
 13112	    by their weight. Present market methods are very
 13113	    chaotic, and are kept purposely so to the detriment of
 13114	    the buyer.
 13115	
 13116	    [3] The original: _et sic frigis_.--_Frigo_ is
 13117	    equivalent to frying, drying, parching; the word here
 13118	    has taken on a broader meaning, because the "frying"
 13119	    process is clearly out of question here. It appears that
 13120	    the terminology of _frigo_ and that of _asso_ in the
 13121	    next formula, has not been clearly defined. As a matter
 13122	    of fact, not many modern cooks today are able to give a
 13123	    clear definition of such terms as frying, broiling,
 13124	    roasting, braising, baking, which are thus subject to
 13125	    various interpretations.
 13126	
 13127	
 13128	
 13129	IV
 13130	
 13131	
 13132	[472] ROAST MEAT BALLS
 13133	    _OFELLAS ASSAS_
 13134	
 13135	MEATBALLS [previously sauté], CAREFULLY PREPARED, ARRANGE IN A SHALLOW
 13136	STEW PAN AND BRAISE THEM IN WINE SAUCE; AFTERWARDS SERVE THEM IN THE
 13137	SAME SAUCE OR GRAVY, SPRINKLED WITH PEPPER.
 13138	
 13139	
 13140	
 13141	V
 13142	
 13143	
 13144	[473] GLAZED CUTLETS
 13145	    _ALITER OFELLAS_
 13146	
 13147	THE MEAT PIECES ARE BRAISED [1] IN BROTH AND ARE GLAZED [2] WITH HOT
 13148	HONEY [3] AND THUS SERVED.
 13149	
 13150	    [1] Cf. note 3 to Excerpta III.
 13151	
 13152	    [2] _unguantur._
 13153	
 13154	    [3] Dann. oil; G.-V. _melle_--_honey_. It is quite
 13155	    common to use honey for glazing foods. Today we sprinkle
 13156	    meats (ham) with sugar, exposing it to the open heat to
 13157	    melt it; the sugar thus forms a glaze or crust.
 13158	
 13159	
 13160	
 13161	VI
 13162	
 13163	
 13164	[474] MEAT BALLS WITH LASER
 13165	    _OFELLAS GARATAS_ [1]
 13166	
 13167	LASER, GINGER, CARDAMOM, AND A DASH OF BROTH; CRUSH THIS ALL, MIX
 13168	WELL, AND COOK THE MEAT BALL THEREIN [2].
 13169	
 13170	    [1] Cf. Summary of Dishes, and note 1 to Excerpta III.
 13171	
 13172	    [2] Dann. adds cumin, due perhaps to the faulty reading
 13173	    of the sentence, _misces cum his omnibus tritis_, etc.
 13174	
 13175	
 13176	
 13177	VII
 13178	
 13179	
 13180	[475] SEA-SCORPION WITH TURNIPS
 13181	    _PISCES SCORPIONES RAPULATOS_ [1]
 13182	
 13183	COOK [the fish] IN BROTH AND OIL, RETIRE WHEN HALF DONE: SOAK BOILED
 13184	TURNIPS, CHOP VERY FINE AND SQUEEZE THEM IN YOUR HANDS SO THAT THEY
 13185	HAVE NO MORE MOISTURE IN THEM; THEN COMBINE THEM WITH THE FISH AND LET
 13186	THEM SIMMER WITH PLENTY OF OIL: AND WHILE THIS COOKS, CRUSH CUMIN,
 13187	HALF OF THAT AMOUNT OF LAUREL BERRIES, AND, BECAUSE OF THE COLOR, ADD
 13188	SAFFRON; BIND WITH RICE FLOUR TO GIVE IT THE RIGHT CONSISTENCY. ADD A
 13189	DASH OF VINEGAR AND SERVE.
 13190	
 13191	    [1] _rapa_, _rapum_: white turnip, rape; "turniped."
 13192	
 13193	
 13194	
 13195	VIII
 13196	
 13197	
 13198	[476] [Sauce for] ANY KIND OF FISH, FRIED MAKE THUS:
 13199	    _PISCES FRIXOS CUIUSCUMQUE GENERIS_
 13200	
 13201	CRUSH PEPPER, CORIANDER SEED, LASER ROOT, ORIGANY, RUE, FIGDATES,
 13202	MOISTEN WITH VINEGAR, OIL, BROTH, ADDING REDUCED MUST, ALL THIS
 13203	PREPARE AND MIX CAREFULLY, PLACE IN SMALL CASSEROLE TO HEAT. WHEN
 13204	THOROUGHLY HEATED, POUR OVER THE FRIED FISH, SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND
 13205	SERVE.
 13206	
 13207	
 13208	
 13209	IX
 13210	
 13211	
 13212	[477] [Sauce for] SAME FRIED FISH MAKE THUS:
 13213	    _ITEM PISCES FRIXOS_
 13214	
 13215	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE [1], LAUREL BERRIES, CORIANDER, AND MOISTEN WITH
 13216	HONEY, BROTH [2], WINE, RAISIN WINE, OR REDUCED SPICED WINE; COOK
 13217	THIS ON A SLOW FIRE, BIND WITH RICE FLOUR AND SERVE.
 13218	
 13219	    [1] Sch. _ligisticum_.
 13220	
 13221	    [2] Wanting in Sch.
 13222	
 13223	
 13224	
 13225	X
 13226	
 13227	
 13228	[478] [Sauce for] ROAST FISH [1]
 13229	    _PISCES ASSOS_
 13230	
 13231	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, SATURY, DRY ONIONS, MOISTEN WITH VINEGAR, ADD
 13232	FIGDATES, DILL, YOLKS OF EGG, HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH, OIL, REDUCED
 13233	MUST; ALL THIS MIX THOROUGHLY AND UNDERLAY [the fish with it].
 13234	
 13235	    [1] The fish was probably broiled on the _craticula_
 13236	    (see our illustration).
 13237	
 13238	    The nature of this sauce is not quite clear. If properly
 13239	    handled, it might turn out to be a highly seasoned
 13240	    mayonnaise, or a vinaigrette, depending on the mode of
 13241	    manipulation; either would be suitable for fried or
 13242	    broiled fish.
 13243	
 13244	
 13245	
 13246	XI
 13247	
 13248	
 13249	[479] FISH AND WINE SAUCE
 13250	    _PISCES ŒNOTEGANON_ [1]
 13251	
 13252	FRY THE FISH; CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, RUE, GREEN HERBS, DRY ONIONS, ADD
 13253	OIL [wine] BROTH AND SERVE.
 13254	
 13255	    [1] Ihm and G.-V. _œnoteganon_; _inotogono_ and in
 13256	    the Summary of Dishes _inotogonon_; Sch. _eleogaro_.
 13257	    Rather an obscure term, owing to the diversity of
 13258	    spelling. We would call it a dish stewed in or prepared
 13259	    with wine, although wine is absent in the present
 13260	    formula. However, it is given in XIII, which bears the
 13261	    same name.
 13262	
 13263	    Dann. is obviously mistaken in styling this preparation
 13264	    "oil broth."
 13265	
 13266	
 13267	
 13268	XII
 13269	
 13270	
 13271	[480] [Cold Sauce for] SARDINES MAKE THUS:
 13272	    _SARDAS _[1]_ SIC FACIES_
 13273	
 13274	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE SEED, ORIGANY, DRY ONIONS, HARD BOILED YOLKS,
 13275	VINEGAR, OIL; THIS MUST BE COMBINED INTO ONE [2] AND UNDERLAID.
 13276	
 13277	    [1] A kind of small tunny, which, like our herring, used
 13278	    to be pickled or salt, corresponding to the anchovy. A
 13279	    "sardine," from the island of Sardinia; _Sardus_, the
 13280	    inhabitant of Sardinia.
 13281	
 13282	    [2] The absence of detailed instructions as to the
 13283	    manipulation of the yolks, oil and vinegar is
 13284	    regrettable; upon them depends the certainty or
 13285	    uncertainty of whether the ancients had our modern
 13286	    mayonnaise.
 13287	
 13288	
 13289	
 13290	XIII
 13291	
 13292	
 13293	[481] FISH STEWED IN WINE
 13294	    _PISCES ŒNOTEGANON_ [1]
 13295	
 13296	RAW FISH ANY KIND YOU PREFER, WASH [prepare, cut into handy size]
 13297	ARRANGE IN A SAUCE PAN; ADD OIL, BROTH, VINEGAR, A BUNCH OF LEEKS AND
 13298	[fresh] CORIANDER, AND COOK: [Meanwhile] CRUSH PEPPER, ORIGANY, LOVAGE
 13299	WITH THE BUNCHES OF LEEKS AND CORIANDER WHICH YOU HAVE COOKED [with
 13300	the fish] AND POUR [this preparation] INTO THE SAUCE PAN. [When the
 13301	fish is done, retire it and arrange the pieces in the serving dish,
 13302	casserole, bowl or platter] BRING THE RESIDUE IN THE SAUCE PAN TO A
 13303	BOILING POINT, ALLOW IT TO REDUCE SLOWLY TO THE RIGHT CONSISTENCY
 13304	[Strain the sauce of the fish] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
 13305	
 13306	    [1] Cf. note to XI. This _œnoteganon_ resembles the
 13307	    _Bouillabaisse_, the famous Marseilles fish chowder. In
 13308	    addition to the above manner it is flavored with
 13309	    saffron. An excellent dish, especially with the
 13310	    judicious addition of onions, parsley, a suspicion of
 13311	    garlic and small sippets of toasted bread.
 13312	
 13313	
 13314	
 13315	XIV
 13316	
 13317	
 13318	[482] MULLET STEWED WITH DILL MAKE THUS:
 13319	    _MULLOS ANETHATOS _[1]_ SIC FACIES_
 13320	
 13321	PREPARE THE FISH [clean, wash, trim, cut into pieces] AND PLACE IN A
 13322	SAUCE PAN, ADDING OIL, BROTH, WINE, BUNCHES OF LEEKS, [fresh]
 13323	CORIANDER, [fresh dill]; PLACE ON FIRE TO COOK. [Meanwhile] PUT PEPPER
 13324	IN THE MORTAR, POUND IT, ADD OIL, AND ONE PART OF VINEGAR AND RAISIN
 13325	WINE TO TASTE. [This preparation] TRANSFER INTO A SAUCE PAN, PLACE ON
 13326	THE FIRE TO HEAT, TIE WITH ROUX, ADD TO THE FISH IN THE SAUCE PAN.
 13327	SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND SERVE.
 13328	
 13329	    [1] From _anethus_--dill--which is omitted in formula.
 13330	    Sch. _anecatos_, i.e. _submersos_, because the original
 13331	    fails to state the dill in the formula. Such conjecture
 13332	    is not justified.
 13333	
 13334	
 13335	
 13336	XV
 13337	
 13338	
 13339	[483] MULLET ANOTHER STYLE
 13340	    _ALITER MULLOS_
 13341	
 13342	SCRAPE, WASH, PLACE [the fish] IN A SAUCE PAN, ADD OIL, BROTH, WINE
 13343	AND A BUNCH OF LEEKS AND [fresh] CORIANDER TO THE MESS, SET ON THE
 13344	FIRE TO COOK. CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, MOISTEN WITH SOME OF THE
 13345	FISH'S OWN LIQUOR [from the sauce pan] ADD RAISIN WINE TO TASTE, PUT
 13346	IT INTO A POT AND ON THE FIRE TO HEAT; TIE WITH ROUX AND PRESENTLY ADD
 13347	IT TO THE CONTENTS IN THE SAUCE PAN [1] SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER AND
 13348	SERVE.
 13349	
 13350	    [1] It appears that the _patina_ mentioned in this and
 13351	    in the foregoing formula is either a finely wrought
 13352	    metal sauce pan or chafing dish, or a plainer _cumana_,
 13353	    an earthenware casserole; either of which may be used
 13354	    for service at the table.
 13355	
 13356	    It may be noticed how this manner of preparing fish has
 13357	    a tendency to preserve all the savory flavors and juices
 13358	    of the fish, a process in this respect both rational and
 13359	    economical.
 13360	
 13361	
 13362	
 13363	XVI
 13364	
 13365	
 13366	[484] MURENA [1], EEL [2] OR MULLET MAKE THUS:
 13367	    _MURENAM AUT ANGUILLAS VEL MULLOS SIC FACIES_
 13368	
 13369	CLEAN THE FISH AND CAREFULLY PLACE IN A SAUCE PAN. IN THE MORTAR PUT
 13370	PEPPER, LOVAGE, ORIGANY, MINT, DRY ONIONS, CRUSH, MOISTEN WITH A SMALL
 13371	GLASS OF WINE, HALF OF THAT OF BROTH, AND OF HONEY ONE THIRD PART, AND
 13372	A MODERATE AMOUNT OF REDUCED MUST, SAY A SPOONFUL. IT IS NECESSARY
 13373	THAT THE FISH BE ENTIRELY COVERED BY THIS LIQUOR SO THAT THERE MAY BE
 13374	SUFFICIENT JUICE DURING THE COOKING.
 13375	
 13376	    [1] The ancients considered the murena one of the finest
 13377	    of fish; the best were brought from the straits of
 13378	    Sicily. Rich Romans kept them alive in their fish ponds,
 13379	    often large and elaborate marble basins called,
 13380	    _piscina_, fattened the fish, kept it ready for use.
 13381	    Pollio fattened murenas on human flesh, killing a slave
 13382	    on the slightest provocation and throwing the body into
 13383	    the fish pond; he would eat only the liver of such
 13384	    murenas. This is the only case of such cruelty on
 13385	    record, and it has often been cited and exaggerated.
 13386	
 13387	    [2] Perhaps the sea-eel, or conger, according to Dann.
 13388	    Also very much esteemed. The witty Plautus names a cook
 13389	    in one of his comedies "Congrio," because the fellow was
 13390	    "slippery."
 13391	
 13392	
 13393	
 13394	XVII
 13395	
 13396	
 13397	[485] [Dressing for] SPINY LOBSTER (AND SQUILL)
 13398	    _LOCUSTAM (ET SCILLAM)_ [1]
 13399	
 13400	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CELERY SEED, POUR IN VINEGAR, BROTH, YOLKS OF
 13401	[hard boiled] EGGS, MIX WELL TOGETHER [2] AND DRESS [the boiled
 13402	shellfish meat with it] AND SERVE.
 13403	
 13404	    [1] Cf. Summary of Dishes.
 13405	
 13406	    [2] Another of Apicii hasty and laconic formulæ. No
 13407	    indication as to how to use the ingredients named.
 13408	    According to our notion of eating, there is only one
 13409	    way: The shellfish is boiled in aromatic water, allowed
 13410	    to cool off; the meat is then taken out of the shells;
 13411	    the above named ingredients are combined in a manner of
 13412	    a mayonnaise or a vinaigrette, although the necessary
 13413	    oil is not mentioned here. The dressing is poured over
 13414	    the shellfish meat, and the result is a sort of salad or
 13415	    "cocktail" as we have today.
 13416	
 13417	
 13418	
 13419	XVIII
 13420	
 13421	
 13422	[486] [Sauce] FOR BOILED FISH
 13423	    _IN PISCIBUS ELIXIS_
 13424	
 13425	CRUSH PEPPER, LOVAGE, CELERY SEED, ORIGANY WHICH MOISTEN WITH VINEGAR;
 13426	ADD PINE NUTS, FIGDATES [1] IN SUFFICIENT QUANTITY, HONEY, VINEGAR,
 13427	BROTH, MUSTARD, MIX AND COMBINE PROPERLY AND BRING FORTH.
 13428	
 13429	    [1] Dann. is undecided as to whether this is dates or
 13430	    date wine; Goll. thinks it is mustard seed, which is not
 13431	    so bad gastronomically; but the original leaves no room
 13432	    for any doubt.
 13433	
 13434	
 13435	
 13436	XIX
 13437	
 13438	
 13439	[487] A DISH OF SOLE WITH EGGS
 13440	    _PATINA SOLEARUM EX OVIS_
 13441	
 13442	SCALE [skin] CLEAN [the soles], PLACE IN A [shallow] SAUCE PAN, ADD
 13443	BROTH, OIL [white] WINE, A BUNCH OF LEEKS AND CORIANDER SEED, PLACE ON
 13444	FIRE TO COOK, GRIND A LITTLE PEPPER, ORIGANY, MOISTEN WITH THE FISH
 13445	LIQUOR [from the sauce pan]. TAKE 10 RAW EGGS, BEAT THEM AND MIX WITH
 13446	THE REMAINING LIQUOR; PUT IT ALL BACK OVER THE FISH, AND ON A SLOW
 13447	FIRE ALLOW TO HEAT [without boiling] AND THICKEN TO THE RIGHT
 13448	CONSISTENCY; SPRINKLE WITH PEPPER [1].
 13449	
 13450	    [1] Very similar to _Sole au vin blanc_. Cf. ℞ No.
 13451	    155.
 13452	
 13453	
 13454	
 13455	XX
 13456	
 13457	
 13458	[488] SUCKLING PIG, CORIANDER SAUCE
 13459	    _PORCELLUM CORIANDRATUM_
 13460	
 13461	ROAST THE PIG CAREFULLY; MAKE THUS A MORTAR MIXTURE: POUND PEPPER,
 13462	DILL, ORIGANY, GREEN CORIANDER, MOISTEN WITH HONEY, WINE, BROTH, OIL,
 13463	VINEGAR, REDUCED MUST. ALL OF THIS WHEN HOT POUR OVER [the roast]
 13464	SPRINKLE RAISINS, PINE NUTS AND CHOPPED ONIONS OVER AND SO SERVE.
 13465	
 13466	
 13467	
 13468	XXI
 13469	
 13470	
 13471	[489] SUCKLING PIG, WINE SAUCE
 13472	    _PORCELLUM ÆNOCOCTUM_ [1]
 13473	
 13474	TAKE THE PIG, GARNISH [with a marinade of herbs, etc.] COOK [roast] IT
 13475	WITH OIL AND BROTH. WHEN DONE, PUT IN THE MORTAR PEPPER, RUE, LAUREL
 13476	BERRIES, BROTH, RAISIN WINE OR REDUCED WINE, OLD WINE, CRUSH ALL, MIX
 13477	AND PREPARE TO A POINT; DRESS THE PIG ON A SHOWY SERVICE [2] PLATTER
 13478	AND SERVE.
 13479	
 13480	    [1] i.e. _œnococtum_, cooked or prepared in wine
 13481	    sauce.
 13482	
 13483	    [2] Dann. is of the opinion that the pig is cooked in a
 13484	    copper vessel, because the instructions are to serve it
 13485	    _in patinam aheneam_.
 13486	
 13487	
 13488	
 13489	XXII
 13490	
 13491	
 13492	[490] PIG, PAN GRAVY
 13493	    _PORCELLUM EO IURE_
 13494	
 13495	ROAST THE PIG IN ITS OWN JUICE; [when done] RETIRE; BIND THE GRAVY
 13496	WITH ROUX; [strain] PUT IN A SAUCE BOAT AND SERVE.
 13497	
 13498	
 13499	
 13500	XXIII
 13501	
 13502	
 13503	[491] PIG SPRINKLED WITH THYME
 13504	    _PORCELLUM THYMO SPARSUM_
 13505	
 13506	MILK-FED PIG, KILLED ON THE PREVIOUS DAY, BOIL WITH SALT AND DILL;
 13507	TRANSFER IT INTO COLD WATER, CAREFULLY KEEPING IT SUBMERGED, TO
 13508	PRESERVE ITS WHITENESS. THEREUPON [make a cold dressing of the
 13509	following] GREEN SAVORY HERBS, [fresh] THYME, A LITTLE FLEABANE, HARD
 13510	BOILED EGGS, ONIONS, [everything] CHOPPED FINE, SPRINKLE EVERYTHING
 13511	[over the pig which has been taken out of the water and allowed to
 13512	drip off] AND SEASON WITH A PINT OF BROTH, ONE MEASURE OF OIL, ONE OF
 13513	RAISIN WINE, AND SO PRESENT IT [1].
 13514	
 13515	    [1] We would first mix the liquid components of this
 13516	    dressing with the chopped ingredients and then spread
 13517	    the finished dressing over the pig. Our author, no
 13518	    doubt, had this very process in mind.
 13519	
 13520	
 13521	
 13522	XXIV
 13523	
 13524	
 13525	[492] PICKLED SUCKLING PIG
 13526	    _PORCELLUM OXYZOMUM_ [1]
 13527	
 13528	GARNISH [prepare and marinate] THE PIG CORRECTLY AND PLACE IT IN A
 13529	LIQUOR PREPARED AS FOLLOWS: PUT IN THE MORTAR 50 GRAINS OF PEPPER, AS
 13530	MUCH HONEY [2] AS IS REQUIRED, 3 DRY ONIONS, A LITTLE GREEN OR DRY
 13531	CORIANDER, A PINT OF BROTH, 1 SEXTARIUS OF OIL, 1 PINT OF WATER; [all
 13532	this] PUT IN A STEW PAN [braisière] PLACE THE PIG IN IT; WHEN IT
 13533	COMMENCES TO BOIL, STIR THE GRAVY QUITE FREQUENTLY [3] SO AS TO
 13534	THICKEN IT. SHOULD THE BROTH THUS BE REDUCED [by evaporation] ADD
 13535	ANOTHER PINT OF WATER. IN THIS MANNER COOK [braise] THE PIG TO
 13536	PERFECTION AND SERVE IT.
 13537	
 13538	    [1] _exodionum_, and in the Summary of Dishes,
 13539	    _exozome_, i.e. _oxyzomum_. It is curious to note the
 13540	    various spellings and meanings of _oxyzomum_. This is
 13541	    supposed to be a sour sauce or an acid preparation of
 13542	    some kind, yet this recipe does not mention acids. In
 13543	    fact, the presence of honey would make it a sweet
 13544	    preparation. We take it, the "garnish" contains the
 13545	    necessary vinegar or other acids such as lemon juice,
 13546	    wine, etc. _Oxyzomum_ is properly rendered "pickle."
 13547	
 13548	    [2] Dann. oil, occurring twice in his version.
 13549	
 13550	    [3] _sæpius_; Dann. confusing _sæpe_ with _cæpa_,
 13551	    renders this "onions sauce." The same occurs to him in
 13552	    XXVII.
 13553	
 13554	
 13555	
 13556	XXV
 13557	
 13558	
 13559	[493] PIG WITH LASER
 13560	    _PORCELLUM LASARATUM_
 13561	
 13562	IN THE MORTAR POUND PEPPER, LOVAGE, CARRAWAY, A LITTLE CUMIN, LIVE
 13563	LASER, LASER ROOT, MOISTEN WITH VINEGAR, ADD PINE NUTS, FIGDATES,
 13564	HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH, PREPARED MUSTARD, FINISH WITH OIL TO TASTE, AND
 13565	POUR OVER [the roast pig].
 13566	
 13567	
 13568	
 13569	XXVI
 13570	
 13571	
 13572	[494] PIG IN SAUCE
 13573	    _PORCELLUM IUSCELLATUM_
 13574	
 13575	IN THE MORTAR PUT PEPPER, LOVAGE, OR ANISE, CORIANDER, RUE, A LAUREL
 13576	BERRY, POUND [all], MOISTENING WITH BROTH, [add] LEEKS, RAISIN WINE,
 13577	OR A LITTLE HONEY, A LITTLE WINE, AND A LIKE AMOUNT OF OIL. WHEN THIS
 13578	HAS BEEN COOKED TIE WITH ROUX.
 13579	
 13580	
 13581	
 13582	XXVII
 13583	
 13584	
 13585	[495] PLAIN LAMB [1]
 13586	    _AGNUM SIMPLICEM_
 13587	
 13588	OF THE SKINNED LAMB MAKE SMALL CUTLETS WHICH WASH CAREFULLY AND
 13589	ARRANGE IN A SAUCE PAN, ADD OIL, BROTH, WINE, LEEKS, CORIANDER CUT
 13590	WITH THE KNIFE; WHEN IT COMMENCES TO BOIL, STIR VERY FREQUENTLY [2]
 13591	AND SERVE.
 13592	
 13593	    [1] Unquestionably the ancient equivalent for "Irish
 13594	    Stew."
 13595	
 13596	    [2] Cf. note 3 to ℞ 492, XXIV; the presence of onion,
 13597	    however, would do no harm here.
 13598	
 13599	
 13600	
 13601	XXVIII
 13602	
 13603	
 13604	[496] KID WITH LASER
 13605	    _HÆDUM LASARATUM_
 13606	
 13607	THE WELL-CLEANED GUTS OF A KID FILL WITH [a preparation of] PEPPER,
 13608	BROTH, LASER, OIL [1], AND PUT THEM BACK INTO THE CARCASS WHICH SEW
 13609	TIGHTLY AND THUS COOK [roast] THE KID [whole]. WHEN DONE PUT IN THE
 13610	MORTAR RUE, LAUREL BERRIES, AND THEN SERVE THE KID WHICH MEANWHILE HAS
 13611	BEEN RETIRED FROM THE POT WITH ITS OWN DRIPPINGS OR GRAVY.
 13612	
 13613	    [1] There being only liquids for this filling of the
 13614	    guts, a more solid substance, such as pork forcemeat,
 13615	    eggs, or cereals would be required to make an acceptable
 13616	    filling for the casings of the kid. Furthermore sausage,
 13617	    for such is this in fact, must be thoroughly cooked
 13618	    before it can be used for the filling of the carcass, as
 13619	    not sufficient heat would penetrate the interior during
 13620	    the roasting to cook any raw dressing.
 13621	
 13622	
 13623	
 13624	XXIX
 13625	
 13626	
 13627	[497] THRUSH "À LA SANTÉ"
 13628	    _TURDOS HAPANTAMYNOS_ [1]
 13629	
 13630	CRUSH PEPPER, LASER, LAUREL BERRY, MIX IN CUMIN [2] GARUM AND STUFF
 13631	THE THRUSH [with this preparation, [3]] THROUGH THE THROAT [4], TYING
 13632	THEM WITH A STRING. THEREUPON MAKE THIS PREPARATION IN WHICH THEY ARE
 13633	COOKED: CONSISTING OF OIL, SALT, WATER [5], DILL AND HEADS OF LEEKS.
 13634	
 13635	    [1] Cf. Summary of Dishes; term not identified, derived
 13636	    from the Greek, meaning to drive away all stomach ills.
 13637	
 13638	    [2] We use juniper berries today instead of cumin.
 13639	
 13640	    [3] Cf. note to ℞ 496, XXVIII.
 13641	
 13642	    [4] Thrush and other game birds of such small size are
 13643	    not emptied in the usual way: they are cooked with the
 13644	    entrails, or, the intestines are taken out, seasoned,
 13645	    sauté, and are either put back into the carcasses, or
 13646	    are served separately on bread croutons. In this
 13647	    instance, the necessary seasoning is introduced through
 13648	    the throat, a most ingenious idea that can only occur to
 13649	    Apicius.
 13650	
 13651	    [5] In other instances we have pointed out where a small
 13652	    amount of water was used to clarify the oil used for
 13653	    frying foods. The presence here of water leads us to
 13654	    believe that the thrush were not "cooked," i.e. "boiled"
 13655	    but that they were fried in a generous amount of oil;
 13656	    this would make the ancient process remarkably similar
 13657	    to the present European way of preparing thrush or
 13658	    fieldfare, or similar game birds.
 13659	
 13660	    For water used to clarify oil see note 3 to ℞ No.
 13661	    250.
 13662	
 13663	
 13664	
 13665	XXX
 13666	
 13667	
 13668	[498] TURTLEDOVES
 13669	    _TURTURES_
 13670	
 13671	OPEN THEM, PREPARE [marinate] CAREFULLY; CRUSH PEPPER, LASER, A LITTLE
 13672	BROTH, IMMERSE THE DOVES IN THIS PREPARATION SO THAT IT WILL BE
 13673	ABSORBED BY THEM, AND THUS ROAST THEM.
 13674	
 13675	
 13676	
 13677	XXXI
 13678	
 13679	
 13680	[499] SAUCE FOR PARTRIDGE [1]
 13681	    _IUS IN PERDICES_
 13682	
 13683	CRUSH IN THE MORTAR PEPPER, CELERY, MINT, AND RUE; MOISTEN WITH
 13684	VINEGAR, ADD FIGDATE [wine], HONEY, VINEGAR, BROTH, OIL; LET IT BOIL
 13685	LIKEWISE AND SERVE.
 13686	
 13687	    [1] This formula evidently is a fragment.
